THE ESCAPE\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER I.\r\nPARIS: SEPTEMBER, 1792\r\n\r\n\r\nA surging, seething, murmuring crowd of beings that are human only in\r\nname, for to the eye and ear they seem naught but savage creatures,\r\nanimated by vile passions and by the lust of vengeance and of hate. The\r\nhour, some little time before sunset, and the place, the West\r\nBarricade, at the very spot where, a decade later, a proud tyrant\r\nraised an undying monument to the nation\xe2\x80\x99s glory and his own vanity.\r\n\r\nDuring the greater part of the day the guillotine had been kept busy at\r\nits ghastly work: all that France had boasted of in the past centuries,\r\nof ancient names, and blue blood, had paid toll to her desire for\r\nliberty and for fraternity. The carnage had only ceased at this late\r\nhour of the day because there were other more interesting sights for\r\nthe people to witness, a little while before the final closing of the\r\nbarricades for the night.\r\n\r\nAnd so the crowd rushed away from the Place de la Gr\xc3\xa8ve and made for\r\nthe various barricades in order to watch this interesting and amusing\r\nsight.\r\n\r\nIt was to be seen every day, for those aristos were such fools! They\r\nwere traitors to the people of course, all of them, men, women, and\r\nchildren, who happened to be descendants of the great men who since the\r\nCrusades had made the glory of France: her old _noblesse_. Their\r\nancestors had oppressed the people, had crushed them under the scarlet\r\nheels of their dainty buckled shoes, and now the people had become the\r\nrulers of France and crushed their former masters\xe2\x80\x94not beneath their\r\nheel, for they went shoeless mostly in these days\xe2\x80\x94but beneath a more\r\neffectual weight, the knife of the guillotine.\r\n\r\nAnd daily, hourly, the hideous instrument of torture claimed its many\r\nvictims\xe2\x80\x94old men, young women, tiny children, even until the day when it\r\nwould finally demand the head of a King and of a beautiful young Queen.\r\n\r\nBut this was as it should be: were not the people now the rulers of\r\nFrance? Every aristocrat was a traitor, as his ancestors had been\r\nbefore him: for two hundred years now the people had sweated, and\r\ntoiled, and starved, to keep a lustful court in lavish extravagance;\r\nnow the descendants of those who had helped to make those courts\r\nbrilliant had to hide for their lives\xe2\x80\x94to fly, if they wished to avoid\r\nthe tardy vengeance of the people.\r\n\r\nAnd they did try to hide, and tried to fly: that was just the fun of\r\nthe whole thing. Every afternoon before the gates closed and the market\r\ncarts went out in procession by the various barricades, some fool of an\r\naristo endeavoured to evade the clutches of the Committee of Public\r\nSafety. In various disguises, under various pretexts, they tried to\r\nslip through the barriers which were so well guarded by citizen\r\nsoldiers of the Republic. Men in women\xe2\x80\x99s clothes, women in male attire,\r\nchildren disguised in beggars\xe2\x80\x99 rags: there were some of all sorts:\r\n_ci-devant_ counts, marquises, even dukes, who wanted to fly from\r\nFrance, reach England or some other equally accursed country, and there\r\ntry to rouse foreign feeling against the glorious Revolution, or to\r\nraise an army in order to liberate the wretched prisoners in the\r\nTemple, who had once called themselves sovereigns of France.\r\n\r\nBut they were nearly always caught at the barricades. Sergeant Bibot\r\nespecially at the West Gate had a wonderful nose for scenting an aristo\r\nin the most perfect disguise. Then, of course, the fun began. Bibot\r\nwould look at his prey as a cat looks upon the mouse, play with him,\r\nsometimes for quite a quarter of an hour, pretend to be hoodwinked by\r\nthe disguise, by the wigs and other bits of theatrical make-up which\r\nhid the identity of a _ci-devant_ noble marquise or count.\r\n\r\nOh! Bibot had a keen sense of humour, and it was well worth hanging\r\nround that West Barricade, in order to see him catch an aristo in the\r\nvery act of trying to flee from the vengeance of the people.\r\n\r\nSometimes Bibot would let his prey actually out by the gates, allowing\r\nhim to think for the space of two minutes at least that he really had\r\nescaped out of Paris, and might even manage to reach the coast of\r\nEngland in safety, but Bibot would let the unfortunate wretch walk\r\nabout ten m\xc3\xa8tres towards the open country, then he would send two men\r\nafter him and bring him back, stripped of his disguise.\r\n\r\nOh! that was extremely funny, for as often as not the fugitive would\r\nprove to be a woman, some proud marchioness, who looked terribly\r\ncomical when she found herself in Bibot\xe2\x80\x99s clutches after all, and knew\r\nthat a summary trial would await her the next day and after that, the\r\nfond embrace of Madame la Guillotine.\r\n\r\nNo wonder that on this fine afternoon in September the crowd round\r\nBibot\xe2\x80\x99s gate was eager and excited. The lust of blood grows with its\r\nsatisfaction, there is no satiety: the crowd had seen a hundred noble\r\nheads fall beneath the guillotine to-day, it wanted to make sure that\r\nit would see another hundred fall on the morrow.\r\n\r\nBibot was sitting on an overturned and empty cask close by the gate of\r\nthe barricade; a small detachment of citoyen soldiers was under his\r\ncommand. The work had been very hot lately. Those cursed aristos were\r\nbecoming terrified and tried their hardest to slip out of Paris: men,\r\nwomen and children, whose ancestors, even in remote ages, had served\r\nthose traitorous Bourbons, were all traitors themselves and right food\r\nfor the guillotine. Every day Bibot had had the satisfaction of\r\nunmasking some fugitive royalists and sending them back to be tried by\r\nthe Committee of Public Safety, presided over by that good patriot,\r\nCitoyen Foucquier-Tinville.\r\n\r\nRobespierre and Danton both had commended Bibot for his zeal, and Bibot\r\nwas proud of the fact that he on his own initiative had sent at least\r\nfifty aristos to the guillotine.\r\n\r\nBut to-day all the sergeants in command at the various barricades had\r\nhad special orders. Recently a very great number of aristos had\r\nsucceeded in escaping out of France and in reaching England safely.\r\nThere were curious rumours about these escapes; they had become very\r\nfrequent and singularly daring; the people\xe2\x80\x99s minds were becoming\r\nstrangely excited about it all. Sergeant Grospierre had been sent to\r\nthe guillotine for allowing a whole family of aristos to slip out of\r\nthe North Gate under his very nose.\r\n\r\nIt was asserted that these escapes were organised by a band of\r\nEnglishmen, whose daring seemed to be unparalleled, and who, from sheer\r\ndesire to meddle in what did not concern them, spent their spare time\r\nin snatching away lawful victims destined for Madame la Guillotine.\r\nThese rumours soon grew in extravagance; there was no doubt that this\r\nband of meddlesome Englishmen did exist; moreover, they seemed to be\r\nunder the leadership of a man whose pluck and audacity were almost\r\nfabulous. Strange stories were afloat of how he and those aristos whom\r\nhe rescued became suddenly invisible as they reached the barricades and\r\nescaped out of the gates by sheer supernatural agency.\r\n\r\nNo one had seen these mysterious Englishmen; as for their leader, he\r\nwas never spoken of, save with a superstitious shudder. Citoyen\r\nFoucquier-Tinville would in the course of the day receive a scrap of\r\npaper from some mysterious source; sometimes he would find it in the\r\npocket of his coat, at others it would be handed to him by someone in\r\nthe crowd, whilst he was on his way to the sitting of the Committee of\r\nPublic Safety. The paper always contained a brief notice that the band\r\nof meddlesome Englishmen were at work, and it was always signed with a\r\ndevice drawn in red\xe2\x80\x94a little star-shaped flower, which we in England\r\ncall the Scarlet Pimpernel. Within a few hours of the receipt of this\r\nimpudent notice, the citoyens of the Committee of Public Safety would\r\nhear that so many royalists and aristocrats had succeeded in reaching\r\nthe coast, and were on their way to England and safety.\r\n\r\nThe guards at the gates had been doubled, the sergeants in command had\r\nbeen threatened with death, whilst liberal rewards were offered for the\r\ncapture of these daring and impudent Englishmen. There was a sum of\r\nfive thousand francs promised to the man who laid hands on the\r\nmysterious and elusive Scarlet Pimpernel.\r\n\r\nEveryone felt that Bibot would be that man, and Bibot allowed that\r\nbelief to take firm root in everybody\xe2\x80\x99s mind; and so, day after day,\r\npeople came to watch him at the West Gate, so as to be present when he\r\nlaid hands on any fugitive aristo who perhaps might be accompanied by\r\nthat mysterious Englishman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBah!\xe2\x80\x9d he said to his trusted corporal, \xe2\x80\x9cCitoyen Grospierre was a fool!\r\nHad it been me now, at that North Gate last week . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCitoyen Bibot spat on the ground to express his contempt for his\r\ncomrade\xe2\x80\x99s stupidity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow did it happen, citoyen?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the corporal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGrospierre was at the gate, keeping good watch,\xe2\x80\x9d began Bibot,\r\npompously, as the crowd closed in round him, listening eagerly to his\r\nnarrative. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve all heard of this meddlesome Englishman, this\r\naccursed Scarlet Pimpernel. He won\xe2\x80\x99t get through _my_ gate, _morbleu!_\r\nunless he be the devil himself. But Grospierre was a fool. The market\r\ncarts were going through the gates; there was one laden with casks, and\r\ndriven by an old man, with a boy beside him. Grospierre was a bit\r\ndrunk, but he thought himself very clever; he looked into the\r\ncasks\xe2\x80\x94most of them, at least\xe2\x80\x94and saw they were empty, and let the cart\r\ngo through.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA murmur of wrath and contempt went round the group of ill-clad\r\nwretches, who crowded round Citoyen Bibot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHalf an hour later,\xe2\x80\x9d continued the sergeant, \xe2\x80\x9cup comes a captain of\r\nthe guard with a squad of some dozen soldiers with him. \xe2\x80\x98Has a cart\r\ngone through?\xe2\x80\x99 he asks of Grospierre, breathlessly. \xe2\x80\x98Yes,\xe2\x80\x99 says\r\nGrospierre, \xe2\x80\x98not half an hour ago.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98And you have let them escape,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nshouts the captain furiously. \xe2\x80\x98You\xe2\x80\x99ll go to the guillotine for this,\r\ncitoyen sergeant! that cart held concealed the _ci-devant_ Duc de\r\nChalis and all his family!\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98What!\xe2\x80\x99 thunders Grospierre, aghast. \xe2\x80\x98Aye!\r\nand the driver was none other than that cursed Englishman, the Scarlet\r\nPimpernel.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA howl of execration greeted this tale. Citoyen Grospierre had paid for\r\nhis blunder on the guillotine, but what a fool! oh! what a fool!\r\n\r\nBibot was laughing so much at his own tale that it was some time before\r\nhe could continue.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98After them, my men,\xe2\x80\x99 shouts the captain,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, after a while,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98remember the reward; after them, they cannot have gone far!\xe2\x80\x99 And with\r\nthat he rushes through the gate, followed by his dozen soldiers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it was too late!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted the crowd, excitedly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey never got them!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCurse that Grospierre for his folly!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe deserved his fate!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFancy not examining those casks properly!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut these sallies seemed to amuse Citoyen Bibot exceedingly; he laughed\r\nuntil his sides ached, and the tears streamed down his cheeks.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, nay!\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last, \xe2\x80\x9cthose aristos weren\xe2\x80\x99t in the cart; the\r\ndriver was not the Scarlet Pimpernel!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo! The captain of the guard was that damned Englishman in disguise,\r\nand every one of his soldiers aristos!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe crowd this time said nothing: the story certainly savoured of the\r\nsupernatural, and though the Republic had abolished God, it had not\r\nquite succeeded in killing the fear of the supernatural in the hearts\r\nof the people. Truly that Englishman must be the devil himself.\r\n\r\nThe sun was sinking low down in the west. Bibot prepared himself to\r\nclose the gates.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_En avant_ the carts,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nSome dozen covered carts were drawn up in a row, ready to leave town,\r\nin order to fetch the produce from the country close by, for market the\r\nnext morning. They were mostly well known to Bibot, as they went\r\nthrough his gate twice every day on their way to and from the town. He\r\nspoke to one or two of their drivers\xe2\x80\x94mostly women\xe2\x80\x94and was at great\r\npains to examine the inside of the carts.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou never know,\xe2\x80\x9d he would say, \xe2\x80\x9cand I\xe2\x80\x99m not going to be caught like\r\nthat fool Grospierre.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe women who drove the carts usually spent their day on the Place de\r\nla Gr\xc3\xa8ve, beneath the platform of the guillotine, knitting and\r\ngossiping, whilst they watched the rows of tumbrils arriving with the\r\nvictims the Reign of Terror claimed every day. It was great fun to see\r\nthe aristos arriving for the reception of Madame la Guillotine, and the\r\nplaces close by the platform were very much sought after. Bibot, during\r\nthe day, had been on duty on the Place. He recognized most of the old\r\nhags, \xe2\x80\x9ctricotteuses,\xe2\x80\x9d as they were called, who sat there and knitted,\r\nwhilst head after head fell beneath the knife, and they themselves got\r\nquite bespattered with the blood of those cursed aristos.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cH\xc3\xa9! la m\xc3\xa8re!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bibot to one of these horrible hags, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat have you\r\ngot there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe had seen her earlier in the day, with her knitting and the whip of\r\nher cart close beside her. Now she had fastened a row of curly locks to\r\nthe whip handle, all colours, from gold to silver, fair to dark, and\r\nshe stroked them with her huge, bony fingers as she laughed at Bibot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI made friends with Madame Guillotine\xe2\x80\x99s lover,\xe2\x80\x9d she said with a coarse\r\nlaugh, \xe2\x80\x9che cut these off for me from the heads as they rolled down. He\r\nhas promised me some more to-morrow, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t know if I shall be at\r\nmy usual place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! how is that, la m\xc3\xa8re?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Bibot, who, hardened soldier though\r\nhe was, could not help shuddering at the awful loathsomeness of this\r\nsemblance of a woman, with her ghastly trophy on the handle of her\r\nwhip.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy grandson has got the small-pox,\xe2\x80\x9d she said with a jerk of her thumb\r\ntowards the inside of her cart, \xe2\x80\x9csome say it\xe2\x80\x99s the plague! If it is, I\r\nsha\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t be allowed to come into Paris to-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt the first mention of the word small-pox, Bibot had stepped hastily\r\nbackwards, and when the old hag spoke of the plague, he retreated from\r\nher as fast as he could.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCurse you!\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered, whilst the whole crowd hastily avoided the\r\ncart, leaving it standing all alone in the midst of the place.\r\n\r\nThe old hag laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCurse you, citoyen, for being a coward,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cBah! what a man to\r\nbe afraid of sickness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Morbleu!_ the plague!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEveryone was awe-struck and silent, filled with horror for the\r\nloathsome malady, the one thing which still had the power to arouse\r\nterror and disgust in these savage, brutalised creatures.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGet out with you and with your plague-stricken brood!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Bibot,\r\nhoarsely.\r\n\r\nAnd with another rough laugh and coarse jest, the old hag whipped up\r\nher lean nag and drove her cart out of the gate.\r\n\r\nThis incident had spoilt the afternoon. The people were terrified of\r\nthese two horrible curses, the two maladies which nothing could cure,\r\nand which were the precursors of an awful and lonely death. They hung\r\nabout the barricades, silent and sullen for a while, eyeing one another\r\nsuspiciously, avoiding each other as if by instinct, lest the plague\r\nlurked already in their midst. Presently, as in the case of Grospierre,\r\na captain of the guard appeared suddenly. But he was known to Bibot,\r\nand there was no fear of his turning out to be a sly Englishman in\r\ndisguise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA cart, . . .\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted breathlessly, even before he had reached the\r\ngates.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat cart?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Bibot, roughly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDriven by an old hag. . . . A covered cart . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere were a dozen . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn old hag who said her son had the plague?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have not let them go?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Morbleu!_\xe2\x80\x9d said Bibot, whose purple cheeks had suddenly become white\r\nwith fear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe cart contained the _ci-devant_ Comtesse de Tournay and her two\r\nchildren, all of them traitors and condemned to death.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd their driver?\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Bibot, as a superstitious shudder ran down\r\nhis spine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Sacr\xc3\xa9 tonnerre_,\xe2\x80\x9d said the captain, \xe2\x80\x9cbut it is feared that it was\r\nthat accursed Englishman himself\xe2\x80\x94the Scarlet Pimpernel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER II.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTHE FISHERMAN\xe2\x80\x99S REST\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nIn the kitchen Sally was extremely busy\xe2\x80\x94saucepans and frying-pans were\r\nstanding in rows on the gigantic hearth, the huge stock-pot stood in a\r\ncorner, and the jack turned with slow deliberation, and presented\r\nalternately to the glow every side of a noble sirloin of beef. The two\r\nlittle kitchen-maids bustled around, eager to help, hot and panting,\r\nwith cotton sleeves well tucked up above the dimpled elbows, and\r\ngiggling over some private jokes of their own, whenever Miss Sally\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nback was turned for a moment. And old Jemima, stolid in temper and\r\nsolid in bulk, kept up a long and subdued grumble, while she stirred\r\nthe stock-pot methodically over the fire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat ho! Sally!\xe2\x80\x9d came in cheerful if none too melodious accents from\r\nthe coffee-room close by.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLud bless my soul!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Sally, with a good-humoured laugh, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat\r\nbe they all wanting now, I wonder!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBeer, of course,\xe2\x80\x9d grumbled Jemima, \xe2\x80\x9cyou don\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99xpect Jimmy Pitkin to\r\n\xe2\x80\x99ave done with one tankard, do ye?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. \xe2\x80\x99Arry, \xe2\x80\x99e looked uncommon thirsty too,\xe2\x80\x9d simpered Martha, one of\r\nthe little kitchen-maids; and her beady black eyes twinkled as they met\r\nthose of her companion, whereupon both started on a round of short and\r\nsuppressed giggles.\r\n\r\nSally looked cross for a moment, and thoughtfully rubbed her hands\r\nagainst her shapely hips; her palms were itching, evidently, to come in\r\ncontact with Martha\xe2\x80\x99s rosy cheeks\xe2\x80\x94but inherent good-humour prevailed,\r\nand with a pout and a shrug of the shoulders, she turned her attention\r\nto the fried potatoes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat ho, Sally! hey, Sally!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd a chorus of pewter mugs, tapped with impatient hands against the\r\noak tables of the coffee-room, accompanied the shouts for mine host\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbuxom daughter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSally!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted a more persistent voice, \xe2\x80\x9care ye goin\xe2\x80\x99 to be all night\r\nwith that there beer?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do think father might get the beer for them,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Sally, as\r\nJemima, stolidly and without further comment, took a couple of\r\nfoam-crowned jugs from the shelf, and began filling a number of pewter\r\ntankards with some of that home-brewed ale for which \xe2\x80\x9cThe Fisherman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nRest\xe2\x80\x9d had been famous since the days of King Charles. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99E knows \xe2\x80\x99ow\r\nbusy we are in \xe2\x80\x99ere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour father is too busy discussing politics with Mr. \xe2\x80\x99Empseed to worry\r\n\xe2\x80\x99isself about you and the kitchen,\xe2\x80\x9d grumbled Jemima under her breath.\r\n\r\nSally had gone to the small mirror which hung in a corner of the\r\nkitchen, and was hastily smoothing her hair and setting her frilled cap\r\nat its most becoming angle over her dark curls; then she took up the\r\ntankards by their handles, three in each strong, brown hand, and\r\nlaughing, grumbling, blushing, carried them through into the\r\ncoffee-room.\r\n\r\nThere, there was certainly no sign of that bustle and activity which\r\nkept four women busy and hot in the glowing kitchen beyond.\r\n\r\nThe coffee-room of \xe2\x80\x9cThe Fisherman\xe2\x80\x99s Rest\xe2\x80\x9d is a show place now at the\r\nbeginning of the twentieth century. At the end of the eighteenth, in\r\nthe year of grace 1792, it had not yet gained that notoriety and\r\nimportance which a hundred additional years and the craze of the age\r\nhave since bestowed upon it. Yet it was an old place, even then, for\r\nthe oak rafters and beams were already black with age\xe2\x80\x94as were the\r\npanelled seats, with their tall backs, and the long polished tables\r\nbetween, on which innumerable pewter tankards had left fantastic\r\npatterns of many-sized rings. In the leaded window, high up, a row of\r\npots of scarlet geraniums and blue larkspur gave the bright note of\r\ncolour against the dull background of the oak.\r\n\r\nThat Mr. Jellyband, landlord of \xe2\x80\x9cThe Fisherman\xe2\x80\x99s Rest\xe2\x80\x9d at Dover, was a\r\nprosperous man, was of course clear to the most casual observer. The\r\npewter on the fine old dressers, the brass above the gigantic hearth,\r\nshone like silver and gold\xe2\x80\x94the red-tiled floor was as brilliant as the\r\nscarlet geranium on the window sill\xe2\x80\x94this meant that his servants were\r\ngood and plentiful, that the custom was constant, and of that order\r\nwhich necessitated the keeping up of the coffee-room to a high standard\r\nof elegance and order.\r\n\r\nAs Sally came in, laughing through her frowns, and displaying a row of\r\ndazzling white teeth, she was greeted with shouts and chorus of\r\napplause.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, here\xe2\x80\x99s Sally! What ho, Sally! Hurrah for pretty Sally!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought you\xe2\x80\x99d grown deaf in that kitchen of yours,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Jimmy\r\nPitkin, as he passed the back of his hand across his very dry lips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll ri\xe2\x80\x99! all ri\xe2\x80\x99!\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Sally, as she deposited the freshly-filled\r\ntankards upon the tables, \xe2\x80\x9cwhy, what a \xe2\x80\x99urry, to be sure! And is your\r\ngran\xe2\x80\x99mother a-dyin\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 you wantin\xe2\x80\x99 to see the pore soul afore she\xe2\x80\x99m\r\ngone! I never see\xe2\x80\x99d such a mighty rushin\xe2\x80\x99!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA chorus of good-humoured laughter greeted this witticism, which gave\r\nthe company there present food for many jokes, for some considerable\r\ntime. Sally now seemed in less of a hurry to get back to her pots and\r\npans. A young man with fair curly hair, and eager, bright blue eyes,\r\nwas engaging most of her attention and the whole of her time, whilst\r\nbroad witticisms anent Jimmy Pitkin\xe2\x80\x99s fictitious grandmother flew from\r\nmouth to mouth, mixed with heavy puffs of pungent tobacco smoke.\r\n\r\nFacing the hearth, his legs wide apart, a long clay pipe in his mouth,\r\nstood mine host himself, worthy Mr. Jellyband, landlord of \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nFisherman\xe2\x80\x99s Rest,\xe2\x80\x9d as his father had been before him, aye, and his\r\ngrandfather and great-grandfather too, for that matter. Portly in\r\nbuild, jovial in countenance and somewhat bald of pate, Mr. Jellyband\r\nwas indeed a typical rural John Bull of those days\xe2\x80\x94the days when our\r\nprejudiced insularity was at its height, when to an Englishman, be he\r\nlord, yeoman, or peasant, the whole of the continent of Europe was a\r\nden of immorality, and the rest of the world an unexploited land of\r\nsavages and cannibals.\r\n\r\nThere he stood, mine worthy host, firm and well set up on his limbs,\r\nsmoking his long churchwarden and caring nothing for nobody at home,\r\nand despising everybody abroad. He wore the typical scarlet waistcoat,\r\nwith shiny brass buttons, the corduroy breeches, the grey worsted\r\nstockings and smart buckled shoes, that characterised every\r\nself-respecting innkeeper in Great Britain in these days\xe2\x80\x94and while\r\npretty, motherless Sally had need of four pairs of brown hands to do\r\nall the work that fell on her shapely shoulders, worthy Jellyband\r\ndiscussed the affairs of nations with his most privileged guests.\r\n\r\nThe coffee-room indeed, lighted by two well-polished lamps, which hung\r\nfrom the raftered ceiling, looked cheerful and cosy in the extreme.\r\nThrough the dense clouds of tobacco smoke that hung about in every\r\ncorner, the faces of Mr. Jellyband\xe2\x80\x99s customers appeared red and\r\npleasant to look at, and on good terms with themselves, their host and\r\nall the world; from every side of the room loud guffaws accompanied\r\npleasant, if not highly intellectual, conversation\xe2\x80\x94while Sally\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nrepeated giggles testified to the good use Mr. Harry Waite was making\r\nof the short time she seemed inclined to spare him.\r\n\r\nThey were mostly fisher-folk who patronised Mr. Jellyband\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncoffee-room, but fishermen are known to be very thirsty people; the\r\nsalt which they breathe in, when they are on the sea, accounts for\r\ntheir parched throats when on shore. But \xe2\x80\x9cThe Fisherman\xe2\x80\x99s Rest\xe2\x80\x9d was\r\nsomething more than a rendezvous for these humble folk. The London and\r\nDover coach started from the hostel daily, and passengers who had come\r\nacross the Channel, and those who started for the \xe2\x80\x9cgrand tour,\xe2\x80\x9d all\r\nbecame acquainted with Mr. Jellyband, his French wines and his\r\nhome-brewed ales.\r\n\r\nIt was towards the close of September, 1792, and the weather which had\r\nbeen brilliant and hot throughout the month had suddenly broken up; for\r\ntwo days torrents of rain had deluged the south of England, doing its\r\nlevel best to ruin what chances the apples and pears and late plums had\r\nof becoming really fine, self-respecting fruit. Even now it was beating\r\nagainst the leaded windows, and tumbling down the chimney, making the\r\ncheerful wood fire sizzle in the hearth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLud! did you ever see such a wet September, Mr. Jellyband?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mr.\r\nHempseed.\r\n\r\nHe sat in one of the seats inside the hearth, did Mr. Hempseed, for he\r\nwas an authority and an important personage not only at \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nFisherman\xe2\x80\x99s Rest,\xe2\x80\x9d where Mr. Jellyband always made a special selection\r\nof him as a foil for political arguments, but throughout the\r\nneighbourhood, where his learning and notably his knowledge of the\r\nScriptures was held in the most profound awe and respect. With one hand\r\nburied in the capacious pockets of his corduroys underneath his\r\nelaborately-worked, well-worn smock, the other holding his long clay\r\npipe, Mr. Hempseed sat there looking dejectedly across the room at the\r\nrivulets of moisture which trickled down the window panes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Mr. Jellyband, sententiously, \xe2\x80\x9cI dunno, Mr. \xe2\x80\x99Empseed, as\r\nI ever did. An\xe2\x80\x99 I\xe2\x80\x99ve been in these parts nigh on sixty years.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye! you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t rec\xe2\x80\x99llect the first three years of them sixty, Mr.\r\nJellyband,\xe2\x80\x9d quietly interposed Mr. Hempseed. \xe2\x80\x9cI dunno as I ever see\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nan infant take much note of the weather, leastways not in these parts,\r\nan\xe2\x80\x99 _I_\xe2\x80\x99ve lived \xe2\x80\x99ere nigh on seventy-five years, Mr. Jellyband.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe superiority of this wisdom was so incontestable that for the moment\r\nMr. Jellyband was not ready with his usual flow of argument.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt do seem more like April than September, don\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d continued Mr.\r\nHempseed, dolefully, as a shower of raindrops fell with a sizzle upon\r\nthe fire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye! that it do,\xe2\x80\x9d assented the worthy host, \xe2\x80\x9cbut then what can you\r\n\xe2\x80\x99xpect, Mr. \xe2\x80\x99Empseed, I says, with sich a government as we\xe2\x80\x99ve got?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Hempseed shook his head with an infinity of wisdom, tempered by\r\ndeeply-rooted mistrust of the British climate and the British\r\nGovernment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99xpect nothing, Mr. Jellyband,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cPore folks like us\r\nis of no account up there in Lunnon, I knows that, and it\xe2\x80\x99s not often\r\nas I do complain. But when it comes to sich wet weather in September,\r\nand all me fruit a-rottin\xe2\x80\x99 and a-dyin\xe2\x80\x99 like the \xe2\x80\x99Guptian mother\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfirst-born, and doin\xe2\x80\x99 no more good than they did, pore dears, save to a\r\nlot of Jews, pedlars and sich, with their oranges and sich like foreign\r\nungodly fruit, which nobody\xe2\x80\x99d buy if English apples and pears was\r\nnicely swelled. As the Scriptures say\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s quite right, Mr. \xe2\x80\x99Empseed,\xe2\x80\x9d retorted Jellyband, \xe2\x80\x9cand as I says,\r\nwhat can you \xe2\x80\x99xpect? There\xe2\x80\x99s all them Frenchy devils over the Channel\r\nyonder a-murderin\xe2\x80\x99 their king and nobility, and Mr. Pitt and Mr. Fox\r\nand Mr. Burke a-fightin\xe2\x80\x99 and a-wranglin\xe2\x80\x99 between them, if we Englishmen\r\nshould \xe2\x80\x99low them to go on in their ungodly way. \xe2\x80\x98Let \xe2\x80\x99em murder!\xe2\x80\x99 says\r\nMr. Pitt. \xe2\x80\x98Stop \xe2\x80\x99em!\xe2\x80\x99 says Mr. Burke.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd let \xe2\x80\x99em murder, says I, and be demmed to \xe2\x80\x99em,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Hempseed,\r\nemphatically, for he had but little liking for his friend Jellyband\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npolitical arguments, wherein he always got out of his depth, and had\r\nbut little chance for displaying those pearls of wisdom which had\r\nearned for him so high a reputation in the neighbourhood and so many\r\nfree tankards of ale at \xe2\x80\x9cThe Fisherman\xe2\x80\x99s Rest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet \xe2\x80\x99em murder,\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated again, \xe2\x80\x9cbut don\xe2\x80\x99t let\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x99ave sich rain in\r\nSeptember, for that is agin the law and the Scriptures which says\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLud! Mr. \xe2\x80\x99Arry, \xe2\x80\x99ow you made me jump!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was unfortunate for Sally and her flirtation that this remark of\r\nhers should have occurred at the precise moment when Mr. Hempseed was\r\ncollecting his breath, in order to deliver himself of one of those\r\nScriptural utterances which had made him famous, for it brought down\r\nupon her pretty head the full flood of her father\xe2\x80\x99s wrath.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow then, Sally, me girl, now then!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, trying to force a frown\r\nupon his good-humoured face, \xe2\x80\x9cstop that fooling with them young\r\njackanapes and get on with the work.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe work\xe2\x80\x99s gettin\xe2\x80\x99 on all ri\xe2\x80\x99, father.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Mr. Jellyband was peremptory. He had other views for his buxom\r\ndaughter, his only child, who would in God\xe2\x80\x99s good time become the owner\r\nof \xe2\x80\x9cThe Fisherman\xe2\x80\x99s Rest,\xe2\x80\x9d than to see her married to one of these\r\nyoung fellows who earned but a precarious livelihood with their net.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid ye hear me speak, me girl?\xe2\x80\x9d he said in that quiet tone, which no\r\none inside the inn dared to disobey. \xe2\x80\x9cGet on with my Lord Tony\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsupper, for, if it ain\xe2\x80\x99t the best we can do, and \xe2\x80\x99e not satisfied, see\r\nwhat you\xe2\x80\x99ll get, that\xe2\x80\x99s all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nReluctantly Sally obeyed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs you \xe2\x80\x99xpecting special guests then to-night, Mr. Jellyband?\xe2\x80\x9d asked\r\nJimmy Pitkin, in a loyal attempt to divert his host\xe2\x80\x99s attention from\r\nthe circumstances connected with Sally\xe2\x80\x99s exit from the room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye! that I be,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Jellyband, \xe2\x80\x9cfriends of my Lord Tony hisself.\r\nDukes and duchesses from over the water yonder, whom the young lord and\r\nhis friend, Sir Andrew Ffoulkes, and other young noblemen have helped\r\nout of the clutches of them murderin\xe2\x80\x99 devils.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut this was too much for Mr. Hempseed\xe2\x80\x99s querulous philosophy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLud!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat they do that for, I wonder? I don\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99old not with\r\ninterferin\xe2\x80\x99 in other folks\xe2\x80\x99 ways. As the Scriptures say\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaybe, Mr. \xe2\x80\x99Empseed,\xe2\x80\x9d interrupted Jellyband, with biting sarcasm, \xe2\x80\x9cas\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99re a personal friend of Mr. Pitt, and as you says along with Mr.\r\nFox: \xe2\x80\x98Let \xe2\x80\x99em murder!\xe2\x80\x99 says you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPardon me, Mr. Jellyband,\xe2\x80\x9d feebly protested Mr. Hempseed, \xe2\x80\x9cI dunno as\r\nI ever did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Mr. Jellyband had at last succeeded in getting upon his favourite\r\nhobby-horse, and had no intention of dismounting in any hurry.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOr maybe you\xe2\x80\x99ve made friends with some of them French chaps \xe2\x80\x99oo they\r\ndo say have come over here o\xe2\x80\x99 purpose to make us Englishmen agree with\r\ntheir murderin\xe2\x80\x99 ways.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI dunno what you mean, Mr. Jellyband,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Mr. Hempseed, \xe2\x80\x9call I\r\nknow is\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll _I_ know is,\xe2\x80\x9d loudly asserted mine host, \xe2\x80\x9cthat there was my friend\r\nPeppercorn, \xe2\x80\x99oo owns the \xe2\x80\x98Blue-Faced Boar,\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 as true and loyal an\r\nEnglishman as you\xe2\x80\x99d see in the land. And now look at \xe2\x80\x99im!\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99E made\r\nfriends with some o\xe2\x80\x99 them frog-eaters, \xe2\x80\x99obnobbed with them just as if\r\nthey was Englishmen, and not just a lot of immoral, God-forsaking\r\nfurrin\xe2\x80\x99 spies. Well! and what happened? Peppercorn \xe2\x80\x99e now ups and talks\r\nof revolutions, and liberty, and down with the aristocrats, just like\r\nMr. \xe2\x80\x99Empseed over \xe2\x80\x99ere!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPardon me, Mr. Jellyband,\xe2\x80\x9d again interposed Mr. Hempseed, feebly, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\ndunno as I ever did\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Jellyband had appealed to the company in general, who were\r\nlistening awe-struck and open-mouthed at the recital of Mr.\r\nPeppercorn\xe2\x80\x99s defalcations. At one table two customers\xe2\x80\x94gentlemen\r\napparently by their clothes\xe2\x80\x94had pushed aside their half-finished game\r\nof dominoes, and had been listening for some time, and evidently with\r\nmuch amusement at Mr. Jellyband\xe2\x80\x99s international opinions. One of them\r\nnow, with a quiet, sarcastic smile still lurking round the corners of\r\nhis mobile mouth, turned towards the centre of the room where Mr.\r\nJellyband was standing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou seem to think, mine honest friend,\xe2\x80\x9d he said quietly, \xe2\x80\x9cthat these\r\nFrenchmen\xe2\x80\x94spies I think you called them\xe2\x80\x94are mighty clever fellows to\r\nhave made mincemeat so to speak of your friend Mr. Peppercorn\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nopinions. How did they accomplish that now, think you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLud! sir, I suppose they talked \xe2\x80\x99im over. Those Frenchies, I\xe2\x80\x99ve \xe2\x80\x99eard\r\nit said, \xe2\x80\x99ave got the gift of gab\xe2\x80\x94and Mr. \xe2\x80\x99Empseed \xe2\x80\x99ere will tell you\r\n\xe2\x80\x99ow it is that they just twist some people round their little finger\r\nlike.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed, and is that so, Mr. Hempseed?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired the stranger politely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d replied Mr. Hempseed, much irritated, \xe2\x80\x9cI dunno as I can\r\ngive you the information you require.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFaith, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said the stranger, \xe2\x80\x9clet us hope, my worthy host, that\r\nthese clever spies will not succeed in upsetting your extremely loyal\r\nopinions.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut this was too much for Mr. Jellyband\xe2\x80\x99s pleasant equanimity. He burst\r\ninto an uproarious fit of laughter, which was soon echoed by those who\r\nhappened to be in his debt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHahaha! hohoho! hehehe!\xe2\x80\x9d He laughed in every key, did my worthy host,\r\nand laughed until his sides ached, and his eyes streamed. \xe2\x80\x9cAt me! hark\r\nat that! Did ye \xe2\x80\x99ear \xe2\x80\x99im say that they\xe2\x80\x99d be upsettin\xe2\x80\x99 my\r\nopinions?\xe2\x80\x94Eh?\xe2\x80\x94Lud love you, sir, but you do say some queer things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Mr. Jellyband,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Hempseed, sententiously, \xe2\x80\x9cyou know what\r\nthe Scriptures say: \xe2\x80\x98Let \xe2\x80\x99im \xe2\x80\x99oo stands take \xe2\x80\x99eed lest \xe2\x80\x99e fall.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut then hark\xe2\x80\x99ee, Mr. \xe2\x80\x99Empseed,\xe2\x80\x9d retorted Jellyband, still holding his\r\nsides with laughter, \xe2\x80\x9cthe Scriptures didn\xe2\x80\x99t know me. Why, I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t so\r\nmuch as drink a glass of ale with one o\xe2\x80\x99 them murderin\xe2\x80\x99 Frenchmen, and\r\nnothin\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99d make me change my opinions. Why! I\xe2\x80\x99ve \xe2\x80\x99eard it said that\r\nthem frog-eaters can\xe2\x80\x99t even speak the King\xe2\x80\x99s English, so, of course, if\r\nany of \xe2\x80\x99em tried to speak their God-forsaken lingo to me, why, I should\r\nspot them directly, see!\xe2\x80\x94and forewarned is forearmed, as the saying\r\ngoes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye! my honest friend,\xe2\x80\x9d assented the stranger cheerfully, \xe2\x80\x9cI see that\r\nyou are much too sharp, and a match for any twenty Frenchmen, and\r\nhere\xe2\x80\x99s to your very good health, my worthy host, if you\xe2\x80\x99ll do me the\r\nhonour to finish this bottle of mine with me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sure you\xe2\x80\x99re very polite, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Jellyband, wiping his\r\neyes which were still streaming with the abundance of his laughter,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand I don\xe2\x80\x99t mind if I do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe stranger poured out a couple of tankards full of wine, and having\r\noffered one to mine host, he took the other himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLoyal Englishmen as we all are,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, whilst the same humorous\r\nsmile played round the corners of his thin lips\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cloyal as we are, we\r\nmust admit that this at least is one good thing which comes to us from\r\nFrance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye! we\xe2\x80\x99ll none of us deny that, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d assented mine host.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd here\xe2\x80\x99s to the best landlord in England, our worthy host, Mr.\r\nJellyband,\xe2\x80\x9d said the stranger in a loud tone of voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHip, hip, hurrah!\xe2\x80\x9d retorted the whole company present. Then there was\r\nloud clapping of hands, and mugs and tankards made a rattling music\r\nupon the tables to the accompaniment of loud laughter at nothing in\r\nparticular, and of Mr. Jellyband\xe2\x80\x99s muttered exclamations:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust fancy _me_ bein\xe2\x80\x99 talked over by any God-forsaken\r\nfurriner!\xe2\x80\x94What?\xe2\x80\x94Lud love you, sir, but you do say some queer things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo which obvious fact the stranger heartily assented. It was certainly\r\na preposterous suggestion that anyone could ever upset Mr. Jellyband\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfirmly-rooted opinions anent the utter worthlessness of the inhabitants\r\nof the whole continent of Europe.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER III.\r\nTHE REFUGEES\r\n\r\n\r\nFeeling in every part of England certainly ran very high at this time\r\nagainst the French and their doings. Smugglers and legitimate traders\r\nbetween the French and English coasts brought snatches of news from\r\nover the water, which made every honest Englishman\xe2\x80\x99s blood boil, and\r\nmade him long to have \xe2\x80\x9ca good go\xe2\x80\x9d at those murderers, who had\r\nimprisoned their king and all his family, subjected the queen and the\r\nroyal children to every species of indignity, and were even now loudly\r\ndemanding the blood of the whole Bourbon family and of every one of its\r\nadherents.\r\n\r\nThe execution of the Princesse de Lamballe, Marie Antoinette\xe2\x80\x99s young\r\nand charming friend, had filled everyone in England with unspeakable\r\nhorror, the daily execution of scores of royalists of good family,\r\nwhose only sin was their aristocratic name, seemed to cry for vengeance\r\nto the whole of civilised Europe.\r\n\r\nYet, with all that, no one dared to interfere. Burke had exhausted all\r\nhis eloquence in trying to induce the British Government to fight the\r\nrevolutionary government of France, but Mr. Pitt, with characteristic\r\nprudence, did not feel that this country was fit yet to embark on\r\nanother arduous and costly war. It was for Austria to take the\r\ninitiative; Austria, whose fairest daughter was even now a dethroned\r\nqueen, imprisoned and insulted by a howling mob; and surely \xe2\x80\x99twas\r\nnot\xe2\x80\x94so argued Mr. Fox\xe2\x80\x94for the whole of England to take up arms, because\r\none set of Frenchmen chose to murder another.\r\n\r\nAs for Mr. Jellyband and his fellow John Bulls, though they looked upon\r\nall foreigners with withering contempt, they were royalist and\r\nanti-revolutionists to a man, and at this present moment were furious\r\nwith Pitt for his caution and moderation, although they naturally\r\nunderstood nothing of the diplomatic reasons which guided that great\r\nman\xe2\x80\x99s policy.\r\n\r\nBut now Sally came running back, very excited and very eager. The\r\njoyous company in the coffee-room had heard nothing of the noise\r\noutside, but she had spied a dripping horse and rider who had stopped\r\nat the door of \xe2\x80\x9cThe Fisherman\xe2\x80\x99s Rest,\xe2\x80\x9d and while the stable boy ran\r\nforward to take charge of the horse, pretty Miss Sally went to the\r\nfront door to greet the welcome visitor.\r\n \xe2\x80\x9cI think I see\xe2\x80\x99d my Lord Antony\xe2\x80\x99s horse out in the yard, father,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\n said, as she ran across the coffee-room.\r\n\r\nBut already the door had been thrown open from outside, and the next\r\nmoment an arm, covered in drab cloth and dripping with the heavy rain,\r\nwas round pretty Sally\xe2\x80\x99s waist, while a hearty voice echoed along the\r\npolished rafters of the coffee-room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye, and bless your brown eyes for being so sharp, my pretty Sally,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid the man who had just entered, whilst worthy Mr. Jellyband came\r\nbustling forward, eager, alert and fussy, as became the advent of one\r\nof the most favoured guests of his hostel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLud, I protest, Sally,\xe2\x80\x9d added Lord Antony, as he deposited a kiss on\r\nMiss Sally\xe2\x80\x99s blooming cheeks, \xe2\x80\x9cbut you are growing prettier and\r\nprettier every time I see you\xe2\x80\x94and my honest friend, Jellyband here,\r\nmust have hard work to keep the fellows off that slim waist of yours.\r\nWhat say you, Mr. Waite?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Waite\xe2\x80\x94torn between his respect for my lord and his dislike of that\r\nparticular type of joke\xe2\x80\x94only replied with a doubtful grunt.\r\n\r\nLord Antony Dewhurst, one of the sons of the Duke of Exeter, was in\r\nthose days a very perfect type of a young English gentleman\xe2\x80\x94tall, well\r\nset-up, broad of shoulders and merry of face, his laughter rang loudly\r\nwherever he went. A good sportsman, a lively companion, a courteous,\r\nwell-bred man of the world, with not too much brains to spoil his\r\ntemper, he was a universal favourite in London drawing-rooms or in the\r\ncoffee-rooms of village inns. At \xe2\x80\x9cThe Fisherman\xe2\x80\x99s Rest\xe2\x80\x9d everyone knew\r\nhim\xe2\x80\x94for he was fond of a trip across to France, and always spent a\r\nnight under worthy Mr. Jellyband\xe2\x80\x99s roof on his way there or back.\r\n\r\nHe nodded to Waite, Pitkin and the others as he at last released\r\nSally\xe2\x80\x99s waist, and crossed over to the hearth to warm and dry himself:\r\nas he did so, he cast a quick, somewhat suspicious glance at the two\r\nstrangers, who had quietly resumed their game of dominoes, and for a\r\nmoment a look of deep earnestness, even of anxiety, clouded his jovial\r\nyoung face.\r\n\r\nBut only for a moment; the next he had turned to Mr. Hempseed, who was\r\nrespectfully touching his forelock.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Mr. Hempseed, and how is the fruit?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBadly, my lord, badly,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Mr. Hempseed, dolefully, \xe2\x80\x9cbut what can\r\nyou \xe2\x80\x99xpect with this \xe2\x80\x99ere government favourin\xe2\x80\x99 them rascals over in\r\nFrance, who would murder their king and all their nobility.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOdd\xe2\x80\x99s life!\xe2\x80\x9d retorted Lord Antony; \xe2\x80\x9cso they would, honest Hempseed,\xe2\x80\x94at\r\nleast those they can get hold of, worse luck! But we have got some\r\nfriends coming here to-night, who at any rate have evaded their\r\nclutches.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt almost seemed, when the young man said these words, as if he threw a\r\ndefiant look towards the quiet strangers in the corner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks to you, my lord, and to your friends, so I\xe2\x80\x99ve heard it said,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Mr. Jellyband.\r\n\r\nBut in a moment Lord Antony\xe2\x80\x99s hand fell warningly on mine host\xe2\x80\x99s arm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush!\xe2\x80\x9d he said peremptorily, and instinctively once again looked\r\ntowards the strangers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! Lud love you, they are all right, my lord,\xe2\x80\x9d retorted Jellyband;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cdon\xe2\x80\x99t you be afraid. I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have spoken, only I knew we were among\r\nfriends. That gentleman over there is as true and loyal a subject of\r\nKing George as you are yourself, my lord, saving your presence. He is\r\nbut lately arrived in Dover, and is settling down in business in these\r\nparts.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn business? Faith, then, it must be as an undertaker, for I vow I\r\nnever beheld a more rueful countenance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, my lord, I believe that the gentleman is a widower, which no\r\ndoubt would account for the melancholy of his bearing\xe2\x80\x94but he is a\r\nfriend, nevertheless, I\xe2\x80\x99ll vouch for that\xe2\x80\x94and you will own, my lord,\r\nthat who should judge of a face better than the landlord of a popular\r\ninn\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that\xe2\x80\x99s all right, then, if we are among friends,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lord\r\nAntony, who evidently did not care to discuss the subject with his\r\nhost. \xe2\x80\x9cBut, tell me, you have no one else staying here, have you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo one, my lord, and no one coming, either, leastways\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLeastways?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo one your lordship would object to, I know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, my lord, Sir Percy Blakeney and his lady will be here presently,\r\nbut they ain\xe2\x80\x99t a-goin\xe2\x80\x99 to stay\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLady Blakeney?\xe2\x80\x9d queried Lord Antony, in some astonishment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye, my lord. Sir Percy\xe2\x80\x99s skipper was here just now. He says that my\r\nlady\xe2\x80\x99s brother is crossing over to France to-day in the _Day Dream_,\r\nwhich is Sir Percy\xe2\x80\x99s yacht, and Sir Percy and my lady will come with\r\nhim as far as here to see the last of him. It don\xe2\x80\x99t put you out, do it,\r\nmy lord?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t put me out, friend; nothing will put me out, unless\r\nthat supper is not the very best which Miss Sally can cook, and which\r\nhas ever been served in \xe2\x80\x98The Fisherman\xe2\x80\x99s Rest.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou need have no fear of that, my lord,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sally, who all this\r\nwhile had been busy setting the table for supper. And very gay and\r\ninviting it looked, with a large bunch of brilliantly coloured dahlias\r\nin the centre, and the bright pewter goblets and blue china about.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow many shall I lay for, my lord?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFive places, pretty Sally, but let the supper be enough for ten at\r\nleast\xe2\x80\x94our friends will be tired, and, I hope, hungry. As for me, I vow\r\nI could demolish a baron of beef to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere they are, I do believe,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sally, excitedly, as a distant\r\nclatter of horses and wheels could now be distinctly heard, drawing\r\nrapidly nearer.\r\n\r\nThere was general commotion in the coffee-room. Everyone was curious to\r\nsee my Lord Antony\xe2\x80\x99s swell friends from over the water. Miss Sally cast\r\none or two quick glances at the little bit of mirror which hung on the\r\nwall, and worthy Mr. Jellyband bustled out in order to give the first\r\nwelcome himself to his distinguished guests. Only the two strangers in\r\nthe corner did not participate in the general excitement. They were\r\ncalmly finishing their game of dominoes, and did not even look once\r\ntowards the door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStraight ahead, Comtesse, the door on your right,\xe2\x80\x9d said a pleasant\r\nvoice outside.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye! there they are, all right enough,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lord Antony, joyfully;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9coff with you, my pretty Sally, and see how quickly you can dish up the\r\nsoup.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe door was thrown wide open, and, preceded by Mr. Jellyband, who was\r\nprofuse in his bows and welcomes, a party of four\xe2\x80\x94two ladies and two\r\ngentlemen\xe2\x80\x94entered the coffee-room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWelcome! Welcome to old England!\xe2\x80\x9d said Lord Antony, effusively, as he\r\ncame eagerly forward with both hands outstretched towards the\r\nnewcomers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you are Lord Antony Dewhurst, I think,\xe2\x80\x9d said one of the ladies,\r\nspeaking with a strong foreign accent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt your service, Madame,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied, as he ceremoniously kissed the\r\nhands of both the ladies, then turned to the men and shook them both\r\nwarmly by the hand.\r\n\r\nSally was already helping the ladies to take off their travelling\r\ncloaks, and both turned, with a shiver, towards the brightly-blazing\r\nhearth.\r\n\r\nThere was a general movement among the company in the coffee-room.\r\nSally had bustled off to her kitchen, whilst Jellyband, still profuse\r\nwith his respectful salutations, arranged one or two chairs around the\r\nfire. Mr. Hempseed, touching his forelock, was quietly vacating the\r\nseat in the hearth. Everyone was staring curiously, yet deferentially,\r\nat the foreigners.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, Messieurs! what can I say?\xe2\x80\x9d said the elder of the two ladies, as\r\nshe stretched a pair of fine, aristocratic hands to the warmth of the\r\nblaze, and looked with unspeakable gratitude first at Lord Antony, then\r\nat one of the young men who had accompanied her party, and who was busy\r\ndivesting himself of his heavy, caped coat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly that you are glad to be in England, Comtesse,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Lord\r\nAntony, \xe2\x80\x9cand that you have not suffered too much from your trying\r\nvoyage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed, indeed, we are glad to be in England,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, while her\r\neyes filled with tears, \xe2\x80\x9cand we have already forgotten all that we have\r\nsuffered.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer voice was musical and low, and there was a great deal of calm\r\ndignity and of many sufferings nobly endured marked in the handsome,\r\naristocratic face, with its wealth of snow-white hair dressed high\r\nabove the forehead, after the fashion of the times.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope my friend, Sir Andrew Ffoulkes, proved an entertaining\r\ntravelling companion, Madame?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, indeed, Sir Andrew was kindness itself. How could my children and\r\nI ever show enough gratitude to you all, Messieurs?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer companion, a dainty, girlish figure, childlike and pathetic in its\r\nlook of fatigue and of sorrow, had said nothing as yet, but her eyes,\r\nlarge, brown, and full of tears, looked up from the fire and sought\r\nthose of Sir Andrew Ffoulkes, who had drawn near to the hearth and to\r\nher; then, as they met his, which were fixed with unconcealed\r\nadmiration upon the sweet face before him, a thought of warmer colour\r\nrushed up to her pale cheeks.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo this is England,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, as she looked round with childlike\r\ncuriosity at the great open hearth, the oak rafters, and the yokels\r\nwith their elaborate smocks and jovial, rubicund, British countenances.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA bit of it, Mademoiselle,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Sir Andrew, smiling, \xe2\x80\x9cbut all of\r\nit, at your service.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young girl blushed again, but this time a bright smile, fleet and\r\nsweet, illumined her dainty face. She said nothing, and Sir Andrew too\r\nwas silent, yet those two young people understood one another, as young\r\npeople have a way of doing all the world over, and have done since the\r\nworld began.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, I say, supper!\xe2\x80\x9d here broke in Lord Antony\xe2\x80\x99s jovial voice,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9csupper, honest Jellyband. Where is that pretty wench of yours and the\r\ndish of soup? Zooks, man, while you stand there gaping at the ladies,\r\nthey will faint with hunger.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne moment! one moment, my lord,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jellyband, as he threw open the\r\ndoor that led to the kitchen and shouted lustily: \xe2\x80\x9cSally! Hey, Sally\r\nthere, are ye ready, my girl?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSally was ready, and the next moment she appeared in the doorway\r\ncarrying a gigantic tureen, from which rose a cloud of steam and an\r\nabundance of savoury odour.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOdd\xe2\x80\x99s my life, supper at last!\xe2\x80\x9d ejaculated Lord Antony, merrily, as he\r\ngallantly offered his arm to the Comtesse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay I have the honour?\xe2\x80\x9d he added ceremoniously, as he led her towards\r\nthe supper table.\r\n\r\nThere was general bustle in the coffee-room: Mr. Hempseed and most of\r\nthe yokels and fisher-folk had gone to make way for \xe2\x80\x9cthe quality,\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\nto finish smoking their pipes elsewhere. Only the two strangers stayed\r\non, quietly and unconcernedly playing their game of dominoes and\r\nsipping their wine; whilst at another table Harry Waite, who was fast\r\nlosing his temper, watched pretty Sally bustling round the table.\r\n\r\nShe looked a very dainty picture of English rural life, and no wonder\r\nthat the susceptible young Frenchman could scarce take his eyes off her\r\npretty face. The Vicomte de Tournay was scarce nineteen, a beardless\r\nboy, on whom the terrible tragedies which were being enacted in his own\r\ncountry had made but little impression. He was elegantly and even\r\nfoppishly dressed, and once safely landed in England he was evidently\r\nready to forget the horrors of the Revolution in the delights of\r\nEnglish life.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPardi, if zis is England,\xe2\x80\x9d he said as he continued to ogle Sally with\r\nmarked satisfaction, \xe2\x80\x9cI am of it satisfied.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt would be impossible at this point to record the exact exclamation\r\nwhich escaped through Mr. Harry Waite\xe2\x80\x99s clenched teeth. Only respect\r\nfor \xe2\x80\x9cthe quality,\xe2\x80\x9d and notably for my Lord Antony, kept his marked\r\ndisapproval of the young foreigner in check.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, but this _is_ England, you abandoned young reprobate,\xe2\x80\x9d interposed\r\nLord Antony with a laugh, \xe2\x80\x9cand do not, I pray, bring your loose foreign\r\nways into this most moral country.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLord Antony had already sat down at the head of the table with the\r\nComtesse on his right. Jellyband was bustling round, filling glasses\r\nand putting chairs straight. Sally waited, ready to hand round the\r\nsoup. Mr. Harry Waite\xe2\x80\x99s friends had at last succeeded in taking him out\r\nof the room, for his temper was growing more and more violent under the\r\nVicomte\xe2\x80\x99s obvious admiration for Sally.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuzanne,\xe2\x80\x9d came in stern, commanding accents from the rigid Comtesse.\r\n\r\nSuzanne blushed again; she had lost count of time and of place whilst\r\nshe had stood beside the fire, allowing the handsome young Englishman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\neyes to dwell upon her sweet face, and his hand, as if unconsciously,\r\nto rest upon hers. Her mother\xe2\x80\x99s voice brought her back to reality once\r\nmore, and with a submissive \xe2\x80\x9cYes, Mama,\xe2\x80\x9d she too took her place at the\r\nsupper table.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IV.\r\nTHE LEAGUE OF THE SCARLET PIMPERNEL\r\n\r\n\r\nThey all looked a merry, even a happy party, as they sat round the\r\ntable; Sir Andrew Ffoulkes and Lord Antony Dewhurst, two typical\r\ngood-looking, well-born and well-bred Englishmen of that year of grace\r\n1792, and the aristocratic French comtesse with her two children, who\r\nhad just escaped from such dire perils, and found a safe retreat at\r\nlast on the shores of protecting England.\r\n\r\nIn the corner the two strangers had apparently finished their game; one\r\nof them arose, and standing with his back to the merry company at the\r\ntable, he adjusted with much deliberation his large triple caped coat.\r\nAs he did so, he gave one quick glance all around him. Everyone was\r\nbusy laughing and chatting, and he murmured the words \xe2\x80\x9cAll safe!\xe2\x80\x9d: his\r\ncompanion then, with the alertness borne of long practice, slipped on\r\nto his knees in a moment, and the next had crept noiselessly under the\r\noak bench. The stranger then, with a loud \xe2\x80\x9cGood-night,\xe2\x80\x9d quietly walked\r\nout of the coffee-room.\r\n\r\nNot one of those at the supper table had noticed this curious and\r\nsilent man\xc5\x93uvre, but when the stranger finally closed the door of the\r\ncoffee-room behind him, they all instinctively sighed a sigh of relief.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlone, at last!\xe2\x80\x9d said Lord Antony, jovially.\r\n\r\nThen the young Vicomte de Tournay rose, glass in hand, and with the\r\ngraceful affectation peculiar to the times, he raised it aloft, and\r\nsaid in broken English,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo His Majesty George Three of England. God bless him for his\r\nhospitality to us all, poor exiles from France.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis Majesty the King!\xe2\x80\x9d echoed Lord Antony and Sir Andrew as they drank\r\nloyally to the toast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo His Majesty King Louis of France,\xe2\x80\x9d added Sir Andrew, with\r\nsolemnity. \xe2\x80\x9cMay God protect him, and give him victory over his\r\nenemies.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEveryone rose and drank this toast in silence. The fate of the\r\nunfortunate King of France, then a prisoner of his own people, seemed\r\nto cast a gloom even over Mr. Jellyband\xe2\x80\x99s pleasant countenance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd to M. le Comte de Tournay de Basserive,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lord Antony,\r\nmerrily. \xe2\x80\x9cMay we welcome him in England before many days are over.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, Monsieur,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Comtesse, as with a slightly trembling hand\r\nshe conveyed her glass to her lips, \xe2\x80\x9cI scarcely dare to hope.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut already Lord Antony had served out the soup, and for the next few\r\nmoments all conversation ceased, while Jellyband and Sally handed round\r\nthe plates and everyone began to eat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFaith, Madame!\xe2\x80\x9d said Lord Antony, after a while, \xe2\x80\x9cmine was no idle\r\ntoast; seeing yourself, Mademoiselle Suzanne and my friend the Vicomte\r\nsafely in England now, surely you must feel reassured as to the fate of\r\nMonsieur le Comte.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, Monsieur,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the Comtesse, with a heavy sigh, \xe2\x80\x9cI trust in\r\nGod\xe2\x80\x94I can but pray\xe2\x80\x94and hope . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye, Madame!\xe2\x80\x9d here interposed Sir Andrew Ffoulkes, \xe2\x80\x9ctrust in God by\r\nall means, but believe also a little in your English friends, who have\r\nsworn to bring the Count safely across the Channel, even as they have\r\nbrought you to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed, indeed, Monsieur,\xe2\x80\x9d she replied, \xe2\x80\x9cI have the fullest confidence\r\nin you and in your friends. Your fame, I assure you, has spread\r\nthroughout the whole of France. The way some of my own friends have\r\nescaped from the clutches of that awful revolutionary tribunal was\r\nnothing short of a miracle\xe2\x80\x94and all done by you and your friends\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe were but the hands, Madame la Comtesse . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut my husband, Monsieur,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Comtesse, whilst unshed tears\r\nseemed to veil her voice, \xe2\x80\x9che is in such deadly peril\xe2\x80\x94I would never\r\nhave left him, only . . . there were my children . . . I was torn\r\nbetween my duty to him, and to them. They refused to go without me . .\r\n. and you and your friends assured me so solemnly that my husband would\r\nbe safe. But, oh! now that I am here\xe2\x80\x94amongst you all\xe2\x80\x94in this beautiful,\r\nfree England\xe2\x80\x94I think of him, flying for his life, hunted like a poor\r\nbeast . . . in such peril. . . . Ah! I should not have left him . . . I\r\nshould not have left him! . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe poor woman had completely broken down; fatigue, sorrow and emotion\r\nhad overmastered her rigid, aristocratic bearing. She was crying gently\r\nto herself, whilst Suzanne ran up to her and tried to kiss away her\r\ntears.\r\n\r\nLord Antony and Sir Andrew had said nothing to interrupt the Comtesse\r\nwhilst she was speaking. There was no doubt that they felt deeply for\r\nher; their very silence testified to that\xe2\x80\x94but in every century, and\r\never since England has been what it is, an Englishman has always felt\r\nsomewhat ashamed of his own emotion and of his own sympathy. And so the\r\ntwo young men said nothing, and busied themselves in trying to hide\r\ntheir feelings, only succeeding in looking immeasurably sheepish.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs for me, Monsieur,\xe2\x80\x9d said Suzanne, suddenly, as she looked through a\r\nwealth of brown curls across at Sir Andrew, \xe2\x80\x9cI trust you absolutely,\r\nand I _know_ that you will bring my dear father safely to England, just\r\nas you brought us to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was said with so much confidence, such unuttered hope and belief,\r\nthat it seemed as if by magic to dry the mother\xe2\x80\x99s eyes, and to bring a\r\nsmile upon everybody\xe2\x80\x99s lips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay! you shame me, Mademoiselle,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Sir Andrew; \xe2\x80\x9cthough my life\r\nis at your service, I have been but a humble tool in the hands of our\r\ngreat leader, who organised and effected your escape.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe had spoken with so much warmth and vehemence that Suzanne\xe2\x80\x99s eyes\r\nfastened upon him in undisguised wonder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour leader, Monsieur?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Comtesse, eagerly. \xe2\x80\x9cAh! of course,\r\nyou must have a leader. And I did not think of that before! But tell me\r\nwhere is he? I must go to him at once, and I and my children must throw\r\nourselves at his feet, and thank him for all that he has done for us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlas, Madame!\xe2\x80\x9d said Lord Antony, \xe2\x80\x9cthat is impossible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cImpossible?\xe2\x80\x94Why?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause the Scarlet Pimpernel works in the dark, and his identity is\r\nonly known under a solemn oath of secrecy to his immediate followers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Scarlet Pimpernel?\xe2\x80\x9d said Suzanne, with a merry laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy! what a\r\ndroll name! What is the Scarlet Pimpernel, Monsieur?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked at Sir Andrew with eager curiosity. The young man\xe2\x80\x99s face had\r\nbecome almost transfigured. His eyes shone with enthusiasm;\r\nhero-worship, love, admiration for his leader seemed literally to glow\r\nupon his face.\r\n\r\n \xe2\x80\x9cThe Scarlet Pimpernel, Mademoiselle,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last, \xe2\x80\x9cis the name\r\n of a humble English wayside flower; but it is also the name chosen to\r\n hide the identity of the best and bravest man in all the world, so\r\n that he may better succeed in accomplishing the noble task he has set\r\n himself to do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, yes,\xe2\x80\x9d here interposed the young Vicomte, \xe2\x80\x9cI have heard speak of\r\nthis Scarlet Pimpernel. A little flower\xe2\x80\x94red?\xe2\x80\x94yes! They say in Paris\r\nthat every time a royalist escapes to England that devil,\r\nFoucquier-Tinville, the Public Prosecutor, receives a paper with that\r\nlittle flower dessinated in red upon it. . . . Yes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that is so,\xe2\x80\x9d assented Lord Antony.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen he will have received one such paper to-day?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUndoubtedly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! I wonder what he will say!\xe2\x80\x9d said Suzanne, merrily. \xe2\x80\x9cI have heard\r\nthat the picture of that little red flower is the only thing that\r\nfrightens him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFaith, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sir Andrew, \xe2\x80\x9che will have many more opportunities\r\nof studying the shape of that small scarlet flower.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! Monsieur,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed the Comtesse, \xe2\x80\x9cit all sounds like a romance, and\r\nI cannot understand it all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy should you try, Madame?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, tell me, why should your leader\xe2\x80\x94why should you all\xe2\x80\x94spend your\r\nmoney and risk your lives\xe2\x80\x94for it is your lives you risk, Messieurs,\r\nwhen you set foot in France\xe2\x80\x94and all for us French men and women, who\r\nare nothing to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSport, Madame la Comtesse, sport,\xe2\x80\x9d asserted Lord Antony, with his\r\njovial, loud and pleasant voice; \xe2\x80\x9cwe are a nation of sportsmen, you\r\nknow, and just now it is the fashion to pull the hare from between the\r\nteeth of the hound.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, no, no, not sport only, Monsieur . . . you have a more noble\r\nmotive, I am sure, for the good work you do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFaith, Madame, I would like you to find it then . . . as for me, I\r\nvow, I love the game, for this is the finest sport I have yet\r\nencountered.\xe2\x80\x94Hair-breadth escapes . . . the devil\xe2\x80\x99s own risks!\xe2\x80\x94Tally\r\nho!\xe2\x80\x94and away we go!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut the Comtesse shook her head, still incredulously. To her it seemed\r\npreposterous that these young men and their great leader, all of them\r\nrich, probably well-born, and young, should for no other motive than\r\nsport, run the terrible risks, which she knew they were constantly\r\ndoing. Their nationality, once they had set foot in France, would be no\r\nsafeguard to them. Anyone found harbouring or assisting suspected\r\nroyalists would be ruthlessly condemned and summarily executed,\r\nwhatever his nationality might be. And this band of young Englishmen\r\nhad, to her own knowledge, bearded the implacable and bloodthirsty\r\ntribunal of the Revolution, within the very walls of Paris itself, and\r\nhad snatched away condemned victims, almost from the very foot of the\r\nguillotine. With a shudder, she recalled the events of the last few\r\ndays, her escape from Paris with her two children, all three of them\r\nhidden beneath the hood of a rickety cart, and lying amidst a heap of\r\nturnips and cabbages, not daring to breathe, whilst the mob howled \xe2\x80\x9c\xc3\x80\r\nla lanterne les aristos!\xe2\x80\x9d at that awful West Barricade.\r\n\r\nIt had all occurred in such a miraculous way; she and her husband had\r\nunderstood that they had been placed on the list of \xe2\x80\x9csuspected\r\npersons,\xe2\x80\x9d which meant that their trial and death were but a matter of\r\ndays\xe2\x80\x94of hours, perhaps.\r\n\r\nThen came the hope of salvation; the mysterious epistle, signed with\r\nthe enigmatical scarlet device; the clear, peremptory directions; the\r\nparting from the Comte de Tournay, which had torn the poor wife\xe2\x80\x99s heart\r\nin two; the hope of reunion; the flight with her two children; the\r\ncovered cart; that awful hag driving it, who looked like some horrible\r\nevil demon, with the ghastly trophy on her whip handle!\r\n\r\nThe Comtesse looked round at the quaint, old-fashioned English inn, the\r\npeace of this land of civil and religious liberty, and she closed her\r\neyes to shut out the haunting vision of that West Barricade, and of the\r\nmob retreating panic-stricken when the old hag spoke of the plague.\r\n\r\nEvery moment under that cart she expected recognition, arrest, herself\r\nand her children tried and condemned, and these young Englishmen, under\r\nthe guidance of their brave and mysterious leader, had risked their\r\nlives to save them all, as they had already saved scores of other\r\ninnocent people.\r\n\r\nAnd all only for sport? Impossible! Suzanne\xe2\x80\x99s eyes as she sought those\r\nof Sir Andrew plainly told him that she thought that _he_ at any rate\r\nrescued his fellow-men from terrible and unmerited death, through a\r\nhigher and nobler motive than his friend would have her believe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow many are there in your brave league, Monsieur?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked timidly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwenty all told, Mademoiselle,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied, \xe2\x80\x9cone to command, and\r\nnineteen to obey. All of us Englishmen, and all pledged to the same\r\ncause\xe2\x80\x94to obey our leader and to rescue the innocent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay God protect you all, Messieurs,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Comtesse, fervently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has done that so far, Madame.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is wonderful to me, wonderful!\xe2\x80\x94That you should all be so brave, so\r\ndevoted to your fellow-men\xe2\x80\x94yet you are English!\xe2\x80\x94and in France treachery\r\nis rife\xe2\x80\x94all in the name of liberty and fraternity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe women even, in France, have been more bitter against us\r\naristocrats than the men,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Vicomte, with a sigh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, yes,\xe2\x80\x9d added the Comtesse, whilst a look of haughty disdain and\r\nintense bitterness shot through her melancholy eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cThere was that\r\nwoman, Marguerite St. Just, for instance. She denounced the Marquis de\r\nSt. Cyr and all his family to the awful tribunal of the Terror.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarguerite St. Just?\xe2\x80\x9d said Lord Antony, as he shot a quick and\r\napprehensive glance across at Sir Andrew. \xe2\x80\x9cMarguerite St. Just?\xe2\x80\x94Surely\r\n. . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes!\xe2\x80\x9d replied the Comtesse, \xe2\x80\x9csurely you know her. She was a leading\r\nactress of the Com\xc3\xa9die Fran\xc3\xa7aise, and she married an Englishman lately.\r\nYou must know her\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKnow her?\xe2\x80\x9d said Lord Antony. \xe2\x80\x9cKnow Lady Blakeney\xe2\x80\x94the most fashionable\r\nwoman in London\xe2\x80\x94the wife of the richest man in England? Of course, we\r\nall know Lady Blakeney.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe was a school-fellow of mine at the convent in Paris,\xe2\x80\x9d interposed\r\nSuzanne, \xe2\x80\x9cand we came over to England together to learn your language.\r\nI was very fond of Marguerite, and I cannot believe that she ever did\r\nanything so wicked.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt certainly seems incredible,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sir Andrew. \xe2\x80\x9cYou say that she\r\nactually denounced the Marquis de St. Cyr? Why should she have done\r\nsuch a thing? Surely there must be some mistake\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo mistake is possible, Monsieur,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined the Comtesse, coldly.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarguerite St. Just\xe2\x80\x99s brother is a noted republican. There was some\r\ntalk of a family feud between him and my cousin, the Marquis de St.\r\nCyr. The St. Justs\xe2\x80\x99 are quite plebeian, and the republican government\r\nemploys many spies. I assure you there is no mistake. . . . You had not\r\nheard this story?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFaith, Madame, I did hear some vague rumours of it, but in England no\r\none would credit it. . . . Sir Percy Blakeney, her husband, is a very\r\nwealthy man, of high social position, the intimate friend of the Prince\r\nof Wales . . . and Lady Blakeney leads both fashion and society in\r\nLondon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat may be, Monsieur, and we shall, of course, lead a very quiet life\r\nin England, but I pray God that while I remain in this beautiful\r\ncountry, I may never meet Marguerite St. Just.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe proverbial wet-blanket seemed to have fallen over the merry little\r\ncompany gathered round the table. Suzanne looked sad and silent; Sir\r\nAndrew fidgeted uneasily with his fork, whilst the Comtesse, encased in\r\nthe plate-armour of her aristocratic prejudices, sat, rigid and\r\nunbending, in her straight-backed chair. As for Lord Antony, he looked\r\nextremely uncomfortable, and glanced once or twice apprehensively\r\ntowards Jellyband, who looked just as uncomfortable as himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt what time do you expect Sir Percy and Lady Blakeney?\xe2\x80\x9d he contrived\r\nto whisper unobserved, to mine host.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAny moment, my lord,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Jellyband in reply.\r\n\r\nEven as he spoke, a distant clatter was heard of an approaching coach;\r\nlouder and louder it grew, one or two shouts became distinguishable,\r\nthen the rattle of horses\xe2\x80\x99 hoofs on the uneven cobble stones, and the\r\nnext moment a stable boy had thrown open the coffee-room door and\r\nrushed in excitedly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSir Percy Blakeney and my lady,\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted at the top of his voice,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthey\xe2\x80\x99re just arriving.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd with more shouting, jingling of harness, and iron hoofs upon the\r\nstones, a magnificent coach, drawn by four superb bays, had halted\r\noutside the porch of \xe2\x80\x9cThe Fisherman\xe2\x80\x99s Rest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER V.\r\nMARGUERITE\r\n\r\n\r\nIn a moment the pleasant oak-raftered coffee-room of the inn became the\r\nscene of hopeless confusion and discomfort. At the first announcement\r\nmade by the stable boy, Lord Antony, with a fashionable oath, had\r\njumped up from his seat and was now giving many and confused directions\r\nto poor bewildered Jellyband, who seemed at his wits\xe2\x80\x99 end what to do.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor goodness\xe2\x80\x99 sake, man,\xe2\x80\x9d admonished his lordship, \xe2\x80\x9ctry to keep Lady\r\nBlakeney talking outside for a moment, while the ladies withdraw.\r\nZounds!\xe2\x80\x9d he added, with another more emphatic oath, \xe2\x80\x9cthis is most\r\nunfortunate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuick, Sally! the candles!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Jellyband, as hopping about from\r\none leg to another, he ran hither and thither, adding to the general\r\ndiscomfort of everybody.\r\n\r\nThe Comtesse, too, had risen to her feet: rigid and erect, trying to\r\nhide her excitement beneath more becoming _sang-froid_, she repeated\r\nmechanically,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will not see her!\xe2\x80\x94I will not see her!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOutside, the excitement attendant upon the arrival of very important\r\nguests grew apace.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-day, Sir Percy!\xe2\x80\x94Good-day to your ladyship! Your servant, Sir\r\nPercy!\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94was heard in one long, continued chorus, with alternate more\r\nfeeble tones of\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cRemember the poor blind man! of your charity, lady and\r\ngentleman!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen suddenly a singularly sweet voice was heard through all the din.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet the poor man be\xe2\x80\x94and give him some supper at my expense.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe voice was low and musical, with a slight sing-song in it, and a\r\nfaint _soup\xc3\xa7on_ of foreign intonation in the pronunciation of the\r\nconsonants.\r\n\r\nEveryone in the coffee-room heard it and paused, instinctively\r\nlistening to it for a moment. Sally was holding the candles by the\r\nopposite door, which led to the bedrooms upstairs, and the Comtesse was\r\nin the act of beating a hasty retreat before that enemy who owned such\r\na sweet musical voice; Suzanne reluctantly was preparing to follow her\r\nmother, whilst casting regretful glances towards the door, where she\r\nhoped still to see her dearly-beloved, erstwhile school-fellow.\r\n\r\nThen Jellyband threw open the door, still stupidly and blindly hoping\r\nto avert the catastrophe which he felt was in the air, and the same\r\nlow, musical voice said, with a merry laugh and mock consternation,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cB-r-r-r-r!
