I am as wet as a herring! _Dieu!_ has anyone ever seen such\r\na contemptible climate?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuzanne, come with me at once\xe2\x80\x94I wish it,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Comtesse,\r\nperemptorily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! Mama!\xe2\x80\x9d pleaded Suzanne.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy lady . . . er . . . h\xe2\x80\x99m! . . . my lady! . . .\xe2\x80\x9d came in feeble\r\naccents from Jellyband, who stood clumsily trying to bar the way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Pardieu_, my good man,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Blakeney, with some impatience,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwhat are you standing in my way for, dancing about like a turkey with\r\na sore foot? Let me get to the fire, I am perished with the cold.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd the next moment Lady Blakeney, gently pushing mine host on one\r\nside, had swept into the coffee-room.\r\n\r\nThere are many portraits and miniatures extant of Marguerite St.\r\nJust\xe2\x80\x94Lady Blakeney as she was then\xe2\x80\x94but it is doubtful if any of these\r\nreally do her singular beauty justice. Tall, above the average, with\r\nmagnificent presence and regal figure, it is small wonder that even the\r\nComtesse paused for a moment in involuntary admiration before turning\r\nher back on so fascinating an apparition.\r\n\r\nMarguerite Blakeney was then scarcely five-and-twenty, and her beauty\r\nwas at its most dazzling stage. The large hat, with its undulating and\r\nwaving plumes, threw a soft shadow across the classic brow with the\r\naureole of auburn hair\xe2\x80\x94free at the moment from any powder; the sweet,\r\nalmost childlike mouth, the straight chiselled nose, round chin, and\r\ndelicate throat, all seemed set off by the picturesque costume of the\r\nperiod. The rich blue velvet robe moulded in its every line the\r\ngraceful contour of the figure, whilst one tiny hand held, with a\r\ndignity all its own, the tall stick adorned with a large bunch of\r\nribbons which fashionable ladies of the period had taken to carrying\r\nrecently.\r\n\r\nWith a quick glance all around the room, Marguerite Blakeney had taken\r\nstock of everyone there. She nodded pleasantly to Sir Andrew Ffoulkes,\r\nwhilst extending a hand to Lord Antony.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHello! my Lord Tony, why\xe2\x80\x94what are _you_ doing here in Dover?\xe2\x80\x9d she said\r\nmerrily.\r\n\r\nThen, without waiting for a reply, she turned and faced the Comtesse\r\nand Suzanne. Her whole face lighted up with additional brightness, as\r\nshe stretched out both arms towards the young girl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy! if that isn\xe2\x80\x99t my little Suzanne over there. _Pardieu_, little\r\ncitizeness, how came you to be in England? And Madame too!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe went up effusively to them both, with not a single touch of\r\nembarrassment in her manner or in her smile. Lord Tony and Sir Andrew\r\nwatched the little scene with eager apprehension. English though they\r\nwere, they had often been in France, and had mixed sufficiently with\r\nthe French to realise the unbending hauteur, the bitter hatred with\r\nwhich the old _noblesse_ of France viewed all those who had helped to\r\ncontribute to their downfall. Armand St. Just, the brother of beautiful\r\nLady Blakeney\xe2\x80\x94though known to hold moderate and conciliatory views\xe2\x80\x94was\r\nan ardent republican; his feud with the ancient family of St. Cyr\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nrights and wrongs of which no outsider ever knew\xe2\x80\x94had culminated in the\r\ndownfall, the almost total extinction, of the latter. In France, St.\r\nJust and his party had triumphed, and here in England, face to face\r\nwith these three refugees driven from their country, flying for their\r\nlives, bereft of all which centuries of luxury had given them, there\r\nstood a fair scion of those same republican families which had hurled\r\ndown a throne, and uprooted an aristocracy whose origin was lost in the\r\ndim and distant vista of bygone centuries.\r\n\r\nShe stood there before them, in all the unconscious insolence of\r\nbeauty, and stretched out her dainty hand to them, as if she would, by\r\nthat one act, bridge over the conflict and bloodshed of the past\r\ndecade.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuzanne, I forbid you to speak to that woman,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Comtesse,\r\nsternly, as she placed a restraining hand upon her daughter\xe2\x80\x99s arm.\r\n\r\nShe had spoken in English, so that all might hear and understand; the\r\ntwo young English gentlemen as well as the common innkeeper and his\r\ndaughter. The latter literally gasped with horror at this foreign\r\ninsolence, this impudence before her ladyship\xe2\x80\x94who was English, now that\r\nshe was Sir Percy\xe2\x80\x99s wife, and a friend of the Princess of Wales to\r\nboot.\r\n\r\nAs for Lord Antony and Sir Andrew Ffoulkes, their very hearts seemed to\r\nstand still with horror at this gratuitous insult. One of them uttered\r\nan exclamation of appeal, the other one of warning, and instinctively\r\nboth glanced hurriedly towards the door, whence a slow, drawly, not\r\nunpleasant voice had already been heard.\r\n\r\nAlone among those present Marguerite Blakeney and the Comtesse de\r\nTournay had remained seemingly unmoved. The latter, rigid, erect and\r\ndefiant, with one hand still upon her daughter\xe2\x80\x99s arm, seemed the very\r\npersonification of unbending pride. For the moment Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s sweet\r\nface had become as white as the soft fichu which swathed her throat,\r\nand a very keen observer might have noted that the hand which held the\r\ntall, beribboned stick was clenched, and trembled somewhat.\r\n\r\nBut this was only momentary; the next instant the delicate eyebrows\r\nwere raised slightly, the lips curved sarcastically upwards, the clear\r\nblue eyes looked straight at the rigid Comtesse, and with a slight\r\nshrug of the shoulders\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHoity-toity, citizeness,\xe2\x80\x9d she said gaily, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat fly stings you, pray?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are in England now, Madame,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined the Comtesse, coldly, \xe2\x80\x9cand I\r\nam at liberty to forbid my daughter to touch your hand in friendship.\r\nCome, Suzanne.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe beckoned to her daughter, and without another look at Marguerite\r\nBlakeney, but with a deep, old-fashioned curtsey to the two young men,\r\nshe sailed majestically out of the room.\r\n\r\nThere was silence in the old inn parlour for a moment, as the rustle of\r\nthe Comtesse\xe2\x80\x99s skirts died away down the passage. Marguerite, rigid as\r\na statue, followed with hard, set eyes the upright figure, as it\r\ndisappeared through the doorway\xe2\x80\x94but as little Suzanne, humble and\r\nobedient, was about to follow her mother, the hard, set expression\r\nsuddenly vanished, and a wistful, almost pathetic and childlike look\r\nstole into Lady Blakeney\xe2\x80\x99s eyes.\r\n\r\nLittle Suzanne caught that look; the child\xe2\x80\x99s sweet nature went out to\r\nthe beautiful woman, scarce older than herself; filial obedience\r\nvanished before girlish sympathy; at the door she turned, ran back to\r\nMarguerite, and putting her arms round her, kissed her effusively; then\r\nonly did she follow her mother, Sally bringing up the rear, with a\r\npleasant smile on her dimpled face, and with a final curtsey to my\r\nlady.\r\n\r\nSuzanne\xe2\x80\x99s sweet and dainty impulse had relieved the unpleasant tension.\r\nSir Andrew\xe2\x80\x99s eyes followed the pretty little figure, until it had quite\r\ndisappeared, then they met Lady Blakeney\xe2\x80\x99s with unassumed merriment.\r\n\r\nMarguerite, with dainty affectation, had kissed her hand to the ladies,\r\nas they disappeared through the door, then a humorous smile began\r\nhovering round the corners of her mouth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo that\xe2\x80\x99s it, is it?\xe2\x80\x9d she said gaily. \xe2\x80\x9cLa! Sir Andrew, did you ever\r\nsee such an unpleasant person? I hope when I grow old I sha\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t look\r\nlike that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe gathered up her skirts, and assuming a majestic gait, stalked\r\ntowards the fireplace.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuzanne,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, mimicking the Comtesse\xe2\x80\x99s voice, \xe2\x80\x9cI forbid you to\r\nspeak to that woman!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe laugh which accompanied this sally sounded perhaps a trifle forced\r\nand hard, but neither Sir Andrew nor Lord Tony were very keen\r\nobservers. The mimicry was so perfect, the tone of the voice so\r\naccurately reproduced, that both the young men joined in a hearty\r\ncheerful \xe2\x80\x9cBravo!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! Lady Blakeney!\xe2\x80\x9d added Lord Tony, \xe2\x80\x9chow they must miss you at the\r\nCom\xc3\xa9die Fran\xc3\xa7aise, and how the Parisians must hate Sir Percy for having\r\ntaken you away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLud, man,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined Marguerite, with a shrug of her graceful\r\nshoulders, \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99tis impossible to hate Sir Percy for anything; his witty\r\nsallies would disarm even Madame la Comtesse herself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young Vicomte, who had not elected to follow his mother in her\r\ndignified exit, now made a step forward, ready to champion the Comtesse\r\nshould Lady Blakeney aim any further shafts at her. But before he could\r\nutter a preliminary word of protest, a pleasant, though distinctly\r\ninane laugh, was heard from outside, and the next moment an unusually\r\ntall and very richly dressed figure appeared in the doorway.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VI.\r\nAN EXQUISITE OF \xe2\x80\x9992\r\n\r\n\r\nSir Percy Blakeney, as the chronicles of the time inform us, was in\r\nthis year of grace 1792, still a year or two on the right side of\r\nthirty. Tall, above the average, even for an Englishman,\r\nbroad-shouldered and massively built, he would have been called\r\nunusually good-looking, but for a certain lazy expression in his\r\ndeep-set blue eyes, and that perpetual inane laugh which seemed to\r\ndisfigure his strong, clearly-cut mouth.\r\n\r\nIt was nearly a year ago now that Sir Percy Blakeney, Bart., one of the\r\nrichest men in England, leader of all the fashions, and intimate friend\r\nof the Prince of Wales, had astonished fashionable society in London\r\nand Bath by bringing home, from one of his journeys abroad, a\r\nbeautiful, fascinating, clever, French wife. He, the sleepiest,\r\ndullest, most British Britisher that had ever set a pretty woman\r\nyawning, had secured a brilliant matrimonial prize for which, as all\r\nchroniclers aver, there had been many competitors.\r\n\r\nMarguerite St. Just had first made her _d\xc3\xa9but_ in artistic Parisian\r\ncircles, at the very moment when the greatest social upheaval the world\r\nhas ever known was taking place within its very walls. Scarcely\r\neighteen, lavishly gifted with beauty and talent, chaperoned only by a\r\nyoung and devoted brother, she had soon gathered round her, in her\r\ncharming apartment in the Rue Richelieu, a coterie which was as\r\nbrilliant as it was exclusive\xe2\x80\x94exclusive, that is to say, only from one\r\npoint of view: Marguerite St. Just was from principle and by conviction\r\na republican\xe2\x80\x94equality of birth was her motto\xe2\x80\x94inequality of fortune was\r\nin her eyes a mere untoward accident, but the only inequality she\r\nadmitted was that of talent. \xe2\x80\x9cMoney and titles may be hereditary,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nwould say, \xe2\x80\x9cbut brains are not,\xe2\x80\x9d and thus her charming salon was\r\nreserved for originality and intellect, for brilliance and wit, for\r\nclever men and talented women, and the entrance into it was soon looked\r\nupon in the world of intellect\xe2\x80\x94which even in those days and in those\r\ntroublous times found its pivot in Paris\xe2\x80\x94as the seal to an artistic\r\ncareer.\r\n\r\nClever men, distinguished men, and even men of exalted station formed a\r\nperpetual and brilliant court round the fascinating young actress of\r\nthe Com\xc3\xa9die Fran\xc3\xa7aise, and she glided through republican,\r\nrevolutionary, bloodthirsty Paris like a shining comet with a trail\r\nbehind her of all that was most distinguished, most interesting, in\r\nintellectual Europe.\r\n\r\nThen the climax came. Some smiled indulgently and called it an artistic\r\neccentricity, others looked upon it as a wise provision, in view of the\r\nmany events which were crowding thick and fast in Paris just then, but\r\nto all, the real motive of that climax remained a puzzle and a mystery.\r\nAnyway, Marguerite St. Just married Sir Percy Blakeney one fine day,\r\njust like that, without any warning to her friends, without a _soir\xc3\xa9e\r\nde contrat_ or _d\xc3\xaener de fian\xc3\xa7ailles_ or other appurtenances of a\r\nfashionable French wedding.\r\n\r\nHow that stupid, dull Englishman ever came to be admitted within the\r\nintellectual circle which revolved round \xe2\x80\x9cthe cleverest woman in\r\nEurope,\xe2\x80\x9d as her friends unanimously called her, no one ventured to\r\nguess\xe2\x80\x94a golden key is said to open every door, asserted the more\r\nmalignantly inclined.\r\n\r\nEnough, she married him, and \xe2\x80\x9cthe cleverest woman in Europe\xe2\x80\x9d had linked\r\nher fate to that \xe2\x80\x9cdemmed idiot\xe2\x80\x9d Blakeney, and not even her most\r\nintimate friends could assign to this strange step any other motive\r\nthan that of supreme eccentricity. Those friends who knew, laughed to\r\nscorn the idea that Marguerite St. Just had married a fool for the sake\r\nof the worldly advantages with which he might endow her. They knew, as\r\na matter of fact, that Marguerite St. Just cared nothing about money,\r\nand still less about a title; moreover, there were at least half a\r\ndozen other men in the cosmopolitan world equally well-born, if not so\r\nwealthy as Blakeney, who would have been only too happy to give\r\nMarguerite St. Just any position she might choose to covet.\r\n\r\nAs for Sir Percy himself, he was universally voted to be totally\r\nunqualified for the onerous post he had taken upon himself. His chief\r\nqualifications for it seemed to consist in his blind adoration for her,\r\nhis great wealth, and the high favour in which he stood at the English\r\ncourt; but London society thought that, taking into consideration his\r\nown intellectual limitations, it would have been wiser on his part had\r\nhe bestowed those worldly advantages upon a less brilliant and witty\r\nwife.\r\n\r\nAlthough lately he had been so prominent a figure in fashionable\r\nEnglish society, he had spent most of his early life abroad. His\r\nfather, the late Sir Algernon Blakeney, had had the terrible misfortune\r\nof seeing an idolized young wife become hopelessly insane after two\r\nyears of happy married life. Percy had just been born when the late\r\nLady Blakeney fell a prey to the terrible malady which in those days\r\nwas looked upon as hopelessly incurable and nothing short of a curse of\r\nGod upon the entire family. Sir Algernon took his afflicted young wife\r\nabroad, and there presumably Percy was educated, and grew up between an\r\nimbecile mother and a distracted father, until he attained his\r\nmajority. The death of his parents following close upon one another\r\nleft him a free man, and as Sir Algernon had led a forcibly simple and\r\nretired life, the large Blakeney fortune had increased tenfold.\r\n\r\nSir Percy Blakeney had travelled a great deal abroad, before he brought\r\nhome his beautiful, young, French wife. The fashionable circles of the\r\ntime were ready to receive them both with open arms. Sir Percy was\r\nrich, his wife was accomplished, the Prince of Wales took a very great\r\nliking to them both. Within six months they were the acknowledged\r\nleaders of fashion and of style. Sir Percy\xe2\x80\x99s coats were the talk of the\r\ntown, his inanities were quoted, his foolish laugh copied by the gilded\r\nyouth at Almack\xe2\x80\x99s or the Mall. Everyone knew that he was hopelessly\r\nstupid, but then that was scarcely to be wondered at, seeing that all\r\nthe Blakeneys, for generations, had been notoriously dull, and that his\r\nmother had died an imbecile.\r\n\r\nThus society accepted him, petted him, made much of him, since his\r\nhorses were the finest in the country, his _f\xc3\xaates_ and wines the most\r\nsought after. As for his marriage with \xe2\x80\x9cthe cleverest woman in Europe,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nwell! the inevitable came with sure and rapid footsteps. No one pitied\r\nhim, since his fate was of his own making. There were plenty of young\r\nladies in England, of high birth and good looks, who would have been\r\nquite willing to help him to spend the Blakeney fortune, whilst smiling\r\nindulgently at his inanities and his good-humoured foolishness.\r\nMoreover, Sir Percy got no pity, because he seemed to require none\xe2\x80\x94he\r\nseemed very proud of his clever wife, and to care little that she took\r\nno pains to disguise that good-natured contempt which she evidently\r\nfelt for him, and that she even amused herself by sharpening her ready\r\nwits at his expense.\r\n\r\nBut then Blakeney was really too stupid to notice the ridicule with\r\nwhich his clever wife covered him, and if his matrimonial relations\r\nwith the fascinating Parisienne had not turned out all that his hopes\r\nand his dog-like devotion for her had pictured, society could never do\r\nmore than vaguely guess at it.\r\n\r\nIn his beautiful house at Richmond he played second fiddle to his\r\nclever wife with imperturbable _bonhomie_; he lavished jewels and\r\nluxuries of all kinds upon her, which she took with inimitable grace,\r\ndispensing the hospitality of his superb mansion with the same\r\ngraciousness with which she had welcomed the intellectual coterie of\r\nParis.\r\n\r\nPhysically, Sir Percy Blakeney was undeniably handsome\xe2\x80\x94always excepting\r\nthe lazy, bored look which was habitual to him. He was always\r\nirreproachably dressed, and wore the exaggerated \xe2\x80\x9cIncroyable\xe2\x80\x9d fashions,\r\nwhich had just crept across from Paris to England, with the perfect\r\ngood taste innate in an English gentleman. On this special afternoon in\r\nSeptember, in spite of the long journey by coach, in spite of rain and\r\nmud, his coat set irreproachably across his fine shoulders, his hands\r\nlooked almost femininely white, as they emerged through billowy frills\r\nof finest Mechlin lace: the extravagantly short-waisted satin coat,\r\nwide-lapelled waistcoat, and tight-fitting striped breeches, set off\r\nhis massive figure to perfection, and in repose one might have admired\r\nso fine a specimen of English manhood, until the foppish ways, the\r\naffected movements, the perpetual inane laugh, brought one\xe2\x80\x99s admiration\r\nof Sir Percy Blakeney to an abrupt close.\r\n\r\nHe had lolled into the old-fashioned inn parlour, shaking the wet off\r\nhis fine overcoat; then putting up a gold-rimmed eye-glass to his lazy\r\nblue eye, he surveyed the company, upon whom an embarrassed silence had\r\nsuddenly fallen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do, Tony? How do, Ffoulkes?\xe2\x80\x9d he said, recognising the two young\r\nmen and shaking them by the hand. \xe2\x80\x9cZounds, my dear fellow,\xe2\x80\x9d he added,\r\nsmothering a slight yawn, \xe2\x80\x9cdid you ever see such a beastly day? Demmed\r\nclimate this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith a quaint little laugh, half of embarrassment and half of sarcasm,\r\nMarguerite had turned towards her husband, and was surveying him from\r\nhead to foot, with an amused little twinkle in her merry blue eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLa!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sir Percy, after a moment or two\xe2\x80\x99s silence, as no one\r\noffered any comment, \xe2\x80\x9chow sheepish you all look. . . . What\xe2\x80\x99s up?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, nothing, Sir Percy,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Marguerite, with a certain amount of\r\ngaiety, which, however, sounded somewhat forced, \xe2\x80\x9cnothing to disturb\r\nyour equanimity\xe2\x80\x94only an insult to your wife.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe laugh which accompanied this remark was evidently intended to\r\nreassure Sir Percy as to the gravity of the incident. It apparently\r\nsucceeded in that, for, echoing the laugh, he rejoined placidly\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLa, m\xe2\x80\x99dear! you don\xe2\x80\x99t say so. Begad! who was the bold man who dared to\r\ntackle you\xe2\x80\x94eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLord Tony tried to interpose, but had no time to do so, for the young\r\nVicomte had already quickly stepped forward.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, prefixing his little speech with an elaborate bow,\r\nand speaking in broken English, \xe2\x80\x9cmy mother, the Comtesse de Tournay de\r\nBasserive, has offenced Madame, who, I see, is your wife. I cannot ask\r\nyour pardon for my mother; what she does is right in my eyes. But I am\r\nready to offer you the usual reparation between men of honour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young man drew up his slim stature to its full height and looked\r\nvery enthusiastic, very proud, and very hot as he gazed at six foot odd\r\nof gorgeousness, as represented by Sir Percy Blakeney, Bart.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLud, Sir Andrew,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marguerite, with one of her merry infectious\r\nlaughs, \xe2\x80\x9clook on that pretty picture\xe2\x80\x94the English turkey and the French\r\nbantam.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe simile was quite perfect, and the English turkey looked down with\r\ncomplete bewilderment upon the dainty little French bantam, which\r\nhovered quite threateningly around him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLa! sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sir Percy at last, putting up his eye-glass and\r\nsurveying the young Frenchman with undisguised wonderment, \xe2\x80\x9cwhere, in\r\nthe cuckoo\xe2\x80\x99s name, did you learn to speak English?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur!\xe2\x80\x9d protested the Vicomte, somewhat abashed at the way his\r\nwarlike attitude had been taken by the ponderous-looking Englishman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI protest \xe2\x80\x99tis marvellous!\xe2\x80\x9d continued Sir Percy, imperturbably,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cdemmed marvellous! Don\xe2\x80\x99t you think so, Tony\xe2\x80\x94eh? I vow I can\xe2\x80\x99t speak\r\nthe French lingo like that. What?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, I\xe2\x80\x99ll vouch for that!\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined Marguerite. \xe2\x80\x9cSir Percy has a\r\nBritish accent you could cut with a knife.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur,\xe2\x80\x9d interposed the Vicomte earnestly, and in still more broken\r\nEnglish, \xe2\x80\x9cI fear you have not understand. I offer you the only\r\nposseeble reparation among gentlemen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat the devil is that?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Sir Percy, blandly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy sword, Monsieur,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the Vicomte, who, though still\r\nbewildered, was beginning to lose his temper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are a sportsman, Lord Tony,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marguerite, merrily; \xe2\x80\x9cten to one\r\non the little bantam.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Sir Percy was staring sleepily at the Vicomte for a moment or two,\r\nthrough his partly closed heavy lids, then he smothered another yawn,\r\nstretched his long limbs, and turned leisurely away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLud love you, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered good-humouredly. \xe2\x80\x9cDemmit, young man,\r\nwhat\xe2\x80\x99s the good of your sword to me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhat the Vicomte thought and felt at that moment, when that long-limbed\r\nEnglishman treated him with such marked insolence, might fill volumes\r\nof sound reflections. . . . What he said resolved itself into a single\r\narticulate word, for all the others were choked in his throat by his\r\nsurging wrath\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA duel, Monsieur,\xe2\x80\x9d he stammered.\r\n\r\nOnce more Blakeney turned, and from his high altitude looked down on\r\nthe choleric little man before him; but not even for a second did he\r\nseem to lose his own imperturbable good-humour. He laughed his own\r\npleasant and inane laugh, and burying his slender, long hands into the\r\ncapacious pockets of his overcoat, he said leisurely\xe2\x80\x94\r\n \xe2\x80\x9cA duel? La! is that what he meant? Odd\xe2\x80\x99s fish! you are a bloodthirsty\r\n young ruffian. Do you want to make a hole in a law-abiding man? . . .\r\n As for me, sir, I never fight duels,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, as he placidly sat\r\n down and stretched his long, lazy legs out before him. \xe2\x80\x9cDemmed\r\n uncomfortable things, duels, ain\xe2\x80\x99t they, Tony?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNow the Vicomte had no doubt vaguely heard that in England the fashion\r\nof duelling amongst gentlemen had been suppressed by the law with a\r\nvery stern hand; still to him, a Frenchman, whose notions of bravery\r\nand honour were based upon a code that had centuries of tradition to\r\nback it, the spectacle of a gentleman actually refusing to fight a duel\r\nwas little short of an enormity. In his mind he vaguely pondered\r\nwhether he should strike that long-legged Englishman in the face and\r\ncall him a coward, or whether such conduct in a lady\xe2\x80\x99s presence might\r\nbe deemed ungentlemanly, when Marguerite happily interposed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI pray you, Lord Tony,\xe2\x80\x9d she said in that gentle, sweet, musical voice\r\nof hers, \xe2\x80\x9cI pray you play the peacemaker. The child is bursting with\r\nrage, and,\xe2\x80\x9d she added with a _soup\xc3\xa7on_ of dry sarcasm, \xe2\x80\x9cmight do Sir\r\nPercy an injury.\xe2\x80\x9d She laughed a mocking little laugh, which, however,\r\ndid not in the least disturb her husband\xe2\x80\x99s placid equanimity. \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nBritish turkey has had the day,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cSir Percy would provoke all\r\nthe saints in the calendar and keep his temper the while.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut already Blakeney, good-humoured as ever, had joined in the laugh\r\nagainst himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDemmed smart that now, wasn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d he said, turning pleasantly to the\r\nVicomte. \xe2\x80\x9cClever woman my wife, sir. . . . You will find _that_ out if\r\nyou live long enough in England.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSir Percy is in the right, Vicomte,\xe2\x80\x9d here interposed Lord Antony,\r\nlaying a friendly hand on the young Frenchman\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder. \xe2\x80\x9cIt would\r\nhardly be fitting that you should commence your career in England by\r\nprovoking him to a duel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor a moment longer the Vicomte hesitated, then with a slight shrug of\r\nthe shoulders directed against the extraordinary code of honour\r\nprevailing in this fog-ridden island, he said with becoming dignity,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, well! if Monsieur is satisfied, I have no griefs. You, mi\xe2\x80\x99lor\xe2\x80\x99,\r\nare our protector. If I have done wrong, I withdraw myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye, do!\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined Blakeney, with a long sigh of satisfaction,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwithdraw yourself over there. Demmed excitable little puppy,\xe2\x80\x9d he added\r\nunder his breath. \xe2\x80\x9cFaith, Ffoulkes, if that\xe2\x80\x99s a specimen of the goods\r\nyou and your friends bring over from France, my advice to you is, drop\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em \xe2\x80\x99mid Channel, my friend, or I shall have to see old Pitt about it,\r\nget him to clap on a prohibitive tariff, and put you in the stocks an\r\nyou smuggle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLa, Sir Percy, your chivalry misguides you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marguerite,\r\ncoquettishly, \xe2\x80\x9cyou forget that you yourself have imported one bundle of\r\ngoods from France.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBlakeney slowly rose to his feet, and, making a deep and elaborate bow\r\nbefore his wife, he said with consummate gallantry,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had the pick of the market, Madame, and my taste is unerring.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore so than your chivalry, I fear,\xe2\x80\x9d she retorted sarcastically.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOdd\xe2\x80\x99s life, m\xe2\x80\x99dear! be reasonable! Do you think I am going to allow my\r\nbody to be made a pincushion of, by every little frog-eater who don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nlike the shape of your nose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLud, Sir Percy!\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Lady Blakeney as she bobbed him a quaint and\r\npretty curtsey, \xe2\x80\x9cyou need not be afraid! \xe2\x80\x99Tis not the _men_ who dislike\r\nthe shape of my nose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfraid be demmed! Do you impugn my bravery, Madame? I don\xe2\x80\x99t patronise\r\nthe ring for nothing, do I, Tony? I\xe2\x80\x99ve put up the fists with Red Sam\r\nbefore now, and\xe2\x80\x94and he didn\xe2\x80\x99t get it all his own way either\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cS\xe2\x80\x99faith, Sir Percy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marguerite, with a long and merry laugh,\r\nthat went echoing along the old oak rafters of the parlour, \xe2\x80\x9cI would I\r\nhad seen you then . . . ha! ha! ha! ha!\xe2\x80\x94you must have looked a pretty\r\npicture . . . and . . . and to be afraid of a little French boy . . .\r\nha! ha! . . . ha! ha!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHa! ha! ha! he! he! he!\xe2\x80\x9d echoed Sir Percy, good-humouredly. \xe2\x80\x9cLa,\r\nMadame, you honour me! Zooks! Ffoulkes, mark ye that! I have made my\r\nwife laugh!\xe2\x80\x94The cleverest woman in Europe! . . . Odd\xe2\x80\x99s fish, we must\r\nhave a bowl on that!\xe2\x80\x9d and he tapped vigorously on the table near him.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHey! Jelly! Quick, man! Here, Jelly!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHarmony was once more restored. Mr. Jellyband, with a mighty effort,\r\nrecovered himself from the many emotions he had experienced within the\r\nlast half hour.\r\n \xe2\x80\x9cA bowl of punch, Jelly, hot and strong, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d said Sir Percy. \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\n wits that have just made a clever woman laugh must be whetted! Ha! ha!\r\n ha! Hasten, my good Jelly!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, there is no time, Sir Percy,\xe2\x80\x9d interposed Marguerite. \xe2\x80\x9cThe skipper\r\nwill be here directly and my brother must get on board, or the _Day\r\nDream_ will miss the tide.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTime, m\xe2\x80\x99dear? There is plenty of time for any gentleman to get drunk\r\nand get on board before the turn of the tide.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think, your ladyship,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jellyband, respectfully, \xe2\x80\x9cthat the young\r\ngentleman is coming along now with Sir Percy\xe2\x80\x99s skipper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s right,\xe2\x80\x9d said Blakeney, \xe2\x80\x9cthen Armand can join us in the merry\r\nbowl. Think you, Tony,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, turning towards the Vicomte, \xe2\x80\x9cthat\r\nthat jackanapes of yours will join us in a glass? Tell him that we\r\ndrink in token of reconciliation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn fact you are all such merry company,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marguerite, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I\r\ntrust you will forgive me if I bid my brother good-bye in another\r\nroom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt would have been bad form to protest. Both Lord Antony and Sir Andrew\r\nfelt that Lady Blakeney could not altogether be in tune with them at\r\nthat moment. Her love for her brother, Armand St. Just, was deep and\r\ntouching in the extreme. He had just spent a few weeks with her in her\r\nEnglish home, and was going back to serve his country, at a moment when\r\ndeath was the usual reward for the most enduring devotion.\r\n\r\nSir Percy also made no attempt to detain his wife. With that perfect,\r\nsomewhat affected gallantry which characterised his every movement, he\r\nopened the coffee-room door for her, and made her the most approved and\r\nelaborate bow, which the fashion of the time dictated, as she sailed\r\nout of the room without bestowing on him more than a passing, slightly\r\ncontemptuous glance. Only Sir Andrew Ffoulkes, whose every thought\r\nsince he had met Suzanne de Tournay seemed keener, more gentle, more\r\ninnately sympathetic, noted the curious look of intense longing, of\r\ndeep and hopeless passion, with which the inane and flippant Sir Percy\r\nfollowed the retreating figure of his brilliant wife.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VII.\r\nTHE SECRET ORCHARD\r\n\r\n\r\nOnce outside the noisy coffee-room, alone in the dimly-lighted passage,\r\nMarguerite Blakeney seemed to breathe more freely. She heaved a deep\r\nsigh, like one who had long been oppressed with the heavy weight of\r\nconstant self-control, and she allowed a few tears to fall unheeded\r\ndown her cheeks.\r\n\r\nOutside the rain had ceased, and through the swiftly passing clouds,\r\nthe pale rays of an after-storm sun shone upon the beautiful white\r\ncoast of Kent and the quaint, irregular houses that clustered round the\r\nAdmiralty Pier. Marguerite Blakeney stepped on to the porch and looked\r\nout to sea. Silhouetted against the ever-changing sky, a graceful\r\nschooner, with white sails set, was gently dancing in the breeze. The\r\n_Day Dream_ it was, Sir Percy Blakeney\xe2\x80\x99s yacht, which was ready to take\r\nArmand St. Just back to France into the very midst of that seething,\r\nbloody Revolution which was overthrowing a monarchy, attacking a\r\nreligion, destroying a society, in order to try and rebuild upon the\r\nashes of tradition a new Utopia, of which a few men dreamed, but which\r\nnone had the power to establish.\r\n\r\nIn the distance two figures were approaching \xe2\x80\x9cThe Fisherman\xe2\x80\x99s Rest\xe2\x80\x9d:\r\none, an oldish man, with a curious fringe of grey hairs round a rotund\r\nand massive chin, and who walked with that peculiar rolling gait which\r\ninvariably betrays the seafaring man: the other, a young, slight\r\nfigure, neatly and becomingly dressed in a dark, many-caped overcoat;\r\nhe was clean-shaved, and his dark hair was taken well back over a clear\r\nand noble forehead.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArmand!\xe2\x80\x9d said Marguerite Blakeney, as soon as she saw him approaching\r\nfrom the distance, and a happy smile shone on her sweet face, even\r\nthrough the tears.\r\n\r\nA minute or two later brother and sister were locked in each other\xe2\x80\x99s\r\narms, while the old skipper stood respectfully on one side.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow much time have we got, Briggs?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Lady Blakeney, \xe2\x80\x9cbefore M.\r\nSt. Just need go on board?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe ought to weigh anchor before half an hour, your ladyship,\xe2\x80\x9d replied\r\nthe old man, pulling at his grey forelock.\r\n\r\nLinking her arm in his, Marguerite led her brother towards the cliffs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHalf an hour,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, looking wistfully out to sea, \xe2\x80\x9chalf an hour\r\nmore and you\xe2\x80\x99ll be far from me, Armand! Oh! I can\xe2\x80\x99t believe that you\r\nare going, dear! These last few days\xe2\x80\x94whilst Percy has been away, and\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve had you all to myself, have slipped by like a dream.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not going far, sweet one,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young man gently, \xe2\x80\x9ca narrow\r\nchannel to cross\xe2\x80\x94a few miles of road\xe2\x80\x94I can soon come back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, \xe2\x80\x99tis not the distance, Armand\xe2\x80\x94but that awful Paris . . . just now\r\n. . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey had reached the edge of the cliff. The gentle sea-breeze blew\r\nMarguerite\xe2\x80\x99s hair about her face, and sent the ends of her soft lace\r\nfichu waving round her, like a white and supple snake. She tried to\r\npierce the distance far away, beyond which lay the shores of France:\r\nthat relentless and stern France which was exacting her pound of flesh,\r\nthe blood-tax from the noblest of her sons.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOur own beautiful country, Marguerite,\xe2\x80\x9d said Armand, who seemed to\r\nhave divined her thoughts.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are going too far, Armand,\xe2\x80\x9d she said vehemently. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are a\r\nrepublican, so am I . . . we have the same thoughts, the same\r\nenthusiasm for liberty and equality . . . but even _you_ must think\r\nthat they are going too far . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush!\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d said Armand, instinctively, as he threw a quick, apprehensive\r\nglance around him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! you see: you don\xe2\x80\x99t think yourself that it is safe even to speak of\r\nthese things\xe2\x80\x94here in England!\xe2\x80\x9d She clung to him suddenly with strong,\r\nalmost motherly, passion: \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t go, Armand!\xe2\x80\x9d she begged; \xe2\x80\x9cdon\xe2\x80\x99t go\r\nback! What should I do if . . . if . . . if . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer voice was choked in sobs, her eyes, tender, blue and loving, gazed\r\nappealingly at the young man, who in his turn looked steadfastly into\r\nhers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou would in any case be my own brave sister,\xe2\x80\x9d he said gently, \xe2\x80\x9cwho\r\nwould remember that, when France is in peril, it is not for her sons to\r\nturn their backs on her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEven as he spoke, that sweet, childlike smile crept back into her face,\r\npathetic in the extreme, for it seemed drowned in tears.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! Armand!\xe2\x80\x9d she said quaintly, \xe2\x80\x9cI sometimes wish you had not so many\r\nlofty virtues. . . . I assure you little sins are far less dangerous\r\nand uncomfortable. But you _will_ be prudent?\xe2\x80\x9d she added earnestly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs far as possible . . . I promise you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRemember, dear, I have only you . . . to . . . to care for me. . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, sweet one, you have other interests now. Percy cares for you. . .\r\n.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA look of strange wistfulness crept into her eyes as she murmured,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe did . . . once . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut surely . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere, there, dear, don\xe2\x80\x99t distress yourself on my account. Percy is\r\nvery good . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay!\xe2\x80\x9d he interrupted energetically, \xe2\x80\x9cI will distress myself on your\r\naccount, my Margot. Listen, dear, I have not spoken of these things to\r\nyou before; something always seemed to stop me when I wished to\r\nquestion you. But, somehow, I feel as if I could not go away and leave\r\nyou now without asking you one question. . . . You need not answer it\r\nif you do not wish,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, as he noted a sudden hard look, almost\r\nof apprehension, darting through her eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked simply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes Sir Percy Blakeney know that . . . I mean, does he know the part\r\nyou played in the arrest of the Marquis de St. Cyr?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe laughed\xe2\x80\x94a mirthless, bitter, contemptuous laugh, which was like a\r\njarring chord in the music of her voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat I denounced the Marquis de St. Cyr, you mean, to the tribunal\r\nthat ultimately sent him and all his family to the guillotine? Yes, he\r\ndoes know. . . . I told him after I married him. . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou told him all the circumstances\xe2\x80\x94which so completely exonerated you\r\nfrom any blame?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was too late to talk of \xe2\x80\x98circumstances\xe2\x80\x99; he heard the story from\r\nother sources; my confession came too tardily, it seems. I could no\r\nlonger plead extenuating circumstances: I could not bemean myself by\r\ntrying to explain\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now I have the satisfaction, Armand, of knowing that the biggest\r\nfool in England has the most complete contempt for his wife.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe spoke with vehement bitterness this time, and Armand St. Just, who\r\nloved her so dearly, felt that he had placed a somewhat clumsy finger\r\nupon an aching wound.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut Sir Percy loved you, Margot,\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated gently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLoved me?\xe2\x80\x94Well, Armand, I thought at one time that he did, or I should\r\nnot have married him. I daresay,\xe2\x80\x9d she added, speaking very rapidly, as\r\nif she were glad at last to lay down a heavy burden, which had\r\noppressed her for months, \xe2\x80\x9cI daresay that even you thought\xe2\x80\x94as everybody\r\nelse did\xe2\x80\x94that I married Sir Percy because of his wealth\xe2\x80\x94but I assure\r\nyou, dear, that it was not so. He seemed to worship me with a curious\r\nintensity of concentrated passion, which went straight to my heart. I\r\nhad never loved anyone before, as you know, and I was four-and-twenty\r\nthen\xe2\x80\x94so I naturally thought that it was not in my nature to love. But\r\nit has always seemed to me that it _must_ be _heavenly_ to be loved\r\nblindly, passionately, wholly . . . worshipped, in fact\xe2\x80\x94and the very\r\nfact that Percy was slow and stupid was an attraction for me, as I\r\nthought he would love me all the more. A clever man would naturally\r\nhave other interests, an ambitious man other hopes. . . . I thought\r\nthat a fool would worship, and think of nothing else. And I was ready\r\nto respond, Armand; I would have allowed myself to be worshipped, and\r\ngiven infinite tenderness in return. . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe sighed\xe2\x80\x94and there was a world of disillusionment in that sigh.\r\nArmand St. Just had allowed her to speak on without interruption: he\r\nlistened to her, whilst allowing his own thoughts to run riot. It was\r\nterrible to see a young and beautiful woman\xe2\x80\x94a girl in all but\r\nname\xe2\x80\x94still standing almost at the threshold of her life, yet bereft of\r\nhope, bereft of illusions, bereft of those golden and fantastic dreams,\r\nwhich should have made her youth one long, perpetual holiday.\r\n\r\nYet perhaps\xe2\x80\x94though he loved his sister dearly\xe2\x80\x94perhaps he understood: he\r\nhad studied men in many countries, men of all ages, men of every grade\r\nof social and intellectual status, and inwardly he understood what\r\nMarguerite had left unsaid. Granted that Percy Blakeney was\r\ndull-witted, but in his slow-going mind, there would still be room for\r\nthat ineradicable pride of a descendant of a long line of English\r\ngentlemen. A Blakeney had died on Bosworth Field, another had\r\nsacrificed life and fortune for the sake of a treacherous Stuart: and\r\nthat same pride\xe2\x80\x94foolish and prejudiced as the republican Armand would\r\ncall it\xe2\x80\x94must have been stung to the quick on hearing of the sin which\r\nlay at Lady Blakeney\xe2\x80\x99s door. She had been young, misguided, ill-advised\r\nperhaps. Armand knew that: and those who took advantage of Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nyouth, her impulses and imprudence, knew it still better; but Blakeney\r\nwas slow-witted, he would not listen to \xe2\x80\x9ccircumstances,\xe2\x80\x9d he only clung\r\nto facts, and these had shown him Lady Blakeney denouncing a fellow-man\r\nto a tribunal that knew no pardon: and the contempt he would feel for\r\nthe deed she had done, however unwittingly, would kill that same love\r\nin him, in which sympathy and intellectuality could never have had a\r\npart.\r\n\r\nYet even now, his own sister puzzled him. Life and love have such\r\nstrange vagaries. Could it be that with the waning of her husband\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nlove, Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s heart had awakened with love for him? Strange\r\nextremes meet in love\xe2\x80\x99s pathway: this woman, who had had half\r\nintellectual Europe at her feet, might perhaps have set her affections\r\non a fool. Marguerite was gazing out towards the sunset. Armand could\r\nnot see her face, but presently it seemed to him that something which\r\nglittered for a moment in the golden evening light, fell from her eyes\r\nonto her dainty fichu of lace.\r\n\r\nBut he could not broach that subject with her. He knew her strange,\r\npassionate nature so well, and knew that reserve which lurked behind\r\nher frank, open ways.\r\n They had always been together, these two, for their parents had died\r\n when Armand was still a youth, and Marguerite but a child. He, some\r\n eight years her senior, had watched over her until her marriage; had\r\n chaperoned her during those brilliant years spent in the flat of the\r\n Rue de Richelieu, and had seen her enter upon this new life of hers,\r\n here in England, with much sorrow and some foreboding.\r\n\r\nThis was his first visit to England since her marriage, and the few\r\nmonths of separation had already seemed to have built up a slight, thin\r\npartition between brother and sister; the same deep, intense love was\r\nstill there, on both sides, but each now seemed to have a secret\r\norchard, into which the other dared not penetrate.\r\n\r\nThere was much Armand St. Just could not tell his sister; the political\r\naspect of the revolution in France was changing almost every day; she\r\nmight not understand how his own views and sympathies might become\r\nmodified, even as the excesses, committed by those who had been his\r\nfriends, grew in horror and in intensity. And Marguerite could not\r\nspeak to her brother about the secrets of her heart; she hardly\r\nunderstood them herself, she only knew that, in the midst of luxury,\r\nshe felt lonely and unhappy.\r\n\r\nAnd now Armand was going away; she feared for his safety, she longed\r\nfor his presence. She would not spoil these last few sadly-sweet\r\nmoments by speaking about herself. She led him gently along the cliffs,\r\nthen down to the beach; their arms linked in one another\xe2\x80\x99s, they had\r\nstill so much to say that lay just outside that secret orchard of\r\ntheirs.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VIII.\r\nTHE ACCREDITED AGENT\r\n\r\n\r\nThe afternoon was rapidly drawing to a close; and a long, chilly\r\nEnglish summer\xe2\x80\x99s evening was throwing a misty pall over the green\r\nKentish landscape.\r\n\r\nThe _Day Dream_ had set sail, and Marguerite Blakeney stood alone on\r\nthe edge of the cliff for over an hour, watching those white sails,\r\nwhich bore so swiftly away from her the only being who really cared for\r\nher, whom she dared to love, whom she knew she could trust.\r\n\r\nSome little distance away to her left the lights from the coffee-room\r\nof \xe2\x80\x9cThe Fisherman\xe2\x80\x99s Rest\xe2\x80\x9d glittered yellow in the gathering mist; from\r\ntime to time it seemed to her aching nerves as if she could catch from\r\nthence the sound of merry-making and of jovial talk, or even that\r\nperpetual, senseless laugh of her husband\xe2\x80\x99s, which grated continually\r\nupon her sensitive ears.\r\n\r\nSir Percy had had the delicacy to leave her severely alone. She\r\nsupposed that, in his own stupid, good-natured way, he may have\r\nunderstood that she would wish to remain alone, while those white sails\r\ndisappeared into the vague horizon, so many miles away. He, whose\r\nnotions of propriety and decorum were supersensitive, had not suggested\r\neven that an attendant should remain within call. Marguerite was\r\ngrateful to her husband for all this; she always tried to be grateful\r\nto him for his thoughtfulness, which was constant, and for his\r\ngenerosity, which really was boundless. She tried even at times to curb\r\nthe sarcastic, bitter thoughts of him, which made her\xe2\x80\x94in spite of\r\nherself\xe2\x80\x94say cruel, insulting things, which she vaguely hoped would\r\nwound him.\r\n\r\nYes! she often wished to wound him, to make him feel that she too held\r\nhim in contempt, that she too had forgotten that once she had almost\r\nloved him. Loved that inane fop! whose thoughts seemed unable to soar\r\nbeyond the tying of a cravat or the new cut of a coat. Bah! And yet! .\r\n. . vague memories, that were sweet and ardent and attuned to this calm\r\nsummer\xe2\x80\x99s evening, came wafted back to her memory, on the invisible\r\nwings of the light sea-breeze: the time when first he worshipped her;\r\nhe seemed so devoted\xe2\x80\x94a very slave\xe2\x80\x94and there was a certain latent\r\nintensity in that love which had fascinated her.\r\n\r\nThen suddenly that love, that devotion, which throughout his courtship\r\nshe had looked upon as the slavish fidelity of a dog, seemed to vanish\r\ncompletely. Twenty-four hours after the simple little ceremony at old\r\nSt. Roch, she had told him the story of how, inadvertently, she had\r\nspoken of certain matters connected with the Marquis de St. Cyr before\r\nsome men\xe2\x80\x94her friends\xe2\x80\x94who had used this information against the\r\nunfortunate Marquis, and sent him and his family to the guillotine.\r\n\r\nShe hated the Marquis. Years ago, Armand, her dear brother, had loved\r\nAng\xc3\xa8le de St. Cyr, but St. Just was a plebeian, and the Marquis full of\r\nthe pride and arrogant prejudices of his caste. One day Armand, the\r\nrespectful, timid lover, ventured on sending a small poem\xe2\x80\x94enthusiastic,\r\nardent, passionate\xe2\x80\x94to the idol of his dreams. The next night he was\r\nwaylaid just outside Paris by the valets of the Marquis de St. Cyr, and\r\nignominiously thrashed\xe2\x80\x94thrashed like a dog within an inch of his\r\nlife\xe2\x80\x94because he had dared to raise his eyes to the daughter of the\r\naristocrat. The incident was one which, in those days, some two years\r\nbefore the great Revolution, was of almost daily occurrence in France;\r\nincidents of that type, in fact, led to the bloody reprisals, which a\r\nfew years later sent most of those haughty heads to the guillotine.\r\n\r\nMarguerite remembered it all: what her brother must have suffered in\r\nhis manhood and his pride must have been appalling; what she suffered\r\nthrough him and with him she never attempted even to analyse.\r\n\r\nThen the day of retribution came. St. Cyr and his kind had found their\r\nmasters, in those same plebeians whom they had despised. Armand and\r\nMarguerite, both intellectual, thinking beings, adopted with the\r\nenthusiasm of their years the Utopian doctrines of the Revolution,\r\nwhile the Marquis de St. Cyr and his family fought inch by inch for the\r\nretention of those privileges which had placed them socially above\r\ntheir fellow-men. Marguerite, impulsive, thoughtless, not calculating\r\nthe purport of her words, still smarting under the terrible insult her\r\nbrother had suffered at the Marquis\xe2\x80\x99 hands, happened to hear\xe2\x80\x94amongst\r\nher own coterie\xe2\x80\x94that the St. Cyrs were in treasonable correspondence\r\nwith Austria, hoping to obtain the Emperor\xe2\x80\x99s support to quell the\r\ngrowing revolution in their own country.\r\n\r\nIn those days one denunciation was sufficient: Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s few\r\nthoughtless words anent the Marquis de St. Cyr bore fruit within\r\ntwenty-four hours. He was arrested. His papers were searched: letters\r\nfrom the Austrian Emperor, promising to send troops against the Paris\r\npopulace, were found in his desk. He was arraigned for treason against\r\nthe nation, and sent to the guillotine, whilst his family, his wife and\r\nhis sons, shared this awful fate.\r\n\r\nMarguerite, horrified at the terrible consequences of her own\r\nthoughtlessness, was powerless to save the Marquis: her own coterie,\r\nthe leaders of the revolutionary movement, all proclaimed her as a\r\nheroine: and when she married Sir Percy Blakeney, she did not perhaps\r\naltogether realise how severely he would look upon the sin, which she\r\nhad so inadvertently committed, and which still lay heavily upon her\r\nsoul. She made full confession of it to her husband, trusting to his\r\nblind love for her, her boundless power over him, to soon make him\r\nforget what might have sounded unpleasant to an English ear.\r\n\r\nCertainly at the moment he seemed to take it very quietly; hardly, in\r\nfact, did he appear to understand the meaning of all she said; but what\r\nwas more certain still, was that never after that could she detect the\r\nslightest sign of that love, which she once believed had been wholly\r\nhers. Now they had drifted quite apart, and Sir Percy seemed to have\r\nlaid aside his love for her, as he would an ill-fitting glove. She\r\ntried to rouse him by sharpening her ready wit against his dull\r\nintellect; endeavoured to excite his jealousy, if she could not rouse\r\nhis love; tried to goad him to self-assertion, but all in vain. He\r\nremained the same, always passive, drawling, sleepy, always courteous,\r\ninvariably a gentleman: she had all that the world and a wealthy\r\nhusband can give to a pretty woman, yet on this beautiful summer\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nevening, with the white sails of the _Day Dream_ finally hidden by the\r\nevening shadows, she felt more lonely than that poor tramp who plodded\r\nhis way wearily along the rugged cliffs.\r\n\r\nWith another heavy sigh, Marguerite Blakeney turned her back upon the\r\nsea and cliffs, and walked slowly back towards \xe2\x80\x9cThe Fisherman\xe2\x80\x99s Rest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nAs she drew near, the sound of revelry, of gay, jovial laughter, grew\r\nlouder and more distinct. She could distinguish Sir Andrew Ffoulkes\xe2\x80\x99\r\npleasant voice, Lord Tony\xe2\x80\x99s boisterous guffaws, her husband\xe2\x80\x99s\r\noccasional, drawly, sleepy comments; then realising the loneliness of\r\nthe road and the fast gathering gloom round her, she quickened her\r\nsteps . . . the next moment she perceived a stranger coming rapidly\r\ntowards her. Marguerite did not look up: she was not the least nervous,\r\nand \xe2\x80\x9cThe Fisherman\xe2\x80\x99s Rest\xe2\x80\x9d was now well within call.\r\n\r\nThe stranger paused when he saw Marguerite coming quickly towards him,\r\nand just as she was about to slip past him, he said very quietly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCitoyenne St. Just.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarguerite uttered a little cry of astonishment, at thus hearing her\r\nown familiar maiden name uttered so close to her. She looked up at the\r\nstranger, and this time, with a cry of unfeigned pleasure, she put out\r\nboth her hands effusively towards him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChauvelin!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHimself, citoyenne, at your service,\xe2\x80\x9d said the stranger, gallantly\r\nkissing the tips of her fingers.\r\n\r\nMarguerite said nothing for a moment or two, as she surveyed with\r\nobvious delight the not very prepossessing little figure before her.\r\nChauvelin was then nearer forty than thirty\xe2\x80\x94a clever, shrewd-looking\r\npersonality, with a curious fox-like expression in the deep, sunken\r\neyes. He was the same stranger who an hour or two previously had joined\r\nMr. Jellyband in a friendly glass of wine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChauvelin . . . my friend . . .\xe2\x80\x9d said Marguerite, with a pretty little\r\nsigh of satisfaction. \xe2\x80\x9cI am mightily pleased to see you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo doubt poor Marguerite St. Just, lonely in the midst of her grandeur,\r\nand of her starchy friends, was happy to see a face that brought back\r\nmemories of that happy time in Paris, when she reigned\xe2\x80\x94a queen\xe2\x80\x94over the\r\nintellectual coterie of the Rue de Richelieu. She did not notice the\r\nsarcastic little smile, however, that hovered round the thin lips of\r\nChauvelin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut tell me,\xe2\x80\x9d she added merrily, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat in the world, or whom in the\r\nworld, are you doing here in England?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n She had resumed her walk towards the inn, and Chauvelin turned and\r\n walked beside her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI might return the subtle compliment, fair lady,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat of\r\nyourself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I?\xe2\x80\x9d she said, with a shrug of the shoulders. \xe2\x80\x9cJe m\xe2\x80\x99ennuie, mon\r\nami, that is all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey had reached the porch of \xe2\x80\x9cThe Fisherman\xe2\x80\x99s Rest,\xe2\x80\x9d but Marguerite\r\nseemed loth to go within. The evening air was lovely after the storm,\r\nand she had found a friend who exhaled the breath of Paris, who knew\r\nArmand well, who could talk of all the merry, brilliant friends whom\r\nshe had left behind. So she lingered on under the pretty porch, while\r\nthrough the gaily-lighted dormer-window of the coffee-room came sounds\r\nof laughter, of calls for \xe2\x80\x9cSally\xe2\x80\x9d and for beer, of tapping of mugs, and\r\nclinking of dice, mingled with Sir Percy Blakeney\xe2\x80\x99s inane and mirthless\r\nlaugh. Chauvelin stood beside her, his shrewd, pale, yellow eyes fixed\r\non the pretty face, which looked so sweet and childlike in this soft\r\nEnglish summer twilight.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou surprise me, citoyenne,\xe2\x80\x9d he said quietly, as he took a pinch of\r\nsnuff.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo I now?\xe2\x80\x9d she retorted gaily. \xe2\x80\x9cFaith, my little Chauvelin, I should\r\nhave thought that, with your penetration, you would have guessed that\r\nan atmosphere composed of fogs and virtues would never suit Marguerite\r\nSt. Just.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear me! is it as bad as that?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, in mock consternation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite,\xe2\x80\x9d she retorted, \xe2\x80\x9cand worse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStrange! Now, I thought that a pretty woman would have found English\r\ncountry life peculiarly attractive.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes! so did I,\xe2\x80\x9d she said with a sigh. \xe2\x80\x9cPretty women,\xe2\x80\x9d she added\r\nmeditatively, \xe2\x80\x9cought to have a good time in England, since all the\r\npleasant things are forbidden them\xe2\x80\x94the very things they do every day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite so!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll hardly believe it, my little Chauvelin,\xe2\x80\x9d she said earnestly,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbut I often pass a whole day\xe2\x80\x94a whole day\xe2\x80\x94without encountering a single\r\ntemptation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo wonder,\xe2\x80\x9d retorted Chauvelin, gallantly, \xe2\x80\x9cthat the cleverest woman\r\nin Europe is troubled with _ennui_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe laughed one of her melodious, rippling, childlike laughs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt must be pretty bad, mustn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d she said archly, \xe2\x80\x9cor I should not\r\nhave been so pleased to see you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd this within a year of a romantic love match! . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes! . . . a year of a romantic love match . . . that\xe2\x80\x99s just the\r\ndifficulty . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! . . . that idyllic folly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chauvelin, with quiet sarcasm,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cdid not then survive the lapse of . . . weeks?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIdyllic follies never last, my little Chauvelin. . . . They come upon\r\nus like the measles . . . and are as easily cured.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChauvelin took another pinch of snuff: he seemed very much addicted to\r\nthat pernicious habit, so prevalent in those days; perhaps, too, he\r\nfound the taking of snuff a convenient veil for disguising the quick,\r\nshrewd glances with which he strove to read the very souls of those\r\nwith whom he came in contact.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo wonder,\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated, with the same gallantry, \xe2\x80\x9cthat the most\r\nactive brain in Europe is troubled with _ennui_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was in hopes that you had a prescription against the malady, my\r\nlittle Chauvelin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can I hope to succeed in that which Sir Percy Blakeney has failed\r\nto accomplish?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall we leave Sir Percy out of the question for the present, my dear\r\nfriend?\xe2\x80\x9d she said drily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! my dear lady, pardon me, but that is just what we cannot very well\r\ndo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chauvelin, whilst once again his eyes, keen as those of a fox\r\non the alert, darted a quick glance at Marguerite. \xe2\x80\x9cI have a most\r\nperfect prescription against the worst form of _ennui_, which I would\r\nhave been happy to submit to you, but\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere _is_ Sir Percy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat has he to do with it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite a good deal, I am afraid. The prescription I would offer, fair\r\nlady, is called by a very plebeian name: Work!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWork?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChauvelin looked at Marguerite long and scrutinisingly. It seemed as if\r\nthose keen, pale eyes of his were reading every one of her thoughts.\r\nThey were alone together; the evening air was quite still, and their\r\nsoft whispers were drowned in the noise which came from the\r\ncoffee-room. Still, Chauvelin took a step or two from under the porch,\r\nlooked quickly and keenly all round him, then, seeing that indeed no\r\none was within earshot, he once more came back close to Marguerite.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you render France a small service, citoyenne?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, with a\r\nsudden change of manner, which lent his thin, fox-like face singular\r\nearnestness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLa, man!\xe2\x80\x9d she replied flippantly, \xe2\x80\x9chow serious you look all of a\r\nsudden. . . . Indeed I do not know if I _would_ render France a small\r\nservice\xe2\x80\x94at any rate, it depends upon the kind of service she\xe2\x80\x94or\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x94want.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you ever heard of the Scarlet Pimpernel, Citoyenne St. Just?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nasked Chauvelin, abruptly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHeard of the Scarlet Pimpernel?\xe2\x80\x9d she retorted with a long and merry\r\nlaugh, \xe2\x80\x9cFaith, man! we talk of nothing else. . . . We have hats \xe2\x80\x98\xc3\xa0 la\r\nScarlet Pimpernel\xe2\x80\x99; our horses are called \xe2\x80\x98Scarlet Pimpernel\xe2\x80\x99; at the\r\nPrince of Wales\xe2\x80\x99 supper party the other night we had a \xe2\x80\x98ouffl\xc3\xa9 \xc3\xa0 la\r\nScarlet Pimpernel.\xe2\x80\x99 . . . Lud!\xe2\x80\x9d she added gaily, \xe2\x80\x9cthe other day I\r\nordered at my milliner\xe2\x80\x99s a blue dress trimmed with green, and bless me,\r\nif she did not call that \xe2\x80\x98\xc3\xa0 la Scarlet Pimpernel.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChauvelin had not moved while she prattled merrily along; he did not\r\neven attempt to stop her when her musical voice and her childlike laugh\r\nwent echoing through the still evening air. But he remained serious and\r\nearnest whilst she laughed, and his voice, clear, incisive, and hard,\r\nwas not raised above his breath as he said,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, as you have heard of that enigmatical personage, citoyenne, you\r\nmust also have guessed, and known, that the man who hides his identity\r\nunder that strange pseudonym, is the most bitter enemy of our republic,\r\nof France . . . of men like Armand St. Just.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLa! . . .\xe2\x80\x9d she said, with a quaint little sigh, \xe2\x80\x9cI dare swear he is. .\r\n. . France has many bitter enemies these days.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you, citoyenne, are a daughter of France, and should be ready to\r\nhelp her in a moment of deadly peril.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy brother Armand devotes his life to France,\xe2\x80\x9d she retorted proudly;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cas for me, I can do nothing . . . here in England. . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you . . .\xe2\x80\x9d he urged still more earnestly, whilst his thin\r\nfox-like face seemed suddenly to have grown impressive and full of\r\ndignity, \xe2\x80\x9chere, in England, citoyenne . . . you alone can help us. . .\r\n. Listen!\xe2\x80\x94I have been sent over here by the Republican Government as\r\nits representative: I present my credentials to Mr. Pitt in London\r\nto-morrow. One of my duties here is to find out all about this League\r\nof the Scarlet Pimpernel, which has become a standing menace to France,\r\nsince it is pledged to help our cursed aristocrats\xe2\x80\x94traitors to their\r\ncountry, and enemies of the people\xe2\x80\x94to escape from the just punishment\r\nwhich they deserve. You know as well as I do, citoyenne, that once they\r\nare over here, those French _\xc3\xa9migr\xc3\xa9s_ try to rouse public feeling\r\nagainst the Republic. . . . They are ready to join issue with any enemy\r\nbold enough to attack France. . . . Now, within the last month, scores\r\nof these _\xc3\xa9migr\xc3\xa9s_, some only suspected of treason, others actually\r\ncondemned by the Tribunal of Public Safety, have succeeded in crossing\r\nthe Channel. Their escape in each instance was planned, organised and\r\neffected by this society of young English jackanapes, headed by a man\r\nwhose brain seems as resourceful as his identity is mysterious. All the\r\nmost strenuous efforts on the part of my spies have failed to discover\r\nwho he is; whilst the others are the hands, he is the head, who beneath\r\nthis strange anonymity calmly works at the destruction of France. I\r\nmean to strike at that head, and for this I want your help\xe2\x80\x94through him\r\nafterwards I can reach the rest of the gang: he is a young buck in\r\nEnglish society, of that I feel sure. Find that man for me, citoyenne!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe urged, \xe2\x80\x9cfind him for France!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarguerite had listened to Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s impassioned speech without\r\nuttering a word, scarce making a movement, hardly daring to breathe.\r\nShe had told him before that this mysterious hero of romance was the\r\ntalk of the smart set to which she belonged; already, before this, her\r\nheart and her imagination had been stirred by the thought of the brave\r\nman, who, unknown to fame, had rescued hundreds of lives from a\r\nterrible, often an unmerciful fate. She had but little real sympathy\r\nwith those haughty French aristocrats, insolent in their pride of\r\ncaste, of whom the Comtesse de Tournay de Basserive was so typical an\r\nexample; but, republican and liberal-minded though she was from\r\nprinciple, she hated and loathed the methods which the young Republic\r\nhad chosen for establishing itself. She had not been in Paris for some\r\nmonths; the horrors and bloodshed of the Reign of Terror, culminating\r\nin the September massacres, had only come across the Channel to her as\r\na faint echo. Robespierre, Danton, Marat, she had not known in their\r\nnew guise of bloody justiciaries, merciless wielders of the guillotine.\r\nHer very soul recoiled in horror from these excesses, to which she\r\nfeared her brother Armand\xe2\x80\x94moderate republican as he was\xe2\x80\x94might become\r\none day the holocaust.\r\n\r\nThen, when first she heard of this band of young English enthusiasts,\r\nwho, for sheer love of their fellow-men, dragged women and children,\r\nold and young men, from a horrible death, her heart had glowed with\r\npride for them, and now, as Chauvelin spoke, her very soul went out to\r\nthe gallant and mysterious leader of the reckless little band, who\r\nrisked his life daily, who gave it freely and without ostentation, for\r\nthe sake of humanity.\r\n\r\nHer eyes were moist when Chauvelin had finished speaking, the lace at\r\nher bosom rose and fell with her quick, excited breathing; she no\r\nlonger heard the noise of drinking from the inn, she did not heed her\r\nhusband\xe2\x80\x99s voice or his inane laugh, her thoughts had gone wandering in\r\nsearch of the mysterious hero! Ah! there was a man she might have\r\nloved, had he come her way: everything in him appealed to her romantic\r\nimagination; his personality, his strength, his bravery, the loyalty of\r\nthose who served under him in the same noble cause, and, above all,\r\nthat anonymity which crowned him, as if with a halo of romantic glory.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFind him for France, citoyenne!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s voice close to her ear roused her from her dreams. The\r\nmysterious hero had vanished, and, not twenty yards away from her, a\r\nman was drinking and laughing, to whom she had sworn faith and loyalty.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLa! man,\xe2\x80\x9d she said with a return of her assumed flippancy, \xe2\x80\x9cyou are\r\nastonishing. Where in the world am I to look for him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou go everywhere, citoyenne,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Chauvelin, insinuatingly,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLady Blakeney is the pivot of social London, so I am told . . . you\r\nsee everything, you _hear_ everything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEasy, my friend,\xe2\x80\x9d retorted Marguerite, drawing herself up to her full\r\nheight and looking down, with a slight thought of contempt on the\r\nsmall, thin figure before her. \xe2\x80\x9cEasy! you seem to forget that there are\r\nsix feet of Sir Percy Blakeney, and a long line of ancestors to stand\r\nbetween Lady Blakeney and such a thing as you propose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor the sake of France, citoyenne!\xe2\x80\x9d reiterated Chauvelin, earnestly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTush, man, you talk nonsense anyway; for even if you did know who this\r\nScarlet Pimpernel is, you could do nothing to him\xe2\x80\x94an Englishman!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d take my chance of that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chauvelin, with a dry, rasping\r\nlittle laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cAt any rate we could send him to the guillotine first to\r\ncool his ardour, then, when there is a diplomatic fuss about it, we can\r\napologise\xe2\x80\x94humbly\xe2\x80\x94to the British Government, and, if necessary, pay\r\ncompensation to the bereaved family.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat you propose is horrible, Chauvelin,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, drawing away from\r\nhim as from some noisome insect. \xe2\x80\x9cWhoever the man may be, he is brave\r\nand noble, and never\xe2\x80\x94do you hear me?\xe2\x80\x94never would I lend a hand to such\r\nvillainy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou prefer to be insulted by every French aristocrat who comes to this\r\ncountry?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChauvelin had taken sure aim when he shot this tiny shaft. Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfresh young cheeks became a thought more pale and she bit her under\r\nlip, for she would not let him see that the shaft had struck home.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is beside the question,\xe2\x80\x9d she said at last with indifference. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\ncan defend myself, but I refuse to do any dirty work for you\xe2\x80\x94or for\r\nFrance. You have other means at your disposal; you must use them, my\r\nfriend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd without another look at Chauvelin, Marguerite Blakeney turned her\r\nback on him and walked straight into the inn.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is not your last word, citoyenne,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chauvelin, as a flood of\r\nlight from the passage illumined her elegant, richly-clad figure, \xe2\x80\x9cwe\r\nmeet in London, I hope!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe meet in London,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, speaking over her shoulder at him, \xe2\x80\x9cbut\r\nthat is my last word.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe threw open the coffee-room door and disappeared from his view, but\r\nhe remained under the porch for a moment or two, taking a pinch of\r\nsnuff. He had received a rebuke and a snub, but his shrewd, fox-like\r\nface looked neither abashed nor disappointed; on the contrary, a\r\ncurious smile, half sarcastic and wholly satisfied, played around the\r\ncorners of his thin lips.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IX.\r\nTHE OUTRAGE\r\n\r\n\r\nA beautiful starlit night had followed on the day of incessant rain: a\r\ncool, balmy, late summer\xe2\x80\x99s night, essentially English in its suggestion\r\nof moisture and scent of wet earth and dripping leaves.\r\n\r\nThe magnificent coach, drawn by four of the finest thoroughbreds in\r\nEngland, had driven off along the London road, with Sir Percy Blakeney\r\non the box, holding the reins in his slender feminine hands, and beside\r\nhim Lady Blakeney wrapped in costly furs. A fifty-mile drive on a\r\nstarlit summer\xe2\x80\x99s night! Marguerite had hailed the notion of it with\r\ndelight. . . . Sir Percy was an enthusiastic whip; his four\r\nthoroughbreds, which had been sent down to Dover a couple of days\r\nbefore, were just sufficiently fresh and restive to add zest to the\r\nexpedition, and Marguerite revelled in anticipation of the few hours of\r\nsolitude, with the soft night breeze fanning her cheeks, her thoughts\r\nwandering, whither away? She knew from old experience that Sir Percy\r\nwould speak little, if at all: he had often driven her on his beautiful\r\ncoach for hours at night, from point to point, without making more than\r\none or two casual remarks upon the weather or the state of the roads.\r\nHe was very fond of driving by night, and she had very quickly adopted\r\nhis fancy: as she sat next to him hour after hour, admiring the\r\ndexterous, certain way in which he handled the reins, she often\r\nwondered what went on in that slow-going head of his. He never told\r\nher, and she had never cared to ask.\r\n\r\nAt \xe2\x80\x9cThe Fisherman\xe2\x80\x99s Rest\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Jellyband was going the round, putting\r\nout the lights. His bar customers had all gone, but upstairs in the\r\nsnug little bedrooms, Mr. Jellyband had quite a few important guests:\r\nthe Comtesse de Tournay, with Suzanne, and the Vicomte, and there were\r\ntwo more bedrooms ready for Sir Andrew Ffoulkes and Lord Antony\r\nDewhurst, if the two young men should elect to honour the ancient\r\nhostelry and stay the night.\r\n\r\nFor the moment these two young gallants were comfortably installed in\r\nthe coffee-room, before the huge log-fire, which, in spite of the\r\nmildness of the evening, had been allowed to burn merrily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say, Jelly, has everyone gone?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Lord Tony, as the worthy\r\nlandlord still busied himself clearing away glasses and mugs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEveryone, as you see, my lord.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd all your servants gone to bed?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll except the boy on duty in the bar, and,\xe2\x80\x9d added Mr. Jellyband with\r\na laugh, \xe2\x80\x9cI expect he\xe2\x80\x99ll be asleep afore long, the rascal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen we can talk here undisturbed for half an hour?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt your service, my lord. . . . I\xe2\x80\x99ll leave your candles on the dresser\r\n. . . and your rooms are quite ready . . . I sleep at the top of the\r\nhouse myself, but if your lordship\xe2\x80\x99ll only call loudly enough, I\r\ndaresay I shall hear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, Jelly . . . and . . . I say, put the lamp out\xe2\x80\x94the fire\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\ngive us all the light we need\xe2\x80\x94and we don\xe2\x80\x99t want to attract the\r\npasser-by.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll ri\xe2\x80\x99, my lord.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Jellyband did as he was bid\xe2\x80\x94he turned out the quaint old lamp that\r\nhung from the raftered ceiling and blew out all the candles.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s have a bottle of wine, Jelly,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Sir Andrew.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll ri\xe2\x80\x99, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJellyband went off to fetch the wine. The room now was quite dark, save\r\nfor the circle of ruddy and fitful light formed by the brightly blazing\r\nlogs in the hearth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that all, gentlemen?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Jellyband, as he returned with a bottle\r\nof wine and a couple of glasses, which he placed on the table.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99ll do nicely, thanks, Jelly!\xe2\x80\x9d said Lord Tony.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-night, my lord! Good-night, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-night, Jelly!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe two young men listened, whilst the heavy tread of Mr. Jellyband was\r\nheard echoing along the passage and staircase. Presently even that\r\nsound died out, and the whole of \xe2\x80\x9cThe Fisherman\xe2\x80\x99s Rest\xe2\x80\x9d seemed wrapt in\r\nsleep, save the two young men drinking in silence beside the hearth.\r\n\r\nFor a while no sound was heard, even in the coffee-room, save the\r\nticking of the old grandfather\xe2\x80\x99s clock and the crackling of the burning\r\nwood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right again this time, Ffoulkes?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Lord Antony at last.\r\n\r\nSir Andrew had been dreaming evidently, gazing into the fire, and\r\nseeing therein, no doubt, a pretty, piquant face, with large brown eyes\r\nand a wealth of dark curls round a childish forehead.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, still musing, \xe2\x80\x9call right!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo hitch?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLord Antony laughed pleasantly as he poured himself out another glass\r\nof wine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI need not ask, I suppose, whether you found the journey pleasant this\r\ntime?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, friend, you need not ask,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Sir Andrew, gaily. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was all\r\nright.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen here\xe2\x80\x99s to her very good health,\xe2\x80\x9d said jovial Lord Tony. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nbonnie lass, though she _is_ a French one. And here\xe2\x80\x99s to your\r\ncourtship\xe2\x80\x94may it flourish and prosper exceedingly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe drained his glass to the last drop, then joined his friend beside\r\nthe hearth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell! you\xe2\x80\x99ll be doing the journey next, Tony, I expect,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sir\r\nAndrew, rousing himself from his meditations, \xe2\x80\x9cyou and Hastings,\r\ncertainly; and I hope you may have as pleasant a task as I had, and as\r\ncharming a travelling companion. You have no idea, Tony. . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo! I haven\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d interrupted his friend pleasantly, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I\xe2\x80\x99ll take your\r\nword for it. And now,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, whilst a sudden earnestness crept over\r\nhis jovial young face, \xe2\x80\x9chow about business?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n The two young men drew their chairs closer together, and\r\n instinctively, though they were alone, their voices sank to a whisper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI saw the Scarlet Pimpernel alone, for a few moments in Calais,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nSir Andrew, \xe2\x80\x9ca day or two ago. He crossed over to England two days\r\nbefore we did. He had escorted the party all the way from Paris,\r\ndressed\xe2\x80\x94you\xe2\x80\x99ll never credit it!\xe2\x80\x94as an old market woman, and\r\ndriving\xe2\x80\x94until they were safely out of the city\xe2\x80\x94the covered cart, under\r\nwhich the Comtesse de Tournay, Mlle. Suzanne, and the Vicomte lay\r\nconcealed among the turnips and cabbages. They, themselves, of course,\r\nnever suspected who their driver was. He drove them right through a\r\nline of soldiery and a yelling mob, who were screaming, \xe2\x80\x98\xc3\x80 bas les\r\naristos!\xe2\x80\x99 But the market cart got through along with some others, and\r\nthe Scarlet Pimpernel, in shawl, petticoat and hood, yelled \xe2\x80\x98\xc3\x80 bas les\r\naristos!\xe2\x80\x99 louder than anybody.
