Faith!\xe2\x80\x9d added the young man, as his eyes\r\nglowed with enthusiasm for the beloved leader, \xe2\x80\x9cthat man\xe2\x80\x99s a marvel!\r\nHis cheek is preposterous, I vow!\xe2\x80\x94and that\xe2\x80\x99s what carries him through.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLord Antony, whose vocabulary was more limited than that of his friend,\r\ncould only find an oath or two with which to show his admiration for\r\nhis leader.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe wants you and Hastings to meet him at Calais,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sir Andrew,\r\nmore quietly, \xe2\x80\x9con the 2nd of next month. Let me see! that will be next\r\nWednesday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is, of course, the case of the Comte de Tournay, this time; a\r\ndangerous task, for the Comte, whose escape from his ch\xc3\xa2teau, after he\r\nhad been declared a \xe2\x80\x98suspect\xe2\x80\x99 by the Committee of Public Safety, was a\r\nmasterpiece of the Scarlet Pimpernel\xe2\x80\x99s ingenuity, is now under sentence\r\nof death. It will be rare sport to get _him_ out of France, and you\r\nwill have a narrow escape, if you get through at all. St. Just has\r\nactually gone to meet him\xe2\x80\x94of course, no one suspects St. Just as yet;\r\nbut after that . . . to get them both out of the country! I\xe2\x80\x99faith,\r\n\xe2\x80\x99twill be a tough job, and tax even the ingenuity of our chief. I hope\r\nI may yet have orders to be of the party.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you any special instructions for me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes! rather more precise ones than usual. It appears that the\r\nRepublican Government have sent an accredited agent over to England, a\r\nman named Chauvelin, who is said to be terribly bitter against our\r\nleague, and determined to discover the identity of our leader, so that\r\nhe may have him kidnapped, the next time he attempts to set foot in\r\nFrance. This Chauvelin has brought a whole army of spies with him, and\r\nuntil the chief has sampled the lot, he thinks we should meet as seldom\r\nas possible on the business of the league, and on no account should\r\ntalk to each other in public places for a time. When he wants to speak\r\nto us, he will contrive to let us know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe two young men were both bending over the fire, for the blaze had\r\ndied down, and only a red glow from the dying embers cast a lurid light\r\non a narrow semicircle in front of the hearth. The rest of the room lay\r\nburied in complete gloom; Sir Andrew had taken a pocket-book from his\r\npocket, and drawn therefrom a paper, which he unfolded, and together\r\nthey tried to read it by the dim red firelight. So intent were they\r\nupon this, so wrapt up in the cause, the business they had so much at\r\nheart, so precious was this document which came from the very hand of\r\ntheir adored leader, that they had eyes and ears only for that. They\r\nlost count of the sounds around them, of the dropping of crisp ash from\r\nthe grate, of the monotonous ticking of the clock, of the soft, almost\r\nimperceptible rustle of something on the floor close beside them. A\r\nfigure had emerged from under one of the benches; with snake-like,\r\nnoiseless movements it crept closer and closer to the two young men,\r\nnot breathing, only gliding along the floor, in the inky blackness of\r\nthe room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are to read these instructions and commit them to memory,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nSir Andrew, \xe2\x80\x9cthen destroy them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was about to replace the letter-case into his pocket, when a tiny\r\nslip of paper fluttered from it and fell on to the floor. Lord Antony\r\nstooped and picked it up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Sir Andrew.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt dropped out of your pocket just now. It certainly did not seem to\r\nbe with the other paper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStrange!\xe2\x80\x94I wonder when it got there? It is from the chief,\xe2\x80\x9d he added,\r\nglancing at the paper.\r\n\r\nBoth stooped to try and decipher this last tiny scrap of paper on which\r\na few words had been hastily scrawled, when suddenly a slight noise\r\nattracted their attention, which seemed to come from the passage\r\nbeyond.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x9d said both instinctively. Lord Antony crossed the room\r\ntowards the door, which he threw open quickly and suddenly; at that\r\nvery moment he received a stunning blow between the eyes, which threw\r\nhim back violently into the room. Simultaneously the crouching,\r\nsnake-like figure in the gloom had jumped up and hurled itself from\r\nbehind upon the unsuspecting Sir Andrew, felling him to the ground.\r\n\r\nAll this occurred within the short space of two or three seconds, and\r\nbefore either Lord Antony or Sir Andrew had time or chance to utter a\r\ncry or to make the faintest struggle. They were each seized by two men,\r\na muffler was quickly tied round the mouth of each, and they were\r\npinioned to one another back to back, their arms, hands, and legs\r\nsecurely fastened.\r\n\r\nOne man had in the meanwhile quietly shut the door; he wore a mask and\r\nnow stood motionless while the others completed their work.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll safe, citoyen!\xe2\x80\x9d said one of the men, as he took a final survey of\r\nthe bonds which secured the two young men.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood!\xe2\x80\x9d replied the man at the door; \xe2\x80\x9cnow search their pockets and give\r\nme all the papers you find.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was promptly and quietly done. The masked man having taken\r\npossession of all the papers, listened for a moment or two if there\r\nwere any sound within \xe2\x80\x9cThe Fisherman\xe2\x80\x99s Rest.\xe2\x80\x9d Evidently satisfied that\r\nthis dastardly outrage had remained unheard, he once more opened the\r\ndoor and pointed peremptorily down the passage. The four men lifted Sir\r\nAndrew and Lord Antony from the ground, and as quietly, as noiselessly\r\nas they had come, they bore the two pinioned young gallants out of the\r\ninn and along the Dover Road into the gloom beyond.\r\n\r\nIn the coffee-room the masked leader of this daring attempt was quickly\r\nglancing through the stolen papers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a bad day\xe2\x80\x99s work on the whole,\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered, as he quietly took\r\noff his mask, and his pale, fox-like eyes glittered in the red glow of\r\nthe fire. \xe2\x80\x9cNot a bad day\xe2\x80\x99s work.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe opened one or two more letters from Sir Andrew Ffoulkes\xe2\x80\x99\r\npocket-book, noted the tiny scrap of paper which the two young men had\r\nonly just had time to read; but one letter specially, signed Armand St.\r\nJust, seemed to give him strange satisfaction.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArmand St. Just a traitor after all,\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, fair\r\nMarguerite Blakeney,\xe2\x80\x9d he added viciously between his clenched teeth, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nthink that you will help me to find the Scarlet Pimpernel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER X.\r\nIN THE OPERA BOX\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was one of the gala nights at Covent Garden Theatre, the first of\r\nthe autumn season in this memorable year of grace 1792.\r\n\r\nThe house was packed, both in the smart orchestra boxes and the pit, as\r\nwell as in the more plebeian balconies and galleries above. Gl\xc3\xbcck\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n_Orpheus_ made a strong appeal to the more intellectual portions of the\r\nhouse, whilst the fashionable women, the gaily-dressed and brilliant\r\nthrong, spoke to the eye of those who cared but little for this \xe2\x80\x9clatest\r\nimportation from Germany.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSelina Storace had been duly applauded after her grand _aria_ by her\r\nnumerous admirers; Benjamin Incledon, the acknowledged favourite of the\r\nladies, had received special gracious recognition from the royal box;\r\nand now the curtain came down after the glorious finale to the second\r\nact, and the audience, which had hung spell-bound on the magic strains\r\nof the great maestro, seemed collectively to breathe a long sigh of\r\nsatisfaction, previous to letting loose its hundreds of waggish and\r\nfrivolous tongues.\r\n\r\n In the smart orchestra boxes many well-known faces were to be seen.\r\n Mr. Pitt, overweighted with cares of state, was finding brief\r\n relaxation in to-night\xe2\x80\x99s musical treat; the Prince of Wales, jovial,\r\n rotund, somewhat coarse and commonplace in appearance, moved about\r\n from box to box, spending brief quarters of an hour with those of his\r\n more intimate friends.\r\n\r\nIn Lord Grenville\xe2\x80\x99s box, too, a curious, interesting personality\r\nattracted everyone\xe2\x80\x99s attention; a thin, small figure with shrewd,\r\nsarcastic face and deep-set eyes, attentive to the music, keenly\r\ncritical of the audience, dressed in immaculate black, with dark hair\r\nfree from any powder. Lord Grenville\xe2\x80\x94Foreign Secretary of State\xe2\x80\x94paid\r\nhim marked, though frigid deference.\r\n\r\nHere and there, dotted about among distinctly English types of beauty,\r\none or two foreign faces stood out in marked contrast: the haughty\r\naristocratic cast of countenance of the many French royalist _\xc3\xa9migr\xc3\xa9s_\r\nwho, persecuted by the relentless, revolutionary faction of their\r\ncountry, had found a peaceful refuge in England. On these faces sorrow\r\nand care were deeply writ; the women especially paid but little heed,\r\neither to the music or to the brilliant audience; no doubt their\r\nthoughts were far away with husband, brother, son maybe, still in\r\nperil, or lately succumbed to a cruel fate.\r\n\r\nAmong these the Comtesse de Tournay de Basserive, but lately arrived\r\nfrom France, was a most conspicuous figure: dressed in deep, heavy\r\nblack silk, with only a white lace kerchief to relieve the aspect of\r\nmourning about her person, she sat beside Lady Portarles, who was\r\nvainly trying by witty sallies and somewhat broad jokes, to bring a\r\nsmile to the Comtesse\xe2\x80\x99s sad mouth. Behind her sat little Suzanne and\r\nthe Vicomte, both silent and somewhat shy among so many strangers.\r\nSuzanne\xe2\x80\x99s eyes seemed wistful; when she first entered the crowded\r\nhouse, she had looked eagerly all around, scanned every face,\r\nscrutinised every box. Evidently the one face she wished to see was not\r\nthere, for she settled herself down quietly behind her mother, listened\r\napathetically to the music, and took no further interest in the\r\naudience itself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, Lord Grenville,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Portarles, as following a discreet\r\nknock, the clever, interesting head of the Secretary of State appeared\r\nin the doorway of the box, \xe2\x80\x9cyou could not arrive more _\xc3\xa0 propos_. Here\r\nis Madame la Comtesse de Tournay positively dying to hear the latest\r\nnews from France.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe distinguished diplomatist had come forward and was shaking hands\r\nwith the ladies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlas!\xe2\x80\x9d he said sadly, \xe2\x80\x9cit is of the very worst. The massacres\r\ncontinue; Paris literally reeks with blood; and the guillotine claims a\r\nhundred victims a day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPale and tearful, the Comtesse was leaning back in her chair, listening\r\nhorror-struck to this brief and graphic account of what went on in her\r\nown misguided country.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, Monsieur!\xe2\x80\x9d she said in broken English, \xe2\x80\x9cit is dreadful to hear all\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x94and my poor husband still in that awful country. It is terrible\r\nfor me to be sitting here, in a theatre, all safe and in peace, whilst\r\nhe is in such peril.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLud, Madame!\xe2\x80\x9d said honest, bluff Lady Portarles, \xe2\x80\x9cyour sitting in a\r\nconvent won\xe2\x80\x99t make your husband safe, and you have your children to\r\nconsider: they are too young to be dosed with anxiety and premature\r\nmourning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Comtesse smiled through her tears at the vehemence of her friend.\r\nLady Portarles, whose voice and manner would not have misfitted a\r\njockey, had a heart of gold, and hid the most genuine sympathy and most\r\ngentle kindliness, beneath the somewhat coarse manners affected by some\r\nladies at that time.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBesides which, Madame,\xe2\x80\x9d added Lord Grenville, \xe2\x80\x9cdid you not tell me\r\nyesterday that the League of the Scarlet Pimpernel had pledged their\r\nhonour to bring M. le Comte safely across the Channel?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, yes!\xe2\x80\x9d replied the Comtesse, \xe2\x80\x9cand that is my only hope. I saw Lord\r\nHastings yesterday . . . he reassured me again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I am sure you need have no fear. What the league have sworn, that\r\nthey surely will accomplish. Ah!\xe2\x80\x9d added the old diplomatist with a\r\nsigh, \xe2\x80\x9cif I were but a few years younger . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLa, man!\xe2\x80\x9d interrupted honest Lady Portarles, \xe2\x80\x9cyou are still young\r\nenough to turn your back on that French scarecrow that sits enthroned\r\nin your box to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish I could . . . but your ladyship must remember that in serving\r\nour country we must put prejudices aside. M. Chauvelin is the\r\naccredited agent of his Government . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOdd\xe2\x80\x99s fish, man!\xe2\x80\x9d she retorted, \xe2\x80\x9cyou don\xe2\x80\x99t call those bloodthirsty\r\nruffians over there a government, do you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt has not been thought advisable as yet,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Minister,\r\nguardedly, \xe2\x80\x9cfor England to break off diplomatic relations with France,\r\nand we cannot therefore refuse to receive with courtesy the agent she\r\nwishes to send to us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDiplomatic relations be demmed, my lord! That sly little fox over\r\nthere is nothing but a spy, I\xe2\x80\x99ll warrant, and you\xe2\x80\x99ll find\xe2\x80\x94an I\xe2\x80\x99m much\r\nmistaken, that he\xe2\x80\x99ll concern himself little with diplomacy, beyond\r\ntrying to do mischief to royalist refugees\xe2\x80\x94to our heroic Scarlet\r\nPimpernel and to the members of that brave little league.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sure,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Comtesse, pursing up her thin lips, \xe2\x80\x9cthat if this\r\nChauvelin wishes to do us mischief, he will find a faithful ally in\r\nLady Blakeney.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBless the woman!\xe2\x80\x9d ejaculated Lady Portarles, \xe2\x80\x9cdid ever anyone see such\r\nperversity? My Lord Grenville, you have the gift of the gab, will you\r\nplease explain to Madame la Comtesse that she is acting like a fool. In\r\nyour position here in England, Madame,\xe2\x80\x9d she added, turning a wrathful\r\nand resolute face towards the Comtesse, \xe2\x80\x9cyou cannot afford to put on\r\nthe hoity-toity airs you French aristocrats are so fond of. Lady\r\nBlakeney may or may not be in sympathy with those ruffians in France;\r\nshe may or may not have had anything to do with the arrest and\r\ncondemnation of St. Cyr, or whatever the man\xe2\x80\x99s name is, but she is the\r\nleader of fashion in this country; Sir Percy Blakeney has more money\r\nthan any half-dozen other men put together, he is hand and glove with\r\nroyalty, and your trying to snub Lady Blakeney will not harm her, but\r\nwill make you look a fool. Isn\xe2\x80\x99t that so, my lord?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut what Lord Grenville thought of this matter, or to what reflections\r\nthis homely tirade of Lady Portarles led the Comtesse de Tournay,\r\nremained unspoken, for the curtain had just risen on the third act of\r\n_Orpheus_, and admonishments to silence came from every part of the\r\nhouse.\r\n\r\nLord Grenville took a hasty farewell of the ladies and slipped back\r\ninto his box, where M. Chauvelin had sat all through this _entr\xe2\x80\x99acte_,\r\nwith his eternal snuff-box in his hand, and with his keen pale eyes\r\nintently fixed upon a box opposite to him, where, with much frou-frou\r\nof silken skirts, much laughter and general stir of curiosity amongst\r\nthe audience, Marguerite Blakeney had just entered, accompanied by her\r\nhusband, and looking divinely pretty beneath the wealth of her golden,\r\nreddish curls, slightly besprinkled with powder, and tied back at the\r\nnape of her graceful neck with a gigantic black bow. Always dressed in\r\nthe very latest vagary of fashion, Marguerite alone among the ladies\r\nthat night had discarded the cross-over fichu and broad-lapelled\r\nover-dress, which had been in fashion for the last two or three years.\r\nShe wore the short-waisted classical-shaped gown, which so soon was to\r\nbecome the approved mode in every country in Europe. It suited her\r\ngraceful, regal figure to perfection, composed as it was of shimmering\r\nstuff which seemed a mass of rich gold embroidery.\r\n\r\nAs she entered, she leant for a moment out of the box, taking stock of\r\nall those present whom she knew. Many bowed to her as she did so, and\r\nfrom the royal box there came also a quick and gracious salute.\r\n\r\nChauvelin watched her intently all through the commencement of the\r\nthird act, as she sat enthralled with the music, her exquisite little\r\nhand toying with a small jewelled fan, her regal head, her throat, arms\r\nand neck covered with magnificent diamonds and rare gems, the gift of\r\nthe adoring husband who sprawled leisurely by her side.\r\n\r\nMarguerite was passionately fond of music. _Orpheus_ charmed her\r\nto-night. The very joy of living was writ plainly upon the sweet young\r\nface, it sparkled out of the merry blue eyes and lit up the smile that\r\nlurked around the lips. She was after all but five-and-twenty, in the\r\nheyday of youth, the darling of a brilliant throng, adored, _f\xc3\xaated_,\r\npetted, cherished. Two days ago the _Day Dream_ had returned from\r\nCalais, bringing her news that her idolised brother had safely landed,\r\nthat he thought of her, and would be prudent for her sake.\r\n\r\nWhat wonder for the moment, and listening to Gl\xc3\xbcck\xe2\x80\x99s impassioned\r\nstrains, that she forgot her disillusionments, forgot her vanished\r\nlove-dreams, forgot even the lazy, good-humoured nonentity who had made\r\nup for his lack of spiritual attainments by lavishing worldly\r\nadvantages upon her.\r\n\r\nHe had stayed beside her in the box just as long as convention\r\ndemanded, making way for His Royal Highness, and for the host of\r\nadmirers who in a continued procession came to pay homage to the queen\r\nof fashion. Sir Percy had strolled away, to talk to more congenial\r\nfriends probably. Marguerite did not even wonder whither he had\r\ngone\xe2\x80\x94she cared so little; she had had a little court round her,\r\ncomposed of the _jeunesse dor\xc3\xa9e_ of London, and had just dismissed them\r\nall, wishing to be alone with Gl\xc3\xbcck for a brief while.\r\n\r\nA discreet knock at the door roused her from her enjoyment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome in,\xe2\x80\x9d she said with some impatience, without turning to look at\r\nthe intruder.\r\n\r\nChauvelin, waiting for his opportunity, noted that she was alone, and\r\nnow, without pausing for that impatient \xe2\x80\x9cCome in,\xe2\x80\x9d he quietly slipped\r\ninto the box, and the next moment was standing behind Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nchair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA word with you, citoyenne,\xe2\x80\x9d he said quietly.\r\n\r\nMarguerite turned quickly, in alarm, which was not altogether feigned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLud, man! you frightened me,\xe2\x80\x9d she said with a forced little laugh,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cyour presence is entirely inopportune. I want to listen to Gl\xc3\xbcck, and\r\nhave no mind for talking.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut this is my only opportunity,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, as quietly, and without\r\nwaiting for permission, he drew a chair close behind her\xe2\x80\x94so close that\r\nhe could whisper in her ear, without disturbing the audience, and\r\nwithout being seen, in the dark background of the box. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is my only\r\nopportunity,\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated, as she vouchsafed him no reply, \xe2\x80\x9cLady\r\nBlakeney is always so surrounded, so _f\xc3\xaated_ by her court, that a mere\r\nold friend has but very little chance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFaith, man!\xe2\x80\x9d she said impatiently, \xe2\x80\x9cyou must seek for another\r\nopportunity then. I am going to Lord Grenville\xe2\x80\x99s ball to-night after\r\nthe opera. So are you, probably. I\xe2\x80\x99ll give you five minutes then. . .\r\n.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThree minutes in the privacy of this box are quite sufficient for me,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe rejoined placidly, \xe2\x80\x9cand I think that you would be wise to listen to\r\nme, Citoyenne St. Just.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarguerite instinctively shivered. Chauvelin had not raised his voice\r\nabove a whisper; he was now quietly taking a pinch of snuff, yet there\r\nwas something in his attitude, something in those pale, foxy eyes,\r\nwhich seemed to freeze the blood in her veins, as would the sight of\r\nsome deadly hitherto unguessed peril.\r\n \xe2\x80\x9cIs that a threat, citoyen?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked at last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, fair lady,\xe2\x80\x9d he said gallantly, \xe2\x80\x9conly an arrow shot into the air.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe paused a moment, like a cat which sees a mouse running heedlessly\r\nby, ready to spring, yet waiting with that feline sense of enjoyment of\r\nmischief about to be done. Then he said quietly\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour brother, St. Just, is in peril.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNot a muscle moved in the beautiful face before him. He could only see\r\nit in profile, for Marguerite seemed to be watching the stage intently,\r\nbut Chauvelin was a keen observer; he noticed the sudden rigidity of\r\nthe eyes, the hardening of the mouth, the sharp, almost paralysed\r\ntension of the beautiful, graceful figure.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLud, then,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, with affected merriment, \xe2\x80\x9csince \xe2\x80\x99tis one of your\r\nimaginary plots, you\xe2\x80\x99d best go back to your own seat and leave me to\r\nenjoy the music.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd with her hand she began to beat time nervously against the cushion\r\nof the box. Selina Storace was singing the \xe2\x80\x9cChe far\xc3\xb2\xe2\x80\x9d to an audience\r\nthat hung spellbound upon the prima donna\xe2\x80\x99s lips. Chauvelin did not\r\nmove from his seat; he quietly watched that tiny nervous hand, the only\r\nindication that his shaft had indeed struck home.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d she said suddenly and irrelevantly, and with the same feigned\r\nunconcern.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, citoyenne?\xe2\x80\x9d he rejoined placidly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout my brother?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have news of him for you which, I think, will interest you, but\r\nfirst let me explain. . . . May I?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe question was unnecessary. He felt, though Marguerite still held her\r\nhead steadily averted from him, that her every nerve was strained to\r\nhear what he had to say.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe other day, citoyenne,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cI asked for your help. . . .\r\nFrance needed it, and I thought I could rely on you, but you gave me\r\nyour answer. . . . Since then the exigencies of my own affairs and your\r\nown social duties have kept us apart . . . although many things have\r\nhappened. . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo the point, I pray you, citoyen,\xe2\x80\x9d she said lightly; \xe2\x80\x9cthe music is\r\nentrancing, and the audience will get impatient of your talk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne moment, citoyenne. The day on which I had the honour of meeting\r\nyou at Dover, and less than an hour after I had your final answer, I\r\nobtained possession of some papers, which revealed another of those\r\nsubtle schemes for the escape of a batch of French aristocrats\xe2\x80\x94that\r\ntraitor de Tournay amongst others\xe2\x80\x94all organised by that arch-meddler,\r\nthe Scarlet Pimpernel. Some of the threads, too, of this mysterious\r\norganisation have fallen into my hands, but not all, and I want\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x94nay! you _must_ help me to gather them together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarguerite seemed to have listened to him with marked impatience; she\r\nnow shrugged her shoulders and said gaily\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBah! man. Have I not already told you that I care nought about your\r\nschemes or about the Scarlet Pimpernel. And had you not spoken about my\r\nbrother . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA little patience, I entreat, citoyenne,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued imperturbably.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo gentlemen, Lord Antony Dewhurst and Sir Andrew Ffoulkes were at\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The Fisherman\xe2\x80\x99s Rest\xe2\x80\x99 at Dover that same night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know. I saw them there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey were already known to my spies as members of that accursed\r\nleague. It was Sir Andrew Ffoulkes who escorted the Comtesse de Tournay\r\nand her children across the Channel. When the two young men were alone,\r\nmy spies forced their way into the coffee-room of the inn, gagged and\r\npinioned the two gallants, seized their papers, and brought them to\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn a moment she had guessed the danger. Papers? . . . Had Armand been\r\nimprudent? . . . The very thought struck her with nameless terror.\r\nStill she would not let this man see that she feared; she laughed gaily\r\nand lightly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFaith! and your impudence passes belief,\xe2\x80\x9d she said merrily. \xe2\x80\x9cRobbery\r\nand violence!\xe2\x80\x94in England!\xe2\x80\x94in a crowded inn! Your men might have been\r\ncaught in the act!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat if they had? They are children of France, and have been trained\r\nby your humble servant. Had they been caught they would have gone to\r\njail, or even to the gallows, without a word of protest or\r\nindiscretion; at any rate it was well worth the risk. A crowded inn is\r\nsafer for these little operations than you think, and my men have\r\nexperience.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell? And those papers?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked carelessly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnfortunately, though they have given me cognisance of certain names .\r\n. . certain movements . . . enough, I think, to thwart their projected\r\n_coup_ for the moment, it would only be for the moment, and still\r\nleaves me in ignorance of the identity of the Scarlet Pimpernel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLa! my friend,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, with the same assumed flippancy of manner,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthen you are where you were before, aren\xe2\x80\x99t you? and you can let me\r\nenjoy the last strophe of the _aria_. Faith!\xe2\x80\x9d she added, ostentatiously\r\nsmothering an imaginary yawn, \xe2\x80\x9chad you not spoken about my brother . .\r\n.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am coming to him now, citoyenne. Among the papers there was a letter\r\nto Sir Andrew Ffoulkes, written by your brother, St. Just.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell? And?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat letter shows him to be not only in sympathy with the enemies of\r\nFrance, but actually a helper, if not a member, of the League of the\r\nScarlet Pimpernel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe blow had been struck at last. All along, Marguerite had been\r\nexpecting it; she would not show fear, she was determined to seem\r\nunconcerned, flippant even. She wished, when the shock came, to be\r\nprepared for it, to have all her wits about her\xe2\x80\x94those wits which had\r\nbeen nicknamed the keenest in Europe. Even now she did not flinch. She\r\nknew that Chauvelin had spoken the truth; the man was too earnest, too\r\nblindly devoted to the misguided cause he had at heart, too proud of\r\nhis countrymen, of those makers of revolutions, to stoop to low,\r\npurposeless falsehoods.\r\n\r\nThat letter of Armand\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94foolish, imprudent Armand\xe2\x80\x94was in Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhands. Marguerite knew that as if she had seen the letter with her own\r\neyes; and Chauvelin would hold that letter for purposes of his own,\r\nuntil it suited him to destroy it or to make use of it against Armand.\r\nAll that she knew, and yet she continued to laugh more gaily, more\r\nloudly than she had done before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLa, man!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, speaking over her shoulder and looking him full\r\nand squarely in the face, \xe2\x80\x9cdid I not say it was some imaginary plot. .\r\n. . Armand in league with that enigmatic Scarlet Pimpernel! . . .\r\nArmand busy helping those French aristocrats whom he despises! . . .\r\nFaith, the tale does infinite credit to your imagination!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me make my point clear, citoyenne,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chauvelin, with the same\r\nunruffled calm, \xe2\x80\x9cI must assure you that St. Just is compromised beyond\r\nthe slightest hope of pardon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nInside the orchestra box all was silent for a moment or two. Marguerite\r\nsat, straight upright, rigid and inert, trying to think, trying to face\r\nthe situation, to realise what had best be done.\r\n\r\nIn the house Storace had finished the _aria_, and was even now bowing\r\nin her classic garb, but in approved eighteenth-century fashion, to the\r\nenthusiastic audience, who cheered her to the echo.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChauvelin,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marguerite Blakeney at last, quietly, and without\r\nthat touch of bravado which had characterised her attitude all along,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChauvelin, my friend, shall we try to understand one another. It seems\r\nthat my wits have become rusty by contact with this damp climate. Now,\r\ntell me, you are very anxious to discover the identity of the Scarlet\r\nPimpernel, isn\xe2\x80\x99t that so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrance\xe2\x80\x99s most bitter enemy, citoyenne . . . all the more dangerous, as\r\nhe works in the dark.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll the more noble, you mean. . . . Well!\xe2\x80\x94and you would now force me\r\nto do some spying work for you in exchange for my brother Armand\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsafety?\xe2\x80\x94Is that it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFie! two very ugly words, fair lady,\xe2\x80\x9d protested Chauvelin, urbanely.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere can be no question of force, and the service which I would ask\r\nof you, in the name of France, could never be called by the shocking\r\nname of spying.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt any rate, that is what it is called over here,\xe2\x80\x9d she said drily.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is your intention, is it not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy intention is, that you yourself win a free pardon for Armand St.\r\nJust by doing me a small service.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly watch for me to-night, Citoyenne St. Just,\xe2\x80\x9d he said eagerly.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cListen: among the papers which were found about the person of Sir\r\nAndrew Ffoulkes there was a tiny note. See!\xe2\x80\x9d he added, taking a tiny\r\nscrap of paper from his pocket-book and handing it to her.\r\n\r\nIt was the same scrap of paper which, four days ago, the two young men\r\nhad been in the act of reading, at the very moment when they were\r\nattacked by Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s minions. Marguerite took it mechanically and\r\nstooped to read it. There were only two lines, written in a distorted,\r\nevidently disguised, handwriting; she read them half aloud\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Remember we must not meet more often than is strictly necessary. You\r\nhave all instructions for the 2nd. If you wish to speak to me again, I\r\nshall be at G.\xe2\x80\x99s ball.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat does it mean?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook again, citoyenne, and you will understand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is a device here in the corner, a small red flower . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Scarlet Pimpernel,\xe2\x80\x9d she said eagerly, \xe2\x80\x9cand G.\xe2\x80\x99s ball means\r\nGrenville\xe2\x80\x99s ball. . . . He will be at my Lord Grenville\xe2\x80\x99s ball\r\nto-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is how I interpret the note, citoyenne,\xe2\x80\x9d concluded Chauvelin,\r\nblandly. \xe2\x80\x9cLord Antony Dewhurst and Sir Andrew Ffoulkes, after they were\r\npinioned and searched by my spies, were carried by my orders to a\r\nlonely house on the Dover Road, which I had rented for the purpose:\r\nthere they remained close prisoners until this morning. But having\r\nfound this tiny scrap of paper, my intention was that they should be in\r\nLondon, in time to attend my Lord Grenville\xe2\x80\x99s ball. You see, do you\r\nnot? that they must have a great deal to say to their chief . . . and\r\nthus they will have an opportunity of speaking to him to-night, just as\r\nhe directed them to do. Therefore, this morning, those two young\r\ngallants found every bar and bolt open in that lonely house on the\r\nDover Road, their jailers disappeared, and two good horses standing\r\nready saddled and tethered in the yard. I have not seen them yet, but I\r\nthink we may safely conclude that they did not draw rein until they\r\nreached London. Now you see how simple it all is, citoyenne!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt does seem simple, doesn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d she said, with a final bitter\r\nattempt at flippancy, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen you want to kill a chicken . . . you take\r\nhold of it . . . then you wring its neck . . . it\xe2\x80\x99s only the chicken\r\nwho does not find it quite so simple. Now you hold a knife at my\r\nthroat, and a hostage for my obedience. . . . You find it simple. . . .\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, citoyenne, I offer you a chance of saving the brother you love\r\nfrom the consequences of his own folly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarguerite\xe2\x80\x99s face softened, her eyes at last grew moist, as she\r\nmurmured, half to herself:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe only being in the world who has loved me truly and constantly. . .\r\n. But what do you want me to do, Chauvelin?\xe2\x80\x9d she said, with a world of\r\ndespair in her tear-choked voice. \xe2\x80\x9cIn my present position, it is\r\nwell-nigh impossible!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, citoyenne,\xe2\x80\x9d he said drily and relentlessly, not heeding that\r\ndespairing, childlike appeal, which might have melted a heart of stone,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cas Lady Blakeney, no one suspects you, and with your help to-night I\r\nmay\xe2\x80\x94who knows?\xe2\x80\x94succeed in finally establishing the identity of the\r\nScarlet Pimpernel. . . . You are going to the ball anon. . . . Watch\r\nfor me there, citoyenne, watch and listen. . . . You can tell me if you\r\nhear a chance word or whisper. . . . You can note everyone to whom Sir\r\nAndrew Ffoulkes or Lord Antony Dewhurst will speak. You are absolutely\r\nbeyond suspicion now. The Scarlet Pimpernel will be at Lord Grenville\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nball to-night. Find out who he is, and I will pledge the word of France\r\nthat your brother shall be safe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChauvelin was putting the knife to her throat. Marguerite felt herself\r\nentangled in one of those webs, from which she could hope for no\r\nescape. A precious hostage was being held for her obedience: for she\r\nknew that this man would never make an empty threat. No doubt Armand\r\nwas already signalled to the Committee of Public Safety as one of the\r\n\xe2\x80\x9csuspect\xe2\x80\x9d; he would not be allowed to leave France again, and would be\r\nruthlessly struck, if she refused to obey Chauvelin. For a\r\nmoment\xe2\x80\x94woman-like\xe2\x80\x94she still hoped to temporise. She held out her hand\r\nto this man, whom she now feared and hated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I promise to help you in this matter, Chauvelin,\xe2\x80\x9d she said\r\npleasantly, \xe2\x80\x9cwill you give me that letter of St. Just\xe2\x80\x99s?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you render me useful assistance to-night, citoyenne,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied\r\nwith a sarcastic smile, \xe2\x80\x9cI will give you that letter . . . to-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou do not trust me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI trust you absolutely, dear lady, but St. Just\xe2\x80\x99s life is forfeit to\r\nhis country . . . it rests with you to redeem it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI may be powerless to help you,\xe2\x80\x9d she pleaded, \xe2\x80\x9cwere I ever so\r\nwilling.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat would be terrible indeed,\xe2\x80\x9d he said quietly, \xe2\x80\x9cfor you . . . and\r\nfor St. Just.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarguerite shuddered. She felt that from this man she could expect no\r\nmercy. All-powerful, he held the beloved life in the hollow of his\r\nhand. She knew him too well not to know that, if he failed in gaining\r\nhis own ends, he would be pitiless.\r\n\r\nShe felt cold in spite of the oppressive air of the opera-house. The\r\nheart-appealing strains of the music seemed to reach her, as from a\r\ndistant land. She drew her costly lace scarf up around her shoulders,\r\nand sat silently watching the brilliant scene, as if in a dream.\r\n\r\nFor a moment her thoughts wandered away from the loved one who was in\r\ndanger, to that other man who also had a claim on her confidence and\r\nher affection. She felt lonely, frightened for Armand\xe2\x80\x99s sake; she\r\nlonged to seek comfort and advice from someone who would know how to\r\nhelp and console. Sir Percy Blakeney had loved her once; he was her\r\nhusband; why should she stand alone through this terrible ordeal? He\r\nhad very little brains, it is true, but he had plenty of muscle:\r\nsurely, if she provided the thought, and he the manly energy and pluck,\r\ntogether they could outwit the astute diplomatist, and save the hostage\r\nfrom his vengeful hands, without imperilling the life of the noble\r\nleader of that gallant little band of heroes. Sir Percy knew St. Just\r\nwell\xe2\x80\x94he seemed attached to him\xe2\x80\x94she was sure that he could help.\r\n\r\nChauvelin was taking no further heed of her. He had said his cruel\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEither\xe2\x80\x94or\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d and left her to decide. He, in his turn now, appeared to\r\nbe absorbed in the soul-stirring melodies of _Orpheus_, and was beating\r\ntime to the music with his sharp, ferret-like head.\r\n\r\nA discreet rap at the door roused Marguerite from her thoughts. It was\r\nSir Percy Blakeney, tall, sleepy, good-humoured, and wearing that\r\nhalf-shy, half-inane smile, which just now seemed to irritate her every\r\nnerve.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEr . . . your chair is outside . . . m\xe2\x80\x99dear,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, with his most\r\nexasperating drawl, \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you will want to go to that demmed ball.\r\n. . . Excuse me\xe2\x80\x94er\xe2\x80\x94Monsieur Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x94I had not observed you. . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe extended two slender, white fingers towards Chauvelin, who had risen\r\nwhen Sir Percy entered the box.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you coming, m\xe2\x80\x99dear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush! Sh! Sh!\xe2\x80\x9d came in angry remonstrance from different parts of the\r\nhouse.\r\n \xe2\x80\x9cDemmed impudence,\xe2\x80\x9d commented Sir Percy with a good-natured smile.\r\n\r\nMarguerite sighed impatiently. Her last hope seemed suddenly to have\r\nvanished away. She wrapped her cloak round her and without looking at\r\nher husband:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am ready to go,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, taking his arm. At the door of the box\r\nshe turned and looked straight at Chauvelin, who, with his\r\n_chapeau-bras_ under his arm, and a curious smile round his thin lips,\r\nwas preparing to follow the strangely ill-assorted couple.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is only _au revoir_, Chauvelin,\xe2\x80\x9d she said pleasantly, \xe2\x80\x9cwe shall\r\nmeet at my Lord Grenville\xe2\x80\x99s ball, anon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd in her eyes the astute Frenchman read, no doubt, something which\r\ncaused him profound satisfaction, for, with a sarcastic smile, he took\r\na delicate pinch of snuff, then, having dusted his dainty lace jabot,\r\nhe rubbed his thin, bony hands contentedly together.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XI.\r\nLORD GRENVILLE\xe2\x80\x99S BALL\r\n\r\n\r\nThe historic ball given by the then Secretary of State for Foreign\r\nAffairs\xe2\x80\x94Lord Grenville\xe2\x80\x94was the most brilliant function of the year.\r\nThough the autumn season had only just begun, everybody who was anybody\r\nhad contrived to be in London in time to be present there, and to shine\r\nat this ball, to the best of his or her respective ability.\r\n\r\nHis Royal Highness the Prince of Wales had promised to be present. He\r\nwas coming on presently from the opera. Lord Grenville himself had\r\nlistened to the two first acts of _Orpheus_, before preparing to\r\nreceive his guests. At ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock\xe2\x80\x94an unusually late hour in those\r\ndays\xe2\x80\x94the grand rooms of the Foreign Office, exquisitely decorated with\r\nexotic palms and flowers, were filled to overflowing. One room had been\r\nset apart for dancing, and the dainty strains of the minuet made a soft\r\naccompaniment to the gay chatter, the merry laughter of the numerous\r\nand brilliant company.\r\n\r\nIn a smaller chamber, facing the top of the fine stairway, the\r\ndistinguished host stood ready to receive his guests. Distinguished\r\nmen, beautiful women, notabilities from every European country had\r\nalready filed past him, had exchanged the elaborate bows and curtsies\r\nwith him, which the extravagant fashion of the time demanded, and then,\r\nlaughing and talking, had dispersed in the ball, reception, and card\r\nrooms beyond.\r\n\r\nNot far from Lord Grenville\xe2\x80\x99s elbow, leaning against one of the console\r\ntables, Chauvelin, in his irreproachable black costume, was taking a\r\nquiet survey of the brilliant throng. He noted that Sir Percy and Lady\r\nBlakeney had not yet arrived, and his keen, pale eyes glanced quickly\r\ntowards the door every time a newcomer appeared.\r\n\r\nHe stood somewhat isolated: the envoy of the Revolutionary Government\r\nof France was not likely to be very popular in England, at a time when\r\nthe news of the awful September massacres, and of the Reign of Terror\r\nand Anarchy, had just begun to filtrate across the Channel.\r\n\r\nIn his official capacity he had been received courteously by his\r\nEnglish colleagues: Mr. Pitt had shaken him by the hand; Lord Grenville\r\nhad entertained him more than once; but the more intimate circles of\r\nLondon society ignored him altogether; the women openly turned their\r\nbacks upon him; the men who held no official position refused to shake\r\nhis hand.\r\n\r\nBut Chauvelin was not the man to trouble himself about these social\r\namenities, which he called mere incidents in his diplomatic career. He\r\nwas blindly enthusiastic for the revolutionary cause, he despised all\r\nsocial inequalities, and he had a burning love for his own country:\r\nthese three sentiments made him supremely indifferent to the snubs he\r\nreceived in this fog-ridden, loyalist, old-fashioned England.\r\n\r\nBut, above all, Chauvelin had a purpose at heart. He firmly believed\r\nthat the French aristocrat was the most bitter enemy of France; he\r\nwould have wished to see every one of them annihilated: he was one of\r\nthose who, during this awful Reign of Terror, had been the first to\r\nutter the historic and ferocious desire \xe2\x80\x9cthat aristocrats might have\r\nbut one head between them, so that it might be cut off with a single\r\nstroke of the guillotine.\xe2\x80\x9d And thus he looked upon every French\r\naristocrat, who had succeeded in escaping from France, as so much prey\r\nof which the guillotine had been unwarrantably cheated. There is no\r\ndoubt that those royalist _\xc3\xa9migr\xc3\xa9s_, once they had managed to cross the\r\nfrontier, did their very best to stir up foreign indignation against\r\nFrance. Plots without end were hatched in England, in Belgium, in\r\nHolland, to try and induce some great power to send troops into\r\nrevolutionary Paris, to free King Louis, and to summarily hang the\r\nbloodthirsty leaders of that monster republic.\r\n\r\nSmall wonder, therefore, that the romantic and mysterious personality\r\nof the Scarlet Pimpernel was a source of bitter hatred to Chauvelin. He\r\nand the few young jackanapes under his command, well furnished with\r\nmoney, armed with boundless daring, and acute cunning, had succeeded in\r\nrescuing hundreds of aristocrats from France. Nine-tenths of the\r\n_\xc3\xa9migr\xc3\xa9s_, who were _f\xc3\xaated_ at the English court, owed their safety to\r\nthat man and to his league.\r\n\r\nChauvelin had sworn to his colleagues in Paris that he would discover\r\nthe identity of that meddlesome Englishman, entice him over to France,\r\nand then . . . Chauvelin drew a deep breath of satisfaction at the very\r\nthought of seeing that enigmatic head falling under the knife of the\r\nguillotine, as easily as that of any other man.\r\n\r\nSuddenly there was a great stir on the handsome staircase, all\r\nconversation stopped for a moment as the major-domo\xe2\x80\x99s voice outside\r\nannounced,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis Royal Highness the Prince of Wales and suite, Sir Percy Blakeney,\r\nLady Blakeney.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLord Grenville went quickly to the door to receive his exalted guest.\r\n\r\nThe Prince of Wales, dressed in a magnificent court suit of\r\nsalmon-coloured velvet richly embroidered with gold, entered with\r\nMarguerite Blakeney on his arm; and on his left Sir Percy, in gorgeous\r\nshimmering cream satin, cut in the extravagant \xe2\x80\x9cIncroyable\xe2\x80\x9d style, his\r\nfair hair free from powder, priceless lace at his neck and wrists, and\r\nthe flat _chapeau-bras_ under his arm.\r\n\r\nAfter the few conventional words of deferential greeting, Lord\r\nGrenville said to his royal guest,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill your Highness permit me to introduce M. Chauvelin, the accredited\r\nagent of the French Government?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChauvelin, immediately the Prince entered, had stepped forward,\r\nexpecting this introduction. He bowed very low, whilst the Prince\r\nreturned his salute with a curt nod of the head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur,\xe2\x80\x9d said His Royal Highness coldly, \xe2\x80\x9cwe will try to forget the\r\ngovernment that sent you, and look upon you merely as our guest\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nprivate gentleman from France. As such you are welcome, Monsieur.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonseigneur,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined Chauvelin, bowing once again. \xe2\x80\x9cMadame,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nadded, bowing ceremoniously before Marguerite.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! my little Chauvelin!\xe2\x80\x9d she said with unconcerned gaiety, and\r\nextending her tiny hand to him. \xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur and I are old friends, your\r\nRoyal Highness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Prince, this time very graciously, \xe2\x80\x9cyou are doubly\r\nwelcome, Monsieur.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is someone else I would crave permission to present to your\r\nRoyal Highness,\xe2\x80\x9d here interposed Lord Grenville.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! who is it?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Prince.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMadame la Comtesse de Tournay de Basserive and her family, who have\r\nbut recently come from France.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy all means!\xe2\x80\x94They are among the lucky ones then!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLord Grenville turned in search of the Comtesse, who sat at the further\r\nend of the room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLud love me!\xe2\x80\x9d whispered His Royal Highness to Marguerite, as soon as\r\nhe had caught sight of the rigid figure of the old lady; \xe2\x80\x9cLud love me!\r\nshe looks very virtuous and very melancholy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFaith, your Royal Highness,\xe2\x80\x9d she rejoined with a smile, \xe2\x80\x9cvirtue is\r\nlike precious odours, most fragrant when it is crushed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVirtue, alas!\xe2\x80\x9d sighed the Prince, \xe2\x80\x9cis mostly unbecoming to your\r\ncharming sex, Madame.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMadame la Comtesse de Tournay de Basserive,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lord Grenville,\r\nintroducing the lady.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is a pleasure, Madame; my royal father, as you know, is ever glad\r\nto welcome those of your compatriots whom France has driven from her\r\nshores.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour Royal Highness is ever gracious,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the Comtesse with\r\nbecoming dignity. Then, indicating her daughter, who stood timidly by\r\nher side: \xe2\x80\x9cMy daughter Suzanne, Monseigneur,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! charming!\xe2\x80\x94charming!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Prince, \xe2\x80\x9cand now allow me, Comtesse,\r\nto introduce to you, Lady Blakeney, who honours us with her friendship.\r\nYou and she will have much to say to one another, I vow. Every\r\ncompatriot of Lady Blakeney\xe2\x80\x99s is doubly welcome for her sake . . . her\r\nfriends are our friends . . . her enemies, the enemies of England.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarguerite\xe2\x80\x99s blue eyes had twinkled with merriment at this gracious\r\nspeech from her exalted friend. The Comtesse de Tournay, who lately had\r\nso flagrantly insulted her, was here receiving a public lesson, at\r\nwhich Marguerite could not help but rejoice. But the Comtesse, for whom\r\nrespect of royalty amounted almost to a religion, was too well-schooled\r\nin courtly etiquette to show the slightest sign of embarrassment, as\r\nthe two ladies curtsied ceremoniously to one another.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis Royal Highness is ever gracious, Madame,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marguerite,\r\ndemurely, and with a wealth of mischief in her twinkling blue eyes,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbut here there is no need for his kind mediation. . . . Your amiable\r\nreception of me at our last meeting still dwells pleasantly in my\r\nmemory.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe poor exiles, Madame,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined the Comtesse, frigidly, \xe2\x80\x9cshow our\r\ngratitude to England by devotion to the wishes of Monseigneur.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMadame!\xe2\x80\x9d said Marguerite, with another ceremonious curtsey.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMadame,\xe2\x80\x9d responded the Comtesse with equal dignity.\r\n\r\nThe Prince in the meanwhile was saying a few gracious words to the\r\nyoung Vicomte.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am happy to know you, Monsieur le Vicomte,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI knew your\r\nfather well when he was ambassador in London.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, Monseigneur!\xe2\x80\x9d replied the Vicomte, \xe2\x80\x9cI was a leetle boy then . . .\r\nand now I owe the honour of this meeting to our protector, the Scarlet\r\nPimpernel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Prince, earnestly and quickly, as he indicated\r\nChauvelin, who had stood a little on one side throughout the whole of\r\nthis little scene, watching Marguerite and the Comtesse with an amused,\r\nsarcastic little smile around his thin lips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, Monseigneur,\xe2\x80\x9d he said now, as if in direct response to the\r\nPrince\xe2\x80\x99s challenge, \xe2\x80\x9cpray do not check this gentleman\xe2\x80\x99s display of\r\ngratitude; the name of that interesting red flower is well known to\r\nme\xe2\x80\x94and to France.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Prince looked at him keenly for a moment or two.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFaith, then, Monsieur,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cperhaps you know more about our\r\nnational hero than we do ourselves . . . perchance you know who he is.\r\n. . . See!\xe2\x80\x9d he added, turning to the groups round the room, \xe2\x80\x9cthe ladies\r\nhang upon your lips . . . you would render yourself popular among the\r\nfair sex if you were to gratify their curiosity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, Monseigneur,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chauvelin, significantly, \xe2\x80\x9crumour has it in\r\nFrance that your Highness could\xe2\x80\x94an you would\xe2\x80\x94give the truest account of\r\nthat enigmatical wayside flower.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe looked quickly and keenly at Marguerite as he spoke; but she\r\nbetrayed no emotion, and her eyes met his quite fearlessly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, man,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the Prince, \xe2\x80\x9cmy lips are sealed! and the members of\r\nthe league jealously guard the secret of their chief . . . so his fair\r\nadorers have to be content with worshipping a shadow. Here in England,\r\nMonsieur,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, with wonderful charm and dignity, \xe2\x80\x9cwe but name the\r\nScarlet Pimpernel, and every fair cheek is suffused with a blush of\r\nenthusiasm. None have seen him save his faithful lieutenants. We know\r\nnot if he be tall or short, fair or dark, handsome or ill-formed; but\r\nwe know that he is the bravest gentleman in all the world, and we all\r\nfeel a little proud, Monsieur, when we remember that he is an\r\nEnglishman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, Monsieur Chauvelin,\xe2\x80\x9d added Marguerite, looking almost with\r\ndefiance across at the placid, sphinx-like face of the Frenchman, \xe2\x80\x9cHis\r\nRoyal Highness should add that we ladies think of him as of a hero of\r\nold . . . we worship him . . . we wear his badge . . . we tremble for\r\nhim when he is in danger, and exult with him in the hour of his\r\nvictory.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChauvelin did no more than bow placidly both to the Prince and to\r\nMarguerite; he felt that both speeches were intended\xe2\x80\x94each in their\r\nway\xe2\x80\x94to convey contempt or defiance. The pleasure-loving, idle Prince he\r\ndespised; the beautiful woman, who in her golden hair wore a spray of\r\nsmall red flowers composed of rubies and diamonds\xe2\x80\x94her he held in the\r\nhollow of his hand: he could afford to remain silent and to await\r\nevents.\r\n\r\nA long, jovial, inane laugh broke the sudden silence which had fallen\r\nover everyone.\r\n \xe2\x80\x9cAnd we poor husbands,\xe2\x80\x9d came in slow, affected accents from gorgeous\r\n Sir Percy, \xe2\x80\x9cwe have to stand by . . . while they worship a demmed\r\n shadow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEveryone laughed\xe2\x80\x94the Prince more loudly than anyone. The tension of\r\nsubdued excitement was relieved, and the next moment everyone was\r\nlaughing and chatting merrily as the gay crowd broke up and dispersed\r\nin the adjoining rooms.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XII.\r\nTHE SCRAP OF PAPER\r\n\r\n\r\nMarguerite suffered intensely. Though she laughed and chatted, though\r\nshe was more admired, more surrounded, more _f\xc3\xaated_ than any woman\r\nthere, she felt like one condemned to death, living her last day upon\r\nthis earth.\r\n\r\nHer nerves were in a state of painful tension, which had increased a\r\nhundredfold during that brief hour which she had spent in her husband\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncompany, between the opera and the ball. The short ray of hope\xe2\x80\x94that she\r\nmight find in this good-natured, lazy individual a valuable friend and\r\nadviser\xe2\x80\x94had vanished as quickly as it had come, the moment she found\r\nherself alone with him. The same feeling of good-humoured contempt\r\nwhich one feels for an animal or a faithful servant, made her turn away\r\nwith a smile from the man who should have been her moral support in\r\nthis heart-rending crisis through which she was passing: who should\r\nhave been her cool-headed adviser, when feminine sympathy and sentiment\r\ntossed her hither and thither, between her love for her brother, who\r\nwas far away and in mortal peril, and horror of the awful service which\r\nChauvelin had exacted from her, in exchange for Armand\xe2\x80\x99s safety.\r\n\r\nThere he stood, the moral support, the cool-headed adviser, surrounded\r\nby a crowd of brainless, empty-headed young fops, who were even now\r\nrepeating from mouth to mouth, and with every sign of the keenest\r\nenjoyment, a doggerel quatrain which he had just given forth.\r\n\r\nEverywhere the absurd, silly words met her: people seemed to have\r\nlittle else to speak about, even the Prince had asked her, with a\r\nlaugh, whether she appreciated her husband\xe2\x80\x99s latest poetic efforts.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll done in the tying of a cravat,\xe2\x80\x9d Sir Percy had declared to his\r\nclique of admirers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe seek him here, we seek him there,\r\nThose Frenchies seek him everywhere.\r\nIs he in heaven?\xe2\x80\x94Is he in hell?\r\nThat demmed, elusive Pimpernel?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nSir Percy\xe2\x80\x99s _bon mot_ had gone the round of the brilliant\r\nreception-rooms. The Prince was enchanted. He vowed that life without\r\nBlakeney would be but a dreary desert. Then, taking him by the arm, had\r\nled him to the card-room, and engaged him in a long game of hazard.\r\n\r\nSir Percy, whose chief interest in most social gatherings seemed to\r\ncentre round the card-table, usually allowed his wife to flirt, dance,\r\nto amuse or bore herself as much as she liked. And to-night, having\r\ndelivered himself of his _bon mot_, he had left Marguerite surrounded\r\nby a crowd of admirers of all ages, all anxious and willing to help her\r\nto forget that somewhere in the spacious reception-rooms, there was a\r\nlong, lazy being who had been fool enough to suppose that the cleverest\r\nwoman in Europe would settle down to the prosaic bonds of English\r\nmatrimony.\r\n\r\nHer still overwrought nerves, her excitement and agitation, lent\r\nbeautiful Marguerite Blakeney much additional charm: escorted by a\r\nveritable bevy of men of all ages and of most nationalities, she called\r\nforth many exclamations of admiration from everyone as she passed.\r\n\r\nShe would not allow herself any more time to think. Her early, somewhat\r\nBohemian training had made her something of a fatalist. She felt that\r\nevents would shape themselves, that the directing of them was not in\r\nher hands. From Chauvelin she knew that she could expect no mercy. He\r\nhad set a price upon Armand\xe2\x80\x99s head, and left it to her to pay or not,\r\nas she chose.\r\n\r\nLater on in the evening she caught sight of Sir Andrew Ffoulkes and\r\nLord Antony Dewhurst, who seemingly had just arrived. She noticed at\r\nonce that Sir Andrew immediately made for little Suzanne de Tournay,\r\nand that the two young people soon managed to isolate themselves in one\r\nof the deep embrasures of the mullioned windows, there to carry on a\r\nlong conversation, which seemed very earnest and very pleasant on both\r\nsides.\r\n\r\nBoth the young men looked a little haggard and anxious, but otherwise\r\nthey were irreproachably dressed, and there was not the slightest sign,\r\nabout their courtly demeanour, of the terrible catastrophe, which they\r\nmust have felt hovering round them and round their chief.\r\n\r\nThat the League of the Scarlet Pimpernel had no intention of abandoning\r\nits cause, she had gathered through little Suzanne herself, who spoke\r\nopenly of the assurance she and her mother had had that the Comte de\r\nTournay would be rescued from France by the league, within the next few\r\ndays. Vaguely she began to wonder, as she looked at the brilliant and\r\nfashionable crowd in the gaily-lighted ball-room, which of these\r\nworldly men round her was the mysterious \xe2\x80\x9cScarlet Pimpernel,\xe2\x80\x9d who held\r\nthe threads of such daring plots, and the fate of valuable lives in his\r\nhands.\r\n\r\nA burning curiosity seized her to know him: although for months she had\r\nheard of him and had accepted his anonymity, as everyone else in\r\nsociety had done; but now she longed to know\xe2\x80\x94quite impersonally, quite\r\napart from Armand, and oh! quite apart from Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x94only for her own\r\nsake, for the sake of the enthusiastic admiration she had always\r\nbestowed on his bravery and cunning.\r\n\r\nHe was at the ball, of course, somewhere, since Sir Andrew Ffoulkes and\r\nLord Antony Dewhurst were here, evidently expecting to meet their\r\nchief\xe2\x80\x94and perhaps to get a fresh _mot d\xe2\x80\x99ordre_ from him.\r\n\r\nMarguerite looked round at everyone, at the aristocratic high-typed\r\nNorman faces, the squarely-built, fair-haired Saxon, the more gentle,\r\nhumorous caste of the Celt, wondering which of these betrayed the\r\npower, the energy, the cunning which had imposed its will and its\r\nleadership upon a number of high-born English gentlemen, among whom\r\nrumour asserted was His Royal Highness himself.\r\n\r\nSir Andrew Ffoulkes? Surely not, with his gentle blue eyes, which were\r\nlooking so tenderly and longingly after little Suzanne, who was being\r\nled away from the pleasant _t\xc3\xaate-\xc3\xa0-t\xc3\xaate_ by her stern mother.\r\nMarguerite watched him across the room, as he finally turned away with\r\na sigh, and seemed to stand, aimless and lonely, now that Suzanne\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndainty little figure had disappeared in the crowd.\r\n\r\nShe watched him as he strolled towards the doorway, which led to a\r\nsmall boudoir beyond, then paused and leaned against the framework of\r\nit, looking still anxiously all round him.\r\n\r\nMarguerite contrived for the moment to evade her present attentive\r\ncavalier, and she skirted the fashionable crowd, drawing nearer to the\r\ndoorway, against which Sir Andrew was leaning. Why she wished to get\r\ncloser to him, she could not have said: perhaps she was impelled by an\r\nall-powerful fatality, which so often seems to rule the destinies of\r\nmen.\r\n\r\nSuddenly she stopped: her very heart seemed to stand still, her eyes,\r\nlarge and excited, flashed for a moment towards that doorway, then as\r\nquickly were turned away again. Sir Andrew Ffoulkes was still in the\r\nsame listless position by the door, but Marguerite had distinctly seen\r\nthat Lord Hastings\xe2\x80\x94a young buck, a friend of her husband\xe2\x80\x99s and one of\r\nthe Prince\xe2\x80\x99s set\xe2\x80\x94had, as he quickly brushed past him, slipped something\r\ninto his hand.\r\n\r\nFor one moment longer\xe2\x80\x94oh! it was the merest flash\xe2\x80\x94Marguerite paused:\r\nthe next she had, with admirably played unconcern, resumed her walk\r\nacross the room\xe2\x80\x94but this time more quickly towards that doorway whence\r\nSir Andrew had now disappeared.\r\n\r\nAll this, from the moment that Marguerite had caught sight of Sir\r\nAndrew leaning against the doorway, until she followed him into the\r\nlittle boudoir beyond, had occurred in less than a minute. Fate is\r\nusually swift when she deals a blow.\r\n\r\nNow Lady Blakeney had suddenly ceased to exist. It was Marguerite St.\r\nJust who was there only: Marguerite St. Just who had passed her\r\nchildhood, her early youth, in the protecting arms of her brother\r\nArmand. She had forgotten everything else\xe2\x80\x94her rank, her dignity, her\r\nsecret enthusiasms\xe2\x80\x94everything save that Armand stood in peril of his\r\nlife, and that there, not twenty feet away from her, in the small\r\nboudoir which was quite deserted, in the very hands of Sir Andrew\r\nFfoulkes, might be the talisman which would save her brother\xe2\x80\x99s life.\r\n\r\nBarely another thirty seconds had elapsed between the moment when Lord\r\nHastings slipped the mysterious \xe2\x80\x9csomething\xe2\x80\x9d into Sir Andrew\xe2\x80\x99s hand, and\r\nthe one when she, in her turn, reached the deserted boudoir. Sir Andrew\r\nwas standing with his back to her and close to a table upon which stood\r\na massive silver candelabra. A slip of paper was in his hand, and he\r\nwas in the very act of perusing its contents.\r\n\r\nUnperceived, her soft clinging robe making not the slightest sound upon\r\nthe heavy carpet, not daring to breathe until she had accomplished her\r\npurpose, Marguerite slipped close behind him. . . . At that moment he\r\nlooked round and saw her; she uttered a groan, passed her hand across\r\nher forehead, and murmured faintly,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe heat in the room was terrible . . . I felt so faint. . . . Ah! . .\r\n.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe tottered almost as if she would fall, and Sir Andrew, quickly\r\nrecovering himself, and crumpling in his hand the tiny note he had been\r\nreading, was only, apparently, just in time to support her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are ill, Lady Blakeney?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked with much concern. \xe2\x80\x9cLet me . .\r\n.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, nothing\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d she interrupted quickly. \xe2\x80\x9cA chair\xe2\x80\x94quick.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe sank into a chair close to the table, and throwing back her head,\r\nclosed her eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere!\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured, still faintly; \xe2\x80\x9cthe giddiness is passing off. .\r\n. . Do not heed me, Sir Andrew; I assure you I already feel better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt moments like these there is no doubt\xe2\x80\x94and psychologists actually\r\nassert it\xe2\x80\x94that there is in us a sense which has absolutely nothing to\r\ndo with the other five: it is not that we see, it is not that we hear\r\nor touch, yet we seem to do all three at once. Marguerite sat there\r\nwith her eyes apparently closed. Sir Andrew was immediately behind her,\r\nand on her right was the table with the five-armed candelabra upon it.\r\nBefore her mental vision there was absolutely nothing but Armand\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nface. Armand, whose life was in the most imminent danger, and who\r\nseemed to be looking at her from a background upon which were dimly\r\npainted the seething crowd of Paris, the bare walls of the Tribunal of\r\nPublic Safety, with Foucquier-Tinville, the Public Prosecutor,\r\ndemanding Armand\xe2\x80\x99s life in the name of the people of France, and the\r\nlurid guillotine with its stained knife waiting for another victim . .\r\n. Armand! . . .\r\n\r\nFor one moment there was dead silence in the little boudoir. Beyond,\r\nfrom the brilliant ball-room, the sweet notes of the gavotte, the\r\nfrou-frou of rich dresses, the talk and laughter of a large and merry\r\ncrowd, came as a strange, weird accompaniment to the drama which was\r\nbeing enacted here.\r\n\r\n Sir Andrew had not uttered another word. Then it was that that extra\r\n sense became potent in Marguerite Blakeney. She could not see, for her\r\n eyes were closed; she could not hear, for the noise from the ball-room\r\n drowned the soft rustle of that momentous scrap of paper; nevertheless\r\n she knew\xe2\x80\x94as if she had both seen and heard\xe2\x80\x94that Sir Andrew was even\r\n now holding the paper to the flame of one of the candles.\r\n\r\nAt the exact moment that it began to catch fire, she opened her eyes,\r\nraised her hand and, with two dainty fingers, had taken the burning\r\nscrap of paper from the young man\xe2\x80\x99s hand. Then she blew out the flame,\r\nand held the paper to her nostril with perfect unconcern.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow thoughtful of you, Sir Andrew,\xe2\x80\x9d she said gaily, \xe2\x80\x9csurely \xe2\x80\x99twas your\r\ngrandmother who taught you that the smell of burnt paper was a\r\nsovereign remedy against giddiness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe sighed with satisfaction, holding the paper tightly between her\r\njewelled fingers; that talisman which perhaps would save her brother\r\nArmand\xe2\x80\x99s life. Sir Andrew was staring at her, too dazed for the moment\r\nto realise what had actually happened; he had been taken so completely\r\nby surprise, that he seemed quite unable to grasp the fact that the\r\nslip of paper, which she held in her dainty hand, was one perhaps on\r\nwhich the life of his comrade might depend.\r\n\r\nMarguerite burst into a long, merry peal of laughter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you stare at me like that?\xe2\x80\x9d she said playfully. \xe2\x80\x9cI assure you I\r\nfeel much better; your remedy has proved most effectual. This room is\r\nmost delightfully cool,\xe2\x80\x9d she added, with the same perfect composure,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand the sound of the gavotte from the ball-room is fascinating and\r\nsoothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was prattling on in the most unconcerned and pleasant way, whilst\r\nSir Andrew, in an agony of mind, was racking his brains as to the\r\nquickest method he could employ to get that bit of paper out of that\r\nbeautiful woman\xe2\x80\x99s hand. Instinctively, vague and tumultuous thoughts\r\nrushed through his mind: he suddenly remembered her nationality, and\r\nworst of all, recollected that horrible tale anent the Marquis de St.\r\nCyr, which in England no one had credited, for the sake of Sir Percy,\r\nas well as for her own.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat? Still dreaming and staring?\xe2\x80\x9d she said, with a merry laugh, \xe2\x80\x9cyou\r\nare most ungallant, Sir Andrew; and now I come to think of it, you\r\nseemed more startled than pleased when you saw me just now. I do\r\nbelieve, after all, that it was not concern for my health, nor yet a\r\nremedy taught you by your grandmother that caused you to burn this tiny\r\nscrap of paper. . . . I vow it must have been your lady love\xe2\x80\x99s last\r\ncruel epistle you were trying to destroy. Now confess!\xe2\x80\x9d she added,\r\nplayfully holding up the scrap of paper, \xe2\x80\x9cdoes this contain her final\r\n_cong\xc3\xa9_, or a last appeal to kiss and make friends?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhichever it is, Lady Blakeney,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sir Andrew, who was gradually\r\nrecovering his self-possession, \xe2\x80\x9cthis little note is undoubtedly mine,\r\nand . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n Not caring whether his action was one that would be styled ill-bred\r\n towards a lady, the young man had made a bold dash for the note; but\r\n Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s thoughts flew quicker than his own; her actions, under\r\n pressure of this intense excitement, were swifter and more sure. She\r\n was tall and strong; she took a quick step backwards and knocked over\r\n the small Sheraton table which was already top-heavy, and which fell\r\n down with a crash, together with the massive candelabra upon it.\r\n\r\nShe gave a quick cry of alarm:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe candles, Sir Andrew\xe2\x80\x94quick!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was not much damage done; one or two of the candles had blown out\r\nas the candelabra fell; others had merely sent some grease upon the\r\nvaluable carpet; one had ignited the paper shade over it. Sir Andrew\r\nquickly and dexterously put out the flames and replaced the candelabra\r\nupon the table; but this had taken him a few seconds to do, and those\r\nseconds had been all that Marguerite needed to cast a quick glance at\r\nthe paper, and to note its contents\xe2\x80\x94a dozen words in the same distorted\r\nhandwriting she had seen before, and bearing the same device\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nstar-shaped flower drawn in red ink.\r\n\r\nWhen Sir Andrew once more looked at her, he only saw on her face alarm\r\nat the untoward accident and relief at its happy issue; whilst the tiny\r\nand momentous note had apparently fluttered to the ground. Eagerly the\r\nyoung man picked it up, and his face looked much relieved, as his\r\nfingers closed tightly over it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor shame, Sir Andrew,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, shaking her head with a playful\r\nsigh, \xe2\x80\x9cmaking havoc in the heart of some impressionable duchess, whilst\r\nconquering the affections of my sweet little Suzanne. Well, well! I do\r\nbelieve it was Cupid himself who stood by you, and threatened the\r\nentire Foreign Office with destruction by fire, just on purpose to make\r\nme drop love\xe2\x80\x99s message, before it had been polluted by my indiscreet\r\neyes. To think that, a moment longer, and I might have known the\r\nsecrets of an erring duchess.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will forgive me, Lady Blakeney,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sir Andrew, now as calm as\r\nshe was herself, \xe2\x80\x9cif I resume the interesting occupation which you had\r\ninterrupted?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy all means, Sir Andrew! How should I venture to thwart the love-god\r\nagain? Perhaps he would mete out some terrible chastisement against my\r\npresumption. Burn your love-token, by all means!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSir Andrew had already twisted the paper into a long spill, and was\r\nonce again holding it to the flame of the candle, which had remained\r\nalight. He did not notice the strange smile on the face of his fair\r\n_vis-\xc3\xa0-vis_, so intent was he on the work of destruction; perhaps, had\r\nhe done so, the look of relief would have faded from his face. He\r\nwatched the fateful note, as it curled under the flame. Soon the last\r\nfragment fell on the floor, and he placed his heel upon the ashes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now, Sir Andrew,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marguerite Blakeney, with the pretty\r\nnonchalance peculiar to herself, and with the most winning of smiles,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwill you venture to excite the jealousy of your fair lady by asking me\r\nto dance the minuet?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIII.\r\nEITHER\xe2\x80\x94OR?\r\n\r\n\r\nThe few words which Marguerite Blakeney had managed to read on the\r\nhalf-scorched piece of paper, seemed literally to be the words of Fate.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStart myself to-morrow. . . .\xe2\x80\x9d This she had read quite distinctly;\r\nthen came a blur caused by the smoke of the candle, which obliterated\r\nthe next few words; but, right at the bottom, there was another\r\nsentence, which was now standing clearly and distinctly, like letters\r\nof fire, before her mental vision. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you wish to speak to me again, I\r\nshall be in the supper-room at one o\xe2\x80\x99clock precisely.\xe2\x80\x9d The whole was\r\nsigned with the hastily-scrawled little device\xe2\x80\x94a tiny star-shaped\r\nflower, which had become so familiar to her.\r\n\r\nOne o\xe2\x80\x99clock precisely! It was now close upon eleven, the last minuet\r\nwas being danced, with Sir Andrew Ffoulkes and beautiful Lady Blakeney\r\nleading the couples, through its delicate and intricate figures.\r\n\r\nClose upon eleven! the hands of the handsome Louis XV. clock upon its\r\normolu bracket seemed to move along with maddening rapidity. Two hours\r\nmore, and her fate and that of Armand would be sealed. In two hours she\r\nmust make up her mind whether she will keep the knowledge so cunningly\r\ngained to herself, and leave her brother to his fate, or whether she\r\nwill wilfully betray a brave man, whose life was devoted to his\r\nfellow-men, who was noble, generous, and above all, unsuspecting. It\r\nseemed a horrible thing to do. But then, there was Armand! Armand, too,\r\nwas noble and brave, Armand, too, was unsuspecting. And Armand loved\r\nher, would have willingly trusted his life in her hands, and now, when\r\nshe could save him from death, she hesitated. Oh! it was monstrous; her\r\nbrother\xe2\x80\x99s kind, gentle face, so full of love for her, seemed to be\r\nlooking reproachfully at her. \xe2\x80\x9cYou might have saved me, Margot!\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nseemed to say to her, \xe2\x80\x9cand you chose the life of a stranger, a man you\r\ndo not know, whom you have never seen, and preferred that he should be\r\nsafe, whilst you sent me to the guillotine!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAll these conflicting thoughts raged through Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s brain, while,\r\nwith a smile upon her lips, she glided through the graceful mazes of\r\nthe minuet. She noted\xe2\x80\x94with that acute sense of hers\xe2\x80\x94that she had\r\nsucceeded in completely allaying Sir Andrew\xe2\x80\x99s fears. Her self-control\r\nhad been absolutely perfect\xe2\x80\x94she was a finer actress at this moment, and\r\nthroughout the whole of this minuet, than she had ever been upon the\r\nboards of the Com\xc3\xa9die Fran\xc3\xa7aise; but then, a beloved brother\xe2\x80\x99s life had\r\nnot depended upon her histrionic powers.\r\n\r\nShe was too clever to overdo her part, and made no further allusions to\r\nthe supposed _billet doux_, which had caused Sir Andrew Ffoulkes such\r\nan agonising five minutes. She watched his anxiety melting away under\r\nher sunny smile, and soon perceived that, whatever doubt may have\r\ncrossed his mind at the moment, she had, by the time the last bars of\r\nthe minuet had been played, succeeded in completely dispelling it; he\r\nnever realised in what a fever of excitement she was, what effort it\r\ncost her to keep up a constant ripple of _banal_ conversation.\r\n\r\nWhen the minuet was over, she asked Sir Andrew to take her into the\r\nnext room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have promised to go down to supper with His Royal Highness,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cbut before we part, tell me . . . am I forgiven?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cForgiven?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes! Confess, I gave you a fright just now. . . . But, remember, I am\r\nnot an Englishwoman, and I do not look upon the exchanging of _billet\r\ndoux_ as a crime, and I vow I\xe2\x80\x99ll not tell my little Suzanne. But now,\r\ntell me, shall I welcome you at my water-party on Wednesday?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not sure, Lady Blakeney,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied evasively. \xe2\x80\x9cI may have to\r\nleave London to-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would not do that, if I were you,\xe2\x80\x9d she said earnestly; then seeing\r\nthe anxious look once more reappearing in his eyes, she added gaily;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo one can throw a ball better than you can, Sir Andrew, we should so\r\nmiss you on the bowling-green.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe had led her across the room, to one beyond, where already His Royal\r\nHighness was waiting for the beautiful Lady Blakeney.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMadame, supper awaits us,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Prince, offering his arm to\r\nMarguerite, \xe2\x80\x9cand I am full of hope. The goddess Fortune has frowned so\r\npersistently on me at hazard, that I look with confidence for the\r\nsmiles of the goddess of Beauty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour Highness has been unfortunate at the card tables?\xe2\x80\x9d asked\r\nMarguerite, as she took the Prince\xe2\x80\x99s arm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye! most unfortunate. Blakeney, not content with being the richest\r\namong my father\xe2\x80\x99s subjects, has also the most outrageous luck. By the\r\nway, where is that inimitable wit? I vow, Madam, that this life would\r\nbe but a dreary desert without your smiles and his sallies.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIV.\r\nONE O\xe2\x80\x99CLOCK PRECISELY!\r\n\r\n\r\nSupper had been extremely gay. All those present declared that never\r\nhad Lady Blakeney been more adorable, nor that \xe2\x80\x9cdemmed idiot\xe2\x80\x9d Sir Percy\r\nmore amusing.\r\n\r\nHis Royal Highness had laughed until the tears streamed down his cheeks\r\nat Blakeney\xe2\x80\x99s foolish yet funny repartees. His doggerel verse, \xe2\x80\x9cWe seek\r\nhim here, we seek him there,\xe2\x80\x9d etc., was sung to the tune of \xe2\x80\x9cHo! Merry\r\nBritons!\xe2\x80\x9d and to the accompaniment of glasses knocked loudly against\r\nthe table.
