Lord Grenville, moreover, had a most perfect cook\xe2\x80\x94some wags\r\nasserted that he was a scion of the old French _noblesse_, who, having\r\nlost his fortune, had come to seek it in the _cuisine_ of the Foreign\r\nOffice.\r\n\r\nMarguerite Blakeney was in her most brilliant mood, and surely not a\r\nsoul in that crowded supper-room had even an inkling of the terrible\r\nstruggle which was raging within her heart.\r\n\r\nThe clock was ticking so mercilessly on. It was long past midnight, and\r\neven the Prince of Wales was thinking of leaving the supper-table.\r\nWithin the next half-hour the destinies of two brave men would be\r\npitted against one another\xe2\x80\x94the dearly-beloved brother and he, the\r\nunknown hero.\r\n\r\nMarguerite had not even tried to see Chauvelin during this last hour;\r\nshe knew that his keen, fox-like eyes would terrify her at once, and\r\nincline the balance of her decision towards Armand. Whilst she did not\r\nsee him, there still lingered in her heart of hearts a vague, undefined\r\nhope that \xe2\x80\x9csomething\xe2\x80\x9d would occur, something big, enormous,\r\nepoch-making, which would shift from her young, weak shoulders this\r\nterrible burden of responsibility, of having to choose between two such\r\ncruel alternatives.\r\n\r\nBut the minutes ticked on with that dull monotony which they invariably\r\nseem to assume when our very nerves ache with their incessant ticking.\r\n\r\nAfter supper, dancing was resumed. His Royal Highness had left, and\r\nthere was general talk of departing among the older guests; the young\r\nones were indefatigable and had started on a new gavotte, which would\r\nfill the next quarter of an hour.\r\n\r\nMarguerite did not feel equal to another dance; there is a limit to the\r\nmost enduring self-control. Escorted by a Cabinet Minister, she had\r\nonce more found her way to the tiny boudoir, still the most deserted\r\namong all the rooms. She knew that Chauvelin must be lying in wait for\r\nher somewhere, ready to seize the first possible opportunity for a\r\n_t\xc3\xaate-\xc3\xa0-t\xc3\xaate_. His eyes had met hers for a moment after the\r\n\xe2\x80\x99fore-supper minuet, and she knew that the keen diplomatist, with those\r\nsearching pale eyes of his, had divined that her work was accomplished.\r\n\r\nFate had willed it so. Marguerite, torn by the most terrible conflict\r\nheart of woman can ever know, had resigned herself to its decrees. But\r\nArmand must be saved at any cost; he, first of all, for he was her\r\nbrother, had been mother, father, friend to her ever since she, a tiny\r\nbabe, had lost both her parents. To think of Armand dying a traitor\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndeath on the guillotine was too horrible even to dwell upon\xe2\x80\x94impossible,\r\nin fact. That could never be, never. . . . As for the stranger, the\r\nhero . . . well! there, let Fate decide. Marguerite would redeem her\r\nbrother\xe2\x80\x99s life at the hands of the relentless enemy, then let that\r\ncunning Scarlet Pimpernel extricate himself after that.\r\n\r\nPerhaps\xe2\x80\x94vaguely\xe2\x80\x94Marguerite hoped that the daring plotter, who for so\r\nmany months had baffled an army of spies, would still manage to evade\r\nChauvelin and remain immune to the end.\r\n\r\nShe thought of all this, as she sat listening to the witty discourse of\r\nthe Cabinet Minister, who, no doubt, felt that he had found in Lady\r\nBlakeney a most perfect listener. Suddenly she saw the keen, fox-like\r\nface of Chauvelin peeping through the curtained doorway.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLord Fancourt,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to the Minister, \xe2\x80\x9cwill you do me a service?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am entirely at your ladyship\xe2\x80\x99s service,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied gallantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you see if my husband is still in the card-room? And if he is,\r\nwill you tell him that I am very tired, and would be glad to go home\r\nsoon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe commands of a beautiful woman are binding on all mankind, even on\r\nCabinet Ministers. Lord Fancourt prepared to obey instantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not like to leave your ladyship alone,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever fear. I shall be quite safe here\xe2\x80\x94and, I think, undisturbed . . .\r\nbut I am really tired. You know Sir Percy will drive back to Richmond.\r\nIt is a long way, and we shall not\xe2\x80\x94an we do not hurry\xe2\x80\x94get home before\r\ndaybreak.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLord Fancourt had perforce to go.\r\n\r\nThe moment he had disappeared, Chauvelin slipped into the room, and the\r\nnext instant stood calm and impassive by her side.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have news for me?\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nAn icy mantle seemed to have suddenly settled round Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nshoulders; though her cheeks glowed with fire, she felt chilled and\r\nnumbed. Oh, Armand! will you ever know the terrible sacrifice of pride,\r\nof dignity, of womanliness a devoted sister is making for your sake?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing of importance,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, staring mechanically before her,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbut it might prove a clue. I contrived\xe2\x80\x94no matter how\xe2\x80\x94to detect Sir\r\nAndrew Ffoulkes in the very act of burning a paper at one of these\r\ncandles, in this very room. That paper I succeeded in holding between\r\nmy fingers for the space of two minutes, and to cast my eye on it for\r\nthat of ten seconds.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTime enough to learn its contents?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Chauvelin, quietly.\r\n\r\nShe nodded. Then she continued in the same even, mechanical tone of\r\nvoice\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the corner of the paper there was the usual rough device of a small\r\nstar-shaped flower. Above it I read two lines, everything else was\r\nscorched and blackened by the flame.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what were these two lines?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer throat seemed suddenly to have contracted. For an instant she felt\r\nthat she could not speak the words, which might send a brave man to his\r\ndeath.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is lucky that the whole paper was not burned,\xe2\x80\x9d added Chauvelin,\r\nwith dry sarcasm, \xe2\x80\x9cfor it might have fared ill with Armand St. Just.\r\nWhat were the two lines, citoyenne?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne was, \xe2\x80\x98I start myself to-morrow,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d she said quietly; \xe2\x80\x9cthe other\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98If\r\nyou wish to speak to me, I shall be in the supper-room at one o\xe2\x80\x99clock\r\nprecisely.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChauvelin looked up at the clock just above the mantelpiece.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I have plenty of time,\xe2\x80\x9d he said placidly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you going to do?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\nShe was pale as a statue, her hands were icy cold, her head and heart\r\nthrobbed with the awful strain upon her nerves. Oh, this was cruel!\r\ncruel! What had she done to have deserved all this? Her choice was\r\nmade: had she done a vile action or one that was sublime? The recording\r\nangel, who writes in the book of gold, alone could give an answer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you going to do?\xe2\x80\x9d she repeated mechanically.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, nothing for the present. After that it will depend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn whom I shall see in the supper-room at one o\xe2\x80\x99clock precisely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will see the Scarlet Pimpernel, of course. But you do not know\r\nhim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. But I shall presently.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSir Andrew will have warned him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think not. When you parted from him after the minuet he stood and\r\nwatched you, for a moment or two, with a look which gave me to\r\nunderstand that something had happened between you. It was only\r\nnatural, was it not? that I should make a shrewd guess as to the nature\r\nof that \xe2\x80\x98something.\xe2\x80\x99 I thereupon engaged the young gallant in a long\r\nand animated conversation\xe2\x80\x94we discussed Herr Gl\xc3\xbcck\xe2\x80\x99s singular success in\r\nLondon\xe2\x80\x94until a lady claimed his arm for supper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSince then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did not lose sight of him through supper. When we all came upstairs\r\nagain, Lady Portarles buttonholed him and started on the subject of\r\npretty Mlle. Suzanne de Tournay. I knew he would not move until Lady\r\nPortarles had exhausted the subject, which will not be for another\r\nquarter of an hour at least, and it is five minutes to one now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was preparing to go, and went up to the doorway, where, drawing\r\naside the curtain, he stood for a moment pointing out to Marguerite the\r\ndistant figure of Sir Andrew Ffoulkes in close conversation with Lady\r\nPortarles.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, with a triumphant smile, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I may safely expect\r\nto find the person I seek in the dining-room, fair lady.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere may be more than one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhoever is there, as the clock strikes one, will be shadowed by one of\r\nmy men; of these, one, or perhaps two, or even three, will leave for\r\nFrance to-morrow. _One_ of these will be the \xe2\x80\x98Scarlet Pimpernel.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes?\xe2\x80\x94And?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI also, fair lady, will leave for France to-morrow. The papers found\r\nat Dover upon the person of Sir Andrew Ffoulkes speak of the\r\nneighbourhood of Calais, of an inn which I know well, called \xe2\x80\x98Le Chat\r\nGris,\xe2\x80\x99 of a lonely place somewhere on the coast\xe2\x80\x94the P\xc3\xa8re Blanchard\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhut\xe2\x80\x94which I must endeavour to find. All these places are given as the\r\npoint where this meddlesome Englishman has bidden the traitor de\r\nTournay and others to meet his emissaries. But it seems that he has\r\ndecided not to send his emissaries, that \xe2\x80\x98he will start himself\r\nto-morrow.\xe2\x80\x99 Now, one of those persons whom I shall see anon in the\r\nsupper-room, will be journeying to Calais, and I shall follow that\r\nperson, until I have tracked him to where those fugitive aristocrats\r\nawait him; for that person, fair lady, will be the man whom I have\r\nsought for, for nearly a year, the man whose energy has outdone me,\r\nwhose ingenuity has baffled me, whose audacity has set me\r\nwondering\xe2\x80\x94yes! me!\xe2\x80\x94who have seen a trick or two in my time\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nmysterious and elusive Scarlet Pimpernel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd Armand?\xe2\x80\x9d she pleaded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave I ever broken my word? I promise you that the day the Scarlet\r\nPimpernel and I start for France, I will send you that imprudent letter\r\nof his by special courier. More than that, I will pledge you the word\r\nof France, that the day I lay hands on that meddlesome Englishman, St.\r\nJust will be here in England, safe in the arms of his charming sister.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd with a deep and elaborate bow and another look at the clock,\r\nChauvelin glided out of the room.\r\n\r\nIt seemed to Marguerite that through all the noise, all the din of\r\nmusic, dancing, and laughter, she could hear his cat-like tread,\r\ngliding through the vast reception-rooms; that she could hear him go\r\ndown the massive staircase, reach the dining-room and open the door.\r\nFate _had_ decided, had made her speak, had made her do a vile and\r\nabominable thing, for the sake of the brother she loved. She lay back\r\nin her chair, passive and still, seeing the figure of her relentless\r\nenemy ever present before her aching eyes.\r\n\r\nWhen Chauvelin reached the supper-room it was quite deserted. It had\r\nthat woebegone, forsaken, tawdry appearance, which reminds one so much\r\nof a ball-dress, the morning after.\r\n\r\nHalf-empty glasses littered the table, unfolded napkins lay about, the\r\nchairs\xe2\x80\x94turned towards one another in groups of twos and threes\xe2\x80\x94seemed\r\nlike the seats of ghosts, in close conversation with one another. There\r\nwere sets of two chairs\xe2\x80\x94very close to one another\xe2\x80\x94in the far corners of\r\nthe room, which spoke of recent whispered flirtations, over cold\r\ngame-pie and champagne; there were sets of three and four chairs, that\r\nrecalled pleasant, animated discussions over the latest scandals; there\r\nwere chairs straight up in a row that still looked starchy, critical,\r\nacid, like antiquated dowagers; there were a few isolated, single\r\nchairs, close to the table, that spoke of gourmands intent on the most\r\n_recherch\xc3\xa9_ dishes, and others overturned on the floor, that spoke\r\nvolumes on the subject of my Lord Grenville\xe2\x80\x99s cellars.\r\n\r\nIt was a ghostlike replica, in fact, of that fashionable gathering\r\nupstairs; a ghost that haunts every house where balls and good suppers\r\nare given; a picture drawn with white chalk on grey cardboard, dull and\r\ncolourless, now that the bright silk dresses and gorgeously embroidered\r\ncoats were no longer there to fill in the foreground, and now that the\r\ncandles flickered sleepily in their sockets.\r\n\r\nChauvelin smiled benignly, and rubbing his long, thin hands together,\r\nhe looked round the deserted supper-room, whence even the last flunkey\r\nhad retired in order to join his friends in the hall below. All was\r\nsilence in the dimly-lighted room, whilst the sound of the gavotte, the\r\nhum of distant talk and laughter, and the rumble of an occasional coach\r\noutside, only seemed to reach this palace of the Sleeping Beauty as the\r\nmurmur of some flitting spooks far away.\r\n\r\nIt all looked so peaceful, so luxurious, and so still, that the keenest\r\nobserver\xe2\x80\x94a veritable prophet\xe2\x80\x94could never have guessed that, at this\r\npresent moment, that deserted supper-room was nothing but a trap laid\r\nfor the capture of the most cunning and audacious plotter those\r\nstirring times had ever seen.\r\n\r\nChauvelin pondered and tried to peer into the immediate future. What\r\nwould this man be like, whom he and the leaders of a whole revolution\r\nhad sworn to bring to his death? Everything about him was weird and\r\nmysterious; his personality, which he had so cunningly concealed, the\r\npower he wielded over nineteen English gentlemen who seemed to obey his\r\nevery command blindly and enthusiastically, the passionate love and\r\nsubmission he had roused in his little trained band, and, above all,\r\nhis marvellous audacity, the boundless impudence which had caused him\r\nto beard his most implacable enemies, within the very walls of Paris.\r\n\r\nNo wonder that in France the _sobriquet_ of the mysterious Englishman\r\nroused in the people a superstitious shudder. Chauvelin himself as he\r\ngazed round the deserted room, where presently the weird hero would\r\nappear, felt a strange feeling of awe creeping all down his spine.\r\n\r\nBut his plans were well laid. He felt sure that the Scarlet Pimpernel\r\nhad not been warned, and felt equally sure that Marguerite Blakeney had\r\nnot played him false. If she had . . . a cruel look, that would have\r\nmade her shudder, gleamed in Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s keen, pale eyes. If she had\r\nplayed him a trick, Armand St. Just would suffer the extreme penalty.\r\n\r\nBut no, no! of course she had not played him false!\r\n\r\nFortunately the supper-room was deserted: this would make Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntask all the easier, when presently that unsuspecting enigma would\r\nenter it alone. No one was here now save Chauvelin himself.\r\n\r\nStay! as he surveyed with a satisfied smile the solitude of the room,\r\nthe cunning agent of the French Government became aware of the\r\npeaceful, monotonous breathing of some one of my Lord Grenville\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nguests, who, no doubt, had supped both wisely and well, and was\r\nenjoying a quiet sleep, away from the din of the dancing above.\r\n\r\nChauvelin looked round once more, and there in the corner of a sofa, in\r\nthe dark angle of the room, his mouth open, his eyes shut, the sweet\r\nsounds of peaceful slumbers proceeding from his nostrils, reclined the\r\ngorgeously-apparelled, long-limbed husband of the cleverest woman in\r\nEurope.\r\n\r\nChauvelin looked at him as he lay there, placid, unconscious, at peace\r\nwith all the world and himself, after the best of suppers, and a smile,\r\nthat was almost one of pity, softened for a moment the hard lines of\r\nthe Frenchman\xe2\x80\x99s face and the sarcastic twinkle of his pale eyes.\r\n\r\nEvidently the slumberer, deep in dreamless sleep, would not interfere\r\nwith Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s trap for catching that cunning Scarlet Pimpernel.\r\nAgain he rubbed his hands together, and, following the example of Sir\r\nPercy Blakeney, he, too, stretched himself out in the corner of another\r\nsofa, shut his eyes, opened his mouth, gave forth sounds of peaceful\r\nbreathing, and . . . waited!\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XV.\r\nDOUBT\r\n\r\n\r\nMarguerite Blakeney had watched the slight sable-clad figure of\r\nChauvelin, as he worked his way through the ball-room. Then perforce\r\nshe had had to wait, while her nerves tingled with excitement.\r\n\r\nListlessly she sat in the small, still deserted boudoir, looking out\r\nthrough the curtained doorway on the dancing couples beyond: looking at\r\nthem, yet seeing nothing, hearing the music, yet conscious of naught\r\nsave a feeling of expectancy, of anxious, weary waiting.\r\n\r\nHer mind conjured up before her the vision of what was, perhaps at this\r\nvery moment, passing downstairs. The half-deserted dining-room, the\r\nfateful hour\xe2\x80\x94Chauvelin on the watch!\xe2\x80\x94then, precise to the moment, the\r\nentrance of a man, he, the Scarlet Pimpernel, the mysterious leader,\r\nwho to Marguerite had become almost unreal, so strange, so weird was\r\nthis hidden identity.\r\n\r\nShe wished she were in the supper-room, too, at this moment, watching\r\nhim as he entered; she knew that her woman\xe2\x80\x99s penetration would at once\r\nrecognise in the stranger\xe2\x80\x99s face\xe2\x80\x94whoever he might be\xe2\x80\x94that strong\r\nindividuality which belongs to a leader of men\xe2\x80\x94to a hero: to the\r\nmighty, high-soaring eagle, whose daring wings were becoming entangled\r\nin the ferret\xe2\x80\x99s trap.\r\n\r\nWoman-like, she thought of him with unmixed sadness; the irony of that\r\nfate seemed so cruel which allowed the fearless lion to succumb to the\r\ngnawing of a rat! Ah! had Armand\xe2\x80\x99s life not been at stake! . . .\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFaith! your ladyship must have thought me very remiss,\xe2\x80\x9d said a voice\r\nsuddenly, close to her elbow. \xe2\x80\x9cI had a deal of difficulty in delivering\r\nyour message, for I could not find Blakeney anywhere at first . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarguerite had forgotten all about her husband and her message to him;\r\nhis very name, as spoken by Lord Fancourt, sounded strange and\r\nunfamiliar to her, so completely had she in the last five minutes lived\r\nher old life in the Rue de Richelieu again, with Armand always near her\r\nto love and protect her, to guard her from the many subtle intrigues\r\nwhich were forever raging in Paris in those days.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did find him at last,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Lord Fancourt, \xe2\x80\x9cand gave him your\r\nmessage. He said that he would give orders at once for the horses to be\r\nput to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, still very absently, \xe2\x80\x9cyou found my husband, and gave\r\nhim my message?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; he was in the dining-room fast asleep. I could not manage to wake\r\nhim up at first.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you very much,\xe2\x80\x9d she said mechanically, trying to collect her\r\nthoughts.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill your ladyship honour me with the _contredanse_ until your coach\r\nis ready?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Lord Fancourt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I thank you, my lord, but\xe2\x80\x94and you will forgive me\xe2\x80\x94I really am too\r\ntired, and the heat in the ball-room has become oppressive.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe conservatory is deliciously cool; let me take you there, and then\r\nget you something. You seem ailing, Lady Blakeney.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am only very tired,\xe2\x80\x9d she repeated wearily, as she allowed Lord\r\nFancourt to lead her, where subdued lights and green plants lent\r\ncoolness to the air. He got her a chair, into which she sank. This long\r\ninterval of waiting was intolerable. Why did not Chauvelin come and\r\ntell her the result of his watch?\r\n\r\nLord Fancourt was very attentive. She scarcely heard what he said, and\r\nsuddenly startled him by asking abruptly,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLord Fancourt, did you perceive who was in the dining-room just now\r\nbesides Sir Percy Blakeney?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly the agent of the French Government, M. Chauvelin, equally fast\r\nasleep in another corner,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy does your ladyship ask?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know not . . . I . . . Did you notice the time when you were there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt must have been about five or ten minutes past one. . . . I wonder\r\nwhat your ladyship is thinking about,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, for evidently the fair\r\nlady\xe2\x80\x99s thoughts were very far away, and she had not been listening to\r\nhis intellectual conversation.\r\n\r\nBut indeed her thoughts were not very far away: only one storey below,\r\nin this same house, in the dining-room where sat Chauvelin still on the\r\nwatch. Had he failed? For one instant that possibility rose before her\r\nas a hope\xe2\x80\x94the hope that the Scarlet Pimpernel had been warned by Sir\r\nAndrew, and that Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s trap had failed to catch his bird; but\r\nthat hope soon gave way to fear. Had he failed? But then\xe2\x80\x94Armand!\r\n\r\nLord Fancourt had given up talking since he found that he had no\r\nlistener. He wanted an opportunity for slipping away: for sitting\r\nopposite to a lady, however fair, who is evidently not heeding the most\r\nvigorous efforts made for her entertainment, is not exhilarating, even\r\nto a Cabinet Minister.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall I find out if your ladyship\xe2\x80\x99s coach is ready,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last,\r\ntentatively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, thank you . . . thank you . . . if you would be so kind . . . I\r\nfear I am but sorry company . . . but I am really tired . . . and,\r\nperhaps, would be best alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n She had been longing to be rid of him, for she hoped that, like the\r\n fox he so resembled, Chauvelin would be prowling round, thinking to\r\n find her alone.\r\n\r\nBut Lord Fancourt went, and still Chauvelin did not come. Oh! what had\r\nhappened? She felt Armand\xe2\x80\x99s fate trembling in the balance . . . she\r\nfeared\xe2\x80\x94now with a deadly fear\xe2\x80\x94that Chauvelin _had_ failed, and that the\r\nmysterious Scarlet Pimpernel had proved elusive once more; then she\r\nknew that she need hope for no pity, no mercy, from him.\r\n\r\nHe had pronounced his \xe2\x80\x9cEither\xe2\x80\x94or\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d and nothing less would content him:\r\nhe was very spiteful, and would affect the belief that she had wilfully\r\nmisled him, and having failed to trap the eagle once again, his\r\nrevengeful mind would be content with the humble prey\xe2\x80\x94Armand!\r\n\r\nYet she had done her best; had strained every nerve for Armand\xe2\x80\x99s sake.\r\nShe could not bear to think that all had failed. She could not sit\r\nstill; she wanted to go and hear the worst at once; she wondered even\r\nthat Chauvelin had not come yet, to vent his wrath and satire upon her.\r\n\r\nLord Grenville himself came presently to tell her that her coach was\r\nready, and that Sir Percy was already waiting for her\xe2\x80\x94ribbons in hand.\r\nMarguerite said \xe2\x80\x9cFarewell\xe2\x80\x9d to her distinguished host; many of her\r\nfriends stopped her, as she crossed the rooms, to talk to her, and\r\nexchange pleasant _au revoirs_.\r\n\r\nThe Minister only took final leave of beautiful Lady Blakeney on the\r\ntop of the stairs; below, on the landing, a veritable army of gallant\r\ngentlemen were waiting to bid \xe2\x80\x9cGood-bye\xe2\x80\x9d to the queen of beauty and\r\nfashion, whilst outside, under the massive portico, Sir Percy\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmagnificent bays were impatiently pawing the ground.\r\n\r\nAt the top of the stairs, just after she had taken final leave of her\r\nhost, she suddenly saw Chauvelin; he was coming up the stairs slowly,\r\nand rubbing his thin hands very softly together.\r\n\r\nThere was a curious look on his mobile face, partly amused and wholly\r\npuzzled, and as his keen eyes met Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s they became strangely\r\nsarcastic.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cM. Chauvelin,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, as he stopped on the top of the stairs,\r\nbowing elaborately before her, \xe2\x80\x9cmy coach is outside; may I claim your\r\narm?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs gallant as ever, he offered her his arm and led her downstairs. The\r\ncrowd was very great, some of the Minister\xe2\x80\x99s guests were departing,\r\nothers were leaning against the banisters watching the throng as it\r\nfiled up and down the wide staircase.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChauvelin,\xe2\x80\x9d she said at last desperately, \xe2\x80\x9cI must know what has\r\nhappened.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat has happened, dear lady?\xe2\x80\x9d he said, with affected surprise.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere? When?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are torturing me, Chauvelin. I have helped you to-night . . .\r\nsurely I have the right to know. What happened in the dining-room at\r\none o\xe2\x80\x99clock just now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe spoke in a whisper, trusting that in the general hubbub of the\r\ncrowd her words would remain unheeded by all, save the man at her side.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuiet and peace reigned supreme, fair lady; at that hour I was asleep\r\nin the corner of one sofa and Sir Percy Blakeney in another.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNobody came into the room at all?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNobody.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen we have failed, you and I? . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes! we have failed\xe2\x80\x94perhaps . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut Armand?\xe2\x80\x9d she pleaded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! Armand St. Just\xe2\x80\x99s chances hang on a thread . . . pray heaven, dear\r\nlady, that that thread may not snap.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChauvelin, I worked for you, sincerely, earnestly . . . remember. . .\r\n.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI remember my promise,\xe2\x80\x9d he said quietly; \xe2\x80\x9cthe day that the Scarlet\r\nPimpernel and I meet on French soil, St. Just will be in the arms of\r\nhis charming sister.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich means that a brave man\xe2\x80\x99s blood will be on my hands,\xe2\x80\x9d she said,\r\nwith a shudder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis blood, or that of your brother. Surely at the present moment you\r\nmust hope, as I do, that the enigmatical Scarlet Pimpernel will start\r\nfor Calais to-day\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am only conscious of one hope, citoyen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd that is?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat Satan, your master, will have need of you elsewhere, before the\r\nsun rises to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou flatter me, citoyenne.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe had detained him for a while, midway down the stairs, trying to get\r\nat the thoughts which lay beyond that thin, fox-like mask. But\r\nChauvelin remained urbane, sarcastic, mysterious; not a line betrayed\r\nto the poor, anxious woman whether she need fear or whether she dared\r\nto hope.\r\n\r\nDownstairs on the landing she was soon surrounded. Lady Blakeney never\r\nstepped from any house into her coach, without an escort of fluttering\r\nhuman moths around the dazzling light of her beauty. But before she\r\nfinally turned away from Chauvelin, she held out a tiny hand to him,\r\nwith that pretty gesture of childish appeal which was so essentially\r\nher own.\r\n\r\n \xe2\x80\x9cGive me some hope, my little Chauvelin,\xe2\x80\x9d she pleaded.\r\n\r\nWith perfect gallantry he bowed over that tiny hand, which looked so\r\ndainty and white through the delicately transparent black lace mitten,\r\nand kissing the tips of the rosy fingers:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPray heaven that the thread may not snap,\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated, with his\r\nenigmatic smile.\r\n\r\nAnd stepping aside, he allowed the moths to flutter more closely round\r\nthe candle, and the brilliant throng of the _jeunesse dor\xc3\xa9e_, eagerly\r\nattentive to Lady Blakeney\xe2\x80\x99s every movement, hid the keen, fox-like\r\nface from her view.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVI.\r\nRICHMOND\r\n\r\n\r\nA few minutes later she was sitting, wrapped in costly furs, near Sir\r\nPercy Blakeney on the box-seat of his magnificent coach, and the four\r\nsplendid bays had thundered down the quiet street.\r\n\r\nThe night was warm in spite of the gentle breeze which fanned\r\nMarguerite\xe2\x80\x99s burning cheeks. Soon London houses were left behind, and\r\nrattling over old Hammersmith Bridge, Sir Percy was driving his bays\r\nrapidly towards Richmond.\r\n\r\nThe river wound in and out in its pretty delicate curves, looking like\r\na silver serpent beneath the glittering rays of the moon. Long shadows\r\nfrom overhanging trees spread occasional deep palls right across the\r\nroad. The bays were rushing along at breakneck speed, held but slightly\r\nback by Sir Percy\xe2\x80\x99s strong, unerring hands.\r\n\r\nThese nightly drives after balls and suppers in London were a source of\r\nperpetual delight to Marguerite, and she appreciated her husband\xe2\x80\x99s\r\neccentricity keenly, which caused him to adopt this mode of taking her\r\nhome every night, to their beautiful home by the river, instead of\r\nliving in a stuffy London house. He loved driving his spirited horses\r\nalong the lonely, moonlit roads, and she loved to sit on the box-seat,\r\nwith the soft air of an English late summer\xe2\x80\x99s night fanning her face\r\nafter the hot atmosphere of a ball or supper-party. The drive was not a\r\nlong one\xe2\x80\x94less than an hour, sometimes, when the bays were very fresh,\r\nand Sir Percy gave them full rein.\r\n\r\nTo-night he seemed to have a very devil in his fingers, and the coach\r\nseemed to fly along the road, beside the river. As usual, he did not\r\nspeak to her, but stared straight in front of him, the ribbons seeming\r\nto lie quite loosely in his slender, white hands. Marguerite looked at\r\nhim tentatively once or twice; she could see his handsome profile, and\r\none lazy eye, with its straight fine brow and drooping heavy lid.\r\n\r\nThe face in the moonlight looked singularly earnest, and recalled to\r\nMarguerite\xe2\x80\x99s aching heart those happy days of courtship, before he had\r\nbecome the lazy nincompoop, the effete fop, whose life seemed spent in\r\ncard and supper rooms.\r\n\r\nBut now, in the moonlight, she could not catch the expression of the\r\nlazy blue eyes; she could only see the outline of the firm chin, the\r\ncorner of the strong mouth, the well-cut massive shape of the forehead;\r\ntruly, nature had meant well by Sir Percy; his faults must all be laid\r\nat the door of that poor, half-crazy mother, and of the distracted\r\nheart-broken father, neither of whom had cared for the young life which\r\nwas sprouting up between them, and which, perhaps, their very\r\ncarelessness was already beginning to wreck.\r\n\r\nMarguerite suddenly felt intense sympathy for her husband. The moral\r\ncrisis she had just gone through made her feel indulgent towards the\r\nfaults, the delinquencies, of others.\r\n\r\nHow thoroughly a human being can be buffeted and overmastered by Fate,\r\nhad been borne in upon her with appalling force. Had anyone told her a\r\nweek ago that she would stoop to spy upon her friends, that she would\r\nbetray a brave and unsuspecting man into the hands of a relentless\r\nenemy, she would have laughed the idea to scorn.\r\n\r\nYet she had done these things; anon, perhaps the death of that brave\r\nman would be at her door, just as two years ago the Marquis de St. Cyr\r\nhad perished through a thoughtless word of hers; but in that case she\r\nwas morally innocent\xe2\x80\x94she had meant no serious harm\xe2\x80\x94fate merely had\r\nstepped in. But this time she had done a thing that obviously was base,\r\nhad done it deliberately, for a motive which, perhaps, high moralists\r\nwould not even appreciate.\r\n\r\nAnd as she felt her husband\xe2\x80\x99s strong arm beside her, she also felt how\r\nmuch more he would dislike and despise her, if he knew of this night\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwork. Thus human beings judge of one another, superficially, casually,\r\nthrowing contempt on one another, with but little reason, and no\r\ncharity. She despised her husband for his inanities and vulgar,\r\nunintellectual occupations; and he, she felt, would despise her still\r\nworse, because she had not been strong enough to do right for right\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsake, and to sacrifice her brother to the dictates of her conscience.\r\n\r\nBuried in her thoughts, Marguerite had found this hour in the breezy\r\nsummer night all too brief; and it was with a feeling of keen\r\ndisappointment, that she suddenly realised that the bays had turned\r\ninto the massive gates of her beautiful English home.\r\n\r\nSir Percy Blakeney\xe2\x80\x99s house on the river has become a historic one:\r\npalatial in its dimensions, it stands in the midst of exquisitely\r\nlaid-out gardens, with a picturesque terrace and frontage to the river.\r\nBuilt in Tudor days, the old red brick of the walls looks eminently\r\npicturesque in the midst of a bower of green, the beautiful lawn, with\r\nits old sun-dial, adding the true note of harmony to its foreground.\r\nGreat secular trees lent cool shadows to the grounds, and now, on this\r\nwarm early autumn night, the leaves slightly turned to russets and\r\ngold, the old garden looked singularly poetic and peaceful in the\r\nmoonlight.\r\n\r\nWith unerring precision, Sir Percy had brought the four bays to a\r\nstandstill immediately in front of the fine Elizabethan entrance hall;\r\nin spite of the lateness of the hour, an army of grooms seemed to have\r\nemerged from the very ground, as the coach had thundered up, and were\r\nstanding respectfully round.\r\n\r\nSir Percy jumped down quickly, then helped Marguerite to alight. She\r\nlingered outside for a moment, whilst he gave a few orders to one of\r\nhis men. She skirted the house, and stepped on to the lawn, looking out\r\ndreamily into the silvery landscape. Nature seemed exquisitely at\r\npeace, in comparison with the tumultuous emotions she had gone through:\r\nshe could faintly hear the ripple of the river and the occasional soft\r\nand ghostlike fall of a dead leaf from a tree.\r\n\r\nAll else was quiet round her. She had heard the horses prancing as they\r\nwere being led away to their distant stables, the hurrying of servants\xe2\x80\x99\r\nfeet as they had all gone within to rest: the house also was quite\r\nstill. In two separate suites of apartments, just above the magnificent\r\nreception-rooms, lights were still burning; they were her rooms, and\r\nhis, well divided from each other by the whole width of the house, as\r\nfar apart as their own lives had become. Involuntarily she sighed\xe2\x80\x94at\r\nthat moment she could really not have told why.\r\n\r\nShe was suffering from unconquerable heartache. Deeply and achingly she\r\nwas sorry for herself. Never had she felt so pitiably lonely, so\r\nbitterly in want of comfort and of sympathy. With another sigh she\r\nturned away from the river towards the house, vaguely wondering if,\r\nafter such a night, she could ever find rest and sleep.\r\n\r\nSuddenly, before she reached the terrace, she heard a firm step upon\r\nthe crisp gravel, and the next moment her husband\xe2\x80\x99s figure emerged out\r\nof the shadow. He, too, had skirted the house, and was wandering along\r\nthe lawn, towards the river. He still wore his heavy driving coat with\r\nthe numerous lapels and collars he himself had set in fashion, but he\r\nhad thrown it well back, burying his hands as was his wont, in the deep\r\npockets of his satin breeches: the gorgeous white costume he had worn\r\nat Lord Grenville\xe2\x80\x99s ball, with its jabot of priceless lace, looked\r\nstrangely ghostly against the dark background of the house.\r\n\r\nHe apparently did not notice her, for, after a few moments\xe2\x80\x99 pause, he\r\npresently turned back towards the house, and walked straight up to the\r\nterrace.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSir Percy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe already had one foot on the lowest of the terrace steps, but at her\r\nvoice he started, and paused, then looked searchingly into the shadows\r\nwhence she had called to him.\r\n\r\nShe came forward quickly into the moonlight, and, as soon as he saw\r\nher, he said, with that air of consummate gallantry he always wore when\r\nspeaking to her,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt your service, Madame!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n But his foot was still on the step, and in his whole attitude there\r\n was a remote suggestion, distinctly visible to her, that he wished to\r\n go, and had no desire for a midnight interview.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe air is deliciously cool,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cthe moonlight peaceful and\r\npoetic, and the garden inviting. Will you not stay in it awhile; the\r\nhour is not yet late, or is my company so distasteful to you, that you\r\nare in a hurry to rid yourself of it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, Madame,\xe2\x80\x9d he rejoined placidly, \xe2\x80\x9cbut \xe2\x80\x99tis on the other foot the\r\nshoe happens to be, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll warrant you\xe2\x80\x99ll find the midnight air more\r\npoetic without my company: no doubt the sooner I remove the obstruction\r\nthe better your ladyship will like it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe turned once more to go.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI protest you mistake me, Sir Percy,\xe2\x80\x9d she said hurriedly, and drawing\r\na little closer to him; \xe2\x80\x9cthe estrangement, which, alas! has arisen\r\nbetween us, was none of my making, remember.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBegad! you must pardon me there, Madame!\xe2\x80\x9d he protested coldly, \xe2\x80\x9cmy\r\nmemory was always of the shortest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe looked her straight in the eyes, with that lazy nonchalance which\r\nhad become second nature to him. She returned his gaze for a moment,\r\nthen her eyes softened, as she came up quite close to him, to the foot\r\nof the terrace steps.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf the shortest, Sir Percy? Faith! how it must have altered! Was it\r\nthree years ago or four that you saw me for one hour in Paris, on your\r\nway to the East? When you came back two years later you had not\r\nforgotten me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked divinely pretty as she stood there in the moonlight, with\r\nthe fur-cloak sliding off her beautiful shoulders, the gold embroidery\r\non her dress shimmering around her, her childlike blue eyes turned up\r\nfully at him.\r\n\r\nHe stood for a moment, rigid and still, but for the clenching of his\r\nhand against the stone balustrade of the terrace.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou desired my presence, Madame,\xe2\x80\x9d he said frigidly. \xe2\x80\x9cI take it that it\r\nwas not with a view to indulging in tender reminiscences.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis voice certainly was cold and uncompromising: his attitude before\r\nher, stiff and unbending. Womanly decorum would have suggested that\r\nMarguerite should return coldness for coldness, and should sweep past\r\nhim without another word, only with a curt nod of the head: but womanly\r\ninstinct suggested that she should remain\xe2\x80\x94that keen instinct, which\r\nmakes a beautiful woman conscious of her powers long to bring to her\r\nknees the one man who pays her no homage. She stretched out her hand to\r\nhim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, Sir Percy, why not? the present is not so glorious but that I\r\nshould not wish to dwell a little in the past.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe bent his tall figure, and taking hold of the extreme tip of the\r\nfingers which she still held out to him, he kissed them ceremoniously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99 faith, Madame,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cthen you will pardon me, if my dull wits\r\ncannot accompany you there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOnce again he attempted to go, once more her voice, sweet, childlike,\r\nalmost tender, called him back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSir Percy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour servant, Madame.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it possible that love can die?\xe2\x80\x9d she said with sudden, unreasoning\r\nvehemence. \xe2\x80\x9cMethought that the passion which you once felt for me would\r\noutlast the span of human life. Is there nothing left of that love,\r\nPercy . . . which might help you . . . to bridge over that sad\r\nestrangement?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis massive figure seemed, while she spoke thus to him, to stiffen\r\nstill more, the strong mouth hardened, a look of relentless obstinacy\r\ncrept into the habitually lazy blue eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith what object, I pray you, Madame?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked coldly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not understand you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYet \xe2\x80\x99tis simple enough,\xe2\x80\x9d he said with sudden bitterness, which seemed\r\nliterally to surge through his words, though he was making visible\r\nefforts to suppress it, \xe2\x80\x9cI humbly put the question to you, for my slow\r\nwits are unable to grasp the cause of this, your ladyship\xe2\x80\x99s sudden new\r\nmood. Is it that you have the taste to renew the devilish sport which\r\nyou played so successfully last year? Do you wish to see me once more a\r\nlove-sick suppliant at your feet, so that you might again have the\r\npleasure of kicking me aside, like a troublesome lap-dog?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe had succeeded in rousing him for the moment: and again she looked\r\nstraight at him, for it was thus she remembered him a year ago.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPercy! I entreat you!\xe2\x80\x9d she whispered, \xe2\x80\x9ccan we not bury the past?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPardon me, Madame, but I understood you to say that your desire was to\r\ndwell in it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay! I spoke not of _that_ past, Percy!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, while a tone of\r\ntenderness crept into her voice. \xe2\x80\x9cRather did I speak of the time when\r\nyou loved me still! and I . . . oh! I was vain and frivolous; your\r\nwealth and position allured me: I married you, hoping in my heart that\r\nyour great love for me would beget in me a love for you . . . but,\r\nalas! . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe moon had sunk low down behind a bank of clouds. In the east a soft\r\ngrey light was beginning to chase away the heavy mantle of the night.\r\nHe could only see her graceful outline now, the small queenly head,\r\nwith its wealth of reddish golden curls, and the glittering gems\r\nforming the small, star-shaped, red flower which she wore as a diadem\r\nin her hair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwenty-four hours after our marriage, Madame, the Marquis de St. Cyr\r\nand all his family perished on the guillotine, and the popular rumour\r\nreached me that it was the wife of Sir Percy Blakeney who helped to\r\nsend them there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay! I myself told you the truth of that odious tale.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot till after it had been recounted to me by strangers, with all its\r\nhorrible details.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you believed them then and there,\xe2\x80\x9d she said with great vehemence,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwithout a proof or question\xe2\x80\x94you believed that I, whom you vowed you\r\nloved more than life, whom you professed you worshipped, that _I_ could\r\ndo a thing so base as these _strangers_ chose to recount. You thought I\r\nmeant to deceive you about it all\xe2\x80\x94that I ought to have spoken before I\r\nmarried you: yet, had you listened, I would have told you that up to\r\nthe very morning on which St. Cyr went to the guillotine, I was\r\nstraining every nerve, using every influence I possessed, to save him\r\nand his family. But my pride sealed my lips, when your love seemed to\r\nperish, as if under the knife of that same guillotine. Yet I would have\r\ntold you how I was duped! Aye! I, whom that same popular rumour had\r\nendowed with the sharpest wits in France! I was tricked into doing this\r\nthing, by men who knew how to play upon my love for an only brother,\r\nand my desire for revenge. Was it unnatural?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer voice became choked with tears. She paused for a moment or two,\r\ntrying to regain some sort of composure. She looked appealingly at him,\r\nalmost as if he were her judge. He had allowed her to speak on in her\r\nown vehement, impassioned way, offering no comment, no word of\r\nsympathy: and now, while she paused, trying to swallow down the hot\r\ntears that gushed to her eyes, he waited, impassive and still. The dim,\r\ngrey light of early dawn seemed to make his tall form look taller and\r\nmore rigid. The lazy, good-natured face looked strangely altered.\r\nMarguerite, excited, as she was, could see that the eyes were no longer\r\nlanguid, the mouth no longer good-humoured and inane. A curious look of\r\nintense passion seemed to glow from beneath his drooping lids, the\r\nmouth was tightly closed, the lips compressed, as if the will alone\r\nheld that surging passion in check.\r\n\r\nMarguerite Blakeney was, above all, a woman, with all a woman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfascinating foibles, all a woman\xe2\x80\x99s most lovable sins. She knew in a\r\nmoment that for the past few months she had been mistaken: that this\r\nman who stood here before her, cold as a statue, when her musical voice\r\nstruck upon his ear, loved her, as he had loved her a year ago: that\r\nhis passion might have been dormant, but that it was there, as strong,\r\nas intense, as overwhelming, as when first her lips met his in one\r\nlong, maddening kiss.\r\n\r\n Pride had kept him from her, and, woman-like, she meant to win back\r\n that conquest which had been hers before. Suddenly it seemed to her\r\n that the only happiness life could ever hold for her again would be in\r\n feeling that man\xe2\x80\x99s kiss once more upon her lips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cListen to the tale, Sir Percy,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, and her voice now was low,\r\nsweet, infinitely tender. \xe2\x80\x9cArmand was all in all to me! We had no\r\nparents, and brought one another up. He was my little father, and I,\r\nhis tiny mother; we loved one another so. Then one day\xe2\x80\x94do you mind me,\r\nSir Percy? the Marquis de St. Cyr had my brother Armand\r\nthrashed\xe2\x80\x94thrashed by his lacqueys\xe2\x80\x94that brother whom I loved better than\r\nall the world! And his offence? That he, a plebeian, had dared to love\r\nthe daughter of the aristocrat; for that he was waylaid and thrashed .\r\n. . thrashed like a dog within an inch of his life! Oh, how I suffered!\r\nhis humiliation had eaten into my very soul! When the opportunity\r\noccurred, and I was able to take my revenge, I took it. But I only\r\nthought to bring that proud marquis to trouble and humiliation. He\r\nplotted with Austria against his own country. Chance gave me knowledge\r\nof this; I spoke of it, but I did not know\xe2\x80\x94how could I guess?\xe2\x80\x94they\r\ntrapped and duped me. When I realised what I had done, it was too\r\nlate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is perhaps a little difficult, Madame,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sir Percy, after a\r\nmoment of silence between them, \xe2\x80\x9cto go back over the past. I have\r\nconfessed to you that my memory is short, but the thought certainly\r\nlingered in my mind that, at the time of the Marquis\xe2\x80\x99 death, I\r\nentreated you for an explanation of those same noisome popular rumours.\r\nIf that same memory does not, even now, play me a trick, I fancy that\r\nyou refused me _all_ explanation then, and demanded of my love a\r\nhumiliating allegiance it was not prepared to give.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wished to test your love for me, and it did not bear the test. You\r\nused to tell me that you drew the very breath of life but for me, and\r\nfor love of me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd to probe that love, you demanded that I should forfeit mine\r\nhonour,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, whilst gradually his impassiveness seemed to leave\r\nhim, his rigidity to relax; \xe2\x80\x9cthat I should accept without murmur or\r\nquestion, as a dumb and submissive slave, every action of my mistress.\r\nMy heart overflowing with love and passion, I _asked_ for no\r\nexplanation\xe2\x80\x94I _waited_ for one, not doubting\xe2\x80\x94only hoping. Had you\r\nspoken but one word, from you I would have accepted any explanation and\r\nbelieved it. But you left me without a word, beyond a bald confession\r\nof the actual horrible facts; proudly you returned to your brother\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhouse, and left me alone . . . for weeks . . . not knowing, now, in\r\nwhom to believe, since the shrine, which contained my one illusion, lay\r\nshattered to earth at my feet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe need not complain now that he was cold and impassive; his very\r\nvoice shook with an intensity of passion, which he was making\r\nsuperhuman efforts to keep in check.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye! the madness of my pride!\xe2\x80\x9d she said sadly. \xe2\x80\x9cHardly had I gone,\r\nalready I had repented. But when I returned, I found you, oh, so\r\naltered! wearing already that mask of somnolent indifference which you\r\nhave never laid aside until . . . until now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was so close to him that her soft, loose hair was wafted against\r\nhis cheek; her eyes, glowing with tears, maddened him, the music in her\r\nvoice sent fire through his veins. But he would not yield to the magic\r\ncharm of this woman whom he had so deeply loved, and at whose hands his\r\npride had suffered so bitterly. He closed his eyes to shut out the\r\ndainty vision of that sweet face, of that snow-white neck and graceful\r\nfigure, round which the faint rosy light of dawn was just beginning to\r\nhover playfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, Madame, it is no mask,\xe2\x80\x9d he said icily; \xe2\x80\x9cI swore to you . . .\r\nonce, that my life was yours. For months now it has been your plaything\r\n. . . it has served its purpose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut now she knew that that very coldness was a mask. The trouble, the\r\nsorrow she had gone through last night, suddenly came back to her mind,\r\nbut no longer with bitterness, rather with a feeling that this man who\r\nloved her, would help her to bear the burden.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSir Percy,\xe2\x80\x9d she said impulsively, \xe2\x80\x9cHeaven knows you have been at pains\r\nto make the task, which I had set to myself, terribly difficult to\r\naccomplish. You spoke of my mood just now; well! we will call it that,\r\nif you will. I wished to speak to you . . . because . . . because I was\r\nin trouble . . . and had need . . . of your sympathy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is yours to command, Madame.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow cold you are!\xe2\x80\x9d she sighed. \xe2\x80\x9cFaith! I can scarce believe that but a\r\nfew months ago one tear in my eye had set you well-nigh crazy. Now I\r\ncome to you . . . with a half-broken heart . . . and . . . and . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI pray you, Madame,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, whilst his voice shook almost as much as\r\nhers, \xe2\x80\x9cin what way can I serve you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPercy!\xe2\x80\x94Armand is in deadly danger. A letter of his . . . rash,\r\nimpetuous, as were all his actions, and written to Sir Andrew Ffoulkes,\r\nhas fallen into the hands of a fanatic. Armand is hopelessly\r\ncompromised . . . to-morrow, perhaps he will be arrested . . . after\r\nthat the guillotine . . . unless . . . unless . . . oh! it is\r\nhorrible!\xe2\x80\x9d . . . she said, with a sudden wail of anguish, as all the\r\nevents of the past night came rushing back to her mind, \xe2\x80\x9chorrible! . .\r\n. and you do not understand . . . you cannot . . . and I have no one to\r\nwhom I can turn . . . for help . . . or even for sympathy. . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTears now refused to be held back. All her trouble, her struggles, the\r\nawful uncertainty of Armand\xe2\x80\x99s fate overwhelmed her. She tottered, ready\r\nto fall, and leaning against the stone balustrade, she buried her face\r\nin her hands and sobbed bitterly.\r\n\r\nAt first mention of Armand St. Just\xe2\x80\x99s name and of the peril in which he\r\nstood, Sir Percy\xe2\x80\x99s face had become a shade more pale; and the look of\r\ndetermination and obstinacy appeared more marked than ever between his\r\neyes. However, he said nothing for the moment, but watched her, as her\r\ndelicate frame was shaken with sobs, watched her until unconsciously\r\nhis face softened, and what looked almost like tears seemed to glisten\r\nin his eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd so,\xe2\x80\x9d he said with bitter sarcasm, \xe2\x80\x9cthe murderous dog of the\r\nrevolution is turning upon the very hands that fed it? . . . Begad,\r\nMadame,\xe2\x80\x9d he added very gently, as Marguerite continued to sob\r\nhysterically, \xe2\x80\x9cwill you dry your tears? . . . I never could bear to see\r\na pretty woman cry, and I . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nInstinctively, with sudden, overmastering passion, at sight of her\r\nhelplessness and of her grief, he stretched out his arms, and the next,\r\nwould have seized her and held her to him, protected from every evil\r\nwith his very life, his very heart\xe2\x80\x99s blood. . . . But pride had the\r\nbetter of it in this struggle once again; he restrained himself with a\r\ntremendous effort of will, and said coldly, though still very gently,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you not turn to me, Madame, and tell me in what way I may have\r\nthe honour to serve you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe made a violent effort to control herself, and turning her\r\ntear-stained face to him, she once more held out her hand, which he\r\nkissed with the same punctilious gallantry; but Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s fingers,\r\nthis time, lingered in his hand for a second or two longer than was\r\nabsolutely necessary, and this was because she had felt that his hand\r\ntrembled perceptibly and was burning hot, whilst his lips felt as cold\r\nas marble.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan you do aught for Armand?\xe2\x80\x9d she said sweetly and simply. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have\r\nso much influence at court . . . so many friends . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, Madame, should you not rather seek the influence of your French\r\nfriend, M. Chauvelin? His extends, if I mistake not, even as far as the\r\nRepublican Government of France.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot ask him, Percy. . . . Oh! I wish I dared to tell you . . .\r\nbut . . . but . . . he has put a price on my brother\xe2\x80\x99s head, which . .\r\n.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe would have given worlds if she had felt the courage then to tell\r\nhim everything . . . all she had done that night\xe2\x80\x94how she had suffered\r\nand how her hand had been forced. But she dared not give way to that\r\nimpulse . . . not now, when she was just beginning to feel that he\r\nstill loved her, when she hoped that she could win him back. She dared\r\nnot make another confession to him. After all, he might not understand;\r\nhe might not sympathise with her struggles and temptation. His love\r\nstill dormant might sleep the sleep of death.\r\n\r\nPerhaps he divined what was passing in her mind. His whole attitude was\r\none of intense longing\xe2\x80\x94a veritable prayer for that confidence, which\r\nher foolish pride withheld from him. When she remained silent he\r\nsighed, and said with marked coldness\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFaith, Madame, since it distresses you, we will not speak of it. . . .\r\nAs for Armand, I pray you have no fear. I pledge you my word that he\r\nshall be safe. Now, have I your permission to go? The hour is getting\r\nlate, and . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will at least accept my gratitude?\xe2\x80\x9d she said, as she drew quite\r\nclose to him, and speaking with real tenderness.\r\n\r\nWith a quick, almost involuntary effort he would have taken her then in\r\nhis arms, for her eyes were swimming in tears, which he longed to kiss\r\naway; but she had lured him once, just like this, then cast him aside\r\nlike an ill-fitting glove. He thought this was but a mood, a caprice,\r\nand he was too proud to lend himself to it once again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is too soon, Madame!\xe2\x80\x9d he said quietly; \xe2\x80\x9cI have done nothing as yet.\r\nThe hour is late, and you must be fatigued. Your women will be waiting\r\nfor you upstairs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe stood aside to allow her to pass. She sighed, a quick sigh of\r\ndisappointment. His pride and her beauty had been in direct conflict,\r\nand his pride had remained the conqueror. Perhaps, after all, she had\r\nbeen deceived just now; what she took to be the light of love in his\r\neyes might only have been the passion of pride or, who knows, of hatred\r\ninstead of love. She stood looking at him for a moment or two longer.\r\nHe was again as rigid, as impassive, as before. Pride had conquered,\r\nand he cared naught for her. The grey of dawn was gradually yielding to\r\nthe rosy light of the rising sun. Birds began to twitter; Nature\r\nawakened, smiling in happy response to the warmth of this glorious\r\nOctober morning. Only between these two hearts there lay a strong,\r\nimpassable barrier, built up of pride on both sides, which neither of\r\nthem cared to be the first to demolish.\r\n\r\nHe had bent his tall figure in a low ceremonious bow, as she finally,\r\nwith another bitter little sigh, began to mount the terrace steps.\r\n\r\nThe long train of her gold-embroidered gown swept the dead leaves off\r\nthe steps, making a faint harmonious sh\xe2\x80\x94sh\xe2\x80\x94sh as she glided up, with\r\none hand resting on the balustrade, the rosy light of dawn making an\r\naureole of gold round her hair, and causing the rubies on her head and\r\narms to sparkle. She reached the tall glass doors which led into the\r\nhouse. Before entering, she paused once again to look at him, hoping\r\nagainst hope to see his arms stretched out to her, and to hear his\r\nvoice calling her back. But he had not moved; his massive figure looked\r\nthe very personification of unbending pride, of fierce obstinacy.\r\n\r\nHot tears again surged to her eyes, and as she would not let him see\r\nthem, she turned quickly within, and ran as fast as she could up to her\r\nown rooms.\r\n\r\nHad she but turned back then, and looked out once more on to the\r\nrose-lit garden, she would have seen that which would have made her own\r\nsufferings seem but light and easy to bear\xe2\x80\x94a strong man, overwhelmed\r\nwith his own passion and his own despair. Pride had given way at last,\r\nobstinacy was gone: the will was powerless. He was but a man madly,\r\nblindly, passionately in love, and as soon as her light footsteps had\r\ndied away within the house, he knelt down upon the terrace steps, and\r\nin the very madness of his love he kissed one by one the places where\r\nher small foot had trodden, and the stone balustrade there, where her\r\ntiny hand had rested last.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVII.\r\nFAREWELL\r\n\r\n\r\nWhen Marguerite reached her room, she found her maid terribly anxious\r\nabout her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour ladyship will be so tired,\xe2\x80\x9d said the poor woman, whose own eyes\r\nwere half closed with sleep. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is past five o\xe2\x80\x99clock.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, yes, Louise, I daresay I shall be tired presently,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMarguerite, kindly; \xe2\x80\x9cbut you are very tired now, so go to bed at once.\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll get into bed alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, my lady . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, don\xe2\x80\x99t argue, Louise, but go to bed. Give me a wrap, and leave me\r\nalone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLouise was only too glad to obey. She took off her mistress\xe2\x80\x99s gorgeous\r\nball-dress, and wrapped her up in a soft billowy gown.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes your ladyship wish for anything else?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, when that was\r\ndone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, nothing more. Put out the lights as you go out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, my lady. Good-night, my lady.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-night, Louise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen the maid was gone, Marguerite drew aside the curtains and threw\r\nopen the windows. The garden and the river beyond were flooded with\r\nrosy light. Far away to the east, the rays of the rising sun had\r\nchanged the rose into vivid gold. The lawn was deserted now, and\r\nMarguerite looked down upon the terrace where she had stood a few\r\nmoments ago trying vainly to win back a man\xe2\x80\x99s love, which once had been\r\nso wholly hers.\r\n\r\nIt was strange that through all her troubles, all her anxiety for\r\nArmand, she was mostly conscious at the present moment of a keen and\r\nbitter heartache.\r\n\r\nHer very limbs seemed to ache with longing for the love of a man who\r\nhad spurned her, who had resisted her tenderness, remained cold to her\r\nappeals, and had not responded to the glow of passion, which had caused\r\nher to feel and hope that those happy olden days in Paris were not all\r\ndead and forgotten.\r\n\r\nHow strange it all was! She loved him still. And now that she looked\r\nback upon the last few months of misunderstandings and of loneliness,\r\nshe realised that she had never ceased to love him; that deep down in\r\nher heart she had always vaguely felt that his foolish inanities, his\r\nempty laugh, his lazy nonchalance were nothing but a mask; that the\r\nreal man, strong, passionate, wilful, was there still\xe2\x80\x94the man she had\r\nloved, whose intensity had fascinated her, whose personality attracted\r\nher, since she always felt that behind his apparently slow wits there\r\nwas a certain something, which he kept hidden from all the world, and\r\nmost especially from her.\r\n\r\nA woman\xe2\x80\x99s heart is such a complex problem\xe2\x80\x94the owner thereof is often\r\nmost incompetent to find the solution of this puzzle.\r\n\r\nDid Marguerite Blakeney, \xe2\x80\x9cthe cleverest woman in Europe,\xe2\x80\x9d really love a\r\nfool? Was it love that she had felt for him a year ago when she married\r\nhim? Was it love she felt for him now that she realised that he still\r\nloved her, but that he would not become her slave, her passionate,\r\nardent lover once again? Nay! Marguerite herself could not have told\r\nthat. Not at this moment at any rate; perhaps her pride had sealed her\r\nmind against a better understanding of her own heart. But this she did\r\nknow\xe2\x80\x94that she meant to capture that obstinate heart back again. That\r\nshe would conquer once more . . . and then, that she would never lose\r\nhim . . . . She would keep him, keep his love, deserve it, and cherish\r\nit; for this much was certain, that there was no longer any happiness\r\npossible for her without that one man\xe2\x80\x99s love.\r\n\r\nThus the most contradictory thoughts and emotions rushed madly through\r\nher mind. Absorbed in them, she had allowed time to slip by; perhaps,\r\ntired out with long excitement, she had actually closed her eyes and\r\nsunk into a troubled sleep, wherein quickly fleeting dreams seemed but\r\nthe continuation of her anxious thoughts\xe2\x80\x94when suddenly she was roused,\r\nfrom dream or meditation, by the noise of footsteps outside her door.\r\n\r\nNervously she jumped up and listened; the house itself was as still as\r\never; the footsteps had retreated. Through her wide-open windows the\r\nbrilliant rays of the morning sun were flooding her room with light.\r\nShe looked up at the clock; it was half-past six\xe2\x80\x94too early for any of\r\nthe household to be already astir.\r\n\r\nShe certainly must have dropped asleep, quite unconsciously. The noise\r\nof the footsteps, also of hushed, subdued voices had awakened her\xe2\x80\x94what\r\ncould they be?\r\n\r\nGently, on tip-toe, she crossed the room and opened the door to listen;\r\nnot a sound\xe2\x80\x94that peculiar stillness of the early morning when sleep\r\nwith all mankind is at its heaviest. But the noise had made her\r\nnervous, and when, suddenly, at her feet, on the very doorstep, she saw\r\nsomething white lying there\xe2\x80\x94a letter evidently\xe2\x80\x94she hardly dared touch\r\nit. It seemed so ghostlike. It certainly was not there when she came\r\nupstairs; had Louise dropped it? or was some tantalising spook at play,\r\nshowing her fairy letters where none existed?\r\n\r\nAt last she stooped to pick it up, and, amazed, puzzled beyond measure,\r\nshe saw that the letter was addressed to herself in her husband\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nlarge, businesslike-looking hand. What could he have to say to her, in\r\nthe middle of the night, which could not be put off until the morning?\r\n\r\nShe tore open the envelope and read:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA most unforeseen circumstance forces me to leave for the North\r\nimmediately, so I beg your ladyship\xe2\x80\x99s pardon if I do not avail myself\r\nof the honour of bidding you good-bye. My business may keep me employed\r\nfor about a week, so I shall not have the privilege of being present at\r\nyour ladyship\xe2\x80\x99s water-party on Wednesday. I remain your ladyship\xe2\x80\x99s most\r\nhumble and most obedient servant,\r\n\r\n\r\nPERCY BLAKENEY.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nMarguerite must suddenly have been imbued with her husband\xe2\x80\x99s slowness\r\nof intellect, for she had perforce to read the few simple lines over\r\nand over again, before she could fully grasp their meaning.\r\n\r\nShe stood on the landing, turning over and over in her hand this curt\r\nand mysterious epistle, her mind a blank, her nerves strained with\r\nagitation and a presentiment she could not very well have explained.\r\n\r\nSir Percy owned considerable property in the North, certainly, and he\r\nhad often before gone there alone and stayed away a week at a time; but\r\nit seemed so very strange that circumstances should have arisen between\r\nfive and six o\xe2\x80\x99clock in the morning that compelled him to start in this\r\nextreme hurry.\r\n\r\nVainly she tried to shake off an unaccustomed feeling of nervousness:\r\nshe was trembling from head to foot. A wild, unconquerable desire\r\nseized her to see her husband again, at once, if only he had not\r\nalready started.\r\n\r\nForgetting the fact that she was only very lightly clad in a morning\r\nwrap, and that her hair lay loosely about her shoulders, she flew down\r\nthe stairs, right through the hall towards the front door.\r\n\r\nIt was as usual barred and bolted, for the indoor servants were not yet\r\nup; but her keen ears had detected the sound of voices and the pawing\r\nof a horse\xe2\x80\x99s hoof against the flag-stones.\r\n\r\nWith nervous, trembling fingers Marguerite undid the bolts one by one,\r\nbruising her hands, hurting her nails, for the locks were heavy and\r\nstiff. But she did not care; her whole frame shook with anxiety at the\r\nvery thought that she might be too late; that he might have gone\r\nwithout her seeing him and bidding him \xe2\x80\x9cGod-speed!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt last, she had turned the key and thrown open the door. Her ears had\r\nnot deceived her. A groom was standing close by holding a couple of\r\nhorses; one of these was Sultan, Sir Percy\xe2\x80\x99s favourite and swiftest\r\nhorse, saddled ready for a journey.\r\n\r\nThe next moment Sir Percy himself appeared round the further corner of\r\nthe house and came quickly towards the horses. He had changed his\r\ngorgeous ball costume, but was as usual irreproachably and richly\r\napparelled in a suit of fine cloth, with lace jabot and ruffles, high\r\ntop-boots, and riding breeches.\r\n\r\nMarguerite went forward a few steps. He looked up and saw her. A slight\r\nfrown appeared between his eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are going?\xe2\x80\x9d she said quickly and feverishly. \xe2\x80\x9cWhither?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs I have had the honour of informing your ladyship, urgent, most\r\nunexpected business calls me to the North this morning,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, in\r\nhis usual cold, drawly manner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut . . . your guests to-morrow . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have prayed your ladyship to offer my humble excuses to His Royal\r\nHighness. You are such a perfect hostess, I do not think that I shall\r\nbe missed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut surely you might have waited for your journey . . . until after\r\nour water-party . . .\xe2\x80\x9d she said, still speaking quickly and nervously.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSurely the business is not so urgent . . . and you said nothing about\r\nit\xe2\x80\x94just now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy business, as I had the honour to tell you, Madame, is as unexpected\r\nas it is urgent. . . . May I therefore crave your permission to\r\ngo. . . . Can I do aught for you in town? . . . on my way back?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo . . . no . . . thanks . . . nothing. . . . But you will be back\r\nsoon?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery soon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBefore the end of the week?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was evidently trying to get away, whilst she was straining every\r\nnerve to keep him back for a moment or two.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPercy,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cwill you not tell me why you go to-day? Surely I,\r\nas your wife, have the right to know. You have _not_ been called away\r\nto the North. I know it. There were no letters, no couriers from there\r\nbefore we left for the opera last night, and nothing was waiting for\r\nyou when we returned from the ball. . . . You are _not_ going to the\r\nNorth, I feel convinced. . . . There is some mystery . . . and . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, there is no mystery, Madame,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied, with a slight tone of\r\nimpatience. \xe2\x80\x9cMy business has to do with Armand . . . there! Now, have I\r\nyour leave to depart?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith Armand? . . . But you will run no danger?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDanger? I? . . . Nay, Madame, your solicitude does me honour. As you\r\nsay, I have some influence; my intention is to exert it before it be\r\ntoo late.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you allow me to thank you at least?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, Madame,\xe2\x80\x9d he said coldly, \xe2\x80\x9cthere is no need for that. My life is\r\nat your service, and I am already more than repaid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd mine will be at yours, Sir Percy, if you will but accept it, in\r\nexchange for what you do for Armand,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, as, impulsively, she\r\nstretched out both her hands to him. \xe2\x80\x9cThere! I will not detain you . .\r\n. my thoughts go with you . . . Farewell! . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHow lovely she looked in this morning sunlight, with her ardent hair\r\nstreaming around her shoulders. He bowed very low and kissed her hand;\r\nshe felt the burning kiss and her heart thrilled with joy and hope.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will come back?\xe2\x80\x9d she said tenderly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery soon!\xe2\x80\x9d he replied, looking longingly into her blue eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd . . . you will remember? . . .\xe2\x80\x9d she asked as her eyes, in response\r\nto his look, gave him an infinity of promise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will always remember, Madame, that you have honoured me by\r\ncommanding my services.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe words were cold and formal, but they did not chill her this time.\r\nHer woman\xe2\x80\x99s heart had read his, beneath the impassive mask his pride\r\nstill forced him to wear.\r\n\r\nHe bowed to her again, then begged her leave to depart. She stood on\r\none side whilst he jumped on to Sultan\xe2\x80\x99s back, then, as he galloped out\r\nof the gates, she waved him a final \xe2\x80\x9cAdieu.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA bend in the road soon hid him from view; his confidential groom had\r\nsome difficulty in keeping pace with him, for Sultan flew along in\r\nresponse to his master\xe2\x80\x99s excited mood. Marguerite, with a sigh that was\r\nalmost a happy one, turned and went within. She went back to her room,\r\nfor suddenly, like a tired child, she felt quite sleepy.\r\n\r\nHer heart seemed all at once to be in complete peace, and, though it\r\nstill ached with undefined longing, a vague and delicious hope soothed\r\nit as with a balm.\r\n\r\nShe felt no longer anxious about Armand. The man who had just ridden\r\naway, bent on helping her brother, inspired her with complete\r\nconfidence in his strength and in his power. She marvelled at herself\r\nfor having ever looked upon him as an inane fool; of course, _that_ was\r\na mask worn to hide the bitter wound she had dealt to his faith and to\r\nhis love. His passion would have overmastered him, and he would not let\r\nher see how much he still cared and how deeply he suffered.\r\n\r\nBut now all would be well: she would crush her own pride, humble it\r\nbefore him, tell him everything, trust him in everything; and those\r\nhappy days would come back, when they used to wander off together in\r\nthe forests of Fontainebleau, when they spoke little\xe2\x80\x94for he was always\r\na silent man\xe2\x80\x94but when she felt that against that strong heart she would\r\nalways find rest and happiness.\r\n\r\nThe more she thought of the events of the past night, the less fear had\r\nshe of Chauvelin and his schemes. He had failed to discover the\r\nidentity of the Scarlet Pimpernel, of that she felt sure. Both Lord\r\nFancourt and Chauvelin himself had assured her that no one had been in\r\nthe dining-room at one o\xe2\x80\x99clock except the Frenchman himself and\r\nPercy\xe2\x80\x94Yes!\xe2\x80\x94Percy! she might have asked him, had she thought of it!\r\nAnyway, she had no fears that the unknown and brave hero would fall in\r\nChauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s trap; his death at any rate would not be at her door.\r\n\r\nArmand certainly was still in danger, but Percy had pledged his word\r\nthat Armand would be safe, and somehow, as Marguerite had seen him\r\nriding away, the possibility that he could fail in whatever he\r\nundertook never even remotely crossed her mind. When Armand was safely\r\nover in England she would not allow him to go back to France.\r\n\r\nShe felt almost happy now, and, drawing the curtains closely together\r\nagain to shut out the piercing sun, she went to bed at last, laid her\r\nhead upon the pillow, and, like a wearied child, soon fell into a\r\npeaceful and dreamless sleep.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVIII.\r\nTHE MYSTERIOUS DEVICE\r\n\r\n\r\nThe day was well advanced when Marguerite woke, refreshed by her long\r\nsleep. Louise had brought her some fresh milk and a dish of fruit, and\r\nshe partook of this frugal breakfast with hearty appetite.\r\n\r\nThoughts crowded thick and fast in her mind as she munched her grapes;\r\nmost of them went galloping away after the tall, erect figure of her\r\nhusband, whom she had watched riding out of sight more than five hours\r\nago.\r\n\r\nIn answer to her eager inquiries, Louise brought back the news that the\r\ngroom had come home with Sultan, having left Sir Percy in London. The\r\ngroom thought that his master was about to get on board his schooner,\r\nwhich was lying off just below London Bridge. Sir Percy had ridden thus\r\nfar, had then met Briggs, the skipper of the _Day Dream_, and had sent\r\nthe groom back to Richmond with Sultan and the empty saddle.\r\n\r\nThis news puzzled Marguerite more than ever. Where could Sir Percy be\r\ngoing just now in the _Day Dream_? On Armand\xe2\x80\x99s behalf, he had said.\r\nWell! Sir Percy had influential friends everywhere. Perhaps he was\r\ngoing to Greenwich, or . . . but Marguerite ceased to conjecture; all\r\nwould be explained anon: he said that he would come back, and that he\r\nwould remember.\r\n A long, idle day lay before Marguerite. She was expecting the visit of\r\n her old school-fellow, little Suzanne de Tournay. With all the merry\r\n mischief at her command, she had tendered her request for Suzanne\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n company to the Comtesse in the presence of the Prince of Wales last\r\n night. His Royal Highness had loudly applauded the notion, and\r\n declared that he would give himself the pleasure of calling on the two\r\n ladies in the course of the afternoon. The Comtesse had not dared to\r\n refuse, and then and there was entrapped into a promise to send little\r\n Suzanne to spend a long and happy day at Richmond with her friend.\r\n\r\nMarguerite expected her eagerly; she longed for a chat about old\r\nschooldays with the child; she felt that she would prefer Suzanne\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncompany to that of anyone else, and together they would roam through\r\nthe fine old garden and rich deer park, or stroll along the river.\r\n\r\nBut Suzanne had not come yet, and Marguerite being dressed, prepared to\r\ngo downstairs. She looked quite a girl this morning in her simple\r\nmuslin frock, with a broad blue sash round her slim waist, and the\r\ndainty cross-over fichu into which, at her bosom, she had fastened a\r\nfew late crimson roses.\r\n\r\nShe crossed the landing outside her own suite of apartments, and stood\r\nstill for a moment at the head of the fine oak staircase, which led to\r\nthe lower floor. On her left were her husband\xe2\x80\x99s apartments, a suite of\r\nrooms which she practically never entered.\r\n\r\nThey consisted of bedroom, dressing and reception-room, and, at the\r\nextreme end of the landing, of a small study, which, when Sir Percy did\r\nnot use it, was always kept locked. His own special and confidential\r\nvalet, Frank, had charge of this room. No one was ever allowed to go\r\ninside. My lady had never cared to do so, and the other servants had,\r\nof course, not dared to break this hard-and-fast rule.\r\n\r\nMarguerite had often, with that good-natured contempt which she had\r\nrecently adopted towards her husband, chaffed him about this secrecy\r\nwhich surrounded his private study. Laughingly she had always declared\r\nthat he strictly excluded all prying eyes from his sanctum for fear\r\nthey should detect how very little \xe2\x80\x9cstudy\xe2\x80\x9d went on within its four\r\nwalls: a comfortable arm-chair for Sir Percy\xe2\x80\x99s sweet slumbers was, no\r\ndoubt, its most conspicuous piece of furniture.\r\n\r\nMarguerite thought of all this on this bright October morning as she\r\nglanced along the corridor.
