Frank was evidently busy with his master\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nrooms, for most of the doors stood open, that of the study amongst the\r\nothers.\r\n\r\nA sudden, burning, childish curiosity seized her to have a peep at Sir\r\nPercy\xe2\x80\x99s sanctum. The restriction, of course, did not apply to her, and\r\nFrank would, of course, not dare to oppose her. Still, she hoped that\r\nthe valet would be busy in one of the other rooms, that she might have\r\nthat one quick peep in secret, and unmolested.\r\n\r\nGently, on tip-toe, she crossed the landing and, like Blue Beard\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwife, trembling half with excitement and wonder, she paused a moment on\r\nthe threshold, strangely perturbed and irresolute.\r\n\r\nThe door was ajar, and she could not see anything within. She pushed it\r\nopen tentatively: there was no sound: Frank was evidently not there,\r\nand she walked boldly in.\r\n\r\nAt once she was struck by the severe simplicity of everything around\r\nher: the dark and heavy hangings, the massive oak furniture, the one or\r\ntwo maps on the wall, in no way recalled to her mind the lazy man about\r\ntown, the lover of race-courses, the dandified leader of fashion, that\r\nwas the outward representation of Sir Percy Blakeney.\r\n\r\nThere was no sign here, at any rate, of hurried departure. Everything\r\nwas in its place, not a scrap of paper littered the floor, not a\r\ncupboard or drawer was left open. The curtains were drawn aside, and\r\nthrough the open window the fresh morning air was streaming in.\r\n\r\nFacing the window, and well into the centre of the room, stood a\r\nponderous business-like desk, which looked as if it had seen much\r\nservice. On the wall to the left of the desk, reaching almost from\r\nfloor to ceiling, was a large full-length portrait of a woman,\r\nmagnificently framed, exquisitely painted, and signed with the name of\r\nBoucher. It was Percy\xe2\x80\x99s mother.\r\n\r\nMarguerite knew very little about her, except that she had died abroad,\r\nailing in body as well as in mind, when Percy was still a lad. She must\r\nhave been a very beautiful woman once, when Boucher painted her, and as\r\nMarguerite looked at the portrait, she could not but be struck by the\r\nextraordinary resemblance which must have existed between mother and\r\nson. There was the same low, square forehead, crowned with thick, fair\r\nhair, smooth and heavy; the same deep-set, somewhat lazy blue eyes\r\nbeneath firmly marked, straight brows; and in those eyes there was the\r\nsame intensity behind that apparent laziness, the same latent passion\r\nwhich used to light up Percy\xe2\x80\x99s face in the olden days before his\r\nmarriage, and which Marguerite had again noted, last night at dawn,\r\nwhen she had come quite close to him, and had allowed a note of\r\ntenderness to creep into her voice.\r\n\r\nMarguerite studied the portrait, for it interested her: after that she\r\nturned and looked again at the ponderous desk. It was covered with a\r\nmass of papers, all neatly tied and docketed, which looked like\r\naccounts and receipts arrayed with perfect method. It had never before\r\nstruck Marguerite\xe2\x80\x94nor had she, alas! found it worth while to inquire\xe2\x80\x94as\r\nto how Sir Percy, whom all the world had credited with a total lack of\r\nbrains, administered the vast fortune which his father had left him.\r\n\r\nSince she had entered this neat, orderly room, she had been taken so\r\nmuch by surprise, that this obvious proof of her husband\xe2\x80\x99s strong\r\nbusiness capacities did not cause her more than a passing thought of\r\nwonder. But it also strengthened her in the now certain knowledge that,\r\nwith his worldly inanities, his foppish ways, and foolish talk, he was\r\nnot only wearing a mask, but was playing a deliberate and studied part.\r\n\r\nMarguerite wondered again. Why should he take all this trouble? Why\r\nshould he\xe2\x80\x94who was obviously a serious, earnest man\xe2\x80\x94wish to appear\r\nbefore his fellow-men as an empty-headed nincompoop?\r\n\r\nHe may have wished to hide his love for a wife who held him in contempt\r\n. . . but surely such an object could have been gained at less\r\nsacrifice, and with far less trouble than constant incessant acting of\r\nan unnatural part.\r\n\r\nShe looked round her quite aimlessly now: she was horribly puzzled, and\r\na nameless dread, before all this strange, unaccountable mystery, had\r\nbegun to seize upon her. She felt cold and uncomfortable suddenly in\r\nthis severe and dark room. There were no pictures on the wall, save the\r\nfine Boucher portrait, only a couple of maps, both of parts of France,\r\none of the North coast and the other of the environs of Paris. What did\r\nSir Percy want with those, she wondered.\r\n\r\nHer head began to ache, she turned away from this strange Blue Beard\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nchamber, which she had entered, and which she did not understand. She\r\ndid not wish Frank to find her here, and with a last look round, she\r\nonce more turned to the door. As she did so, her foot knocked against a\r\nsmall object, which had apparently been lying close to the desk, on the\r\ncarpet, and which now went rolling, right across the room.\r\n\r\nShe stooped to pick it up. It was a solid gold ring, with a flat\r\nshield, on which was engraved a small device.\r\n\r\nMarguerite turned it over in her fingers, and then studied the\r\nengraving on the shield. It represented a small star-shaped flower, of\r\na shape she had seen so distinctly twice before: once at the opera, and\r\nonce at Lord Grenville\xe2\x80\x99s ball.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIX.\r\nTHE SCARLET PIMPERNEL\r\n\r\n\r\nAt what particular moment the strange doubt first crept into\r\nMarguerite\xe2\x80\x99s mind, she could not herself afterwards have said. With the\r\nring tightly clutched in her hand, she had run out of the room, down\r\nthe stairs, and out into the garden, where, in complete seclusion,\r\nalone with the flowers, and the river and the birds, she could look\r\nagain at the ring, and study that device more closely.\r\n\r\nStupidly, senselessly, now, sitting beneath the shade of an overhanging\r\nsycamore, she was looking at the plain gold shield, with the\r\nstar-shaped little flower engraved upon it.\r\n\r\nBah! It was ridiculous! she was dreaming! her nerves were overwrought,\r\nand she saw signs and mysteries in the most trivial coincidences. Had\r\nnot everybody about town recently made a point of affecting the device\r\nof that mysterious and heroic Scarlet Pimpernel?\r\n\r\nDid she not herself wear it embroidered on her gowns? set in gems and\r\nenamel in her hair? What was there strange in the fact that Sir Percy\r\nshould have chosen to use the device as a seal-ring? He might easily\r\nhave done that . . . yes . . . quite easily . . . and . . . besides . .\r\n. what connection could there be between her exquisite dandy of a\r\nhusband, with his fine clothes and refined, lazy ways, and the daring\r\nplotter who rescued French victims from beneath the very eyes of the\r\nleaders of a bloodthirsty revolution?\r\n\r\nHer thoughts were in a whirl\xe2\x80\x94her mind a blank . . . She did not see\r\nanything that was going on around her, and was quite startled when a\r\nfresh young voice called to her across the garden.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Ch\xc3\xa9rie!\xe2\x80\x94ch\xc3\xa9rie!_ where are you?\xe2\x80\x9d and little Suzanne, fresh as a\r\nrosebud, with eyes dancing with glee, and brown curls fluttering in the\r\nsoft morning breeze, came running across the lawn.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey told me you were in the garden,\xe2\x80\x9d she went on prattling merrily,\r\nand throwing herself with pretty, girlish impulse into Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s\r\narms, \xe2\x80\x9cso I ran out to give you a surprise. You did not expect me quite\r\nso soon, did you, my darling little Margot _ch\xc3\xa9rie_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarguerite, who had hastily concealed the ring in the folds of her\r\nkerchief, tried to respond gaily and unconcernedly to the young girl\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nimpulsiveness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed, sweet one,\xe2\x80\x9d she said with a smile, \xe2\x80\x9cit is delightful to have\r\nyou all to myself, and for a nice whole long day. . . . You won\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\nbored?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! bored! Margot, how _can_ you say such a wicked thing. Why! when we\r\nwere in the dear old convent together, we were always happy when we\r\nwere allowed to be alone together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd to talk secrets.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe two young girls had linked their arms in one another\xe2\x80\x99s and began\r\nwandering round the garden.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! how lovely your home is, Margot, darling,\xe2\x80\x9d said little Suzanne,\r\nenthusiastically, \xe2\x80\x9cand how happy you must be!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye, indeed! I ought to be happy\xe2\x80\x94oughtn\xe2\x80\x99t I, sweet one?\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMarguerite, with a wistful little sigh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow sadly you say it, _ch\xc3\xa9rie_. . . . Ah, well, I suppose now that you\r\nare a married woman you won\xe2\x80\x99t care to talk secrets with me any longer.\r\nOh! what lots and lots of secrets we used to have at school! Do you\r\nremember?\xe2\x80\x94some we did not even confide to Sister Theresa of the Holy\r\nAngels\xe2\x80\x94though she was such a dear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now you have one all-important secret, eh, little one?\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMarguerite, merrily, \xe2\x80\x9cwhich you are forthwith going to confide to me.\r\nNay, you need not blush, _ch\xc3\xa9rie_,\xe2\x80\x9d she added, as she saw Suzanne\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npretty little face crimson with blushes. \xe2\x80\x9cFaith, there\xe2\x80\x99s naught to be\r\nashamed of! He is a noble and true man, and one to be proud of as a\r\nlover, and . . . as a husband.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed, _ch\xc3\xa9rie_, I am not ashamed,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined Suzanne, softly; \xe2\x80\x9cand it\r\nmakes me very, very proud to hear you speak so well of him. I think\r\nmaman will consent,\xe2\x80\x9d she added thoughtfully, \xe2\x80\x9cand I shall be\xe2\x80\x94oh! so\r\nhappy\xe2\x80\x94but, of course, nothing is to be thought of until papa is safe. .\r\n. .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarguerite started. Suzanne\xe2\x80\x99s father! the Comte de Tournay!\xe2\x80\x94one of\r\nthose whose life would be jeopardised if Chauvelin succeeded in\r\nestablishing the identity of the Scarlet Pimpernel.\r\n\r\nShe had understood all along from the Comtesse, and also from one or\r\ntwo of the members of the league, that their mysterious leader had\r\npledged his honour to bring the fugitive Comte de Tournay safely out of\r\nFrance. Whilst little Suzanne\xe2\x80\x94unconscious of all\xe2\x80\x94save her own\r\nall-important little secret, went prattling on, Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s thoughts\r\nwent back to the events of the past night.\r\n\r\nArmand\xe2\x80\x99s peril, Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s threat, his cruel \xe2\x80\x9cEither\xe2\x80\x94or\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d which she\r\nhad accepted.\r\n\r\nAnd then her own work in the matter, which should have culminated at\r\none o\xe2\x80\x99clock in Lord Grenville\xe2\x80\x99s dining-room, when the relentless agent\r\nof the French Government would finally learn who was this mysterious\r\nScarlet Pimpernel, who so openly defied an army of spies and placed\r\nhimself so boldly, and for mere sport, on the side of the enemies of\r\nFrance.\r\n\r\nSince then she had heard nothing from Chauvelin. She had concluded that\r\nhe had failed, and yet, she had not felt anxious about Armand, because\r\nher husband had promised her that Armand would be safe.\r\n\r\nBut now, suddenly, as Suzanne prattled merrily along, an awful horror\r\ncame upon her for what she had done. Chauvelin had told her nothing, it\r\nwas true; but she remembered how sarcastic and evil he looked when she\r\ntook final leave of him after the ball. Had he discovered something\r\nthen? Had he already laid his plans for catching the daring plotter,\r\nred-handed, in France, and sending him to the guillotine without\r\ncompunction or delay?\r\n\r\nMarguerite turned sick with horror, and her hand convulsively clutched\r\nthe ring in her dress.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are not listening, _ch\xc3\xa9rie_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Suzanne, reproachfully, as she\r\npaused in her long, highly interesting narrative.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, yes, darling\xe2\x80\x94indeed I am,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marguerite with an effort,\r\nforcing herself to smile. \xe2\x80\x9cI love to hear you talking . . . and your\r\nhappiness makes me so very glad. . . . Have no fear, we will manage to\r\npropitiate maman. Sir Andrew Ffoulkes is a noble English gentleman; he\r\nhas money and position, the Comtesse will not refuse her consent. . . .\r\nBut . . . now, little one . . . tell me . . . what is the latest news\r\nabout your father?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Suzanne, with mad glee, \xe2\x80\x9cthe best we could possibly hear. My\r\nLord Hastings came to see maman early this morning. He said that all is\r\nnow well with dear papa, and we may safely expect him here in England\r\nin less than four days.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marguerite, whose glowing eyes were fastened on Suzanne\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nlips, as she continued merrily:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, we have no fear now! You don\xe2\x80\x99t know, _ch\xc3\xa9rie_, that that great and\r\nnoble Scarlet Pimpernel himself has gone to save papa. He has gone,\r\n_ch\xc3\xa9rie_ . . . actually gone . . .\xe2\x80\x9d added Suzanne excitedly. \xe2\x80\x9cHe was in\r\nLondon this morning; he will be in Calais, perhaps, to-morrow . . .\r\nwhere he will meet papa . . . and then . . . and then . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe blow had fallen. She had expected it all along, though she had\r\ntried for the last half-hour to delude herself and to cheat her fears.\r\nHe had gone to Calais, had been in London this morning . . . he . . .\r\nthe Scarlet Pimpernel . . . Percy Blakeney . . . her husband . . . whom\r\nshe had betrayed last night to Chauvelin. . . .\r\n\r\nPercy . . . Percy . . . her husband . . . the Scarlet Pimpernel. . . .\r\nOh! how could she have been so blind? She understood it now\xe2\x80\x94all at once\r\n. . . that part he played\xe2\x80\x94the mask he wore . . . in order to throw dust\r\nin everybody\xe2\x80\x99s eyes.\r\n\r\nAnd all for sheer sport and devilry of course!\xe2\x80\x94saving men, women and\r\nchildren from death, as other men destroy and kill animals for the\r\nexcitement, the love of the thing. The idle, rich man wanted some aim\r\nin life\xe2\x80\x94he, and the few young bucks he enrolled under his banner, had\r\namused themselves for months in risking their lives for the sake of an\r\ninnocent few.\r\n\r\nPerhaps he had meant to tell her when they were first married; and then\r\nthe story of the Marquis de St. Cyr had come to his ears, and he had\r\nsuddenly turned from her, thinking, no doubt, that she might some day\r\nbetray him and his comrades, who had sworn to follow him; and so he had\r\ntricked her, as he tricked all others, whilst hundreds now owed their\r\nlives to him, and many families owed him both life and happiness.\r\n\r\nThe mask of the inane fop had been a good one, and the part\r\nconsummately well played. No wonder that Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s spies had failed\r\nto detect, in the apparently brainless nincompoop, the man whose\r\nreckless daring and resourceful ingenuity had baffled the keenest\r\nFrench spies, both in France and in England. Even last night when\r\nChauvelin went to Lord Grenville\xe2\x80\x99s dining-room to seek that daring\r\nScarlet Pimpernel, he only saw that inane Sir Percy Blakeney fast\r\nasleep in a corner of the sofa.\r\n\r\nHad his astute mind guessed the secret, then? Here lay the whole awful,\r\nhorrible, amazing puzzle. In betraying a nameless stranger to his fate\r\nin order to save her brother, had Marguerite Blakeney sent her husband\r\nto his death?\r\n\r\nNo! no! no! a thousand times no! Surely Fate could not deal a blow like\r\nthat: Nature itself would rise in revolt: her hand, when it held that\r\ntiny scrap of paper last night, would surely have been struck numb ere\r\nit committed a deed so appalling and so terrible.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what is it, _ch\xc3\xa9rie_?\xe2\x80\x9d said little Suzanne, now genuinely alarmed,\r\nfor Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s colour had become dull and ashen. \xe2\x80\x9cAre you ill,\r\nMarguerite? What is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing, nothing, child,\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured, as in a dream. \xe2\x80\x9cWait a moment\r\n. . . let me think . . . think! . . . You said . . . the Scarlet\r\nPimpernel had gone to-day. . . . ?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarguerite, _ch\xc3\xa9rie_, what is it? You frighten me. . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is nothing, child, I tell you . . . nothing. . . . I must be alone\r\na minute\xe2\x80\x94and\xe2\x80\x94dear one . . . I may have to curtail our time together\r\nto-day. . . . I may have to go away\xe2\x80\x94you\xe2\x80\x99ll understand?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI understand that something has happened, _ch\xc3\xa9rie_, and that you want\r\nto be alone. I won\xe2\x80\x99t be a hindrance to you. Don\xe2\x80\x99t think of me. My maid,\r\nLucile, has not yet gone . . . we will go back together . . . don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthink of me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe threw her arms impulsively round Marguerite. Child as she was, she\r\nfelt the poignancy of her friend\xe2\x80\x99s grief, and with the infinite tact of\r\nher girlish tenderness, she did not try to pry into it, but was ready\r\nto efface herself.\r\n\r\nShe kissed Marguerite again and again, then walked sadly back across\r\nthe lawn. Marguerite did not move, she remained there, thinking . . .\r\nwondering what was to be done.\r\n\r\nJust as little Suzanne was about to mount the terrace steps, a groom\r\ncame running round the house towards his mistress. He carried a sealed\r\nletter in his hand. Suzanne instinctively turned back; her heart told\r\nher that here perhaps was further ill news for her friend, and she felt\r\nthat her poor Margot was not in a fit state to bear any more.\r\n\r\nThe groom stood respectfully beside his mistress, then he handed her\r\nthe sealed letter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is that?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Marguerite.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust come by runner, my lady.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarguerite took the letter mechanically, and turned it over in her\r\ntrembling fingers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho sent it?\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe runner said, my lady,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the groom, \xe2\x80\x9cthat his orders were to\r\ndeliver this, and that your ladyship would understand from whom it\r\ncame.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarguerite tore open the envelope. Already her instinct had told her\r\nwhat it contained, and her eyes only glanced at it mechanically.\r\n\r\nIt was a letter written by Armand St. Just to Sir Andrew Ffoulkes\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nletter which Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s spies had stolen at \xe2\x80\x9cThe Fisherman\xe2\x80\x99s Rest,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nand which Chauvelin had held as a rod over her to enforce her\r\nobedience.\r\n\r\nNow he had kept his word\xe2\x80\x94he had sent her back St. Just\xe2\x80\x99s compromising\r\nletter . . . for he was on the track of the Scarlet Pimpernel.\r\n\r\nMarguerite\xe2\x80\x99s senses reeled, her very soul seemed to be leaving her\r\nbody; she tottered, and would have fallen but for Suzanne\xe2\x80\x99s arm round\r\nher waist. With superhuman effort she regained control over\r\nherself\xe2\x80\x94there was yet much to be done.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBring that runner here to me,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to the servant, with much\r\ncalm. \xe2\x80\x9cHe has not gone?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, my lady.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe groom went, and Marguerite turned to Suzanne.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you, child, run within. Tell Lucile to get ready. I fear I must\r\nsend you home, child. And\xe2\x80\x94stay, tell one of the maids to prepare a\r\ntravelling dress and cloak for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSuzanne made no reply. She kissed Marguerite tenderly, and obeyed\r\nwithout a word; the child was overawed by the terrible, nameless misery\r\nin her friend\xe2\x80\x99s face.\r\n\r\nA minute later the groom returned, followed by the runner who had\r\nbrought the letter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho gave you this packet?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Marguerite.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA gentleman, my lady,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the man, \xe2\x80\x9cat \xe2\x80\x98The Rose and Thistle\xe2\x80\x99 inn\r\nopposite Charing Cross. He said you would understand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt \xe2\x80\x98The Rose and Thistle\xe2\x80\x99? What was he doing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was waiting for the coach, your ladyship, which he had ordered.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe coach?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, my lady. A special coach he had ordered. I understood from his\r\nman that he was posting straight to Dover.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s enough. You may go.\xe2\x80\x9d Then she turned to the groom: \xe2\x80\x9cMy coach\r\nand the four swiftest horses in the stables, to be ready at once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe groom and runner both went quickly off to obey. Marguerite remained\r\nstanding for a moment on the lawn quite alone. Her graceful figure was\r\nas rigid as a statue, her eyes were fixed, her hands were tightly\r\nclasped across her breast; her lips moved as they murmured with\r\npathetic heart-breaking persistence,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s to be done? What\xe2\x80\x99s to be done? Where to find him?\xe2\x80\x94Oh, God!\r\ngrant me light.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut this was not the moment for remorse and despair. She had\r\ndone\xe2\x80\x94unwittingly\xe2\x80\x94an awful and terrible thing\xe2\x80\x94the very worst crime, in\r\nher eyes, that woman ever committed\xe2\x80\x94she saw it in all its horror. Her\r\nvery blindness in not having guessed her husband\xe2\x80\x99s secret seemed now to\r\nher another deadly sin. She ought to have known! she ought to have\r\nknown!\r\n\r\nHow could she imagine that a man who could love with so much intensity\r\nas Percy Blakeney had loved her from the first\xe2\x80\x94how could such a man be\r\nthe brainless idiot he chose to appear? She, at least, ought to have\r\nknown that he was wearing a mask, and having found that out, she should\r\nhave torn it from his face, whenever they were alone together.\r\n\r\nHer love for him had been paltry and weak, easily crushed by her own\r\npride; and she, too, had worn a mask in assuming a contempt for him,\r\nwhilst, as a matter of fact, she completely misunderstood him.\r\n\r\nBut there was no time now to go over the past. By her own blindness she\r\nhad sinned; now she must repay, not by empty remorse, but by prompt and\r\nuseful action.\r\n\r\nPercy had started for Calais, utterly unconscious of the fact that his\r\nmost relentless enemy was on his heels. He had set sail early that\r\nmorning from London Bridge. Provided he had a favourable wind, he would\r\nno doubt be in France within twenty-four hours; no doubt he had\r\nreckoned on the wind and chosen this route.\r\n\r\nChauvelin, on the other hand, would post to Dover, charter a vessel\r\nthere, and undoubtedly reach Calais much about the same time. Once in\r\nCalais, Percy would meet all those who were eagerly waiting for the\r\nnoble and brave Scarlet Pimpernel, who had come to rescue them from\r\nhorrible and unmerited death. With Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s eyes now fixed upon his\r\nevery movement, Percy would thus not only be endangering his own life,\r\nbut that of Suzanne\xe2\x80\x99s father, the old Comte de Tournay, and of those\r\nother fugitives who were waiting for him and trusting in him. There was\r\nalso Armand, who had gone to meet de Tournay, secure in the knowledge\r\nthat the Scarlet Pimpernel was watching over his safety.\r\n\r\nAll these lives, and that of her husband, lay in Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s hands;\r\nthese she must save, if human pluck and ingenuity were equal to the\r\ntask.\r\n\r\nUnfortunately, she could not do all this quite alone. Once in Calais\r\nshe would not know where to find her husband, whilst Chauvelin, in\r\nstealing the papers at Dover, had obtained the whole itinerary. Above\r\neverything, she wished to warn Percy.\r\n\r\nShe knew enough about him by now to understand that he would never\r\nabandon those who trusted in him, that he would not turn back from\r\ndanger, and leave the Comte de Tournay to fall into the bloodthirsty\r\nhands that knew of no mercy. But if he were warned, he might form new\r\nplans, be more wary, more prudent. Unconsciously, he might fall into a\r\ncunning trap, but\xe2\x80\x94once warned\xe2\x80\x94he might yet succeed.\r\n\r\nAnd if he failed\xe2\x80\x94if indeed Fate, and Chauvelin, with all the resources\r\nat his command, proved too strong for the daring plotter after all\xe2\x80\x94then\r\nat least she would be there by his side, to comfort, love and cherish,\r\nto cheat death perhaps at the last by making it seem sweet, if they\r\ndied both together, locked in each other\xe2\x80\x99s arms, with the supreme\r\nhappiness of knowing that passion had responded to passion, and that\r\nall misunderstandings were at an end.\r\n\r\nHer whole body stiffened as with a great and firm resolution. This she\r\nmeant to do, if God gave her wits and strength. Her eyes lost their\r\nfixed look; they glowed with inward fire at the thought of meeting him\r\nagain so soon, in the very midst of most deadly perils; they sparkled\r\nwith the joy of sharing these dangers with him\xe2\x80\x94of helping him\r\nperhaps\xe2\x80\x94of being with him at the last\xe2\x80\x94if she failed.\r\n\r\nThe childlike sweet face had become hard and set, the curved mouth was\r\nclosed tightly over her clenched teeth. She meant to do or die, with\r\nhim and for his sake. A frown, which spoke of an iron will and\r\nunbending resolution, appeared between the two straight brows; already\r\nher plans were formed. She would go and find Sir Andrew Ffoulkes first;\r\nhe was Percy\xe2\x80\x99s best friend, and Marguerite remembered with a thrill,\r\nwith what blind enthusiasm the young man always spoke of his mysterious\r\nleader.\r\n\r\nHe would help her where she needed help; her coach was ready. A change\r\nof raiment, and a farewell to little Suzanne, and she could be on her\r\nway.\r\n\r\nWithout haste, but without hesitation, she walked quietly into the\r\nhouse.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XX.\r\nTHE FRIEND\r\n\r\n\r\nLess than half an hour later, Marguerite, buried in thoughts, sat\r\ninside her coach, which was bearing her swiftly to London.\r\n\r\nShe had taken an affectionate farewell of little Suzanne, and seen the\r\nchild safely started with her maid, and in her own coach, back to town.\r\nShe had sent one courier with a respectful letter of excuse to His\r\nRoyal Highness, begging for a postponement of the august visit on\r\naccount of pressing and urgent business, and another on ahead to\r\nbespeak a fresh relay of horses at Faversham.\r\n\r\nThen she had changed her muslin frock for a dark travelling costume and\r\nmantle, had provided herself with money\xe2\x80\x94which her husband\xe2\x80\x99s lavishness\r\nalways placed fully at her disposal\xe2\x80\x94and had started on her way.\r\n\r\nShe did not attempt to delude herself with any vain and futile hopes;\r\nthe safety of her brother Armand was to have been conditional on the\r\nimminent capture of the Scarlet Pimpernel. As Chauvelin had sent her\r\nback Armand\xe2\x80\x99s compromising letter, there was no doubt that he was quite\r\nsatisfied in his own mind that Percy Blakeney was the man whose death\r\nhe had sworn to bring about.\r\n\r\nNo! there was no room for any fond delusions! Percy, the husband whom\r\nshe loved with all the ardour which her admiration for his bravery had\r\nkindled, was in immediate, deadly peril, through her hand. She had\r\nbetrayed him to his enemy\xe2\x80\x94unwittingly \xe2\x80\x99tis true\xe2\x80\x94but she _had_ betrayed\r\nhim, and if Chauvelin succeeded in trapping him, who so far was unaware\r\nof his danger, then his death would be at her door. His death! when\r\nwith her very heart\xe2\x80\x99s blood, she would have defended him and given\r\nwillingly her life for his.\r\n\r\nShe had ordered her coach to drive her to the \xe2\x80\x9cCrown\xe2\x80\x9d inn; once there,\r\nshe told her coachman to give the horses food and rest. Then she\r\nordered a chair, and had herself carried to the house in Pall Mall\r\nwhere Sir Andrew Ffoulkes lived.\r\n\r\nAmong all Percy\xe2\x80\x99s friends who were enrolled under his daring banner,\r\nshe felt that she would prefer to confide in Sir Andrew Ffoulkes. He\r\nhad always been her friend, and now his love for little Suzanne had\r\nbrought him closer to her still. Had he been away from home, gone on\r\nthe mad errand with Percy, perhaps, then she would have called on Lord\r\nHastings or Lord Tony\xe2\x80\x94for she wanted the help of one of these young\r\nmen, or she would be indeed powerless to save her husband.\r\n\r\nSir Andrew Ffoulkes, however, was at home, and his servant introduced\r\nher ladyship immediately. She went upstairs to the young man\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncomfortable bachelor\xe2\x80\x99s chambers, and was shown into a small, though\r\nluxuriously furnished, dining-room. A moment or two later Sir Andrew\r\nhimself appeared.\r\n\r\nHe had evidently been much startled when he heard who his lady visitor\r\nwas, for he looked anxiously\xe2\x80\x94even suspiciously\xe2\x80\x94at Marguerite, whilst\r\nperforming the elaborate bows before her, which the rigid etiquette of\r\nthe time demanded.\r\n\r\nMarguerite had laid aside every vestige of nervousness; she was\r\nperfectly calm, and having returned the young man\xe2\x80\x99s elaborate salute,\r\nshe began very calmly,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSir Andrew, I have no desire to waste valuable time in much talk. You\r\nmust take certain things I am going to tell you for granted. These will\r\nbe of no importance. What is important is that your leader and comrade,\r\nthe Scarlet Pimpernel . . . my husband . . . Percy Blakeney . . . is in\r\ndeadly peril.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHad she had the remotest doubt of the correctness of her deductions,\r\nshe would have had them confirmed now, for Sir Andrew, completely taken\r\nby surprise, had grown very pale, and was quite incapable of making the\r\nslightest attempt at clever parrying.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo matter how I know this, Sir Andrew,\xe2\x80\x9d she continued quietly, \xe2\x80\x9cthank\r\nGod that I do, and that perhaps it is not too late to save him.\r\nUnfortunately, I cannot do this quite alone, and therefore have come to\r\nyou for help.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLady Blakeney,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young man, trying to recover himself, \xe2\x80\x9cI . .\r\n.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you hear me first?\xe2\x80\x9d she interrupted. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is how the matter\r\nstands. When the agent of the French Government stole your papers that\r\nnight in Dover, he found amongst them certain plans, which you or your\r\nleader meant to carry out for the rescue of the Comte de Tournay and\r\nothers. The Scarlet Pimpernel\xe2\x80\x94Percy, my husband\xe2\x80\x94has gone on this errand\r\nhimself to-day. Chauvelin knows that the Scarlet Pimpernel and Percy\r\nBlakeney are one and the same person. He will follow him to Calais, and\r\nthere will lay hands on him. You know as well as I do the fate that\r\nawaits him at the hands of the Revolutionary Government of France. No\r\ninterference from England\xe2\x80\x94from King George himself\xe2\x80\x94would save him.\r\nRobespierre and his gang would see to it that the interference came too\r\nlate. But not only that, the much-trusted leader will also have been\r\nunconsciously the means of revealing the hiding-place of the Comte de\r\nTournay and of all those who, even now, are placing their hopes in\r\nhim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe had spoken quietly, dispassionately, and with firm, unbending\r\nresolution. Her purpose was to make that young man trust and help her,\r\nfor she could do nothing without him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not understand,\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated, trying to gain time, to think what\r\nwas best to be done.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye! but I think you do, Sir Andrew. You must know that I am speaking\r\nthe truth. Look these facts straight in the face. Percy has sailed for\r\nCalais, I presume for some lonely part of the coast, and Chauvelin is\r\non his track. _He_ has posted for Dover, and will cross the Channel\r\nprobably to-night. What do you think will happen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young man was silent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPercy will arrive at his destination: unconscious of being followed he\r\nwill seek out de Tournay and the others\xe2\x80\x94among these is Armand St. Just,\r\nmy brother\xe2\x80\x94he will seek them out, one after another, probably, not\r\nknowing that the sharpest eyes in the world are watching his every\r\nmovement. When he has thus unconsciously betrayed those who blindly\r\ntrust in him, when nothing can be gained from him, and he is ready to\r\ncome back to England, with those whom he has gone so bravely to save,\r\nthe doors of the trap will close upon him, and he will be sent to end\r\nhis noble life upon the guillotine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nStill Sir Andrew was silent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou do not trust me,\xe2\x80\x9d she said passionately. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, God! cannot you see\r\nthat I am in deadly earnest? Man, man,\xe2\x80\x9d she added, while, with her tiny\r\nhands she seized the young man suddenly by the shoulders, forcing him\r\nto look straight at her, \xe2\x80\x9ctell me, do I look like that vilest thing on\r\nearth\xe2\x80\x94a woman who would betray her own husband?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod forbid, Lady Blakeney,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young man at last, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I should\r\nattribute such evil motives to you, but . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what? . . . tell me. . . . Quick, man! . . . the very seconds are\r\nprecious!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you tell me,\xe2\x80\x9d he asked resolutely, and looking searchingly into\r\nher blue eyes, \xe2\x80\x9cwhose hand helped to guide M. Chauvelin to the\r\nknowledge which you say he possesses?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMine,\xe2\x80\x9d she said quietly, \xe2\x80\x9cI own it\xe2\x80\x94I will not lie to you, for I wish\r\nyou to trust me absolutely. But I had no idea\xe2\x80\x94how _could_ I have?\xe2\x80\x94of\r\nthe identity of the Scarlet Pimpernel . . . and my brother\xe2\x80\x99s safety was\r\nto be my prize if I succeeded.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn helping Chauvelin to track the Scarlet Pimpernel?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe nodded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is no use telling you how he forced my hand. Armand is more than a\r\nbrother to me, and . . . and . . . how _could_ I guess? . . . But we\r\nwaste time, Sir Andrew . . . every second is precious . . . in the name\r\nof God! . . . my husband is in peril . . . your friend!\xe2\x80\x94your\r\ncomrade!\xe2\x80\x94Help me to save him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSir Andrew felt his position to be a very awkward one. The oath he had\r\ntaken before his leader and comrade was one of obedience and secrecy;\r\nand yet the beautiful woman, who was asking him to trust her, was\r\nundoubtedly in earnest; his friend and leader was equally undoubtedly\r\nin imminent danger and . . .\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLady Blakeney,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last, \xe2\x80\x9cGod knows you have perplexed me, so\r\nthat I do not know which way my duty lies. Tell me what you wish me to\r\ndo. There are nineteen of us ready to lay down our lives for the\r\nScarlet Pimpernel if he is in danger.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no need for lives just now, my friend,\xe2\x80\x9d she said drily; \xe2\x80\x9cmy\r\nwits and four swift horses will serve the necessary purpose. But I must\r\nknow where to find him. See,\xe2\x80\x9d she added, while her eyes filled with\r\ntears, \xe2\x80\x9cI have humbled myself before you, I have owned my fault to you;\r\nshall I also confess my weakness?\xe2\x80\x94My husband and I have been estranged,\r\nbecause he did not trust me, and because I was too blind to understand.\r\nYou must confess that the bandage which he put over my eyes was a very\r\nthick one. Is it small wonder that I did not see through it? But last\r\nnight, after I led him unwittingly into such deadly peril, it suddenly\r\nfell from my eyes. If you will not help me, Sir Andrew, I would still\r\nstrive to save my husband. I would still exert every faculty I possess\r\nfor his sake; but I might be powerless, for I might arrive too late,\r\nand nothing would be left for you but lifelong remorse, and . . . and .\r\n. . for me, a broken heart.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, Lady Blakeney,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young man, touched by the gentle\r\nearnestness of this exquisitely beautiful woman, \xe2\x80\x9cdo you know that what\r\nyou propose doing is man\xe2\x80\x99s work?\xe2\x80\x94you cannot possibly journey to Calais\r\nalone. You would be running the greatest possible risks to yourself,\r\nand your chances of finding your husband now\xe2\x80\x94were I to direct you ever\r\nso carefully\xe2\x80\x94are infinitely remote.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I hope there are risks!\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured softly. \xe2\x80\x9cI hope there are\r\ndangers, too!\xe2\x80\x94I have so much to atone for. But I fear you are mistaken.\r\nChauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s eyes are fixed upon you all, he will scarce notice me.\r\nQuick, Sir Andrew!\xe2\x80\x94the coach is ready, and there is not a moment to be\r\nlost. . . . I _must_ get to him! I _must_!\xe2\x80\x9d she repeated with almost\r\nsavage energy, \xe2\x80\x9cto warn him that that man is on his track. . . . Can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nyou see\xe2\x80\x94can\xe2\x80\x99t you see, that I _must_ get to him . . . even . . . even\r\nif it be too late to save him . . . at least . . . to be by his side .\r\n. . at the last.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFaith, Madame, you must command me. Gladly would I or any of my\r\ncomrades lay down our lives for your husband. If you _will_ go yourself\r\n. . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, friend, do you not see that I would go mad if I let you go\r\nwithout me?\xe2\x80\x9d She stretched out her hand to him. \xe2\x80\x9cYou _will_ trust me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI await your orders,\xe2\x80\x9d he said simply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cListen, then. My coach is ready to take me to Dover. Do you follow me,\r\nas swiftly as horses will take you. We meet at nightfall at \xe2\x80\x98The\r\nFisherman\xe2\x80\x99s Rest.\xe2\x80\x99 Chauvelin would avoid it, as he is known there, and\r\nI think it would be the safest. I will gladly accept your escort to\r\nCalais . . . as you say, I might miss Sir Percy were you to direct me\r\never so carefully. We\xe2\x80\x99ll charter a schooner at Dover and cross over\r\nduring the night. Disguised, if you will agree to it, as my lacquey,\r\nyou will, I think, escape detection.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am entirely at your service, Madame,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined the young man\r\nearnestly. \xe2\x80\x9cI trust to God that you will sight the _Day Dream_ before\r\nwe reach Calais. With Chauvelin at his heels, every step the Scarlet\r\nPimpernel takes on French soil is fraught with danger.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod grant it, Sir Andrew. But now, farewell. We meet to-night at\r\nDover! It will be a race between Chauvelin and me across the Channel\r\nto-night\xe2\x80\x94and the prize\xe2\x80\x94the life of the Scarlet Pimpernel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe kissed her hand, and then escorted her to her chair. A quarter of an\r\nhour later she was back at the \xe2\x80\x9cCrown\xe2\x80\x9d inn, where her coach and horses\r\nwere ready and waiting for her. The next moment they thundered along\r\nthe London streets, and then straight on to the Dover road at maddening\r\nspeed.\r\n\r\nShe had no time for despair now. She was up and doing and had no\r\nleisure to think. With Sir Andrew Ffoulkes as her companion and ally,\r\nhope had once again revived in her heart.\r\n\r\nGod would be merciful. He would not allow so appalling a crime to be\r\ncommitted, as the death of a brave man, through the hand of a woman who\r\nloved him, and worshipped him, and who would gladly have died for his\r\nsake.\r\n\r\nMarguerite\xe2\x80\x99s thoughts flew back to him, the mysterious hero, whom she\r\nhad always unconsciously loved, when his identity was still unknown to\r\nher. Laughingly, in the olden days, she used to call him the shadowy\r\nking of her heart, and now she had suddenly found that this enigmatic\r\npersonality whom she had worshipped, and the man who loved her so\r\npassionately, were one and the same: what wonder that one or two\r\nhappier Visions began to force their way before her mind? She vaguely\r\nwondered what she would say to him when first they would stand face to\r\nface.\r\n\r\nShe had had so many anxieties, so much excitement during the past few\r\nhours, that she allowed herself the luxury of nursing these few more\r\nhopeful, brighter thoughts. Gradually the rumble of the coach wheels,\r\nwith its incessant monotony, acted soothingly on her nerves: her eyes,\r\naching with fatigue and many shed and unshed tears, closed\r\ninvoluntarily, and she fell into a troubled sleep.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXI.\r\nSUSPENSE\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was late into the night when she at last reached \xe2\x80\x9cThe Fisherman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nRest.\xe2\x80\x9d She had done the whole journey in less than eight hours, thanks\r\nto innumerable changes of horses at the various coaching stations, for\r\nwhich she always paid lavishly, thus obtaining the very best and\r\nswiftest that could be had.\r\n\r\nHer coachman, too, had been indefatigable; the promise of special and\r\nrich reward had no doubt helped to keep him up, and he had literally\r\nburned the ground beneath his mistress\xe2\x80\x99 coach wheels.\r\n\r\nThe arrival of Lady Blakeney in the middle of the night caused a\r\nconsiderable flutter at \xe2\x80\x9cThe Fisherman\xe2\x80\x99s Rest.\xe2\x80\x9d Sally jumped hastily\r\nout of bed, and Mr. Jellyband was at great pains how to make his\r\nimportant guest comfortable.\r\n\r\nBoth these good folk were far too well drilled in the manners\r\nappertaining to innkeepers, to exhibit the slightest surprise at Lady\r\nBlakeney\xe2\x80\x99s arrival, alone, at this extraordinary hour. No doubt they\r\nthought all the more, but Marguerite was far too absorbed in the\r\nimportance\xe2\x80\x94the deadly earnestness\xe2\x80\x94of her journey, to stop and ponder\r\nover trifles of that sort.\r\n\r\nThe coffee-room\xe2\x80\x94the scene lately of the dastardly outrage on two\r\nEnglish gentlemen\xe2\x80\x94was quite deserted. Mr. Jellyband hastily relit the\r\nlamp, rekindled a cheerful bit of fire in the great hearth, and then\r\nwheeled a comfortable chair by it, into which Marguerite gratefully\r\nsank.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill your ladyship stay the night?\xe2\x80\x9d asked pretty Miss Sally, who was\r\nalready busy laying a snow-white cloth on the table, preparatory to\r\nproviding a simple supper for her ladyship.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo! not the whole night,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Marguerite. \xe2\x80\x9cAt any rate, I shall\r\nnot want any room but this, if I can have it to myself for an hour or\r\ntwo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is at your ladyship\xe2\x80\x99s service,\xe2\x80\x9d said honest Jellyband, whose\r\nrubicund face was set in its tightest folds, lest it should betray\r\nbefore \xe2\x80\x9cthe quality\xe2\x80\x9d that boundless astonishment which the worthy\r\nfellow had begun to feel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall be crossing over at the first turn of the tide,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMarguerite, \xe2\x80\x9cand in the first schooner I can get. But my coachman and\r\nmen will stay the night, and probably several days longer, so I hope\r\nyou will make them comfortable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, my lady; I\xe2\x80\x99ll look after them. Shall Sally bring your ladyship\r\nsome supper?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, please. Put something cold on the table, and as soon as Sir\r\nAndrew Ffoulkes comes, show him in here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, my lady.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHonest Jellyband\xe2\x80\x99s face now expressed distress in spite of himself. He\r\nhad great regard for Sir Percy Blakeney, and did not like to see his\r\nlady running away with young Sir Andrew. Of course, it was no business\r\nof his, and Mr. Jellyband was no gossip. Still, in his heart, he\r\nrecollected that her ladyship was after all only one of them\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cfurriners\xe2\x80\x9d; what wonder that she was immoral like the rest of them?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t sit up, honest Jellyband,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Marguerite, kindly, \xe2\x80\x9cnor\r\nyou either, Mistress Sally. Sir Andrew may be late.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJellyband was only too willing that Sally should go to bed. He was\r\nbeginning not to like these goings-on at all. Still, Lady Blakeney\r\nwould pay handsomely for the accommodation, and it certainly was no\r\nbusiness of his.\r\n\r\nSally arranged a simple supper of cold meat, wine, and fruit on the\r\ntable, then with a respectful curtsey, she retired, wondering in her\r\nlittle mind why her ladyship looked so serious, when she was about to\r\nelope with her gallant.\r\n\r\nThen commenced a period of weary waiting for Marguerite. She knew that\r\nSir Andrew\xe2\x80\x94who would have to provide himself with clothes befitting a\r\nlacquey\xe2\x80\x94could not possibly reach Dover for at least a couple of hours.\r\nHe was a splendid horseman of course, and would make light in such an\r\nemergency of the seventy odd miles between London and Dover. He would,\r\ntoo, literally burn the ground beneath his horse\xe2\x80\x99s hoofs, but he might\r\nnot always get very good remounts, and in any case, he could not have\r\nstarted from London until at least an hour after she did.\r\n\r\nShe had seen nothing of Chauvelin on the road. Her coachman, whom she\r\nquestioned, had not seen anyone answering the description his mistress\r\ngave him of the wizened figure of the little Frenchman.\r\n\r\nEvidently, therefore, he had been ahead of her all the time. She had\r\nnot dared to question the people at the various inns, where they had\r\nstopped to change horses. She feared that Chauvelin had spies all along\r\nthe route, who might overhear her questions, then outdistance her and\r\nwarn her enemy of her approach.\r\n\r\nNow she wondered at what inn he might be stopping, or whether he had\r\nhad the good luck of chartering a vessel already, and was now himself\r\non the way to France. That thought gripped her at the heart as with an\r\niron vice. If indeed she should be too late already!\r\n\r\nThe loneliness of the room overwhelmed her; everything within was so\r\nhorribly still; the ticking of the grandfather\xe2\x80\x99s clock\xe2\x80\x94dreadfully slow\r\nand measured\xe2\x80\x94was the only sound which broke this awful loneliness.\r\n\r\nMarguerite had need of all her energy, all her steadfastness of\r\npurpose, to keep up her courage through this weary midnight waiting.\r\n\r\nEveryone else in the house but herself must have been asleep. She had\r\nheard Sally go upstairs. Mr. Jellyband had gone to see to her coachman\r\nand men, and then had returned and taken up a position under the porch\r\noutside, just where Marguerite had first met Chauvelin about a week\r\nago. He evidently meant to wait up for Sir Andrew Ffoulkes, but was\r\nsoon overcome by sweet slumbers, for presently\xe2\x80\x94in addition to the slow\r\nticking of the clock\xe2\x80\x94Marguerite could hear the monotonous and dulcet\r\ntones of the worthy fellow\xe2\x80\x99s breathing.\r\n\r\nFor some time now, she had realised that the beautiful warm October\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nday, so happily begun, had turned into a rough and cold night. She had\r\nfelt very chilly, and was glad of the cheerful blaze in the hearth: but\r\ngradually, as time wore on, the weather became more rough, and the\r\nsound of the great breakers against the Admiralty Pier, though some\r\ndistance from the inn, came to her as the noise of muffled thunder.\r\n\r\nThe wind was becoming boisterous, rattling the leaded windows and the\r\nmassive doors of the old-fashioned house: it shook the trees outside\r\nand roared down the vast chimney. Marguerite wondered if the wind would\r\nbe favourable for her journey. She had no fear of the storm, and would\r\nhave braved worse risks sooner than delay the crossing by an hour.\r\n\r\nA sudden commotion outside roused her from her meditations. Evidently\r\nit was Sir Andrew Ffoulkes, just arrived in mad haste, for she heard\r\nhis horse\xe2\x80\x99s hoofs thundering on the flag-stones outside, then Mr.\r\nJellyband\xe2\x80\x99s sleepy, yet cheerful tones bidding him welcome.\r\n\r\nFor a moment, then, the awkwardness of her position struck Marguerite;\r\nalone at this hour, in a place where she was well known, and having\r\nmade an assignation with a young cavalier equally well known, and who\r\narrives in disguise! What food for gossip to those mischievously\r\ninclined.\r\n\r\nThe idea struck Marguerite chiefly from its humorous side: there was\r\nsuch quaint contrast between the seriousness of her errand, and the\r\nconstruction which would naturally be put on her actions by honest Mr.\r\nJellyband, that, for the first time since many hours, a little smile\r\nbegan playing round the corners of her childlike mouth, and when,\r\npresently, Sir Andrew, almost unrecognisable in his lacquey-like garb,\r\nentered the coffee-room, she was able to greet him with quite a merry\r\nlaugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFaith! Monsieur, my lacquey,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI am satisfied with your\r\nappearance!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Jellyband had followed Sir Andrew, looking strangely perplexed. The\r\nyoung gallant\xe2\x80\x99s disguise had confirmed his worst suspicions. Without a\r\nsmile upon his jovial face, he drew the cork from the bottle of wine,\r\nset the chairs ready, and prepared to wait.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks, honest friend,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marguerite, who was still smiling at the\r\nthought of what the worthy fellow must be thinking at that very moment,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwe shall require nothing more; and here\xe2\x80\x99s for all the trouble you have\r\nbeen put to on our account.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe handed two or three gold pieces to Jellyband, who took them\r\nrespectfully, and with becoming gratitude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStay, Lady Blakeney,\xe2\x80\x9d interposed Sir Andrew, as Jellyband was about to\r\nretire, \xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid we shall require something more of my friend\r\nJelly\xe2\x80\x99s hospitality. I am sorry to say we cannot cross over to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot cross over to-night?\xe2\x80\x9d she repeated in amazement. \xe2\x80\x9cBut we must, Sir\r\nAndrew, we must! There can be no question of cannot, and whatever it\r\nmay cost, we must get a vessel to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut the young man shook his head sadly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid it is not a question of cost, Lady Blakeney. There is a\r\nnasty storm blowing from France, the wind is dead against us, we cannot\r\npossibly sail until it has changed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarguerite became deadly pale. She had not foreseen this. Nature\r\nherself was playing her a horrible, cruel trick. Percy was in danger,\r\nand she could not go to him, because the wind happened to blow from the\r\ncoast of France.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we must go!\xe2\x80\x94we must!\xe2\x80\x9d she repeated with strange, persistent\r\nenergy, \xe2\x80\x9cyou know, we must go!\xe2\x80\x94can\xe2\x80\x99t you find a way?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been down to the shore already,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cand had a talk to\r\none or two skippers. It is quite impossible to set sail to-night, so\r\nevery sailor assured me. No one,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, looking significantly at\r\nMarguerite, \xe2\x80\x9c_no one_ could possibly put out of Dover to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarguerite at once understood what he meant. _No one_ included\r\nChauvelin as well as herself. She nodded pleasantly to Jellyband.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, I must resign myself,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to him. \xe2\x80\x9cHave you a room\r\nfor me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, your ladyship. A nice, bright, airy room. I\xe2\x80\x99ll see to it at\r\nonce. . . . And there is another one for Sir Andrew\xe2\x80\x94both quite ready.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s brave now, mine honest Jelly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sir Andrew, gaily, and\r\nclapping his worthy host vigorously on the back. \xe2\x80\x9cYou unlock both those\r\nrooms, and leave our candles here on the dresser. I vow you are dead\r\nwith sleep, and her ladyship must have some supper before she retires.\r\nThere, have no fear, friend of the rueful countenance, her ladyship\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nvisit, though at this unusual hour, is a great honour to thy house, and\r\nSir Percy Blakeney will reward thee doubly, if thou seest well to her\r\nprivacy and comfort.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSir Andrew had no doubt guessed the many conflicting doubts and fears\r\nwhich raged in honest Jellyband\xe2\x80\x99s head; and, as he was a gallant\r\ngentleman, he tried by this brave hint to allay some of the worthy\r\ninnkeeper\xe2\x80\x99s suspicions. He had the satisfaction of seeing that he had\r\npartially succeeded. Jellyband\xe2\x80\x99s rubicund countenance brightened\r\nsomewhat, at mention of Sir Percy\xe2\x80\x99s name.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll go and see to it at once, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d he said with alacrity, and with\r\nless frigidity in his manner. \xe2\x80\x9cHas her ladyship everything she wants\r\nfor supper?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverything, thanks, honest friend, and as I am famished and dead with\r\nfatigue, I pray you see to the rooms.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow tell me,\xe2\x80\x9d she said eagerly, as soon as Jellyband had gone from the\r\nroom, \xe2\x80\x9ctell me all your news.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is nothing else much to tell you, Lady Blakeney,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the\r\nyoung man. \xe2\x80\x9cThe storm makes it quite impossible for any vessel to put\r\nout of Dover this tide. But, what seemed to you at first a terrible\r\ncalamity is really a blessing in disguise. If we cannot cross over to\r\nFrance to-night, Chauvelin is in the same quandary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe may have left before the storm broke out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod grant he may,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sir Andrew, merrily, \xe2\x80\x9cfor very likely then\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99ll have been driven out of his course! Who knows? He may now even be\r\nlying at the bottom of the sea, for there is a furious storm raging,\r\nand it will fare ill with all small craft which happen to be out. But I\r\nfear me we cannot build our hopes upon the shipwreck of that cunning\r\ndevil, and of all his murderous plans. The sailors I spoke to, all\r\nassured me that no schooner had put out of Dover for several hours: on\r\nthe other hand, I ascertained that a stranger had arrived by coach this\r\nafternoon, and had, like myself, made some inquiries about crossing\r\nover to France.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen Chauvelin is still in Dover?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUndoubtedly. Shall I go waylay him and run my sword through him? That\r\nwere indeed the quickest way out of the difficulty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay! Sir Andrew, do not jest! Alas! I have often since last night\r\ncaught myself wishing for that fiend\xe2\x80\x99s death. But what you suggest is\r\nimpossible! The laws of this country do not permit of murder! It is\r\nonly in our beautiful France that wholesale slaughter is done lawfully,\r\nin the name of Liberty and of brotherly love.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSir Andrew had persuaded her to sit down to the table, to partake of\r\nsome supper and to drink a little wine. This enforced rest of at least\r\ntwelve hours, until the next tide, was sure to be terribly difficult to\r\nbear in the state of intense excitement in which she was. Obedient in\r\nthese small matters like a child, Marguerite tried to eat and drink.\r\n\r\nSir Andrew, with that profound sympathy born in all those who are in\r\nlove, made her almost happy by talking to her about her husband. He\r\nrecounted to her some of the daring escapes the brave Scarlet Pimpernel\r\nhad contrived for the poor French fugitives, whom a relentless and\r\nbloody revolution was driving out of their country. He made her eyes\r\nglow with enthusiasm by telling her of his bravery, his ingenuity, his\r\nresourcefulness, when it meant snatching the lives of men, women, and\r\neven children from beneath the very edge of that murderous, ever-ready\r\nguillotine.\r\n\r\nHe even made her smile quite merrily by telling her of the Scarlet\r\nPimpernel\xe2\x80\x99s quaint and many disguises, through which he had baffled the\r\nstrictest watch set against him at the barricades of Paris. This last\r\ntime, the escape of the Comtesse de Tournay and her children had been a\r\nveritable masterpiece\xe2\x80\x94Blakeney disguised as a hideous old market-woman,\r\nin filthy cap and straggling grey locks, was a sight fit to make the\r\ngods laugh.\r\n\r\nMarguerite laughed heartily as Sir Andrew tried to describe Blakeney\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nappearance, whose gravest difficulty always consisted in his great\r\nheight, which in France made disguise doubly difficult.\r\n\r\nThus an hour wore on. There were many more to spend in enforced\r\ninactivity in Dover. Marguerite rose from the table with an impatient\r\nsigh. She looked forward with dread to the night in the bed upstairs,\r\nwith terribly anxious thoughts to keep her company, and the howling of\r\nthe storm to help chase sleep away.\r\n\r\nShe wondered where Percy was now. The _Day Dream_ was a strong,\r\nwell-built, sea-going yacht. Sir Andrew had expressed the opinion that\r\nno doubt she had got in the lee of the wind before the storm broke out,\r\nor else perhaps had not ventured into the open at all, but was lying\r\nquietly at Gravesend.\r\n\r\nBriggs was an expert skipper, and Sir Percy handled a schooner as well\r\nas any master mariner. There was no danger for them from the storm.\r\n\r\nIt was long past midnight when at last Marguerite retired to rest. As\r\nshe had feared, sleep sedulously avoided her eyes. Her thoughts were of\r\nthe blackest during these long, weary hours, whilst that incessant\r\nstorm raged which was keeping her away from Percy. The sound of the\r\ndistant breakers made her heart ache with melancholy. She was in the\r\nmood when the sea has a saddening effect upon the nerves. It is only\r\nwhen we are very happy, that we can bear to gaze merrily upon the vast\r\nand limitless expanse of water, rolling on and on with such persistent,\r\nirritating monotony, to the accompaniment of our thoughts, whether\r\ngrave or gay. When they are gay, the waves echo their gaiety; but when\r\nthey are sad, then every breaker, as it rolls, seems to bring\r\nadditional sadness, and to speak to us of hopelessness and of the\r\npettiness of all our joys.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXII.\r\nCALAIS\r\n\r\n\r\nThe weariest nights, the longest days, sooner or later must perforce\r\ncome to an end.\r\n\r\nMarguerite had spent over fifteen hours in such acute mental torture as\r\nwell-nigh drove her crazy. After a sleepless night, she rose early,\r\nwild with excitement, dying to start on her journey, terrified lest\r\nfurther obstacles lay in her way. She rose before anyone else in the\r\nhouse was astir, so frightened was she, lest she should miss the one\r\ngolden opportunity of making a start.\r\n\r\nWhen she came downstairs, she found Sir Andrew Ffoulkes sitting in the\r\ncoffee-room. He had been out half an hour earlier, and had gone to the\r\nAdmiralty Pier, only to find that neither the French packet nor any\r\nprivately chartered vessel could put out of Dover yet. The storm was\r\nthen at its fullest, and the tide was on the turn. If the wind did not\r\nabate or change, they would perforce have to wait another ten or twelve\r\nhours until the next tide, before a start could be made. And the storm\r\nhad not abated, the wind had not changed, and the tide was rapidly\r\ndrawing out.\r\n\r\nMarguerite felt the sickness of despair when she heard this melancholy\r\nnews. Only the most firm resolution kept her from totally breaking\r\ndown, and thus adding to the young man\xe2\x80\x99s anxiety, which evidently had\r\nbecome very keen.\r\n\r\nThough he tried to disguise it, Marguerite could see that Sir Andrew\r\nwas just as anxious as she was to reach his comrade and friend. This\r\nenforced inactivity was terrible to them both.\r\n\r\nHow they spent that wearisome day at Dover, Marguerite could never\r\nafterwards say. She was in terror of showing herself, lest Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nspies happened to be about, so she had a private sitting-room, and she\r\nand Sir Andrew sat there hour after hour, trying to take, at long\r\nintervals, some perfunctory meals, which little Sally would bring them,\r\nwith nothing to do but to think, to conjecture, and only occasionally\r\nto hope.\r\n\r\nThe storm had abated just too late; the tide was by then too far out to\r\nallow a vessel to put off to sea. The wind had changed, and was\r\nsettling down to a comfortable north-westerly breeze\xe2\x80\x94a veritable\r\ngodsend for a speedy passage across to France.\r\n\r\nAnd there those two waited, wondering if the hour would ever come when\r\nthey could finally make a start. There had been one happy interval in\r\nthis long weary day, and that was when Sir Andrew went down once again\r\nto the pier, and presently came back to tell Marguerite that he had\r\nchartered a quick schooner, whose skipper was ready to put to sea the\r\nmoment the tide was favourable.\r\n\r\nFrom that moment the hours seemed less wearisome; there was less\r\nhopelessness in the waiting; and at last, at five o\xe2\x80\x99clock in the\r\nafternoon, Marguerite, closely veiled and followed by Sir Andrew\r\nFfoulkes, who, in the guise of her lacquey, was carrying a number of\r\nimpedimenta, found her way down to the pier.\r\n\r\nOnce on board, the keen, fresh sea-air revived her, the breeze was just\r\nstrong enough to nicely swell the sails of the _Foam Crest_, as she cut\r\nher way merrily towards the open.\r\n\r\nThe sunset was glorious after the storm, and Marguerite, as she watched\r\nthe white cliffs of Dover gradually disappearing from view, felt more\r\nat peace and once more almost hopeful.\r\n\r\nSir Andrew was full of kind attentions, and she felt how lucky she had\r\nbeen to have him by her side in this, her great trouble.\r\n\r\nGradually the grey coast of France began to emerge from the\r\nfast-gathering evening mists. One or two lights could be seen\r\nflickering, and the spires of several churches to rise out of the\r\nsurrounding haze.\r\n\r\nHalf an hour later Marguerite had landed upon French shore. She was\r\nback in that country where at this very moment men slaughtered their\r\nfellow-creatures by the hundreds, and sent innocent women and children\r\nin thousands to the block.\r\n\r\nThe very aspect of the country and its people, even in this remote\r\nsea-coast town, spoke of that seething revolution, three hundred miles\r\naway, in beautiful Paris, now rendered hideous by the constant flow of\r\nthe blood of her noblest sons, by the wailing of the widows, and the\r\ncries of fatherless children.\r\n\r\nThe men all wore red caps\xe2\x80\x94in various stages of cleanliness\xe2\x80\x94but all with\r\nthe tricolour cockade pinned on the left-hand side. Marguerite noticed\r\nwith a shudder that, instead of the laughing, merry countenance\r\nhabitual to her own countrymen, their faces now invariably wore a look\r\nof sly distrust.\r\n\r\nEvery man nowadays was a spy upon his fellows: the most innocent word\r\nuttered in jest might at any time be brought up as a proof of\r\naristocratic tendencies, or of treachery against the people. Even the\r\nwomen went about with a curious look of fear and of hate lurking in\r\ntheir brown eyes; and all watched Marguerite as she stepped on shore,\r\nfollowed by Sir Andrew, and murmured as she passed along: \xe2\x80\x9c_Sacr\xc3\xa9s\r\naristos!_\xe2\x80\x9d or else \xe2\x80\x9c_Sacr\xc3\xa9s Anglais!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOtherwise their presence excited no further comment. Calais, even in\r\nthose days, was in constant business communication with England, and\r\nEnglish merchants were often to be seen on this coast. It was well\r\nknown that in view of the heavy duties in England, a vast deal of\r\nFrench wines and brandies were smuggled across. This pleased the French\r\n_bourgeois_ immensely; he liked to see the English Government and the\r\nEnglish king, both of whom he hated, cheated out of their revenues; and\r\nan English smuggler was always a welcome guest at the tumble-down\r\ntaverns of Calais and Boulogne.\r\n\r\nSo, perhaps, as Sir Andrew gradually directed Marguerite through the\r\ntortuous streets of Calais, many of the population, who turned with an\r\noath to look at the strangers clad in the English fashion, thought that\r\nthey were bent on purchasing dutiable articles for their own fog-ridden\r\ncountry, and gave them no more than a passing thought.\r\n\r\nMarguerite, however, wondered how her husband\xe2\x80\x99s tall, massive figure\r\ncould have passed through Calais unobserved: she marvelled what\r\ndisguise he assumed to do his noble work, without exciting too much\r\nattention.\r\n\r\nWithout exchanging more than a few words, Sir Andrew was leading her\r\nright across the town, to the other side from that where they had\r\nlanded, and on the way towards Cap Gris Nez. The streets were narrow,\r\ntortuous, and mostly evil-smelling, with a mixture of stale fish and\r\ndamp cellar odours. There had been heavy rain here during the storm\r\nlast night, and sometimes Marguerite sank ankle-deep in the mud, for\r\nthe roads were not lighted save by the occasional glimmer from a lamp\r\ninside a house.\r\n\r\nBut she did not heed any of these petty discomforts: \xe2\x80\x9cWe may meet\r\nBlakeney at the \xe2\x80\x98Chat Gris,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d Sir Andrew had said, when they landed,\r\nand she was walking as if on a carpet of rose-leaves, for she was going\r\nto meet him almost at once.\r\n\r\nAt last they reached their destination. Sir Andrew evidently knew the\r\nroad, for he had walked unerringly in the dark, and had not asked his\r\nway from anyone. It was too dark then for Marguerite to notice the\r\noutside aspect of this house. The \xe2\x80\x9cChat Gris,\xe2\x80\x9d as Sir Andrew had called\r\nit, was evidently a small wayside inn on the outskirts of Calais, and\r\non the way to Gris Nez. It lay some little distance from the coast, for\r\nthe sound of the sea seemed to come from afar.\r\n\r\nSir Andrew knocked at the door with the knob of his cane, and from\r\nwithin Marguerite heard a sort of grunt and the muttering of a number\r\nof oaths. Sir Andrew knocked again, this time more peremptorily: more\r\noaths were heard, and then shuffling steps seemed to draw near the\r\ndoor. Presently this was thrown open, and Marguerite found herself on\r\nthe threshold of the most dilapidated, most squalid room she had ever\r\nseen in all her life.\r\n\r\nThe paper, such as it was, was hanging from the walls in strips; there\r\ndid not seem to be a single piece of furniture in the room that could,\r\nby the wildest stretch of imagination, be called \xe2\x80\x9cwhole.\xe2\x80\x9d Most of the\r\nchairs had broken backs, others had no seats to them, one corner of the\r\ntable was propped up with a bundle of faggots, there where the fourth\r\nleg had been broken.\r\n\r\nIn one corner of the room there was a huge hearth, over which hung a\r\nstock-pot, with a not altogether unpalatable odour of hot soup\r\nemanating therefrom. On one side of the room, high up in the wall,\r\nthere was a species of loft, before which hung a tattered\r\nblue-and-white checked curtain. A rickety set of steps led up to this\r\nloft.\r\n\r\nOn the great bare walls, with their colourless paper, all stained with\r\nvaried filth, there were chalked up at intervals in great bold\r\ncharacters, the words: \xe2\x80\x9cLibert\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x94Egalit\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x94Fraternit\xc3\xa9.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe whole of this sordid abode was dimly lighted by an evil-smelling\r\noil-lamp, which hung from the rickety rafters of the ceiling. It all\r\nlooked so horribly squalid, so dirty and uninviting, that Marguerite\r\nhardly dared to cross the threshold.\r\n\r\nSir Andrew, however, had stepped unhesitatingly forward.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEnglish travellers, citoyen!\xe2\x80\x9d he said boldly, and speaking in French.\r\n\r\nThe individual who had come to the door in response to Sir Andrew\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nknock, and who, presumably, was the owner of this squalid abode, was an\r\nelderly, heavily-built peasant, dressed in a dirty blue blouse, heavy\r\nsabots, from which wisps of straw protruded all round, shabby blue\r\ntrousers, and the inevitable red cap with the tricolour cockade, that\r\nproclaimed his momentary political views. He carried a short wooden\r\npipe, from which the odour of rank tobacco emanated. He looked with\r\nsome suspicion and a great deal of contempt at the two travellers,\r\nmuttered \xe2\x80\x9c_Sacrrr\xc3\xa9s Anglais!_\xe2\x80\x9d and spat upon the ground to further show\r\nhis independence of spirit, but, nevertheless, he stood aside to let\r\nthem enter, no doubt well aware that these same _sacrrr\xc3\xa9s Anglais_\r\nalways had well-filled purses.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, lud!\xe2\x80\x9d said Marguerite, as she advanced into the room, holding her\r\nhandkerchief to her dainty nose, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat a dreadful hole! Are you sure\r\nthis is the place?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye! \xe2\x80\x99tis the place, sure enough,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the young man as, with his\r\nlace-edged, fashionable handkerchief, he dusted a chair for Marguerite\r\nto sit on; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I vow I never saw a more villainous hole.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFaith!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, looking round with some curiosity and a great deal\r\nof horror at the dilapidated walls, the broken chairs, the rickety\r\ntable, \xe2\x80\x9cit certainly does not look inviting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe landlord of the \xe2\x80\x9cChat Gris\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94by name, Brogard\xe2\x80\x94had taken no further\r\nnotice of his guests; he concluded that presently they would order\r\nsupper, and in the meanwhile it was not for a free citizen to show\r\ndeference, or even courtesy, to anyone, however smartly they might be\r\ndressed.\r\n\r\nBy the hearth sat a huddled-up figure clad, seemingly, mostly in rags:\r\nthat figure was apparently a woman, although even that would have been\r\nhard to distinguish, except for the cap, which had once been white, and\r\nfor what looked like the semblance of a petticoat. She was sitting\r\nmumbling to herself, and from time to time stirring the brew in her\r\nstock-pot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHey, my friend!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sir Andrew at last, \xe2\x80\x9cwe should like some supper.\r\n. . . The citoyenne there,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, pointing to the huddled-up bundle\r\nof rags by the hearth, \xe2\x80\x9cis concocting some delicious soup, I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nwarrant, and my mistress has not tasted food for several hours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt took Brogard some few moments to consider the question. A free\r\ncitizen does not respond too readily to the wishes of those who happen\r\nto require something of him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Sacrrr\xc3\xa9s aristos!_\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured, and once more spat upon the ground.\r\n\r\nThen he went very slowly up to a dresser which stood in a corner of the\r\nroom; from this he took an old pewter soup-tureen and slowly, and\r\nwithout a word, he handed it to his better-half, who, in the same\r\nsilence, began filling the tureen with the soup out of her stock-pot.\r\n\r\nMarguerite had watched all these preparations with absolute horror;\r\nwere it not for the earnestness of her purpose, she would incontinently\r\nhave fled from this abode of dirt and evil smells.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFaith! our host and hostess are not cheerful people,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sir Andrew,\r\nseeing the look of horror on Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s face. \xe2\x80\x9cI would I could offer\r\nyou a more hearty and more appetising meal . . . but I think you will\r\nfind the soup eatable and the wine good; these people wallow in dirt,\r\nbut live well as a rule.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay! I pray you, Sir Andrew,\xe2\x80\x9d she said gently, \xe2\x80\x9cbe not anxious about\r\nme. My mind is scarce inclined to dwell on thoughts of supper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBrogard was slowly pursuing his gruesome preparations; he had placed a\r\ncouple of spoons, also two glasses on the table, both of which Sir\r\nAndrew took the precaution of wiping carefully.\r\n\r\nBrogard had also produced a bottle of wine and some bread, and\r\nMarguerite made an effort to draw her chair to the table and to make\r\nsome pretence at eating. Sir Andrew, as befitting his _r\xc3\xb4le_ of\r\nlacquey, stood behind her chair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, Madame, I pray you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, seeing that Marguerite seemed quite\r\nunable to eat, \xe2\x80\x9cI beg of you to try and swallow some food\xe2\x80\x94remember you\r\nhave need of all your strength.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe soup certainly was not bad; it smelt and tasted good. Marguerite\r\nmight have enjoyed it, but for the horrible surroundings. She broke the\r\nbread, however, and drank some of the wine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, Sir Andrew,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI do not like to see you standing. You\r\nhave need of food just as much as I have. This creature will only think\r\nthat I am an eccentric Englishwoman eloping with her lacquey, if you\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nsit down and partake of this semblance of supper beside me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIndeed, Brogard having placed what was strictly necessary upon the\r\ntable, seemed not to trouble himself any further about his guests. The\r\nMere Brogard had quietly shuffled out of the room, and the man stood\r\nand lounged about, smoking his evil-smelling pipe, sometimes under\r\nMarguerite\xe2\x80\x99s very nose, as any free-born citizen who was anybody\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nequal should do.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cConfound the brute!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sir Andrew, with native British wrath, as\r\nBrogard leant up against the table, smoking and looking down\r\nsuperciliously at these two _sacrrr\xc3\xa9s Anglais_.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn Heaven\xe2\x80\x99s name, man,\xe2\x80\x9d admonished Marguerite, hurriedly, seeing that\r\nSir Andrew, with British-born instinct, was ominously clenching his\r\nfist, \xe2\x80\x9cremember that you are in France, and that in this year of grace\r\nthis is the temper of the people.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d like to scrag the brute!\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Sir Andrew, savagely.\r\n\r\nHe had taken Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s advice and sat next to her at table, and they\r\nwere both making noble efforts to deceive one another, by pretending to\r\neat and drink.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI pray you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marguerite, \xe2\x80\x9ckeep the creature in a good temper, so\r\nthat he may answer the questions we must put to him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll do my best, but, begad! I\xe2\x80\x99d sooner scrag him than question him.\r\nHey! my friend,\xe2\x80\x9d he said pleasantly in French, and tapping Brogard\r\nlightly on the shoulder, \xe2\x80\x9cdo you see many of our quality along these\r\nparts? Many English travellers, I mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBrogard looked round at him, over his near shoulder, puffed away at his\r\npipe for a moment or two as he was in no hurry, then muttered,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHeu!\xe2\x80\x94sometimes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sir Andrew, carelessly, \xe2\x80\x9cEnglish travellers always know\r\nwhere they can get good wine, eh! my friend?\xe2\x80\x94Now, tell me, my lady was\r\ndesiring to know if by any chance you happen to have seen a great\r\nfriend of hers, an English gentleman, who often comes to Calais on\r\nbusiness; he is tall, and recently was on his way to Paris\xe2\x80\x94my lady\r\nhoped to have met him in Calais.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarguerite tried not to look at Brogard, lest she should betray before\r\nhim the burning anxiety with which she waited for his reply. But a\r\nfree-born French citizen is never in any hurry to answer questions:\r\nBrogard took his time, then he said very slowly,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTall Englishman?\xe2\x80\x94To-day!\xe2\x80\x94Yes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n \xe2\x80\x9cYou have seen him?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Sir Andrew, carelessly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, to-day,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Brogard, sullenly. Then he quietly took Sir\r\nAndrew\xe2\x80\x99s hat from a chair close by, put it on his own head, tugged at\r\nhis dirty blouse, and generally tried to express in pantomime that the\r\nindividual in question wore very fine clothes. \xe2\x80\x9c_Sacrr\xc3\xa9 aristo!_\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nmuttered, \xe2\x80\x9cthat tall Englishman!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarguerite could scarce repress a scream.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s Sir Percy right enough,\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured, \xe2\x80\x9cand not even in\r\ndisguise!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe smiled, in the midst of all her anxiety and through her gathering\r\ntears, at thought of \xe2\x80\x9cthe ruling passion strong in death\xe2\x80\x9d; of Percy\r\nrunning into the wildest, maddest dangers, with the latest-cut coat\r\nupon his back, and the laces of his jabot unruffled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! the foolhardiness of it!\xe2\x80\x9d she sighed. \xe2\x80\x9cQuick, Sir Andrew! ask the\r\nman when he went.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, yes, my friend,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sir Andrew, addressing Brogard, with the\r\nsame assumption of carelessness, \xe2\x80\x9cmy lord always wears beautiful\r\nclothes; the tall Englishman you saw, was certainly my lady\xe2\x80\x99s friend.\r\nAnd he has gone, you say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe went . . . yes . . . but he\xe2\x80\x99s coming back . . . here\xe2\x80\x94he ordered\r\nsupper . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSir Andrew put his hand with a quick gesture of warning upon\r\nMarguerite\xe2\x80\x99s arm; it came none too soon, for the next moment her wild,\r\nmad joy would have betrayed her. He was safe and well, was coming back\r\nhere presently, she would see him in a few moments perhaps. . . .
