Oh!\r\nthe wildness of her joy seemed almost more than she could bear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere!\xe2\x80\x9d she said to Brogard, who seemed suddenly to have been\r\ntransformed in her eyes into some heaven-born messenger of bliss.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere!\xe2\x80\x94did you say the English gentleman was coming back here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe heaven-born messenger of bliss spat upon the floor, to express his\r\ncontempt for all and sundry _aristos_, who chose to haunt the \xe2\x80\x9cChat\r\nGris.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHeu!\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered, \xe2\x80\x9che ordered supper\xe2\x80\x94he will come back. . . . _Sacrr\xc3\xa9\r\nAnglais!_\xe2\x80\x9d he added, by way of protest against all this fuss for a mere\r\nEnglishman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut where is he now?\xe2\x80\x94Do you know?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked eagerly, placing her\r\ndainty white hand upon the dirty sleeve of his blue blouse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe went to get a horse and cart,\xe2\x80\x9d said Brogard, laconically, as, with\r\na surly gesture, he shook off from his arm that pretty hand which\r\nprinces had been proud to kiss.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt what time did he go?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Brogard had evidently had enough of these questionings. He did not\r\nthink that it was fitting for a citizen\xe2\x80\x94who was the equal of anybody\xe2\x80\x94to\r\nbe thus catechised by these _sacrr\xc3\xa9s aristos_, even though they were\r\nrich English ones. It was distinctly more fitting to his new-born\r\ndignity to be as rude as possible; it was a sure sign of servility to\r\nmeekly reply to civil questions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x9d he said surlily. \xe2\x80\x9cI have said enough, _voyons, les\r\naristos!_ . . . He came to-day. He ordered supper. He went out.\xe2\x80\x94He\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\ncome back. _Voil\xc3\xa0!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd with this parting assertion of his rights as a citizen and a free\r\nman, to be as rude as he well pleased, Brogard shuffled out of the\r\nroom, banging the door after him.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIII.\r\nHOPE\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFaith, Madame!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sir Andrew, seeing that Marguerite seemed\r\ndesirous to call her surly host back again, \xe2\x80\x9cI think we\xe2\x80\x99d better leave\r\nhim alone. We shall not get anything more out of him, and we might\r\narouse his suspicions. One never knows what spies may be lurking around\r\nthese God-forsaken places.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat care I?\xe2\x80\x9d she replied lightly, \xe2\x80\x9cnow I know that my husband is\r\nsafe, and that I shall see him almost directly!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush!\xe2\x80\x9d he said in genuine alarm, for she had talked quite loudly, in\r\nthe fulness of her glee, \xe2\x80\x9cthe very walls have ears in France, these\r\ndays.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe rose quickly from the table, and walked round the bare, squalid\r\nroom, listening attentively at the door, through which Brogard had just\r\ndisappeared, and whence only muttered oaths and shuffling footsteps\r\ncould be heard. He also ran up the rickety steps that led to the attic,\r\nto assure himself that there were no spies of Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s about the\r\nplace.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre we alone, Monsieur, my lacquey?\xe2\x80\x9d said Marguerite, gaily, as the\r\nyoung man once more sat down beside her. \xe2\x80\x9cMay we talk?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs cautiously as possible!\xe2\x80\x9d he entreated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFaith, man! but you wear a glum face! As for me, I could dance with\r\njoy! Surely there is no longer any cause for fear. Our boat is on the\r\nbeach, the _Foam Crest_ not two miles out at sea, and my husband will\r\nbe here, under this very roof, within the next half hour perhaps. Sure!\r\nthere is naught to hinder us. Chauvelin and his gang have not yet\r\narrived.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, madam! that I fear we do not know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was at Dover at the same time that we were.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHeld up by the same storm, which kept us from starting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly. But\xe2\x80\x94I did not speak of it before, for I feared to alarm you\xe2\x80\x94I\r\nsaw him on the beach not five minutes before we embarked. At least, I\r\nswore to myself at the time that it was himself; he was disguised as a\r\n_cur\xc3\xa9_, so that Satan, his own guardian, would scarce have known him.\r\nBut I heard him then, bargaining for a vessel to take him swiftly to\r\nCalais; and he must have set sail less than an hour after we did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarguerite\xe2\x80\x99s face had quickly lost its look of joy. The terrible danger\r\nin which Percy stood, now that he was actually on French soil, became\r\nsuddenly and horribly clear to her. Chauvelin was close upon his heels;\r\nhere in Calais, the astute diplomatist was all-powerful; a word from\r\nhim and Percy could be tracked and arrested and . . .\r\n\r\nEvery drop of blood seemed to freeze in her veins; not even during the\r\nmoments of her wildest anguish in England had she so completely\r\nrealised the imminence of the peril in which her husband stood.\r\nChauvelin had sworn to bring the Scarlet Pimpernel to the guillotine,\r\nand now the daring plotter, whose anonymity hitherto had been his\r\nsafeguard, stood revealed through her own hand, to his most bitter,\r\nmost relentless enemy.\r\n\r\nChauvelin\xe2\x80\x94when he waylaid Lord Tony and Sir Andrew Ffoulkes in the\r\ncoffee-room of \xe2\x80\x9cThe Fisherman\xe2\x80\x99s Rest\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94had obtained possession of all\r\nthe plans of this latest expedition. Armand St. Just, the Comte de\r\nTournay and other fugitive royalists were to have met the Scarlet\r\nPimpernel\xe2\x80\x94or rather, as it had been originally arranged, two of his\r\nemissaries\xe2\x80\x94on this day, the 2nd of October, at a place evidently known\r\nto the league, and vaguely alluded to as the \xe2\x80\x9cP\xc3\xa8re Blanchard\xe2\x80\x99s hut.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArmand, whose connection with the Scarlet Pimpernel and disavowal of\r\nthe brutal policy of the Reign of Terror was still unknown to his\r\ncountrymen, had left England a little more than a week ago, carrying\r\nwith him the necessary instructions, which would enable him to meet the\r\nother fugitives and to convey them to this place of safety.\r\n\r\nThis much Marguerite had fully understood from the first, and Sir\r\nAndrew Ffoulkes had confirmed her surmises. She knew, too, that when\r\nSir Percy realised that his own plans and his directions to his\r\nlieutenants had been stolen by Chauvelin, it was too late to\r\ncommunicate with Armand, or to send fresh instructions to the\r\nfugitives.\r\n\r\nThey would, of necessity, be at the appointed time and place, not\r\nknowing how grave was the danger which now awaited their brave rescuer.\r\n\r\nBlakeney, who as usual had planned and organised the whole expedition,\r\nwould not allow any of his younger comrades to run the risk of almost\r\ncertain capture. Hence his hurried note to them at Lord Grenville\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nball\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cStart myself to-morrow\xe2\x80\x94alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd now with his identity known to his most bitter enemy, his every\r\nstep would be dogged, the moment he set foot in France. He would be\r\ntracked by Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s emissaries, followed until he reached that\r\nmysterious hut where the fugitives were waiting for him, and there the\r\ntrap would be closed on him and on them.\r\n\r\nThere was but one hour\xe2\x80\x94the hour\xe2\x80\x99s start which Marguerite and Sir Andrew\r\nhad of their enemy\xe2\x80\x94in which to warn Percy of the imminence of his\r\ndanger, and to persuade him to give up the foolhardy expedition, which\r\ncould only end in his own death.\r\n\r\nBut there _was_ that one hour.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChauvelin knows of this inn, from the papers he stole,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sir\r\nAndrew, earnestly, \xe2\x80\x9cand on landing will make straight for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has not landed yet,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cwe have an hour\xe2\x80\x99s start on him, and\r\nPercy will be here directly. We shall be mid-Channel ere Chauvelin has\r\nrealised that we have slipped through his fingers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe spoke excitedly and eagerly, wishing to infuse into her young\r\nfriend some of that buoyant hope which still clung to her heart. But he\r\nshook his head sadly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSilent again, Sir Andrew?\xe2\x80\x9d she said with some impatience. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you\r\nshake your head and look so glum?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFaith, Madame,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied, \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99tis only because in making your\r\nrose-coloured plans, you are forgetting the most important factor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat in the world do you mean?\xe2\x80\x94I am forgetting nothing. . . . What\r\nfactor do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d she added with more impatience.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt stands six foot odd high,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Sir Andrew, quietly, \xe2\x80\x9cand hath\r\nname Percy Blakeney.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t understand,\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think that Blakeney would leave Calais without having\r\naccomplished what he set out to do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean . . . ?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s the old Comte de Tournay . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Comte . . . ?\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd St. Just . . . and others . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy brother!\xe2\x80\x9d she said with a heart-broken sob of anguish. \xe2\x80\x9cHeaven help\r\nme, but I fear I had forgotten.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFugitives as they are, these men at this moment await with perfect\r\nconfidence and unshaken faith the arrival of the Scarlet Pimpernel, who\r\nhas pledged his honour to take them safely across the Channel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIndeed, she had forgotten! With the sublime selfishness of a woman who\r\nloves with her whole heart, she had in the last twenty-four hours had\r\nno thought save for him. His precious, noble life, his danger\xe2\x80\x94he, the\r\nloved one, the brave hero, he alone dwelt in her mind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy brother!\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured, as one by one the heavy tears gathered in\r\nher eyes, as memory came back to her of Armand, the companion and\r\ndarling of her childhood, the man for whom she had committed the deadly\r\nsin, which had so hopelessly imperilled her brave husband\xe2\x80\x99s life.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSir Percy Blakeney would not be the trusted, honoured leader of a\r\nscore of English gentlemen,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sir Andrew, proudly, \xe2\x80\x9cif he abandoned\r\nthose who placed their trust in him. As for breaking his word, the very\r\nthought is preposterous!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was silence for a moment or two. Marguerite had buried her face\r\nin her hands, and was letting the tears slowly trickle through her\r\ntrembling fingers. The young man said nothing; his heart ached for this\r\nbeautiful woman in her awful grief. All along he had felt the terrible\r\n_impasse_ in which her own rash act had plunged them all. He knew his\r\nfriend and leader so well, with his reckless daring, his mad bravery,\r\nhis worship of his own word of honour. Sir Andrew knew that Blakeney\r\nwould brave any danger, run the wildest risks sooner than break it,\r\nand, with Chauvelin at his very heels, would make a final attempt,\r\nhowever desperate, to rescue those who trusted in him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFaith, Sir Andrew,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marguerite at last, making brave efforts to\r\ndry her tears, \xe2\x80\x9cyou are right, and I would not now shame myself by\r\ntrying to dissuade him from doing his duty. As you say, I should plead\r\nin vain. God grant him strength and ability,\xe2\x80\x9d she added fervently and\r\nresolutely, \xe2\x80\x9cto outwit his pursuers. He will not refuse to take you\r\nwith him, perhaps, when he starts on his noble work; between you, you\r\nwill have cunning as well as valour! God guard you both! In the\r\nmeanwhile I think we should lose no time. I still believe that his\r\nsafety depends upon his knowing that Chauvelin is on his track.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUndoubtedly. He has wonderful resources at his command. As soon as he\r\nis aware of his danger he will exercise more caution: his ingenuity is\r\na veritable miracle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, what say you to a voyage of reconnaissance in the village whilst\r\nI wait here against his coming!\xe2\x80\x94You might come across Percy\xe2\x80\x99s track and\r\nthus save valuable time. If you find him, tell him to beware!\xe2\x80\x94his\r\nbitterest enemy is on his heels!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut this is such a villainous hole for you to wait in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, that I do not mind!\xe2\x80\x94But you might ask our surly host if he could\r\nlet me wait in another room, where I could be safer from the prying\r\neyes of any chance traveller. Offer him some ready money, so that he\r\nshould not fail to give me word the moment the tall Englishman\r\nreturns.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe spoke quite calmly, even cheerfully now, thinking out her plans,\r\nready for the worst if need be; she would show no more weakness, she\r\nwould prove herself worthy of him, who was about to give his life for\r\nthe sake of his fellow-men.\r\n\r\nSir Andrew obeyed her without further comment. Instinctively he felt\r\nthat hers now was the stronger mind; he was willing to give himself\r\nover to her guidance, to become the hand, whilst she was the directing\r\nhead.\r\n\r\nHe went to the door of the inner room, through which Brogard and his\r\nwife had disappeared before, and knocked; as usual, he was answered by\r\na salvo of muttered oaths.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHey! friend Brogard!\xe2\x80\x9d said the young man peremptorily, \xe2\x80\x9cmy lady would\r\nwish to rest here awhile. Could you give her the use of another room?\r\nShe would wish to be alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe took some money out of his pocket, and allowed it to jingle\r\nsignificantly in his hand. Brogard had opened the door, and listened,\r\nwith his usual surly apathy, to the young man\xe2\x80\x99s request. At sight of\r\nthe gold, however, his lazy attitude relaxed slightly; he took his pipe\r\nfrom his mouth and shuffled into the room.\r\n\r\nHe then pointed over his shoulder at the attic up in the wall.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe can wait up there!\xe2\x80\x9d he said with a grunt. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s comfortable, and I\r\nhave no other room.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing could be better,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marguerite in English; she at once\r\nrealised the advantages such a position hidden from view would give\r\nher. \xe2\x80\x9cGive him the money, Sir Andrew; I shall be quite happy up there,\r\nand can see everything without being seen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe nodded to Brogard, who condescended to go up to the attic, and to\r\nshake up the straw that lay on the floor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay I entreat you, madam, to do nothing rash,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sir Andrew, as\r\nMarguerite prepared in her turn to ascend the rickety flight of steps.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRemember this place is infested with spies. Do not, I beg of you,\r\nreveal yourself to Sir Percy, unless you are absolutely certain that\r\nyou are alone with him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEven as he spoke, he felt how unnecessary was this caution: Marguerite\r\nwas as calm, as clear-headed as any man. There was no fear of her doing\r\nanything that was rash.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay,\xe2\x80\x9d she said with a slight attempt at cheerfulness, \xe2\x80\x9cthat can I\r\nfaithfully promise you. I would not jeopardise my husband\xe2\x80\x99s life, nor\r\nyet his plans, by speaking to him before strangers. Have no fear, I\r\nwill watch my opportunity, and serve him in the manner I think he needs\r\nit most.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBrogard had come down the steps again, and Marguerite was ready to go\r\nup to her safe retreat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI dare not kiss your hand, madam,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sir Andrew, as she began to\r\nmount the steps, \xe2\x80\x9csince I am your lacquey, but I pray you be of good\r\ncheer. If I do not come across Blakeney in half an hour, I shall\r\nreturn, expecting to find him here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that will be best. We can afford to wait for half an hour.\r\nChauvelin cannot possibly be here before that. God grant that either\r\nyou or I may have seen Percy by then. Good luck to you, friend! Have no\r\nfear for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLightly she mounted the rickety wooden steps that led to the attic.\r\nBrogard was taking no further heed of her. She could make herself\r\ncomfortable there or not as she chose. Sir Andrew watched her until she\r\nhad reached the loft and sat down upon the straw. She pulled the\r\ntattered curtains across, and the young man noted that she was\r\nsingularly well placed there, for seeing and hearing, whilst remaining\r\nunobserved.\r\n\r\nHe had paid Brogard well; the surly old innkeeper would have no object\r\nin betraying her. Then Sir Andrew prepared to go. At the door he turned\r\nonce again and looked up at the loft. Through the ragged curtains\r\nMarguerite\xe2\x80\x99s sweet face was peeping down at him, and the young man\r\nrejoiced to see that it looked serene, and even gently smiling. With a\r\nfinal nod of farewell to her, he walked out into the night.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIV.\r\nTHE DEATH-TRAP\r\n\r\n\r\nThe next quarter of an hour went by swiftly and noiselessly. In the\r\nroom downstairs, Brogard had for a while busied himself with clearing\r\nthe table, and re-arranging it for another guest.\r\n\r\nIt was because she watched these preparations that Marguerite found the\r\ntime slipping by more pleasantly. It was for Percy that this semblance\r\nof supper was being got ready. Evidently Brogard had a certain amount\r\nof respect for the tall Englishman, as he seemed to take some trouble\r\nin making the place look a trifle less uninviting than it had done\r\nbefore.\r\n\r\nHe even produced, from some hidden recess in the old dresser, what\r\nactually looked like a table-cloth; and when he spread it out, and saw\r\nit was full of holes, he shook his head dubiously for a while, then was\r\nat much pains so to spread it over the table as to hide most of its\r\nblemishes.\r\n\r\nThen he got out a serviette, also old and ragged, but possessing some\r\nmeasure of cleanliness, and with this he carefully wiped the glasses,\r\nspoons and plates, which he put on the table.\r\n\r\nMarguerite could not help smiling to herself as she watched all these\r\npreparations, which Brogard accomplished to an accompaniment of\r\nmuttered oaths. Clearly the great height and bulk of the Englishman, or\r\nperhaps the weight of his fist, had overawed this free-born citizen of\r\nFrance, or he would never have been at such trouble for any _sacrr\xc3\xa9\r\naristo_.\r\n\r\nWhen the table was set\xe2\x80\x94such as it was\xe2\x80\x94Brogard surveyed it with evident\r\nsatisfaction. He then dusted one of the chairs with the corner of his\r\nblouse, gave a stir to the stock-pot, threw a fresh bundle of faggots\r\non to the fire, and slouched out of the room.\r\n\r\nMarguerite was left alone with her reflections. She had spread her\r\ntravelling cloak over the straw, and was sitting fairly comfortably, as\r\nthe straw was fresh, and the evil odours from below came up to her only\r\nin a modified form.\r\n\r\nBut, momentarily, she was almost happy; happy because, when she peeped\r\nthrough the tattered curtains, she could see a rickety chair, a torn\r\ntable-cloth, a glass, a plate and a spoon; that was all. But those mute\r\nand ugly things seemed to say to her that they were waiting for Percy;\r\nthat soon, very soon, he would be here, that the squalid room being\r\nstill empty, they would be alone together.\r\n\r\nThat thought was so heavenly, that Marguerite closed her eyes in order\r\nto shut out everything but that. In a few minutes she would be alone\r\nwith him; she would run down the ladder, and let him see her; then he\r\nwould take her in his arms, and she would let him see that, after that,\r\nshe would gladly die for him, and with him, for earth could hold no\r\ngreater happiness than that.\r\n\r\nAnd then what would happen? She could not even remotely conjecture. She\r\nknew, of course, that Sir Andrew was right, that Percy would do\r\neverything he had set out to accomplish; that she\xe2\x80\x94now she was\r\nhere\xe2\x80\x94could do nothing, beyond warning him to be cautious, since\r\nChauvelin himself was on his track. After having cautioned him, she\r\nwould perforce have to see him go off upon his terrible and daring\r\nmission; she could not even with a word or look, attempt to keep him\r\nback. She would have to obey, whatever he told her to do, even perhaps\r\nhave to efface herself, and wait, in indescribable agony, whilst he,\r\nperhaps, went to his death.\r\n\r\nBut even that seemed less terrible to bear than the thought that he\r\nshould never know how much she loved him\xe2\x80\x94that at any rate would be\r\nspared her; the squalid room itself, which seemed to be waiting for\r\nhim, told her that he would be here soon.\r\n\r\nSuddenly her over-sensitive ears caught the sound of distant footsteps\r\ndrawing near; her heart gave a wild leap of joy! Was it Percy at last?\r\nNo! the step did not seem quite as long, nor quite as firm as his; she\r\nalso thought that she could hear two distinct sets of footsteps. Yes!\r\nthat was it! two men were coming this way. Two strangers perhaps, to\r\nget a drink, or . . .\r\n\r\nBut she had not time to conjecture, for presently there was a\r\nperemptory call at the door, and the next moment it was violently\r\nthrown open from the outside, whilst a rough, commanding voice\r\nshouted,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHey! Citoyen Brogard! Hol\xc3\xa1!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarguerite could not see the newcomers, but, through a hole in one of\r\nthe curtains, she could observe one portion of the room below.\r\n\r\nShe heard Brogard\xe2\x80\x99s shuffling footsteps, as he came out of the inner\r\nroom, muttering his usual string of oaths. On seeing the strangers,\r\nhowever, he paused in the middle of the room, well within range of\r\nMarguerite\xe2\x80\x99s vision, looked at them, with even more withering contempt\r\nthan he had bestowed upon his former guests, and muttered, \xe2\x80\x9c_Sacrrr\xc3\xa9e\r\nsoutane!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarguerite\xe2\x80\x99s heart seemed all at once to stop beating; her eyes, large\r\nand dilated, had fastened on one of the newcomers, who, at this point,\r\nhad taken a quick step forward towards Brogard. He was dressed in the\r\nsoutane, broad-brimmed hat and buckled shoes habitual to the French\r\n_cur\xc3\xa9_, but as he stood opposite the innkeeper, he threw open his\r\nsoutane for a moment, displaying the tricolour scarf of officialism,\r\nwhich sight immediately had the effect of transforming Brogard\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nattitude of contempt, into one of cringing obsequiousness.\r\n\r\nIt was the sight of this French _cur\xc3\xa9_, which seemed to freeze the very\r\nblood in Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s veins. She could not see his face, which was\r\nshaded by his broad-brimmed hat, but she recognised the thin, bony\r\nhands, the slight stoop, the whole gait of the man! It was Chauvelin!\r\n\r\nThe horror of the situation struck her as with a physical blow; the\r\nawful disappointment, the dread of what was to come, made her very\r\nsenses reel, and she needed almost superhuman effort, not to fall\r\nsenseless beneath it all.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA plate of soup and a bottle of wine,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chauvelin imperiously to\r\nBrogard, \xe2\x80\x9cthen clear out of here\xe2\x80\x94understand? I want to be alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSilently, and without any muttering this time, Brogard obeyed.\r\nChauvelin sat down at the table, which had been prepared for the tall\r\nEnglishman, and the innkeeper busied himself obsequiously round him,\r\ndishing up the soup and pouring out the wine. The man who had entered\r\nwith Chauvelin and whom Marguerite could not see, stood waiting close\r\nby the door.\r\n\r\nAt a brusque sign from Chauvelin, Brogard had hurried back to the inner\r\nroom, and the former now beckoned to the man who had accompanied him.\r\n\r\nIn him Marguerite at once recognised Desgas, Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s secretary and\r\nconfidential factotum, whom she had often seen in Paris, in the days\r\ngone by. He crossed the room, and for a moment or two listened\r\nattentively at the Brogards\xe2\x80\x99 door.\r\n \xe2\x80\x9cNot listening?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Chauvelin, curtly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, citoyen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor a second Marguerite dreaded lest Chauvelin should order Desgas to\r\nsearch the place; what would happen if she were to be discovered, she\r\nhardly dared to imagine. Fortunately, however, Chauvelin seemed more\r\nimpatient to talk to his secretary than afraid of spies, for he called\r\nDesgas quickly back to his side.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe English schooner?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe was lost sight of at sundown, citoyen,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Desgas, \xe2\x80\x9cbut was\r\nthen making west, towards Cap Gris Nez.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x94good!\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Chauvelin, \xe2\x80\x9cand now, about Captain Jutley?\xe2\x80\x94what\r\ndid he say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe assured me that all the orders you sent him last week have been\r\nimplicitly obeyed. All the roads which converge to this place have been\r\npatrolled night and day ever since: and the beach and cliffs have been\r\nmost rigorously searched and guarded.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes he know where this \xe2\x80\x98P\xc3\xa8re Blanchard\xe2\x80\x99s hut\xe2\x80\x99 is?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, citoyen, nobody seems to know of it by that name. There are any\r\namount of fishermen\xe2\x80\x99s huts all along the coast, of course . . . but . .\r\n.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99ll do. Now about to-night?\xe2\x80\x9d interrupted Chauvelin, impatiently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe roads and the beach are patrolled as usual, citoyen, and Captain\r\nJutley awaits further orders.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo back to him at once, then. Tell him to send reinforcements to the\r\nvarious patrols; and especially to those along the beach\xe2\x80\x94you\r\nunderstand?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChauvelin spoke curtly and to the point, and every word he uttered\r\nstruck at Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s heart like the death-knell of her fondest hopes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe men,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued, \xe2\x80\x9care to keep the sharpest possible look-out\r\nfor any stranger who may be walking, riding, or driving, along the road\r\nor the beach, more especially for a tall stranger, whom I need not\r\ndescribe further, as probably he will be disguised; but he cannot very\r\nwell conceal his height, except by stooping. You understand?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerfectly, citoyen,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Desgas.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs soon as any of the men have sighted a stranger, two of them are to\r\nkeep him in view. The man who loses sight of the tall stranger, after\r\nhe is once seen, will pay for his negligence with his life; but one man\r\nis to ride straight back here and report to me. Is that clear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbsolutely clear, citoyen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, then. Go and see Jutley at once. See the reinforcements\r\nstart off for the patrol duty, then ask the captain to let you have\r\nhalf-a-dozen more men and bring them here with you. You can be back in\r\nten minutes. Go\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDesgas saluted and went to the door.\r\n\r\nAs Marguerite, sick with horror, listened to Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s directions to\r\nhis underling, the whole of the plan for the capture of the Scarlet\r\nPimpernel became appallingly clear to her. Chauvelin wished that the\r\nfugitives should be left in false security waiting in their hidden\r\nretreat until Percy joined them. Then the daring plotter was to be\r\nsurrounded and caught red-handed, in the very act of aiding and\r\nabetting royalists, who were traitors to the republic. Thus, if his\r\ncapture were noised abroad, even the British Government could not\r\nlegally protest in his favour; having plotted with the enemies of the\r\nFrench Government, France had the right to put him to death.\r\n\r\nEscape for him and them would be impossible. All the roads patrolled\r\nand watched, the trap well set, the net, wide at present, but drawing\r\ntogether tighter and tighter, until it closed upon the daring plotter,\r\nwhose superhuman cunning even could not rescue him from its meshes now.\r\n\r\nDesgas was about to go, but Chauvelin once more called him back.\r\nMarguerite vaguely wondered what further devilish plans he could have\r\nformed, in order to entrap one brave man, alone, against two-score of\r\nothers. She looked at him as he turned to speak to Desgas; she could\r\njust see his face beneath the broad-brimmed _cur\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s_ hat. There was at\r\nthat moment so much deadly hatred, such fiendish malice in the thin\r\nface and pale, small eyes, that Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s last hope died in her\r\nheart, for she felt that from this man she could expect no mercy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had forgotten,\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Chauvelin, with a weird chuckle, as he\r\nrubbed his bony, talon-like hands one against the other, with a gesture\r\nof fiendish satisfaction. \xe2\x80\x9cThe tall stranger may show fight. In any\r\ncase no shooting, remember, except as a last resort. I want that tall\r\nstranger alive . . . if possible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe laughed, as Dante has told us that the devils laugh at sight of the\r\ntorture of the damned. Marguerite had thought that by now she had lived\r\nthrough the whole gamut of horror and anguish that human heart could\r\nbear; yet now, when Desgas left the house, and she remained alone in\r\nthis lonely, squalid room, with that fiend for company, she felt as if\r\nall that she had suffered was nothing compared with this. He continued\r\nto laugh and chuckle to himself for a while, rubbing his hands together\r\nin anticipation of his triumph.\r\n\r\nHis plans were well laid, and he might well triumph! Not a loophole was\r\nleft, through which the bravest, the most cunning man might escape.\r\nEvery road guarded, every corner watched, and in that lonely hut\r\nsomewhere on the coast, a small band of fugitives waiting for their\r\nrescuer, and leading him to his death\xe2\x80\x94nay! to worse than death. That\r\nfiend there, in a holy man\xe2\x80\x99s garb, was too much of a devil to allow a\r\nbrave man to die the quick, sudden death of a soldier at the post of\r\nduty.\r\n\r\nHe, above all, longed to have the cunning enemy, who had so long\r\nbaffled him, helpless in his power; he wished to gloat over him, to\r\nenjoy his downfall, to inflict upon him what moral and mental torture a\r\ndeadly hatred alone can devise. The brave eagle, captured, and with\r\nnoble wings clipped, was doomed to endure the gnawing of the rat. And\r\nshe, his wife, who loved him, and who had brought him to this, could do\r\nnothing to help him.\r\n\r\nNothing, save to hope for death by his side, and for one brief moment\r\nin which to tell him that her love\xe2\x80\x94whole, true and passionate\xe2\x80\x94was\r\nentirely his.\r\n\r\nChauvelin was now sitting close to the table; he had taken off his hat,\r\nand Marguerite could just see the outline of his thin profile and\r\npointed chin, as he bent over his meagre supper. He was evidently quite\r\ncontented, and awaited events with perfect calm; he even seemed to\r\nenjoy Brogard\xe2\x80\x99s unsavoury fare. Marguerite wondered how so much hatred\r\ncould lurk in one human being against another.\r\n\r\nSuddenly, as she watched Chauvelin, a sound caught her ear, which\r\nturned her very heart to stone. And yet that sound was not calculated\r\nto inspire anyone with horror, for it was merely the cheerful sound of\r\na gay, fresh voice singing lustily, \xe2\x80\x9cGod save the King!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXV.\r\nTHE EAGLE AND THE FOX\r\n\r\n\r\nMarguerite\xe2\x80\x99s breath stopped short; she seemed to feel her very life\r\nstanding still momentarily whilst she listened to that voice and to\r\nthat song. In the singer she had recognised her husband. Chauvelin,\r\ntoo, had heard it, for he darted a quick glance towards the door, then\r\nhurriedly took up his broad-brimmed hat and clapped it over his head.\r\n\r\nThe voice drew nearer; for one brief second the wild desire seized\r\nMarguerite to rush down the steps and fly across the room, to stop that\r\nsong at any cost, to beg the cheerful singer to fly\xe2\x80\x94fly for his life,\r\nbefore it be too late. She checked the impulse just in time. Chauvelin\r\nwould stop her before she reached the door, and, moreover, she had no\r\nidea if he had any soldiers posted within his call. Her impetuous act\r\nmight prove the death-signal of the man she would have died to save.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLong to reign over us,\r\nGod save the King!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nsang the voice more lustily than ever. The next moment the door was\r\nthrown open and there was dead silence for a second or so.\r\n\r\nMarguerite could not see the door: she held her breath, trying to\r\nimagine what was happening.\r\n\r\nPercy Blakeney on entering had, of course, at once caught sight of the\r\n_cur\xc3\xa9_ at the table; his hesitation lasted less than five seconds, the\r\nnext moment Marguerite saw his tall figure crossing the room, whilst he\r\ncalled in a loud, cheerful voice,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHello, there! no one about? Where\xe2\x80\x99s that fool Brogard?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe wore the magnificent coat and riding-suit which he had on when\r\nMarguerite last saw him at Richmond, so many hours ago. As usual, his\r\nget-up was absolutely irreproachable, the fine Mechlin lace at his neck\r\nand wrists was immaculate in its gossamer daintiness, his hands looked\r\nslender and white, his fair hair was carefully brushed, and he carried\r\nhis eye-glass with his usual affected gesture. In fact, at this moment,\r\nSir Percy Blakeney, Bart., might have been on his way to a garden-party\r\nat the Prince of Wales\xe2\x80\x99, instead of deliberately, cold-bloodedly\r\nrunning his head in a trap, set for him by his deadliest enemy.\r\n\r\nHe stood for a moment in the middle of the room, whilst Marguerite,\r\nabsolutely paralysed with horror, seemed unable even to breathe.\r\n\r\nEvery moment she expected that Chauvelin would give a signal, that the\r\nplace would fill with soldiers, that she would rush down and help Percy\r\nto sell his life dearly. As he stood there, suavely unconscious, she\r\nvery nearly screamed out to him,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFly, Percy!\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99tis your deadly enemy!\xe2\x80\x94fly before it be too late!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut she had not time even to do that, for the next moment Blakeney\r\nquietly walked to the table, and, jovially clapping the _cur\xc3\xa9_ on the\r\nback, said in his own drawly, affected way,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOdd\xe2\x80\x99s fish! . . . er . . . M. Chauvelin. . . . I vow I never thought\r\nof meeting you here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChauvelin, who had been in the very act of conveying soup to his mouth,\r\nfairly choked. His thin face became absolutely purple, and a violent\r\nfit of coughing saved this cunning representative of France from\r\nbetraying the most boundless surprise he had ever experienced. There\r\nwas no doubt that this bold move on the part of the enemy had been\r\nwholly unexpected, as far as he was concerned: and the daring impudence\r\nof it completely nonplussed him for the moment.\r\n\r\nObviously he had not taken the precaution of having the inn surrounded\r\nwith soldiers. Blakeney had evidently guessed that much, and no doubt\r\nhis resourceful brain had already formed some plan by which he could\r\nturn this unexpected interview to account.\r\n\r\nMarguerite up in the loft had not moved. She had made a solemn promise\r\nto Sir Andrew not to speak to her husband before strangers, and she had\r\nsufficient self-control not to throw herself unreasoningly and\r\nimpulsively across his plans. To sit still and watch these two men\r\ntogether was a terrible trial of fortitude. Marguerite had heard\r\nChauvelin give the orders for the patrolling of all the roads. She knew\r\nthat if Percy now left the \xe2\x80\x9cChat Gris\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94in whichever direction he\r\nhappened to go\xe2\x80\x94he could not go far without being sighted by some of\r\nCaptain Jutley\xe2\x80\x99s men on patrol. On the other hand, if he stayed, then\r\nDesgas would have time to come back with the half-dozen men Chauvelin\r\nhad specially ordered.\r\n\r\nThe trap was closing in, and Marguerite could do nothing but watch and\r\nwonder. The two men looked such a strange contrast, and of the two it\r\nwas Chauvelin who exhibited a slight touch of fear. Marguerite knew him\r\nwell enough to guess what was passing in his mind. He had no fear for\r\nhis own person, although he certainly was alone in a lonely inn with a\r\nman who was powerfully built, and who was daring and reckless beyond\r\nthe bounds of probability. She knew that Chauvelin would willingly have\r\nbraved perilous encounters for the sake of the cause he had at heart,\r\nbut what he did fear was that this impudent Englishman would, by\r\nknocking him down, double his own chances of escape; his underlings\r\nmight not succeed so well in capturing the Scarlet Pimpernel, when not\r\ndirected by the cunning hand and the shrewd brain, which had deadly\r\nhate for an incentive.\r\n\r\nEvidently, however, the representative of the French Government had\r\nnothing to fear for the moment, at the hands of his powerful adversary.\r\nBlakeney, with his most inane laugh and pleasant good-nature, was\r\nsolemnly patting him on the back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am so demmed sorry . . .\xe2\x80\x9d he was saying cheerfully, \xe2\x80\x9cso very sorry .\r\n. . I seem to have upset you . . . eating soup, too . . . nasty,\r\nawkward thing, soup . . . er . . . Begad!\xe2\x80\x94a friend of mine died once .\r\n. . er . . . choked . . . just like you . . . with a spoonful of soup.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd he smiled shyly, good-humouredly, down at Chauvelin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOdd\xe2\x80\x99s life!\xe2\x80\x9d he continued, as soon as the latter had somewhat\r\nrecovered himself, \xe2\x80\x9cbeastly hole this . . . ain\xe2\x80\x99t it now? La! you don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmind?\xe2\x80\x9d he added, apologetically, as he sat down on a chair close to the\r\ntable and drew the soup tureen towards him. \xe2\x80\x9cThat fool Brogard seems to\r\nbe asleep or something.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a second plate on the table, and he calmly helped himself to\r\nsoup, then poured himself out a glass of wine.\r\n\r\nFor a moment Marguerite wondered what Chauvelin would do. His disguise\r\nwas so good that perhaps he meant, on recovering himself, to deny his\r\nidentity: but Chauvelin was too astute to make such an obviously false\r\nand childish move, and already he too had stretched out his hand and\r\nsaid pleasantly,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am indeed charmed to see you, Sir Percy. You must excuse me\xe2\x80\x94h\xe2\x80\x99m\xe2\x80\x94I\r\nthought you the other side of the Channel. Sudden surprise almost took\r\nmy breath away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLa!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sir Percy, with a good-humoured grin, \xe2\x80\x9cit did that quite,\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t it\xe2\x80\x94er\xe2\x80\x94M.\xe2\x80\x94er\xe2\x80\x94Chaubertin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPardon me\xe2\x80\x94Chauvelin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beg pardon\xe2\x80\x94a thousand times. Yes\xe2\x80\x94Chauvelin of course. . . . Er . . .\r\nI never could cotton to foreign names. . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was calmly eating his soup, laughing with pleasant good-humour, as\r\nif he had come all the way to Calais for the express purpose of\r\nenjoying supper at this filthy inn, in the company of his arch-enemy.\r\n\r\nFor the moment Marguerite wondered why Percy did not knock the little\r\nFrenchman down then and there\xe2\x80\x94and no doubt something of the sort must\r\nhave darted through his mind, for every now and then his lazy eyes\r\nseemed to flash ominously, as they rested on the slight figure of\r\nChauvelin, who had now quite recovered himself and was also calmly\r\neating his soup.\r\n\r\nBut the keen brain, which had planned and carried through so many\r\ndaring plots, was too far-seeing to take unnecessary risks. This place,\r\nafter all, might be infested with spies; the innkeeper might be in\r\nChauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s pay. One call on Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s part might bring twenty men\r\nabout Blakeney\xe2\x80\x99s ears for aught he knew, and he might be caught and\r\ntrapped before he could help or, at least, warn the fugitives. This he\r\nwould not risk; he meant to help the others, to get _them_ safely away;\r\nfor he had pledged his word to them, and his word he _would_ keep. And\r\nwhilst he ate and chatted, he thought and planned, whilst, up in the\r\nloft, the poor, anxious woman racked her brain as to what she should\r\ndo, and endured agonies of longing to rush down to him, yet not daring\r\nto move for fear of upsetting his plans.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x9d Blakeney was saying jovially, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you . . . er . . .\r\nwere in holy orders.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI . . . er . . . hem . . .\xe2\x80\x9d stammered Chauvelin. The calm impudence of\r\nhis antagonist had evidently thrown him off his usual balance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, la! I should have known you anywhere,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Sir Percy,\r\nplacidly, as he poured himself out another glass of wine, \xe2\x80\x9calthough the\r\nwig and hat have changed you a bit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLud! they alter a man so . . . but . . . begad! I hope you don\xe2\x80\x99t mind\r\nmy having made the remark? . . . Demmed bad form making remarks. . . .\r\nI hope you don\xe2\x80\x99t mind?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, not at all\xe2\x80\x94hem! I hope Lady Blakeney is well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chauvelin,\r\nhurriedly changing the topic of conversation.\r\n\r\nBlakeney, with much deliberation, finished his plate of soup, drank his\r\nglass of wine, and, momentarily, it seemed to Marguerite as if he\r\nglanced quickly all round the room.\r\n \xe2\x80\x9cQuite well, thank you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last, drily. There was a pause,\r\n during which Marguerite could watch these two antagonists who,\r\n evidently in their minds, were measuring themselves against one\r\n another. She could see Percy almost full face where he sat at the\r\n table not ten yards from where she herself was crouching, puzzled, not\r\n knowing what to do, or what she should think. She had quite controlled\r\n her impulse by now of rushing down and disclosing herself to her\r\n husband. A man capable of acting a part, in the way he was doing at\r\n the present moment, did not need a woman\xe2\x80\x99s word to warn him to be\r\n cautious.\r\n\r\nMarguerite indulged in the luxury, dear to every tender woman\xe2\x80\x99s heart,\r\nof looking at the man she loved. She looked through the tattered\r\ncurtain, across at the handsome face of her husband, in whose lazy blue\r\neyes, and behind whose inane smile, she could now so plainly see the\r\nstrength, energy, and resourcefulness which had caused the Scarlet\r\nPimpernel to be reverenced and trusted by his followers. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are\r\nnineteen of us ready to lay down our lives for your husband, Lady\r\nBlakeney,\xe2\x80\x9d Sir Andrew had said to her; and as she looked at the\r\nforehead, low, but square and broad, the eyes, blue, yet deep-set and\r\nintense, the whole aspect of the man, of indomitable energy, hiding,\r\nbehind a perfectly acted comedy, his almost superhuman strength of will\r\nand marvellous ingenuity, she understood the fascination which he\r\nexercised over his followers, for had he not also cast his spells over\r\nher heart and her imagination?\r\n\r\nChauvelin, who was trying to conceal his impatience beneath his usual\r\nurbane manner, took a quick look at his watch. Desgas should not be\r\nlong: another two or three minutes, and this impudent Englishman would\r\nbe secure in the keeping of half a dozen of Captain Jutley\xe2\x80\x99s most\r\ntrusted men.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are on your way to Paris, Sir Percy?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked carelessly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOdd\xe2\x80\x99s life, no,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Blakeney, with a laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cOnly as far as\r\nLille\xe2\x80\x94not Paris for me . . . beastly uncomfortable place Paris, just\r\nnow . . . eh, Monsieur Chaubertin . . . beg pardon . . . Chauvelin!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot for an English gentleman like yourself, Sir Percy,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined\r\nChauvelin, sarcastically, \xe2\x80\x9cwho takes no interest in the conflict that\r\nis raging there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLa! you see it\xe2\x80\x99s no business of mine, and our demmed government is all\r\non your side of the business. Old Pitt daren\xe2\x80\x99t say \xe2\x80\x98Bo\xe2\x80\x99 to a goose. You\r\nare in a hurry, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, as Chauvelin once again took out his\r\nwatch; \xe2\x80\x9can appointment, perhaps. . . . I pray you take no heed of\r\nme. . . . My time\xe2\x80\x99s my own.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe rose from the table and dragged a chair to the hearth. Once more\r\nMarguerite was terribly tempted to go to him, for time was getting on;\r\nDesgas might be back at any moment with his men. Percy did not know\r\nthat and . . . oh! how horrible it all was\xe2\x80\x94and how helpless she felt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am in no hurry,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Percy, pleasantly, \xe2\x80\x9cbut, la! I don\xe2\x80\x99t want\r\nto spend any more time than I can help in this God-forsaken hole! But,\r\nbegad! sir,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, as Chauvelin had surreptitiously looked at his\r\nwatch for the third time, \xe2\x80\x9cthat watch of yours won\xe2\x80\x99t go any faster for\r\nall the looking you give it. You are expecting a friend, maybe?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye\xe2\x80\x94a friend!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a lady\xe2\x80\x94I trust, Monsieur l\xe2\x80\x99Abb\xc3\xa9,\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Blakeney; \xe2\x80\x9csurely the\r\nholy Church does not allow? . . . eh? . . . what! But, I say, come by\r\nthe fire . . . it\xe2\x80\x99s getting demmed cold.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe kicked the fire with the heel of his boot, making the logs blaze in\r\nthe old hearth. He seemed in no hurry to go, and apparently was quite\r\nunconscious of his immediate danger. He dragged another chair to the\r\nfire, and Chauvelin, whose impatience was by now quite beyond control,\r\nsat down beside the hearth, in such a way as to command a view of the\r\ndoor. Desgas had been gone nearly a quarter of an hour. It was quite\r\nplain to Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s aching senses that as soon as he arrived,\r\nChauvelin would abandon all his other plans with regard to the\r\nfugitives, and capture this impudent Scarlet Pimpernel at once.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHey, M. Chauvelin,\xe2\x80\x9d the latter was saying airily, \xe2\x80\x9ctell me, I pray\r\nyou, is your friend pretty? Demmed smart these little French women\r\nsometimes\xe2\x80\x94what? But I protest I need not ask,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, as he\r\ncarelessly strode back towards the supper-table. \xe2\x80\x9cIn matters of taste\r\nthe Church has never been backward. . . . Eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Chauvelin was not listening. His every faculty was now concentrated\r\non that door through which presently Desgas would enter. Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nthoughts, too, were centred there, for her ears had suddenly caught,\r\nthrough the stillness of the night, the sound of numerous and measured\r\ntreads some distance away.\r\n\r\nIt was Desgas and his men. Another three minutes and they would be\r\nhere! Another three minutes and the awful thing would have occurred:\r\nthe brave eagle would have fallen in the ferret\xe2\x80\x99s trap! She would have\r\nmoved now and screamed, but she dared not; for whilst she heard the\r\nsoldiers approaching, she was looking at Percy and watching his every\r\nmovement. He was standing by the table whereon the remnants of the\r\nsupper, plates, glasses, spoons, salt and pepper-pots were scattered\r\npell-mell. His back was turned to Chauvelin and he was still prattling\r\nalong in his own affected and inane way, but from his pocket he had\r\ntaken his snuff-box, and quickly and suddenly he emptied the contents\r\nof the pepper-pot into it.\r\n\r\nThen he again turned with an inane laugh to Chauvelin,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh? Did you speak, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChauvelin had been too intent on listening to the sound of those\r\napproaching footsteps, to notice what his cunning adversary had been\r\ndoing. He now pulled himself together, trying to look unconcerned in\r\nthe very midst of his anticipated triumph.\r\n \xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d he said presently, \xe2\x80\x9cthat is\xe2\x80\x94as you were saying, Sir Percy\xe2\x80\x94?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was saying,\xe2\x80\x9d said Blakeney, going up to Chauvelin, by the fire,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthat the Jew in Piccadilly has sold me better snuff this time than I\r\nhave ever tasted. Will you honour me, Monsieur l\xe2\x80\x99Abb\xc3\xa9?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe stood close to Chauvelin in his own careless, _d\xc3\xa9bonnaire_ way,\r\nholding out his snuff-box to his arch-enemy.\r\n\r\nChauvelin, who, as he told Marguerite once, had seen a trick or two in\r\nhis day, had never dreamed of this one. With one ear fixed on those\r\nfast-approaching footsteps, one eye turned to that door where Desgas\r\nand his men would presently appear, lulled into false security by the\r\nimpudent Englishman\xe2\x80\x99s airy manner, he never even remotely guessed the\r\ntrick which was being played upon him.\r\n\r\nHe took a pinch of snuff.\r\n\r\nOnly he, who has ever by accident sniffed vigorously a dose of pepper,\r\ncan have the faintest conception of the hopeless condition in which\r\nsuch a sniff would reduce any human being.\r\n\r\nChauvelin felt as if his head would burst\xe2\x80\x94sneeze after sneeze seemed\r\nnearly to choke him; he was blind, deaf, and dumb for the moment, and\r\nduring that moment Blakeney quietly, without the slightest haste, took\r\nup his hat, took some money out of his pocket, which he left on the\r\ntable, then calmly stalked out of the room!\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVI.\r\nTHE JEW\r\n\r\n\r\nIt took Marguerite some time to collect her scattered senses; the whole\r\nof this last short episode had taken place in less than a minute, and\r\nDesgas and the soldiers were still about two hundred yards away from\r\nthe \xe2\x80\x9cChat Gris.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen she realised what had happened, a curious mixture of joy and\r\nwonder filled her heart. It all was so neat, so ingenious. Chauvelin\r\nwas still absolutely helpless, far more so than he could even have been\r\nunder a blow from the fist, for now he could neither see, nor hear, nor\r\nspeak, whilst his cunning adversary had quietly slipped through his\r\nfingers.\r\n\r\nBlakeney was gone, obviously to try and join the fugitives at the P\xc3\xa8re\r\nBlanchard\xe2\x80\x99s hut. For the moment, true, Chauvelin was helpless; for the\r\nmoment the daring Scarlet Pimpernel had not been caught by Desgas and\r\nhis men. But all the roads and the beach were patrolled. Every place\r\nwas watched, and every stranger kept in sight. How far could Percy go,\r\nthus arrayed in his gorgeous clothes, without being sighted and\r\nfollowed?\r\n\r\nNow she blamed herself terribly for not having gone down to him sooner, and\r\ngiven him that word of warning and of love which, perhaps, after all, he\r\nneeded. He could not know of the orders which Chauvelin had given for his\r\ncapture, and even now, perhaps . . .\r\n\r\nBut before all these horrible thoughts had taken concrete form in her\r\nbrain, she heard the grounding of arms outside, close to the door, and\r\nDesgas\xe2\x80\x99 voice shouting \xe2\x80\x9cHalt!\xe2\x80\x9d to his men.\r\n\r\nChauvelin had partially recovered; his sneezing had become less\r\nviolent, and he had struggled to his feet. He managed to reach the door\r\njust as Desgas\xe2\x80\x99 knock was heard on the outside.\r\n\r\nChauvelin threw open the door, and before his secretary could say a\r\nword, he had managed to stammer between two sneezes\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe tall stranger\xe2\x80\x94quick!\xe2\x80\x94did any of you see him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere, citoyen?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Desgas, in surprise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere, man! through that door! not five minutes ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe saw nothing, citoyen! The moon is not yet up, and . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you are just five minutes too late, my friend,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chauvelin,\r\nwith concentrated fury.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCitoyen . . . I . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou did what I ordered you to do,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chauvelin, with impatience. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nknow that, but you were a precious long time about it. Fortunately,\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s not much harm done, or it had fared ill with you, Citoyen\r\nDesgas.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDesgas turned a little pale. There was so much rage and hatred in his\r\nsuperior\xe2\x80\x99s whole attitude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe tall stranger, citoyen\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d he stammered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas here, in this room, five minutes ago, having supper at that table.\r\nDamn his impudence! For obvious reasons, I dared not tackle him alone.\r\nBrogard is too big a fool, and that cursed Englishman appears to have\r\nthe strength of a bullock, and so he slipped away under your very\r\nnose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe cannot go far without being sighted, citoyen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCaptain Jutley sent forty men as reinforcements for the patrol duty:\r\ntwenty went down to the beach. He again assured me that the watch has\r\nbeen constant all day, and that no stranger could possibly get to the\r\nbeach, or reach a boat, without being sighted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s good.\xe2\x80\x94Do the men know their work?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey have had very clear orders, citoyen: and I myself spoke to those\r\nwho were about to start. They are to shadow\xe2\x80\x94as secretly as possible\xe2\x80\x94any\r\nstranger they may see, especially if he be tall, or stoop as if he\r\nwould disguise his height.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn no case to detain such a person, of course,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chauvelin,\r\neagerly. \xe2\x80\x9cThat impudent Scarlet Pimpernel would slip through clumsy\r\nfingers. We must let him get to the P\xc3\xa8re Blanchard\xe2\x80\x99s hut now; there\r\nsurround and capture him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe men understand that, citoyen, and also that, as soon as a tall\r\nstranger has been sighted, he must be shadowed, whilst one man is to\r\nturn straight back and report to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is right,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chauvelin, rubbing his hands, well pleased.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have further news for you, citoyen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA tall Englishman had a long conversation about three-quarters of an\r\nhour ago with a Jew, Reuben by name, who lives not ten paces from\r\nhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94and?\xe2\x80\x9d queried Chauvelin, impatiently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe conversation was all about a horse and cart, which the tall\r\nEnglishman wished to hire, and which was to have been ready for him by\r\neleven o\xe2\x80\x99clock.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is past that now. Where does that Reuben live?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA few minutes\xe2\x80\x99 walk from this door.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSend one of the men to find out if the stranger has driven off in\r\nReuben\xe2\x80\x99s cart.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, citoyen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDesgas went to give the necessary orders to one of the men. Not a word\r\nof this conversation between him and Chauvelin had escaped Marguerite,\r\nand every word they had spoken seemed to strike at her heart, with\r\nterrible hopelessness and dark foreboding.\r\n\r\nShe had come all this way, and with such high hopes and firm\r\ndetermination to help her husband, and so far she had been able to do\r\nnothing, but to watch, with a heart breaking with anguish, the meshes\r\nof the deadly net closing round the daring Scarlet Pimpernel.\r\n\r\nHe could not now advance many steps, without spying eyes to track and\r\ndenounce him. Her own helplessness struck her with the terrible sense\r\nof utter disappointment. The possibility of being of the slightest use\r\nto her husband had become almost _nil_, and her only hope rested in\r\nbeing allowed to share his fate, whatever it might ultimately be.\r\n\r\nFor the moment, even her chance of ever seeing the man she loved again,\r\nhad become a remote one. Still, she was determined to keep a close\r\nwatch over his enemy, and a vague hope filled her heart, that whilst\r\nshe kept Chauvelin in sight, Percy\xe2\x80\x99s fate might still be hanging in the\r\nbalance.\r\n\r\nDesgas had left Chauvelin moodily pacing up and down the room, whilst\r\nhe himself waited outside for the return of the man whom he had sent in\r\nsearch of Reuben. Thus several minutes went by. Chauvelin was evidently\r\ndevoured with impatience. Apparently he trusted no one: this last trick\r\nplayed upon him by the daring Scarlet Pimpernel had made him suddenly\r\ndoubtful of success, unless he himself was there to watch, direct and\r\nsuperintend the capture of this impudent Englishman.\r\n\r\nAbout five minutes later, Desgas returned, followed by an elderly Jew,\r\nin a dirty, threadbare gaberdine, worn greasy across the shoulders. His\r\nred hair, which he wore after the fashion of the Polish Jews, with the\r\ncorkscrew curls each side of his face, was plentifully sprinkled with\r\ngrey\xe2\x80\x94a general coating of grime, about his cheeks and his chin, gave\r\nhim a peculiarly dirty and loathsome appearance. He had the habitual\r\nstoop, those of his race affected in mock humility in past centuries,\r\nbefore the dawn of equality and freedom in matters of faith, and he\r\nwalked behind Desgas with the peculiar shuffling gait which has\r\nremained the characteristic of the Jew trader in continental Europe to\r\nthis day.\r\n\r\nChauvelin, who had all the Frenchman\xe2\x80\x99s prejudice against the despised\r\nrace, motioned to the fellow to keep at a respectful distance. The\r\ngroup of the three men were standing just underneath the hanging\r\noil-lamp, and Marguerite had a clear view of them all.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs this the man?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Chauvelin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, citoyen,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Desgas, \xe2\x80\x9cReuben could not be found, so\r\npresumably his cart has gone with the stranger; but this man here seems\r\nto know something, which he is willing to sell for a consideration.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Chauvelin, turning away with disgust from the loathsome\r\nspecimen of humanity before him.\r\n\r\nThe Jew, with characteristic patience, stood humbly on one side,\r\nleaning on a thick knotted staff, his greasy, broad-brimmed hat casting\r\na deep shadow over his grimy face, waiting for the noble Excellency to\r\ndeign to put some questions to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe citoyen tells me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chauvelin peremptorily to him, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you\r\nknow something of my friend, the tall Englishman, whom I desire to\r\nmeet. . . . _Morbleu!_ keep your distance, man,\xe2\x80\x9d he added hurriedly, as\r\nthe Jew took a quick and eager step forward.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, your Excellency,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the Jew, who spoke the language with\r\nthat peculiar lisp which denotes Eastern origin, \xe2\x80\x9cI and Reuben\r\nGoldstein met a tall Englishman, on the road, close by here this\r\nevening.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you speak to him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe spoke to us, your Excellency. He wanted to know if he could hire a\r\nhorse and cart to go down along the St. Martin Road, to a place he\r\nwanted to reach to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did you say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did not say anything,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Jew in an injured tone, \xe2\x80\x9cReuben\r\nGoldstein, that accursed traitor, that son of Belial . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCut that short, man,\xe2\x80\x9d interrupted Chauvelin, roughly, \xe2\x80\x9cand go on with\r\nyour story.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe took the words out of my mouth, your Excellency; when I was about\r\nto offer the wealthy Englishman my horse and cart, to take him\r\nwheresoever he chose, Reuben had already spoken, and offered his\r\nhalf-starved nag, and his broken-down cart.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what did the Englishman do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe listened to Reuben Goldstein, your Excellency, and put his hand in\r\nhis pocket then and there, and took out a handful of gold, which he\r\nshowed to that descendant of Beelzebub, telling him that all that would\r\nbe his, if the horse and cart were ready for him by eleven o\xe2\x80\x99clock.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd, of course, the horse and cart were ready?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell! they were ready in a manner, so to speak, your Excellency.\r\nReuben\xe2\x80\x99s nag was lame as usual; she refused to budge at first. It was\r\nonly after a time and with plenty of kicks, that she at last could be\r\nmade to move,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Jew with a malicious chuckle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen they started?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, they started about five minutes ago. I was disgusted with that\r\nstranger\xe2\x80\x99s folly. An Englishman too!\xe2\x80\x94He ought to have known Reuben\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nnag was not fit to drive.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut if he had no choice?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo choice, your Excellency?\xe2\x80\x9d protested the Jew, in a rasping voice,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cdid I not repeat to him a dozen times, that my horse and cart would\r\ntake him quicker, and more comfortably than Reuben\xe2\x80\x99s bag of bones. He\r\nwould not listen. Reuben is such a liar, and has such insinuating ways.\r\nThe stranger was deceived. If he was in a hurry, he would have had\r\nbetter value for his money by taking my cart.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have a horse and cart too, then?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Chauvelin, peremptorily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye! that I have, your Excellency, and if your Excellency wants to\r\ndrive . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you happen to know which way my friend went in Reuben Goldstein\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncart?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThoughtfully the Jew rubbed his dirty chin. Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s heart was\r\nbeating well-nigh to bursting. She had heard the peremptory question;\r\nshe looked anxiously at the Jew, but could not read his face beneath\r\nthe shadow of his broad-brimmed hat. Vaguely she felt somehow as if he\r\nheld Percy\xe2\x80\x99s fate in his long, dirty hands.\r\n\r\nThere was a long pause, whilst Chauvelin frowned impatiently at the\r\nstooping figure before him: at last the Jew slowly put his hand in his\r\nbreast pocket, and drew out from its capacious depths a number of\r\nsilver coins. He gazed at them thoughtfully, then remarked, in a quiet\r\ntone of voice,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is what the tall stranger gave me, when he drove away with\r\nReuben, for holding my tongue about him, and his doings.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChauvelin shrugged his shoulders impatiently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow much is there there?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwenty francs, your Excellency,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the Jew, \xe2\x80\x9cand I have been an\r\nhonest man all my life.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChauvelin without further comment took a few pieces of gold out of his\r\nown pocket, and leaving them in the palm of his hand, he allowed them\r\nto jingle as he held them out towards the Jew.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow many gold pieces are there in the palm of my hand?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked\r\nquietly.\r\n\r\nEvidently he had no desire to terrorise the man, but to conciliate him,\r\nfor his own purposes, for his manner was pleasant and suave. No doubt\r\nhe feared that threats of the guillotine, and various other persuasive\r\nmethods of that type, might addle the old man\xe2\x80\x99s brains, and that he\r\nwould be more likely to be useful through greed of gain, than through\r\nterror of death.\r\n\r\nThe eyes of the Jew shot a quick, keen glance at the gold in his\r\ninterlocutor\xe2\x80\x99s hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt least five, I should say, your Excellency,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied\r\nobsequiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEnough, do you think, to loosen that honest tongue of yours?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat does your Excellency wish to know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhether your horse and cart can take me to where I can find my friend\r\nthe tall stranger, who has driven off in Reuben Goldstein\xe2\x80\x99s cart?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy horse and cart can take your Honour there, where you please.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo a place called the P\xc3\xa8re Blanchard\xe2\x80\x99s hut?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour Honour has guessed?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Jew in astonishment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know the place?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know it, your Honour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich road leads to it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe St. Martin Road, your Honour, then a footpath from there to the\r\ncliffs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know the road?\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Chauvelin, roughly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEvery stone, every blade of grass, your Honour,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the Jew\r\nquietly.\r\n\r\nChauvelin without another word threw the five pieces of gold one by one\r\nbefore the Jew, who knelt down, and on his hands and knees struggled to\r\ncollect them. One rolled away, and he had some trouble to get it, for\r\nit had lodged underneath the dresser. Chauvelin quietly waited while\r\nthe old man scrambled on the floor, to find the piece of gold.\r\n\r\nWhen the Jew was again on his feet, Chauvelin said,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow soon can your horse and cart be ready?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are ready now, your Honour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot ten m\xc3\xa8tres from this door. Will your Excellency deign to look?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want to see it. How far can you drive me in it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs far as the P\xc3\xa8re Blanchard\xe2\x80\x99s hut, your Honour, and further than\r\nReuben\xe2\x80\x99s nag took your friend. I am sure that, not two leagues from\r\nhere, we shall come across that wily Reuben, his nag, his cart and the\r\ntall stranger all in a heap in the middle of the road.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow far is the nearest village from here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn the road which the Englishman took, Miquelon is the nearest\r\nvillage, not two leagues from here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere he could get fresh conveyance, if he wanted to go further?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe could\xe2\x80\x94if he ever got so far.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill your Excellency try?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Jew simply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is my intention,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chauvelin very quietly, \xe2\x80\x9cbut remember, if\r\nyou have deceived me, I shall tell off two of my most stalwart soldiers\r\nto give you such a beating, that your breath will perhaps leave your\r\nugly body for ever. But if we find my friend the tall Englishman,\r\neither on the road or at the P\xc3\xa8re Blanchard\xe2\x80\x99s hut, there will be ten\r\nmore gold pieces for you. Do you accept the bargain?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Jew again thoughtfully rubbed his chin. He looked at the money in\r\nhis hand, then at his stern interlocutor, and at Desgas, who had stood\r\nsilently behind him all this while. After a moment\xe2\x80\x99s pause, he said\r\ndeliberately,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI accept.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo and wait outside then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chauvelin, \xe2\x80\x9cand remember to stick to\r\nyour bargain, or by Heaven, I will keep to mine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith a final, most abject and cringing bow, the old Jew shuffled out of\r\nthe room. Chauvelin seemed pleased with his interview, for he rubbed\r\nhis hands together, with that usual gesture of his, of malignant\r\nsatisfaction.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy coat and boots,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to Desgas at last.\r\n\r\nDesgas went to the door, and apparently gave the necessary orders, for\r\npresently a soldier entered, carrying Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s coat, boots, and hat.\r\n\r\nHe took off his soutane, beneath which he was wearing close-fitting\r\nbreeches and a cloth waistcoat, and began changing his attire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou, citoyen, in the meanwhile,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to Desgas, \xe2\x80\x9cgo back to\r\nCaptain Jutley as fast as you can, and tell him to let you have another\r\ndozen men, and bring them with you along the St. Martin Road, where I\r\ndaresay you will soon overtake the Jew\xe2\x80\x99s cart with myself in it. There\r\nwill be hot work presently, if I mistake not, in the P\xc3\xa8re Blanchard\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhut. We shall corner our game there, I\xe2\x80\x99ll warrant, for this impudent\r\nScarlet Pimpernel has had the audacity\xe2\x80\x94or the stupidity, I hardly know\r\nwhich\xe2\x80\x94to adhere to his original plans. He has gone to meet de Tournay,\r\nSt. Just and the other traitors, which for the moment, I thought,\r\nperhaps, he did not intend to do. When we find them, there will be a\r\nband of desperate men at bay. Some of our men will, I presume, be put\r\n_hors de combat_. These royalists are good swordsmen, and the\r\nEnglishman is devilish cunning, and looks very powerful. Still, we\r\nshall be five against one at least. You can follow the cart closely\r\nwith your men, all along the St. Martin Road, through Miquelon. The\r\nEnglishman is ahead of us, and not likely to look behind him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhilst he gave these curt and concise orders, he had completed his\r\nchange of attire. The priest\xe2\x80\x99s costume had been laid aside, and he was\r\nonce more dressed in his usual dark, tight-fitting clothes. At last he\r\ntook up his hat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall have an interesting prisoner to deliver into your hands,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid with a chuckle, as with unwonted familiarity he took Desgas\xe2\x80\x99 arm,\r\nand led him towards the door. \xe2\x80\x9cWe won\xe2\x80\x99t kill him outright, eh, friend\r\nDesgas? The P\xc3\xa8re Blanchard\xe2\x80\x99s hut is\xe2\x80\x94an I mistake not\xe2\x80\x94a lonely spot upon\r\nthe beach, and our men will enjoy a bit of rough sport there with the\r\nwounded fox. Choose your men well, friend Desgas . . . of the sort who\r\nwould enjoy that type of sport\xe2\x80\x94eh? We must see that Scarlet Pimpernel\r\nwither a bit\xe2\x80\x94what?\xe2\x80\x94shrink and tremble, eh? . . . before we finally . .\r\n.\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he made an expressive gesture, whilst he laughed a low, evil laugh,\r\nwhich filled Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s soul with sickening horror.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChoose your men well, Citoyen Desgas,\xe2\x80\x9d he said once more, as he led\r\nhis secretary finally out of the room.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVII.\r\nON THE TRACK\r\n\r\n\r\nNever for a moment did Marguerite Blakeney hesitate. The last sounds\r\noutside the \xe2\x80\x9cChat Gris\xe2\x80\x9d had died away in the night. She had heard\r\nDesgas giving orders to his men, and then starting off towards the\r\nfort, to get a reinforcement of a dozen more men: six were not thought\r\nsufficient to capture the cunning Englishman, whose resourceful brain\r\nwas even more dangerous than his valour and his strength.\r\n\r\nThen a few minutes later, she heard the Jew\xe2\x80\x99s husky voice again,\r\nevidently shouting to his nag, then the rumble of wheels, and noise of\r\na rickety cart bumping over the rough road.\r\n\r\nInside the inn, everything was still. Brogard and his wife, terrified\r\nof Chauvelin, had given no sign of life; they hoped to be forgotten,\r\nand at any rate to remain unperceived: Marguerite could not even hear\r\ntheir usual volleys of muttered oaths.\r\n\r\nShe waited a moment or two longer, then she quietly slipped down the\r\nbroken stairs, wrapped her dark cloak closely round her and slipped out\r\nof the inn.\r\n\r\nThe night was fairly dark, sufficiently so at any rate to hide her dark\r\nfigure from view, whilst her keen ears kept count of the sound of the\r\ncart going on ahead. She hoped by keeping well within the shadow of the\r\nditches which lined the road, that she would not be seen by Desgas\xe2\x80\x99\r\nmen, when they approached, or by the patrols, which she concluded were\r\nstill on duty.\r\n\r\nThus she started to do this, the last stage of her weary journey,\r\nalone, at night, and on foot. Nearly three leagues to Miquelon, and\r\nthen on to the P\xc3\xa8re Blanchard\xe2\x80\x99s hut, wherever that fatal spot might be,\r\nprobably over rough roads: she cared not.\r\n\r\nThe Jew\xe2\x80\x99s nag could not get on very fast, and though she was weary with\r\nmental fatigue and nerve strain, she knew that she could easily keep up\r\nwith it, on a hilly road, where the poor beast, who was sure to be\r\nhalf-starved, would have to be allowed long and frequent rests. The\r\nroad lay some distance from the sea, bordered on either side by shrubs\r\nand stunted trees, sparsely covered with meagre foliage, all turning\r\naway from the North, with their branches looking in the semi-darkness,\r\nlike stiff, ghostly hair, blown by a perpetual wind.\r\n\r\nFortunately, the moon showed no desire to peep between the clouds, and\r\nMarguerite hugging the edge of the road, and keeping close to the low\r\nline of shrubs, was fairly safe from view. Everything around her was so\r\nstill: only from far, very far away, there came like a long, soft moan,\r\nthe sound of the distant sea.\r\n\r\nThe air was keen and full of brine; after that enforced period of\r\ninactivity, inside the evil-smelling, squalid inn, Marguerite would\r\nhave enjoyed the sweet scent of this autumnal night, and the distant\r\nmelancholy rumble of the waves; she would have revelled in the calm and\r\nstillness of this lonely spot, a calm, broken only at intervals by the\r\nstrident and mournful cry of some distant gull, and by the creaking of\r\nthe wheels, some way down the road: she would have loved the cool\r\natmosphere, the peaceful immensity of Nature, in this lonely part of\r\nthe coast: but her heart was too full of cruel foreboding, of a great\r\nache and longing for a being who had become infinitely dear to her.\r\n\r\nHer feet slipped on the grassy bank, for she thought it safest not to\r\nwalk near the centre of the road, and she found it difficult to keep up\r\na sharp pace along the muddy incline. She even thought it best not to\r\nkeep too near to the cart; everything was so still, that the rumble of\r\nthe wheels could not fail to be a safe guide.\r\n\r\nThe loneliness was absolute. Already the few dim lights of Calais lay\r\nfar behind, and on this road there was not a sign of human habitation,\r\nnot even the hut of a fisherman or of a woodcutter anywhere near; far\r\naway on her right was the edge of the cliff, below it the rough beach,\r\nagainst which the incoming tide was dashing itself with its constant,\r\ndistant murmur. And ahead the rumble of the wheels, bearing an\r\nimplacable enemy to his triumph.\r\n\r\nMarguerite wondered at what particular spot, on this lonely coast,\r\nPercy could be at this moment. Not very far surely, for he had had less\r\nthan a quarter of an hour\xe2\x80\x99s start of Chauvelin. She wondered if he knew\r\nthat in this cool, ocean-scented bit of France, there lurked many\r\nspies, all eager to sight his tall figure, to track him to where his\r\nunsuspecting friends waited for him, and then, to close the net over\r\nhim and them.\r\n\r\nChauvelin, on ahead, jolted and jostled in the Jew\xe2\x80\x99s vehicle, was\r\nnursing comfortable thoughts. He rubbed his hands together, with\r\ncontent, as he thought of the web which he had woven, and through which\r\nthat ubiquitous and daring Englishman could not hope to escape. As the\r\ntime went on, and the old Jew drove him leisurely but surely along the\r\ndark road, he felt more and more eager for the grand finale of this\r\nexciting chase after the mysterious Scarlet Pimpernel.\r\n The capture of the audacious plotter would be the finest leaf in\r\n Citoyen Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s wreath of glory. Caught, red-handed, on the spot,\r\n in the very act of aiding and abetting the traitors against the\r\n Republic of France, the Englishman could claim no protection from his\r\n own country. Chauvelin had, in any case, fully made up his mind that\r\n all intervention should come too late.\r\n\r\nNever for a moment did the slightest remorse enter his heart, as to the\r\nterrible position in which he had placed the unfortunate wife, who had\r\nunconsciously betrayed her husband. As a matter of fact, Chauvelin had\r\nceased even to think of her: she had been a useful tool, that was all.\r\n\r\nThe Jew\xe2\x80\x99s lean nag did little more than walk. She was going along at a\r\nslow jog trot, and her driver had to give her long and frequent halts.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre we a long way yet from Miquelon?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Chauvelin from time to\r\ntime.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot very far, your Honour,\xe2\x80\x9d was the uniform placid reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have not yet come across your friend and mine, lying in a heap in\r\nthe roadway,\xe2\x80\x9d was Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s sarcastic comment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPatience, noble Excellency,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined the son of Moses, \xe2\x80\x9cthey are\r\nahead of us.
