I can see the imprint of the cart wheels, driven by that\r\ntraitor, that son of the Amalekite.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are sure of the road?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs sure as I am of the presence of those ten gold pieces in the noble\r\nExcellency\xe2\x80\x99s pockets, which I trust will presently be mine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs soon as I have shaken hands with my friend the tall stranger, they\r\nwill certainly be yours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHark, what was that?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Jew suddenly.\r\n\r\nThrough the stillness, which had been absolute, there could now be\r\nheard distinctly the sound of horses\xe2\x80\x99 hoofs on the muddy road.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are soldiers,\xe2\x80\x9d he added in an awed whisper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStop a moment, I want to hear,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chauvelin.\r\n\r\nMarguerite had also heard the sound of galloping hoofs, coming towards\r\nthe cart, and towards herself. For some time she had been on the alert\r\nthinking that Desgas and his squad would soon overtake them, but these\r\ncame from the opposite direction, presumably from Miquelon. The\r\ndarkness lent her sufficient cover. She had perceived that the cart had\r\nstopped, and with utmost caution, treading noiselessly on the soft\r\nroad, she crept a little nearer.\r\n\r\nHer heart was beating fast, she was trembling in every limb; already\r\nshe had guessed what news these mounted men would bring. \xe2\x80\x9cEvery\r\nstranger on these roads or on the beach must be shadowed, especially if\r\nhe be tall or stoops as if he would disguise his height; when sighted a\r\nmounted messenger must at once ride back and report.\xe2\x80\x9d Those had been\r\nChauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s orders. Had then the tall stranger been sighted, and was\r\nthis the mounted messenger, come to bring the great news, that the\r\nhunted hare had run its head into the noose at last?\r\n\r\nMarguerite, realising that the cart had come to a standstill, managed\r\nto slip nearer to it in the darkness; she crept close up, hoping to get\r\nwithin earshot, to hear what the messenger had to say.\r\n\r\nShe heard the quick words of challenge\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLibert\xc3\xa9, Fraternit\xc3\xa9, Egalit\xc3\xa9!\xe2\x80\x9d then Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s quick query:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat news?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTwo men on horseback had halted beside the vehicle.\r\n\r\nMarguerite could see them silhouetted against the midnight sky. She\r\ncould hear their voices, and the snorting of their horses, and now,\r\nbehind her, some little distance off, the regular and measured tread of\r\na body of advancing men: Desgas and his soldiers.\r\n\r\nThere had been a long pause, during which, no doubt, Chauvelin\r\nsatisfied the men as to his identity, for presently, questions and\r\nanswers followed each other in quick succession.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have seen the stranger?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Chauvelin, eagerly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, citoyen, we have seen no tall stranger; we came by the edge of the\r\ncliff.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLess than a quarter of a league beyond Miquelon, we came across a\r\nrough construction of wood, which looked like the hut of a fisherman,\r\nwhere he might keep his tools and nets. When we first sighted it, it\r\nseemed to be empty, and at first we thought that there was nothing\r\nsuspicious about it, until we saw some smoke issuing through an\r\naperture at the side. I dismounted and crept close to it. It was then\r\nempty, but in one corner of the hut, there was a charcoal fire, and a\r\ncouple of stools were also in the hut. I consulted with my comrades,\r\nand we decided that they should take cover with the horses, well out of\r\nsight, and that I should remain on the watch, which I did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell! and did you see anything?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout half an hour later, I heard voices, citoyen, and presently, two\r\nmen came along towards the edge of the cliff; they seemed to me to have\r\ncome from the Lille Road. One was young, the other quite old. They were\r\ntalking in a whisper, to one another, and I could not hear what they\r\nsaid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOne was young, the other quite old. Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s aching heart almost stopped\r\nbeating as she listened: was the young one Armand?\xe2\x80\x94her brother?\xe2\x80\x94and the old one\r\nde Tournay\xe2\x80\x94were they the two fugitives who, unconsciously, were used as a\r\ndecoy, to entrap their fearless and noble rescuer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe two men presently went into the hut,\xe2\x80\x9d continued the soldier,\r\nwhilst Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s aching nerves seemed to catch the sound of\r\nChauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s triumphant chuckle, \xe2\x80\x9cand I crept nearer to it then. The hut\r\nis very roughly built, and I caught snatches of their conversation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes?\xe2\x80\x94Quick!\xe2\x80\x94What did you hear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe old man asked the young one if he were sure that was the right\r\nplace. \xe2\x80\x98Oh, yes,\xe2\x80\x99 he replied, \xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x99tis the place sure enough,\xe2\x80\x99 and by the\r\nlight of the charcoal fire he showed to his companion a paper, which he\r\ncarried. \xe2\x80\x98Here is the plan,\xe2\x80\x99 he said, \xe2\x80\x98which he gave me before I left\r\nLondon. We were to adhere strictly to that plan, unless I had contrary\r\norders, and I have had none. Here is the road we followed, see . . .\r\nhere the fork . . . here we cut across the St. Martin Road . . . and\r\nhere is the footpath which brought us to the edge of the cliff.\xe2\x80\x99 I must\r\nhave made a slight noise then, for the young man came to the door of\r\nthe hut, and peered anxiously all round him. When he again joined his\r\ncompanion, they whispered so low, that I could no longer hear them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x94and?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Chauvelin, impatiently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere were six of us altogether, patrolling that part of the beach, so\r\nwe consulted together, and thought it best that four should remain\r\nbehind and keep the hut in sight, and I and my comrade rode back at\r\nonce to make report of what we had seen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou saw nothing of the tall stranger?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing, citoyen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf your comrades see him, what would they do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot lose sight of him for a moment, and if he showed signs of escape,\r\nor any boat came in sight, they would close in on him, and, if\r\nnecessary, they would shoot: the firing would bring the rest of the\r\npatrol to the spot. In any case they would not let the stranger go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye! but I did not want the stranger hurt\xe2\x80\x94not just yet,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured\r\nChauvelin, savagely, \xe2\x80\x9cbut there, you\xe2\x80\x99ve done your best. The Fates grant\r\nthat I may not be too late . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe met half a dozen men just now, who have been patrolling this road\r\nfor several hours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey have seen no stranger either.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYet he is on ahead somewhere, in a cart or else . . . . Here! there is\r\nnot a moment to lose. How far is that hut from here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout a couple of leagues, citoyen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can find it again?\xe2\x80\x94at once?\xe2\x80\x94without hesitation?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have absolutely no doubt, citoyen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe footpath, to the edge of the cliff?\xe2\x80\x94Even in the dark?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not a dark night, citoyen, and I know I can find my way,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nrepeated the soldier firmly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFall in behind then. Let your comrade take both your horses back to\r\nCalais. You won\xe2\x80\x99t want them. Keep beside the cart, and direct the Jew\r\nto drive straight ahead; then stop him, within a quarter of a league of\r\nthe footpath; see that he takes the most direct road.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhilst Chauvelin spoke, Desgas and his men were fast approaching, and\r\nMarguerite could hear their footsteps within a hundred yards behind her\r\nnow. She thought it unsafe to stay where she was, and unnecessary too,\r\nas she had heard enough. She seemed suddenly to have lost all faculty\r\neven for suffering: her heart, her nerves, her brain seemed to have\r\nbecome numb after all these hours of ceaseless anguish, culminating in\r\nthis awful despair.\r\n\r\nFor now there was absolutely not the faintest hope. Within two short\r\nleagues of this spot, the fugitives were waiting for their brave\r\ndeliverer. He was on his way, somewhere on this lonely road, and\r\npresently he would join them; then the well-laid trap would close, two\r\ndozen men, led by one whose hatred was as deadly as his cunning was\r\nmalicious, would close round the small band of fugitives, and their\r\ndaring leader. They would all be captured. Armand, according to\r\nChauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s pledged word, would be restored to her, but her husband,\r\nPercy, whom with every breath she drew she seemed to love and worship\r\nmore and more, he would fall into the hands of a remorseless enemy, who\r\nhad no pity for a brave heart, no admiration for the courage of a noble\r\nsoul, who would show nothing but hatred for the cunning antagonist, who\r\nhad baffled him so long.\r\n\r\nShe heard the soldier giving a few brief directions to the Jew, then\r\nshe retired quickly to the edge of the road, and cowered behind some\r\nlow shrubs, whilst Desgas and his men came up.\r\n\r\nAll fell in noiselessly behind the cart, and slowly they all started\r\ndown the dark road. Marguerite waited until she reckoned that they were\r\nwell outside the range of earshot, then, she too in the darkness, which\r\nsuddenly seemed to have become more intense, crept noiselessly along.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVIII.\r\nTHE P\xc3\x88RE BLANCHARD\xe2\x80\x99S HUT\r\n\r\n\r\nAs in a dream, Marguerite followed on; the web was drawing more and\r\nmore tightly every moment round the beloved life, which had become\r\ndearer than all. To see her husband once again, to tell him how she had\r\nsuffered, how much she had wronged, and how little understood him, had\r\nbecome now her only aim. She had abandoned all hope of saving him: she\r\nsaw him gradually hemmed in on all sides, and, in despair, she gazed\r\nround her into the darkness, and wondered whence he would presently\r\ncome, to fall into the death-trap which his relentless enemy had\r\nprepared for him.\r\n\r\nThe distant roar of the waves now made her shudder; the occasional\r\ndismal cry of an owl, or a sea-gull, filled her with unspeakable\r\nhorror. She thought of the ravenous beasts\xe2\x80\x94in human shape\xe2\x80\x94who lay in\r\nwait for their prey, and destroyed them, as mercilessly as any hungry\r\nwolf, for the satisfaction of their own appetite of hate. Marguerite\r\nwas not afraid of the darkness, she only feared that man, on ahead, who\r\nwas sitting at the bottom of a rough wooden cart, nursing thoughts of\r\nvengeance, which would have made the very demons in hell chuckle with\r\ndelight.\r\n\r\nHer feet were sore. Her knees shook under her, from sheer bodily\r\nfatigue. For days now she had lived in a wild turmoil of excitement;\r\nshe had not had a quiet rest for three nights; now, she had walked on a\r\nslippery road for nearly two hours, and yet her determination never\r\nswerved for a moment. She would see her husband, tell him all, and, if\r\nhe was ready to forgive the crime, which she had committed in her blind\r\nignorance, she would yet have the happiness of dying by his side.\r\n\r\nShe must have walked on almost in a trance, instinct alone keeping her\r\nup, and guiding her in the wake of the enemy, when suddenly her ears,\r\nattuned to the slightest sound, by that same blind instinct, told her\r\nthat the cart had stopped, and that the soldiers had halted. They had\r\ncome to their destination. No doubt on the right, somewhere close\r\nahead, was the footpath that led to the edge of the cliff and to the\r\nhut.\r\n\r\nHeedless of any risks, she crept quite close up to where Chauvelin\r\nstood, surrounded by his little troop: he had descended from the cart,\r\nand was giving some orders to the men. These she wanted to hear: what\r\nlittle chance she yet had, of being useful to Percy, consisted in\r\nhearing absolutely every word of his enemy\xe2\x80\x99s plans.\r\n\r\nThe spot where all the party had halted must have lain some eight\r\nhundred m\xc3\xa8tres from the coast; the sound of the sea came only very\r\nfaintly, as from a distance. Chauvelin and Desgas, followed by the\r\nsoldiers, had turned off sharply to the right of the road, apparently\r\non to the footpath, which led to the cliffs. The Jew had remained on\r\nthe road, with his cart and nag.\r\n\r\nMarguerite, with infinite caution, and literally crawling on her hands\r\nand knees, had also turned off to the right: to accomplish this she had\r\nto creep through the rough, low shrubs, trying to make as little noise\r\nas possible as she went along, tearing her face and hands against the\r\ndry twigs, intent only upon hearing without being seen or heard.\r\nFortunately\xe2\x80\x94as is usual in this part of France\xe2\x80\x94the footpath was\r\nbordered by a low, rough hedge, beyond which was a dry ditch, filled\r\nwith coarse grass. In this Marguerite managed to find shelter; she was\r\nquite hidden from view, yet could contrive to get within three yards of\r\nwhere Chauvelin stood, giving orders to his men.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow,\xe2\x80\x9d he was saying in a low and peremptory whisper, \xe2\x80\x9cwhere is the\r\nP\xc3\xa8re Blanchard\xe2\x80\x99s hut?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout eight hundred m\xc3\xa8tres from here, along the footpath,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nsoldier who had lately been directing the party, \xe2\x80\x9cand half-way down the\r\ncliff.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery good. You shall lead us. Before we begin to descend the cliff,\r\nyou shall creep down to the hut, as noiselessly as possible, and\r\nascertain if the traitor royalists are there? Do you understand?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI understand, citoyen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow listen very attentively, all of you,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Chauvelin,\r\nimpressively, and addressing the soldiers collectively, \xe2\x80\x9cfor after this\r\nwe may not be able to exchange another word, so remember every syllable\r\nI utter, as if your very lives depended on your memory. Perhaps they\r\ndo,\xe2\x80\x9d he added drily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe listen, citoyen,\xe2\x80\x9d said Desgas, \xe2\x80\x9cand a soldier of the Republic never\r\nforgets an order.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou, who have crept up to the hut, will try to peep inside. If an\r\nEnglishman is there with those traitors, a man who is tall above the\r\naverage, or who stoops as if he would disguise his height, then give a\r\nsharp, quick whistle as a signal to your comrades. All of you,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nadded, once more speaking to the soldiers collectively, \xe2\x80\x9cthen quickly\r\nsurround and rush into the hut, and each seize one of the men there,\r\nbefore they have time to draw their firearms; if any of them struggle,\r\nshoot at their legs or arms, but on no account kill the tall man. Do\r\nyou understand?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe understand, citoyen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe man who is tall above the average is probably also strong above\r\nthe average; it will take four or five of you at least to overpower\r\nhim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a little pause, then Chauvelin continued,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf the royalist traitors are still alone, which is more than likely to\r\nbe the case, then warn your comrades who are lying in wait there, and\r\nall of you creep and take cover behind the rocks and boulders round the\r\nhut, and wait there, in dead silence, until the tall Englishman\r\narrives; then only rush the hut, when he is safely within its doors.\r\nBut remember that you must be as silent as the wolf is at night, when\r\nhe prowls around the pens. I do not wish those royalists to be on the\r\nalert\xe2\x80\x94the firing of a pistol, a shriek or call on their part would be\r\nsufficient, perhaps, to warn the tall personage to keep clear of the\r\ncliffs, and of the hut, and,\xe2\x80\x9d he added emphatically, \xe2\x80\x9cit is the tall\r\nEnglishman whom it is your duty to capture to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou shall be implicitly obeyed, citoyen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen get along as noiselessly as possible, and I will follow you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat about the Jew, citoyen?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Desgas, as silently like noiseless\r\nshadows, one by one the soldiers began to creep along the rough and\r\nnarrow footpath.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, yes; I had forgotten the Jew,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chauvelin, and, turning\r\ntowards the Jew, he called him peremptorily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere, you . . . Aaron, Moses, Abraham, or whatever your confounded\r\nname may be,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to the old man, who had quietly stood beside his\r\nlean nag, as far away from the soldiers as possible.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBenjamin Rosenbaum, so it please your Honour,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied humbly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt does not please me to hear your voice, but it does please me to\r\ngive you certain orders, which you will find it wise to obey.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo it please your Honour . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHold your confounded tongue. You shall stay here, do you hear? with\r\nyour horse and cart until our return. You are on no account to utter\r\nthe faintest sound, or even to breathe louder than you can help; nor\r\nare you, on any consideration whatever, to leave your post, until I\r\ngive you orders to do so. Do you understand?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut your Honour\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d protested the Jew pitiably.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no question of \xe2\x80\x98but\xe2\x80\x99 or of any argument,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chauvelin, in\r\na tone that made the timid old man tremble from head to foot. \xe2\x80\x9cIf, when\r\nI return, I do not find you here, I most solemnly assure you that,\r\nwherever you may try to hide yourself, I can find you, and that\r\npunishment swift, sure and terrible, will sooner or later overtake you.\r\nDo you hear me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut your Excellency . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI said, do you hear me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe soldiers had all crept away; the three men stood alone together in\r\nthe dark and lonely road, with Marguerite there, behind the hedge,\r\nlistening to Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s orders, as she would to her own death\r\nsentence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI heard your Honour,\xe2\x80\x9d protested the Jew again, while he tried to draw\r\nnearer to Chauvelin, \xe2\x80\x9cand I swear by Abraham, Isaac and Jacob that I\r\nwould obey your Honour most absolutely, and that I would not move from\r\nthis place until your Honour once more deigned to shed the light of\r\nyour countenance upon your humble servant; but remember, your Honour, I\r\nam a poor old man; my nerves are not as strong as those of a young\r\nsoldier. If midnight marauders should come prowling round this lonely\r\nroad, I might scream or run in my fright! And is my life to be forfeit,\r\nis some terrible punishment to come on my poor old head for that which\r\nI cannot help?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Jew seemed in real distress; he was shaking from head to foot.\r\nClearly he was not the man to be left by himself on this lonely road.\r\nThe man spoke truly; he might unwittingly, in sheer terror, utter the\r\nshriek that might prove a warning to the wily Scarlet Pimpernel.\r\n\r\nChauvelin reflected for a moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill your horse and cart be safe alone, here, do you think?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked\r\nroughly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI fancy, citoyen,\xe2\x80\x9d here interposed Desgas, \xe2\x80\x9cthat they will be safer\r\nwithout that dirty, cowardly Jew than with him. There seems no doubt\r\nthat, if he gets scared, he will either make a bolt of it, or shriek\r\nhis head off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what am I to do with the brute?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you send him back to Calais, citoyen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, for we shall want him to drive back the wounded presently,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nChauvelin, with grim significance.\r\n\r\nThere was a pause again\xe2\x80\x94Desgas, waiting for the decision of his chief,\r\nand the old Jew whining beside his nag.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you lazy, lumbering old coward,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chauvelin at last, \xe2\x80\x9cyou\r\nhad better shuffle along behind us. Here, Citoyen Desgas, tie this\r\nhandkerchief tightly round the fellow\xe2\x80\x99s mouth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChauvelin handed a scarf to Desgas, who solemnly began winding it round\r\nthe Jew\xe2\x80\x99s mouth. Meekly Benjamin Rosenbaum allowed himself to be\r\ngagged; he, evidently, preferred this uncomfortable state to that of\r\nbeing left alone, on the dark St. Martin Road. Then the three men fell\r\nin line.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuick!\xe2\x80\x9d said Chauvelin, impatiently, \xe2\x80\x9cwe have already wasted much\r\nvaluable time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd the firm footsteps of Chauvelin and Desgas, the shuffling gait of\r\nthe old Jew, soon died away along the footpath.\r\n\r\nMarguerite had not lost a single one of Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s words of command.\r\nHer every nerve was strained to completely grasp the situation first,\r\nthen to make a final appeal to those wits which had so often been\r\ncalled the sharpest in Europe, and which alone might be of service now.\r\n\r\nCertainly the situation was desperate enough; a tiny band of\r\nunsuspecting men, quietly awaiting the arrival of their rescuer, who\r\nwas equally unconscious of the trap laid for them all. It seemed so\r\nhorrible, this net, as it were drawn in a circle, at dead of night, on\r\na lonely beach, round a few defenceless men, defenceless because they\r\nwere tricked and unsuspecting; of these one was the husband she\r\nidolised, another the brother she loved. She vaguely wondered who the\r\nothers were, who were also calmly waiting for the Scarlet Pimpernel,\r\nwhile death lurked behind every boulder of the cliffs.\r\n\r\nFor the moment she could do nothing but follow the soldiers and\r\nChauvelin. She feared to lose her way, or she would have rushed forward\r\nand found that wooden hut, and perhaps been in time to warn the\r\nfugitives and their brave deliverer yet.\r\n\r\nFor a second, the thought flashed through her mind of uttering the\r\npiercing shrieks, which Chauvelin seemed to dread, as a possible\r\nwarning to the Scarlet Pimpernel and his friends\xe2\x80\x94in the wild hope that\r\nthey would hear, and have yet time to escape before it was too late.\r\nBut she did not know how far from the edge of the cliff she was; she\r\ndid not know if her shrieks would reach the ears of the doomed men. Her\r\neffort might be premature, and she would never be allowed to make\r\nanother. Her mouth would be securely gagged, like that of the Jew, and\r\nshe, a helpless prisoner in the hands of Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s men.\r\n\r\nLike a ghost she flitted noiselessly behind that hedge: she had taken\r\nher shoes off, and her stockings were by now torn off her feet. She\r\nfelt neither soreness nor weariness; indomitable will to reach her\r\nhusband in spite of adverse Fate, and of a cunning enemy, killed all\r\nsense of bodily pain within her, and rendered her instincts doubly\r\nacute.\r\n\r\nShe heard nothing save the soft and measured footsteps of Percy\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nenemies on in front; she saw nothing but\xe2\x80\x94in her mind\xe2\x80\x99s eye\xe2\x80\x94that wooden\r\nhut, and he, her husband, walking blindly to his doom.\r\n\r\nSuddenly, those same keen instincts within her made her pause in her\r\nmad haste, and cower still further within the shadow of the hedge. The\r\nmoon, which had proved a friend to her by remaining hidden behind a\r\nbank of clouds, now emerged in all the glory of an early autumn night,\r\nand in a moment flooded the weird and lonely landscape with a rush of\r\nbrilliant light.\r\n\r\nThere, not two hundred m\xc3\xa8tres ahead, was the edge of the cliff, and\r\nbelow, stretching far away to free and happy England, the sea rolled on\r\nsmoothly and peaceably. Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s gaze rested for an instant on the\r\nbrilliant, silvery waters; and as she gazed, her heart, which had been\r\nnumb with pain for all these hours, seemed to soften and distend, and\r\nher eyes filled with hot tears: not three miles away, with white sails\r\nset, a graceful schooner lay in wait.\r\n\r\nMarguerite had guessed rather than recognised her. It was the _Day\r\nDream_, Percy\xe2\x80\x99s favourite yacht, with old Briggs, that prince of\r\nskippers, aboard, and all her crew of British sailors: her white sails,\r\nglistening in the moonlight, seemed to convey a message to Marguerite\r\nof joy and hope, which yet she feared could never be. She waited there,\r\nout at sea, waited for her master, like a beautiful white bird all\r\nready to take flight, and he would never reach her, never see her\r\nsmooth deck again, never gaze any more on the white cliffs of England,\r\nthe land of liberty and of hope.\r\n\r\nThe sight of the schooner seemed to infuse into the poor, wearied woman\r\nthe superhuman strength of despair. There was the edge of the cliff,\r\nand some way below was the hut, where presently, her husband would meet\r\nhis death. But the moon was out: she could see her way now: she would\r\nsee the hut from a distance, run to it, rouse them all, warn them at\r\nany rate to be prepared and to sell their lives dearly, rather than be\r\ncaught like so many rats in a hole.\r\n\r\nShe stumbled on behind the hedge in the low, thick grass of the ditch.\r\nShe must have run on very fast, and had outdistanced Chauvelin and\r\nDesgas, for presently she reached the edge of the cliff, and heard\r\ntheir footsteps distinctly behind her. But only a very few yards away,\r\nand now the moonlight was full upon her, her figure must have been\r\ndistinctly silhouetted against the silvery background of the sea.\r\n\r\nOnly for a moment, though; the next she had cowered, like some animal\r\ndoubled up within itself. She peeped down the great rugged cliffs\xe2\x80\x94the\r\ndescent would be easy enough, as they were not precipitous, and the\r\ngreat boulders afforded plenty of foothold. Suddenly, as she gazed, she\r\nsaw at some little distance on her left, and about midway down the\r\ncliffs, a rough wooden construction, through the walls of which a tiny\r\nred light glimmered like a beacon. Her very heart seemed to stand\r\nstill, the eagerness of joy was so great that it felt like an awful\r\npain.\r\n\r\nShe could not gauge how distant the hut was, but without hesitation she\r\nbegan the steep descent, creeping from boulder to boulder, caring\r\nnothing for the enemy behind, or for the soldiers, who evidently had\r\nall taken cover since the tall Englishman had not yet appeared.\r\n\r\nOn she pressed, forgetting the deadly foe on her track, running,\r\nstumbling, foot-sore, half-dazed, but still on . . . When, suddenly, a\r\ncrevice, or stone, or slippery bit of rock, threw her violently to the\r\nground. She struggled again to her feet, and started running forward\r\nonce more to give them that timely warning, to beg them to flee before\r\nhe came, and to tell him to keep away\xe2\x80\x94away from this death-trap\xe2\x80\x94away\r\nfrom this awful doom. But now she realised that other steps, quicker\r\nthan her own, were already close at her heels. The next instant a hand\r\ndragged at her skirt, and she was down on her knees again, whilst\r\nsomething was wound round her mouth to prevent her uttering a scream.\r\n\r\nBewildered, half frantic with the bitterness of disappointment, she\r\nlooked round her helplessly, and, bending down quite close to her, she\r\nsaw through the mist, which seemed to gather round her, a pair of keen,\r\nmalicious eyes, which appeared to her excited brain to have a weird,\r\nsupernatural green light in them.\r\n She lay in the shadow of a great boulder; Chauvelin could not see her\r\n features, but he passed his thin, white fingers over her face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA woman!\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered, \xe2\x80\x9cby all the Saints in the calendar.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe cannot let her loose, that\xe2\x80\x99s certain,\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered to himself. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwonder now . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSuddenly he paused, and after a few seconds of deadly silence, he gave\r\nforth a long, low, curious chuckle, while once again Marguerite felt,\r\nwith a horrible shudder, his thin fingers wandering over her face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear me! dear me!\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered, with affected gallantry, \xe2\x80\x9cthis is\r\nindeed a charming surprise,\xe2\x80\x9d and Marguerite felt her resistless hand\r\nraised to Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s thin, mocking lips.\r\n\r\nThe situation was indeed grotesque, had it not been at the same time so\r\nfearfully tragic: the poor, weary woman, broken in spirit, and half\r\nfrantic with the bitterness of her disappointment, receiving on her\r\nknees the _banal_ gallantries of her deadly enemy.\r\n\r\nHer senses were leaving her; half choked with the tight grip round her\r\nmouth, she had no strength to move or to utter the faintest sound. The\r\nexcitement which all along had kept up her delicate body seemed at once\r\nto have subsided, and the feeling of blank despair to have completely\r\nparalysed her brain and nerves.\r\n\r\nChauvelin must have given some directions, which she was too dazed to\r\nhear, for she felt herself lifted from off her feet: the bandage round\r\nher mouth was made more secure, and a pair of strong arms carried her\r\ntowards that tiny, red light, on ahead, which she had looked upon as a\r\nbeacon and the last faint glimmer of hope.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIX.\r\nTRAPPED\r\n\r\n\r\nShe did not know how long she was thus carried along, she had lost all\r\nnotion of time and space, and for a few seconds tired nature,\r\nmercifully, deprived her of consciousness.\r\n\r\nWhen she once more realised her state, she felt that she was placed\r\nwith some degree of comfort upon a man\xe2\x80\x99s coat, with her back resting\r\nagainst a fragment of rock. The moon was hidden again behind some\r\nclouds, and the darkness seemed in comparison more intense. The sea was\r\nroaring some two hundred feet below her, and on looking all round she\r\ncould no longer see any vestige of the tiny glimmer of red light.\r\n\r\nThat the end of the journey had been reached, she gathered from the\r\nfact that she heard rapid questions and answers spoken in a whisper\r\nquite close to her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are four men in there, citoyen; they are sitting by the fire,\r\nand seem to be waiting quietly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe hour?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNearly two o\xe2\x80\x99clock.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe tide?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cComing in quickly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe schooner?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cObviously an English one, lying some three kilometres out. But we\r\ncannot see her boat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave the men taken cover?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, citoyen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey will not blunder?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey will not stir until the tall Englishman comes, then they will\r\nsurround and overpower the five men.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRight. And the lady?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStill dazed, I fancy. She\xe2\x80\x99s close beside you, citoyen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the Jew?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s gagged, and his legs strapped together. He cannot move or\r\nscream.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood. Then have your gun ready, in case you want it. Get close to the\r\nhut and leave me to look after the lady.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDesgas evidently obeyed, for Marguerite heard him creeping away along\r\nthe stony cliff, then she felt that a pair of warm, thin, talon-like\r\nhands took hold of both her own, and held them in a grip of steel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBefore that handkerchief is removed from your pretty mouth, fair\r\nlady,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Chauvelin close to her ear, \xe2\x80\x9cI think it right to give\r\nyou one small word of warning. What has procured me the honour of being\r\nfollowed across the Channel by so charming a companion, I cannot, of\r\ncourse, conceive, but, if I mistake not, the purpose of this flattering\r\nattention is not one that would commend itself to my vanity, and I\r\nthink that I am right in surmising, moreover, that the first sound\r\nwhich your pretty lips would utter, as soon as the cruel gag is\r\nremoved, would be one that would perhaps prove a warning to the cunning\r\nfox, which I have been at such pains to track to his lair.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe paused a moment, while the steel-like grasp seemed to tighten round\r\nher wrist; then he resumed in the same hurried whisper:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cInside that hut, if again I am not mistaken, your brother, Armand St.\r\nJust, waits with that traitor de Tournay, and two other men unknown to\r\nyou, for the arrival of the mysterious rescuer, whose identity has for\r\nso long puzzled our Committee of Public Safety\xe2\x80\x94the audacious Scarlet\r\nPimpernel. No doubt if you scream, if there is a scuffle here, if shots\r\nare fired, it is more than likely that the same long legs that brought\r\nthis scarlet enigma here, will as quickly take him to some place of\r\nsafety. The purpose then, for which I have travelled all these miles,\r\nwill remain unaccomplished. On the other hand it only rests with\r\nyourself that your brother\xe2\x80\x94Armand\xe2\x80\x94shall be free to go off with you\r\nto-night if you like, to England, or any other place of safety.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarguerite could not utter a sound, as the handkerchief was wound very\r\ntightly round her mouth, but Chauvelin was peering through the darkness\r\nvery closely into her face; no doubt too her hand gave a responsive\r\nappeal to his last suggestion, for presently he continued:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat I want you to do to ensure Armand\xe2\x80\x99s safety is a very simple\r\nthing, dear lady.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s hand seemed to convey to his, in response.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo remain\xe2\x80\x94on this spot, without uttering a sound, until I give you\r\nleave to speak. Ah! but I think you will obey,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, with that\r\nfunny dry chuckle of his as Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s whole figure seemed to\r\nstiffen, in defiance of this order, \xe2\x80\x9cfor let me tell you that if you\r\nscream, nay! if you utter one sound, or attempt to move from here, my\r\nmen\xe2\x80\x94there are thirty of them about\xe2\x80\x94will seize St. Just, de Tournay, and\r\ntheir two friends, and shoot them here\xe2\x80\x94by my orders\xe2\x80\x94before your eyes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarguerite had listened to her implacable enemy\xe2\x80\x99s speech with\r\never-increasing terror. Numbed with physical pain, she yet had\r\nsufficient mental vitality in her to realise the full horror of this\r\nterrible \xe2\x80\x9ceither\xe2\x80\x94or\xe2\x80\x9d he was once more putting before her; an\r\n\xe2\x80\x9ceither\xe2\x80\x94or\xe2\x80\x9d ten thousand times more appalling and horrible, than the\r\none he had suggested to her that fatal night at the ball.\r\n\r\nThis time it meant that she should keep still, and allow the husband\r\nshe worshipped to walk unconsciously to his death, or that she should,\r\nby trying to give him a word of warning, which perhaps might even be\r\nunavailing, actually give the signal for her own brother\xe2\x80\x99s death, and\r\nthat of three other unsuspecting men.\r\n\r\nShe could not see Chauvelin, but she could almost feel those keen, pale\r\neyes of his fixed maliciously upon her helpless form, and his hurried,\r\nwhispered words reached her ear, as the death-knell of her last faint,\r\nlingering hope.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, fair lady,\xe2\x80\x9d he added urbanely, \xe2\x80\x9cyou can have no interest in\r\nanyone save in St. Just, and all you need do for his safety is to\r\nremain where you are, and to keep silent. My men have strict orders to\r\nspare him in every way. As for that enigmatic Scarlet Pimpernel, what\r\nis he to you? Believe me, no warning from you could possibly save him.\r\nAnd now dear lady, let me remove this unpleasant coercion, which has\r\nbeen placed before your pretty mouth. You see I wish you to be\r\nperfectly free, in the choice which you are about to make.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer thoughts in a whirl, her temples aching, her nerves paralyzed, her\r\nbody numb with pain, Marguerite sat there, in the darkness which\r\nsurrounded her as with a pall. From where she sat she could not see the\r\nsea, but she heard the incessant mournful murmur of the incoming tide,\r\nwhich spoke of her dead hopes, her lost love, the husband she had with\r\nher own hand betrayed, and sent to his death.\r\n\r\nChauvelin removed the handkerchief from her mouth. She certainly did\r\nnot scream: at that moment, she had no strength to do anything but\r\nbarely to hold herself upright, and to force herself to think.\r\n\r\nOh! think! think! think! of what she should do. The minutes flew on; in\r\nthis awful stillness she could not tell how fast or how slowly; she\r\nheard nothing, she saw nothing: she did not feel the sweet-smelling\r\nautumn air, scented with the briny odour of the sea, she no longer\r\nheard the murmur of the waves, the occasional rattling of a pebble, as\r\nit rolled down some steep incline. More and more unreal did the whole\r\nsituation seem. It was impossible that she, Marguerite Blakeney, the\r\nqueen of London society, should actually be sitting here on this bit of\r\nlonely coast, in the middle of the night, side by side with a most\r\nbitter enemy: and oh! it was not possible that somewhere, not many\r\nhundred feet away perhaps, from where she stood, the being she had once\r\ndespised, but who now, in every moment of this weird, dreamlike life,\r\nbecame more and more dear\xe2\x80\x94it was not possible that _he_ was\r\nunconsciously, even now walking to his doom, whilst she did nothing to\r\nsave him.\r\n\r\nWhy did she not with unearthly screams, that would re-echo from one end\r\nof the lonely beach to the other, send out a warning to him to desist,\r\nto retrace his steps, for death lurked here whilst he advanced? Once or\r\ntwice the screams rose to her throat\xe2\x80\x94as if by instinct: then, before\r\nher eyes there stood the awful alternative: her brother and those three\r\nmen shot before her eyes, practically by her orders: she their\r\nmurderer.\r\n\r\nOh! that fiend in human shape, next to her, knew human\xe2\x80\x94female\xe2\x80\x94nature\r\nwell. He had played upon her feelings as a skilful musician plays upon\r\nan instrument. He had gauged her very thoughts to a nicety.\r\n\r\nShe could not give that signal\xe2\x80\x94for she was weak, and she was a woman.\r\nHow could she deliberately order Armand to be shot before her eyes, to\r\nhave his dear blood upon her head, he dying perhaps with a curse on\r\nher, upon his lips. And little Suzanne\xe2\x80\x99s father, too! he, an old man;\r\nand the others!\xe2\x80\x94oh! it was all too, too horrible.\r\n\r\nWait! wait! wait! how long? The early morning hours sped on, and yet it\r\nwas not dawn: the sea continued its incessant mournful murmur, the\r\nautumnal breeze sighed gently in the night: the lonely beach was\r\nsilent, even as the grave.\r\n\r\nSuddenly from somewhere, not very far away, a cheerful, strong voice\r\nwas heard singing \xe2\x80\x9cGod save the King!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXX.\r\nTHE SCHOONER\r\n\r\n\r\nMarguerite\xe2\x80\x99s aching heart stood still. She felt, more than she heard,\r\nthe men on the watch preparing for the fight. Her senses told her that\r\neach, with sword in hand, was crouching, ready for the spring.\r\n\r\nThe voice came nearer and nearer; in the vast immensity of these lonely\r\ncliffs, with the loud murmur of the sea below, it was impossible to say\r\nhow near, or how far, nor yet from which direction came that cheerful\r\nsinger, who sang to God to save his King, whilst he himself was in such\r\ndeadly danger. Faint at first, the voice grew louder and louder; from\r\ntime to time a small pebble detached itself apparently from beneath the\r\nfirm tread of the singer, and went rolling down the rocky cliffs to the\r\nbeach below.\r\n\r\nMarguerite as she heard, felt that her very life was slipping away, as\r\nif when that voice drew nearer, when that singer became entrapped . . .\r\n\r\nShe distinctly heard the click of Desgas\xe2\x80\x99 gun close to her. . . .\r\n\r\nNo! no! no! no! Oh, God in heaven! this cannot be! let Armand\xe2\x80\x99s blood\r\nthen be upon her own head! let her be branded as his murderer! let even\r\nhe, whom she loved, despise and loathe her for this, but God! oh God!\r\nsave him at any cost!\r\n\r\nWith a wild shriek, she sprang to her feet, and darted round the rock,\r\nagainst which she had been cowering; she saw the little red gleam\r\nthrough the chinks of the hut; she ran up to it and fell against its\r\nwooden walls, which she began to hammer with clenched fists in an\r\nalmost maniacal frenzy, while she shouted,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArmand! Armand! for God\xe2\x80\x99s sake fire! your leader is near! he is\r\ncoming! he is betrayed! Armand! Armand! fire in Heaven\xe2\x80\x99s name!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was seized and thrown to the ground. She lay there moaning,\r\nbruised, not caring, but still half-sobbing, half-shrieking,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPercy, my husband, for God\xe2\x80\x99s sake fly! Armand! Armand! why don\xe2\x80\x99t you\r\nfire?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne of you stop that woman screaming,\xe2\x80\x9d hissed Chauvelin, who hardly\r\ncould refrain from striking her.\r\n\r\nSomething was thrown over her face; she could not breathe, and perforce\r\nshe was silent.\r\n\r\nThe bold singer, too, had become silent, warned, no doubt, of his\r\nimpending danger by Marguerite\xe2\x80\x99s frantic shrieks. The men had sprung to\r\ntheir feet, there was no need for further silence on their part; the\r\nvery cliffs echoed the poor, heart-broken woman\xe2\x80\x99s screams.\r\n\r\nChauvelin, with a muttered oath, which boded no good to her, who had\r\ndared to upset his most cherished plans, had hastily shouted the word\r\nof command,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cInto it, my men, and let no one escape from that hut alive!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe moon had once more emerged from between the clouds: the darkness on\r\nthe cliffs had gone, giving place once more to brilliant, silvery\r\nlight. Some of the soldiers had rushed to the rough, wooden door of the\r\nhut, whilst one of them kept guard over Marguerite.\r\n\r\nThe door was partially open; one of the soldiers pushed it further, but\r\nwithin all was darkness, the charcoal fire only lighting with a dim,\r\nred light the furthest corner of the hut. The soldiers paused\r\nautomatically at the door, like machines waiting for further orders.\r\n\r\nChauvelin, who was prepared for a violent onslaught from within, and\r\nfor a vigorous resistance from the four fugitives, under cover of the\r\ndarkness, was for the moment paralyzed with astonishment when he saw\r\nthe soldiers standing there at attention, like sentries on guard,\r\nwhilst not a sound proceeded from the hut.\r\n\r\nFilled with strange, anxious foreboding, he, too, went to the door of\r\nthe hut, and peering into the gloom, he asked quickly,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the meaning of this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think, citoyen, that there is no one there now,\xe2\x80\x9d replied one of the\r\nsoldiers imperturbably.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have not let those four men go?\xe2\x80\x9d thundered Chauvelin, menacingly.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI ordered you to let no man escape alive!\xe2\x80\x94Quick, after them all of\r\nyou! Quick, in every direction!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe men, obedient as machines, rushed down the rocky incline towards\r\nthe beach, some going off to right and left, as fast as their feet\r\ncould carry them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou and your men will pay with your lives for this blunder, citoyen\r\nsergeant,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chauvelin viciously to the sergeant who had been in\r\ncharge of the men; \xe2\x80\x9cand you, too, citoyen,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, turning with a\r\nsnarl to Desgas, \xe2\x80\x9cfor disobeying my orders.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou ordered us to wait, citoyen, until the tall Englishman arrived and\r\njoined the four men in the hut. No one came,\xe2\x80\x9d said the sergeant\r\nsullenly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I ordered you just now, when the woman screamed, to rush in and\r\nlet no one escape.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, citoyen, the four men who were there before had been gone some\r\ntime, I think . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou think?\xe2\x80\x94You? . . .\xe2\x80\x9d said Chauvelin, almost choking with fury, \xe2\x80\x9cand\r\nyou let them go . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou ordered us to wait, citoyen,\xe2\x80\x9d protested the sergeant, \xe2\x80\x9cand to\r\nimplicitly obey your commands on pain of death. We waited.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI heard the men creep out of the hut, not many minutes after we took\r\ncover, and long before the woman screamed,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, as Chauvelin\r\nseemed still quite speechless with rage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHark!\xe2\x80\x9d said Desgas suddenly.\r\n\r\nIn the distance the sound of repeated firing was heard. Chauvelin tried\r\nto peer along the beach below, but as luck would have it, the fitful\r\nmoon once more hid her light behind a bank of clouds, and he could see\r\nnothing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne of you go into the hut and strike a light,\xe2\x80\x9d he stammered at last.\r\n\r\nStolidly the sergeant obeyed: he went up to the charcoal fire and lit\r\nthe small lantern he carried in his belt; it was evident that the hut\r\nwas quite empty.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich way did they go?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Chauvelin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI could not tell, citoyen,\xe2\x80\x9d said the sergeant; \xe2\x80\x9cthey went straight\r\ndown the cliff first, then disappeared behind some boulders.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush! what was that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAll three men listened attentively. In the far, very far distance,\r\ncould be heard faintly echoing and already dying away, the quick, sharp\r\nsplash of half a dozen oars. Chauvelin took out his handkerchief and\r\nwiped the perspiration from his forehead.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe schooner\xe2\x80\x99s boat!\xe2\x80\x9d was all he gasped.\r\n\r\nEvidently Armand St. Just and his three companions had managed to creep\r\nalong the side of the cliffs, whilst the men, like true soldiers of the\r\nwell-drilled Republican army, had with blind obedience, and in fear of\r\ntheir lives, implicitly obeyed Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s orders\xe2\x80\x94to wait for the tall\r\nEnglishman, who was the important capture.\r\n\r\nThey had no doubt reached one of the creeks which jut far out to sea on\r\nthis coast at intervals; behind this, the boat of the _Day Dream_ must\r\nhave been on the look-out for them, and they were by now safely on\r\nboard the British schooner.\r\n\r\nAs if to confirm this last supposition, the dull boom of a gun was\r\nheard from out at sea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe schooner, citoyen,\xe2\x80\x9d said Desgas, quietly; \xe2\x80\x9cshe\xe2\x80\x99s off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt needed all Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s nerve and presence of mind not to give way to\r\na useless and undignified access of rage. There was no doubt now, that\r\nonce again, that accursed British head had completely outwitted him.\r\nHow he had contrived to reach the hut, without being seen by one of the\r\nthirty soldiers who guarded the spot, was more than Chauvelin could\r\nconceive. That he had done so before the thirty men had arrived on the\r\ncliff was, of course, fairly clear, but how he had come over in Reuben\r\nGoldstein\xe2\x80\x99s cart, all the way from Calais, without being sighted by the\r\nvarious patrols on duty was impossible of explanation. It really seemed\r\nas if some potent Fate watched over that daring Scarlet Pimpernel, and\r\nhis astute enemy almost felt a superstitious shudder pass through him,\r\nas he looked round at the towering cliffs, and the loneliness of this\r\noutlying coast.\r\n\r\nBut surely this was reality! and the year of grace 1792: there were no\r\nfairies and hobgoblins about. Chauvelin and his thirty men had all\r\nheard with their own ears that accursed voice singing \xe2\x80\x9cGod save the\r\nKing,\xe2\x80\x9d fully twenty minutes _after_ they had all taken cover around the\r\nhut; by that time the four fugitives must have reached the creek, and\r\ngot into the boat, and the nearest creek was more than a mile from the\r\nhut.\r\n\r\nWhere had that daring singer got to? Unless Satan himself had lent him\r\nwings, he could not have covered that mile on a rocky cliff in the\r\nspace of two minutes; and only two minutes had elapsed between his song\r\nand the sound of the boat\xe2\x80\x99s oars away at sea. He must have remained\r\nbehind, and was even now hiding somewhere about the cliffs; the patrols\r\nwere still about, he would still be sighted, no doubt. Chauvelin felt\r\nhopeful once again.\r\n\r\nOne or two of the men, who had run after the fugitives, were now slowly\r\nworking their way up the cliff: one of them reached Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s side,\r\nat the very moment that this hope arose in the astute diplomatist\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nheart.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe were too late, citoyen,\xe2\x80\x9d the soldier said, \xe2\x80\x9cwe reached the beach\r\njust before the moon was hidden by that bank of clouds. The boat had\r\nundoubtedly been on the look-out behind that first creek, a mile off,\r\nbut she had shoved off some time ago, when we got to the beach, and was\r\nalready some way out to sea. We fired after her, but of course, it was\r\nno good. She was making straight and quickly for the schooner. We saw\r\nher very clearly in the moonlight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chauvelin, with eager impatience, \xe2\x80\x9cshe had shoved off some\r\ntime ago, you said, and the nearest creek is a mile further on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, citoyen! I ran all the way, straight to the beach, though I\r\nguessed the boat would have waited somewhere near the creek, as the\r\ntide would reach there earliest. The boat must have shoved off some\r\nminutes before the woman began to scream.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n Some minutes before the woman began to scream! Then Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s hopes\r\n had not deceived him. The Scarlet Pimpernel may have contrived to send\r\n the fugitives on ahead by the boat, but he himself had not had time to\r\n reach it; he was still on shore, and all the roads were well\r\n patrolled. At any rate, all was not yet lost, and would not be, whilst\r\n that impudent Britisher was still on French soil.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBring the light in here!\xe2\x80\x9d he commanded eagerly, as he once more\r\nentered the hut.\r\n\r\nThe sergeant brought his lantern, and together the two men explored the\r\nlittle place: with a rapid glance Chauvelin noted its contents: the\r\ncauldron placed close under an aperture in the wall, and containing the\r\nlast few dying embers of burned charcoal, a couple of stools,\r\noverturned as if in the haste of sudden departure, then the fisherman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntools and his nets lying in one corner, and beside them, something\r\nsmall and white.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPick that up,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chauvelin to the sergeant, pointing to this white\r\nscrap, \xe2\x80\x9cand bring it to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was a crumpled piece of paper, evidently forgotten there by the\r\nfugitives, in their hurry to get away. The sergeant, much awed by the\r\ncitoyen\xe2\x80\x99s obvious rage and impatience, picked the paper up and handed\r\nit respectfully to Chauvelin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRead it, sergeant,\xe2\x80\x9d said the latter curtly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is almost illegible, citoyen . . . a fearful scrawl. . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI ordered you to read it,\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Chauvelin, viciously.\r\n\r\nThe sergeant, by the light of his lantern, began deciphering the few\r\nhastily scrawled words.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot quite reach you, without risking your lives and endangering\r\nthe success of your rescue. When you receive this, wait two minutes,\r\nthen creep out of the hut one by one, turn to your left sharply, and\r\ncreep cautiously down the cliff; keep to the left all the time, till\r\nyou reach the first rock, which you see jutting far out to sea\xe2\x80\x94behind\r\nit in the creek the boat is on the look-out for you\xe2\x80\x94give a long, sharp\r\nwhistle\xe2\x80\x94she will come up\xe2\x80\x94get into her\xe2\x80\x94my men will row you to the\r\nschooner, and thence to England and safety\xe2\x80\x94once on board the _Day\r\nDream_ send the boat back for me, tell my men that I shall be at the\r\ncreek, which is in a direct line opposite the \xe2\x80\x98Chat Gris\xe2\x80\x99 near Calais.\r\nThey know it. I shall be there as soon as possible\xe2\x80\x94they must wait for\r\nme at a safe distance out at sea, till they hear the usual signal. Do\r\nnot delay\xe2\x80\x94and obey these instructions implicitly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen there is the signature, citoyen,\xe2\x80\x9d added the sergeant, as he\r\nhanded the paper back to Chauvelin.\r\n\r\nBut the latter had not waited an instant. One phrase of the momentous\r\nscrawl had caught his ear. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall be at the creek which is in a\r\ndirect line opposite the \xe2\x80\x98Chat Gris\xe2\x80\x99 near Calais\xe2\x80\x9d: that phrase might\r\nyet mean victory for him.\r\n \xe2\x80\x9cWhich of you knows this coast well?\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted to his men who now\r\n one by one had all returned from their fruitless run, and were all\r\n assembled once more round the hut.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do, citoyen,\xe2\x80\x9d said one of them, \xe2\x80\x9cI was born in Calais, and know\r\nevery stone of these cliffs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is a creek in a direct line from the \xe2\x80\x98Chat Gris\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is, citoyen. I know it well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Englishman is hoping to reach that creek. He does _not_ know every\r\nstone of these cliffs, he may go there by the longest way round, and in\r\nany case he will proceed cautiously for fear of the patrols. At any\r\nrate, there is a chance to get him yet. A thousand francs to each man\r\nwho gets to that creek before that long-legged Englishman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know a short cut across the cliffs,\xe2\x80\x9d said the soldier, and with an\r\nenthusiastic shout, he rushed forward, followed closely by his\r\ncomrades.\r\n\r\nWithin a few minutes their running footsteps had died away in the\r\ndistance. Chauvelin listened to them for a moment; the promise of the\r\nreward was lending spurs to the soldiers of the Republic. The gleam of\r\nhate and anticipated triumph was once more apparent on his face.\r\n\r\nClose to him Desgas still stood mute and impassive, waiting for further\r\norders, whilst two soldiers were kneeling beside the prostrate form of\r\nMarguerite. Chauvelin gave his secretary a vicious look. His well-laid\r\nplan had failed, its sequel was problematical; there was still a great\r\nchance now that the Scarlet Pimpernel might yet escape, and Chauvelin,\r\nwith that unreasoning fury, which sometimes assails a strong nature,\r\nwas longing to vent his rage on somebody.\r\n\r\nThe soldiers were holding Marguerite pinioned to the ground, though\r\nshe, poor soul, was not making the faintest struggle. Overwrought\r\nnature had at last peremptorily asserted herself, and she lay there in\r\na dead swoon: her eyes circled by deep purple lines, that told of long,\r\nsleepless nights, her hair matted and damp round her forehead, her lips\r\nparted in a sharp curve that spoke of physical pain.\r\n\r\nThe cleverest woman in Europe, the elegant and fashionable Lady\r\nBlakeney, who had dazzled London society with her beauty, her wit and\r\nher extravagances, presented a very pathetic picture of tired-out,\r\nsuffering womanhood, which would have appealed to any, but the hard,\r\nvengeful heart of her baffled enemy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is no use mounting guard over a woman who is half dead,\xe2\x80\x9d he said\r\nspitefully to the soldiers, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen you have allowed five men who were\r\nvery much alive to escape.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nObediently the soldiers rose to their feet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99d better try and find that footpath again for me, and that\r\nbroken-down cart we left on the road.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen suddenly a bright idea seemed to strike him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! by-the-bye! where is the Jew?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClose by here, citoyen,\xe2\x80\x9d said Desgas; \xe2\x80\x9cI gagged him and tied his legs\r\ntogether as you commanded.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFrom the immediate vicinity, a plaintive moan reached Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s ears.\r\nHe followed his secretary, who led the way to the other side of the\r\nhut, where, fallen into an absolute heap of dejection, with his legs\r\ntightly pinioned together and his mouth gagged, lay the unfortunate\r\ndescendant of Israel.\r\n\r\nHis face in the silvery light of the moon looked positively ghastly\r\nwith terror: his eyes were wide open and almost glassy, and his whole\r\nbody was trembling, as if with ague, while a piteous wail escaped his\r\nbloodless lips. The rope which had originally been wound round his\r\nshoulders and arms had evidently given way, for it lay in a tangle\r\nabout his body, but he seemed quite unconscious of this, for he had not\r\nmade the slightest attempt to move from the place where Desgas had\r\noriginally put him: like a terrified chicken which looks upon a line of\r\nwhite chalk, drawn on a table, as on a string which paralyzes its\r\nmovements.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBring the cowardly brute here,\xe2\x80\x9d commanded Chauvelin.\r\n\r\nHe certainly felt exceedingly vicious, and since he had no reasonable\r\ngrounds for venting his ill-humour on the soldiers who had but too\r\npunctually obeyed his orders, he felt that the son of the despised race\r\nwould prove an excellent butt. With true French contempt of the Jew,\r\nwhich has survived the lapse of centuries even to this day, he would\r\nnot go too near him, but said with biting sarcasm, as the wretched old\r\nman was brought in full light of the moon by the two soldiers,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose now, that being a Jew, you have a good memory for bargains?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnswer!\xe2\x80\x9d he again commanded, as the Jew with trembling lips seemed too\r\nfrightened to speak.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, your Honour,\xe2\x80\x9d stammered the poor wretch.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou remember, then, the one you and I made together in Calais, when\r\nyou undertook to overtake Reuben Goldstein, his nag and my friend the\r\ntall stranger? Eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cB . . . b . . . but . . . your Honour . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no \xe2\x80\x98but.\xe2\x80\x99 I said, do you remember?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cY . . . y . . . y . . . yes . . . your Honour!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat was the bargain?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was dead silence. The unfortunate man looked round at the great\r\ncliffs, the moon above, the stolid faces of the soldiers, and even at\r\nthe poor, prostrate, inanimate woman close by, but said nothing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you speak?\xe2\x80\x9d thundered Chauvelin, menacingly.\r\n\r\nHe did try, poor wretch, but, obviously, he could not. There was no\r\ndoubt, however, that he knew what to expect from the stern man before\r\nhim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour Honour . . .\xe2\x80\x9d he ventured imploringly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSince your terror seems to have paralyzed your tongue,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nChauvelin, sarcastically, \xe2\x80\x9cI must needs refresh your memory. It was\r\nagreed between us, that if we overtook my friend the tall stranger,\r\nbefore he reached this place, you were to have ten pieces of gold.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA low moan escaped from the Jew\xe2\x80\x99s trembling lips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut,\xe2\x80\x9d added Chauvelin, with slow emphasis, \xe2\x80\x9cif you deceived me in your\r\npromise, you were to have a sound beating, one that would teach you not\r\nto tell lies.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did not, your Honour; I swear it by Abraham . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd by all the other patriarchs, I know. Unfortunately, they are still\r\nin Hades, I believe, according to your creed, and cannot help you much\r\nin your present trouble. Now, you did not fulfil your share of the\r\nbargain, but I am ready to fulfil mine. Here,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, turning to the\r\nsoldiers, \xe2\x80\x9cthe buckle-end of your two belts to this confounded Jew.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs the soldiers obediently unbuckled their heavy leather belts, the Jew\r\nset up a howl that surely would have been enough to bring all the\r\npatriarchs out of Hades and elsewhere, to defend their descendant from\r\nthe brutality of this French official.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I can rely on you, citoyen soldiers,\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Chauvelin,\r\nmaliciously, \xe2\x80\x9cto give this old liar the best and soundest beating he\r\nhas ever experienced. But don\xe2\x80\x99t kill him,\xe2\x80\x9d he added drily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will obey, citoyen,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the soldiers as imperturbably as ever.\r\n\r\nHe did not wait to see his orders carried out: he knew that he could\r\ntrust these soldiers\xe2\x80\x94who were still smarting under his rebuke\xe2\x80\x94not to\r\nmince matters, when given a free hand to belabour a third party.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen that lumbering coward has had his punishment,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to Desgas,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthe men can guide us as far as the cart, and one of them can drive us\r\nin it back to Calais. The Jew and the woman can look after each other,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe added roughly, \xe2\x80\x9cuntil we can send somebody for them in the morning.\r\nThey can\xe2\x80\x99t run away very far, in their present condition, and we cannot\r\nbe troubled with them just now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChauvelin had not given up all hope. His men, he knew, were spurred on\r\nby the hope of the reward. That enigmatic and audacious Scarlet\r\nPimpernel, alone and with thirty men at his heels, could not reasonably\r\nbe expected to escape a second time.\r\n\r\nBut he felt less sure now: the Englishman\xe2\x80\x99s audacity had baffled him\r\nonce, whilst the wooden-headed stupidity of the soldiers, and the\r\ninterference of a woman had turned his hand, which held all the trumps,\r\ninto a losing one. If Marguerite had not taken up his time, if the\r\nsoldiers had had a grain of intelligence, if . . . it was a long \xe2\x80\x9cif,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nand Chauvelin stood for a moment quite still, and enrolled thirty odd\r\npeople in one long, overwhelming anathema. Nature, poetic, silent,\r\nbalmy, the bright moon, the calm, silvery sea spoke of beauty and of\r\nrest, and Chauvelin cursed nature, cursed man and woman, and, above\r\nall, he cursed all long-legged, meddlesome British enigmas with one\r\ngigantic curse.\r\n\r\nThe howls of the Jew behind him, undergoing his punishment, sent a balm\r\nthrough his heart, overburdened as it was with revengeful malice. He\r\nsmiled. It eased his mind to think that some human being at least was,\r\nlike himself, not altogether at peace with mankind.\r\n\r\nHe turned and took a last look at the lonely bit of coast, where stood\r\nthe wooden hut, now bathed in moonlight, the scene of the greatest\r\ndiscomfiture ever experienced by a leading member of the Committee of\r\nPublic Safety.\r\n\r\nAgainst a rock, on a hard bed of stone, lay the unconscious figure of\r\nMarguerite Blakeney, while some few paces further on, the unfortunate\r\nJew was receiving on his broad back the blows of two stout leather\r\nbelts, wielded by the stolid arms of two sturdy soldiers of the\r\nRepublic. The howls of Benjamin Rosenbaum were fit to make the dead\r\nrise from their graves. They must have wakened all the gulls from\r\nsleep, and made them look down with great interest at the doings of the\r\nlords of the creation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat will do,\xe2\x80\x9d commanded Chauvelin, as the Jew\xe2\x80\x99s moans became more\r\nfeeble, and the poor wretch seemed to have fainted away, \xe2\x80\x9cwe don\xe2\x80\x99t want\r\nto kill him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nObediently the soldiers buckled on their belts, one of them viciously\r\nkicking the Jew to one side.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLeave him there,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chauvelin, \xe2\x80\x9cand lead the way now quickly to the\r\ncart. I\xe2\x80\x99ll follow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe walked up to where Marguerite lay, and looked down into her face.\r\nShe had evidently recovered consciousness, and was making feeble\r\nefforts to raise herself. Her large, blue eyes were looking at the\r\nmoonlit scene round her with a scared and terrified look; they rested\r\nwith a mixture of horror and pity on the Jew, whose luckless fate and\r\nwild howls had been the first signs that struck her, with her returning\r\nsenses; then she caught sight of Chauvelin, in his neat, dark clothes,\r\nwhich seemed hardly crumpled after the stirring events of the last few\r\nhours. He was smiling sarcastically, and his pale eyes peered down at\r\nher with a look of intense malice.\r\n\r\nWith mock gallantry, he stooped and raised her icy-cold hand to his\r\nlips, which sent a thrill of indescribable loathing through\r\nMarguerite\xe2\x80\x99s weary frame.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI much regret, fair lady,\xe2\x80\x9d he said in his most suave tones, \xe2\x80\x9cthat\r\ncircumstances, over which I have no control, compel me to leave you\r\nhere for the moment. But I go away, secure in the knowledge that I do\r\nnot leave you unprotected. Our friend Benjamin here, though a trifle\r\nthe worse for wear at the present moment, will prove a gallant defender\r\nof your fair person, I have no doubt. At dawn I will send an escort for\r\nyou; until then, I feel sure that you will find him devoted, though\r\nperhaps a trifle slow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarguerite only had the strength to turn her head away. Her heart was\r\nbroken with cruel anguish. One awful thought had returned to her mind,\r\ntogether with gathering consciousness: \xe2\x80\x9cWhat had become of Percy?\xe2\x80\x94What\r\nof Armand?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe knew nothing of what had happened after she heard the cheerful\r\nsong, \xe2\x80\x9cGod save the King,\xe2\x80\x9d which she believed to be the signal of\r\ndeath.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI, myself,\xe2\x80\x9d concluded Chauvelin, \xe2\x80\x9cmust now very reluctantly leave you.\r\n_Au revoir_, fair lady. We meet, I hope, soon in London. Shall I see\r\nyou at the Prince of Wales\xe2\x80\x99 garden party?\xe2\x80\x94No?\xe2\x80\x94Ah, well, _au\r\nrevoir!_\xe2\x80\x94Remember me, I pray, to Sir Percy Blakeney.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd, with a last ironical smile and bow, he once more kissed her hand,\r\nand disappeared down the footpath in the wake of the soldiers, and\r\nfollowed by the imperturbable Desgas.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXI.\r\nTHE ESCAPE\r\n\r\n\r\nMarguerite listened\xe2\x80\x94half-dazed as she was\xe2\x80\x94to the fast-retreating, firm\r\nfootsteps of the four men.\r\n\r\nAll nature was so still that she, lying with her ear close to the\r\nground, could distinctly trace the sound of their tread, as they\r\nultimately turned into the road, and presently the faint echo of the\r\nold cart-wheels, the halting gait of the lean nag, told her that her\r\nenemy was a quarter of a league away. How long she lay there she knew\r\nnot. She had lost count of time; dreamily she looked up at the moonlit\r\nsky, and listened to the monotonous roll of the waves.\r\n\r\nThe invigorating scent of the sea was nectar to her wearied body, the\r\nimmensity of the lonely cliffs was silent and dreamlike. Her brain only\r\nremained conscious of its ceaseless, its intolerable torture of\r\nuncertainty.\r\n\r\nShe did not know!\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nShe did not know whether Percy was even now, at this moment, in the\r\nhands of the soldiers of the Republic, enduring\xe2\x80\x94as she had done\r\nherself\xe2\x80\x94the gibes and jeers of his malicious enemy. She did not know,\r\non the other hand, whether Armand\xe2\x80\x99s lifeless body did not lie there, in\r\nthe hut, whilst Percy had escaped, only to hear that his wife\xe2\x80\x99s hands\r\nhad guided the human bloodhounds to the murder of Armand and his\r\nfriends.\r\n\r\nThe physical pain of utter weariness was so great, that she hoped\r\nconfidently her tired body could rest here for ever, after all the\r\nturmoil, the passion, and the intrigues of the last few days\xe2\x80\x94here,\r\nbeneath that clear sky, within sound of the sea, and with this balmy\r\nautumn breeze whispering to her a last lullaby. All was so solitary, so\r\nsilent, like unto dreamland. Even the last faint echo of the distant\r\ncart had long ago died away, afar.\r\n\r\nSuddenly . . . a sound . . . the strangest, undoubtedly, that these\r\nlonely cliffs of France had ever heard, broke the silent solemnity of\r\nthe shore.\r\n\r\nSo strange a sound was it that the gentle breeze ceased to murmur, the\r\ntiny pebbles to roll down the steep incline! So strange, that\r\nMarguerite, wearied, overwrought as she was, thought that the\r\nbeneficial unconsciousness of the approach of death was playing her\r\nhalf-sleeping senses a weird and elusive trick.\r\n\r\nIt was the sound of a good, solid, absolutely British \xe2\x80\x9cDamn!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe sea-gulls in their nests awoke and looked round in astonishment; a\r\ndistant and solitary owl set up a midnight hoot, the tall cliffs\r\nfrowned down majestically at the strange, unheard-of sacrilege.\r\n\r\nMarguerite did not trust her ears. Half-raising herself on her hands,\r\nshe strained every sense to see or hear, to know the meaning of this\r\nvery earthly sound.\r\n\r\nAll was still again for the space of a few seconds; the same silence\r\nonce more fell upon the great and lonely vastness.\r\n\r\nThen Marguerite, who had listened as in a trance, who felt she must be\r\ndreaming with that cool, magnetic moonlight overhead, heard again; and\r\nthis time her heart stood still, her eyes large and dilated, looked\r\nround her, not daring to trust to her other sense.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOdd\xe2\x80\x99s life! but I wish those demmed fellows had not hit quite so\r\nhard!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis time it was quite unmistakable, only one particular pair of\r\nessentially British lips could have uttered those words, in sleepy,\r\ndrawly, affected tones.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDamn!\xe2\x80\x9d repeated those same British lips, emphatically. \xe2\x80\x9cZounds! but\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m as weak as a rat!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn a moment Marguerite was on her feet.\r\n\r\nWas she dreaming? Were those great, stony cliffs the gates of paradise?\r\nWas the fragrant breath of the breeze suddenly caused by the flutter of\r\nangels\xe2\x80\x99 wings, bringing tidings of unearthly joys to her, after all her\r\nsuffering, or\xe2\x80\x94faint and ill\xe2\x80\x94was she the prey of delirium?\r\n\r\nShe listened again, and once again she heard the same very earthly\r\nsounds of good, honest British language, not the least akin to\r\nwhisperings from paradise or flutter of angels\xe2\x80\x99 wings.\r\n\r\nShe looked round her eagerly at the tall cliffs, the lonely hut, the\r\ngreat stretch of rocky beach. Somewhere there, above or below her,\r\nbehind a boulder or inside a crevice, but still hidden from her\r\nlonging, feverish eyes, must be the owner of that voice, which once\r\nused to irritate her, but which now would make her the happiest woman\r\nin Europe, if only she could locate it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPercy! Percy!\xe2\x80\x9d she shrieked hysterically, tortured between doubt and\r\nhope, \xe2\x80\x9cI am here! Come to me! Where are you? Percy! Percy! . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s all very well calling me, m\xe2\x80\x99dear!\xe2\x80\x9d said the same sleepy, drawly\r\nvoice, \xe2\x80\x9cbut odd\xe2\x80\x99s my life, I cannot come to you: those demmed\r\nfrog-eaters have trussed me like a goose on a spit, and I am as weak as\r\na mouse . . . I cannot get away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd still Marguerite did not understand. She did not realise for at\r\nleast another ten seconds whence came that voice, so drawly, so dear,\r\nbut alas! with a strange accent of weakness and of suffering. There was\r\nno one within sight . . . except by that rock . . . Great God! . . .\r\nthe Jew! . . . Was she mad or dreaming? . . .\r\n\r\nHis back was against the pale moonlight, he was half-crouching, trying\r\nvainly to raise himself with his arms tightly pinioned. Marguerite ran\r\nup to him, took his head in both her hands . . . and looked straight\r\ninto a pair of blue eyes, good-natured, even a trifle amused\xe2\x80\x94shining\r\nout of the weird and distorted mask of the Jew.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPercy! . . . Percy! . . . my husband!\xe2\x80\x9d she gasped, faint with the\r\nfulness of her joy. \xe2\x80\x9cThank God! Thank God!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLa! m\xe2\x80\x99dear,\xe2\x80\x9d he rejoined good-humouredly, \xe2\x80\x9cwe will both do that anon,\r\nan you think you can loosen these demmed ropes, and release me from my\r\ninelegant attitude.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe had no knife, her fingers were numb and weak, but she worked away\r\nwith her teeth, while great welcome tears poured from her eyes, onto\r\nthose poor, pinioned hands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOdd\xe2\x80\x99s life!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, when at last, after frantic efforts on her part,\r\nthe ropes seemed at last to be giving way, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I marvel whether it has\r\never happened before, that an English gentleman allowed himself to be\r\nlicked by a demmed foreigner, and made no attempt to give as good as he\r\ngot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was very obvious that he was exhausted from sheer physical pain, and\r\nwhen at last the rope gave way, he fell in a heap against the rock.\r\n\r\nMarguerite looked helplessly round her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! for a drop of water on this awful beach!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried in agony,\r\nseeing that he was ready to faint again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, m\xe2\x80\x99dear,\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured with his good-humoured smile, \xe2\x80\x9cpersonally I\r\nshould prefer a drop of good French brandy! an you\xe2\x80\x99ll dive in the\r\npocket of this dirty old garment, you\xe2\x80\x99ll find my flask. . . . I am\r\ndemmed if I can move.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen he had drunk some brandy, he forced Marguerite to do likewise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLa! that\xe2\x80\x99s better now! Eh! little woman?\xe2\x80\x9d he said, with a sigh of\r\nsatisfaction. \xe2\x80\x9cHeigh-ho! but this is a queer rig-up for Sir Percy\r\nBlakeney, Bart., to be found in by his lady, and no mistake. Begad!\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nadded, passing his hand over his chin, \xe2\x80\x9cI haven\xe2\x80\x99t been shaved for\r\nnearly twenty hours: I must look a disgusting object. As for these\r\ncurls . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd laughingly he took off the disfiguring wig and curls, and stretched\r\nout his long limbs, which were cramped from many hours\xe2\x80\x99 stooping. Then\r\nhe bent forward and looked long and searchingly into his wife\xe2\x80\x99s blue\r\neyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPercy,\xe2\x80\x9d she whispered, while a deep blush suffused her delicate cheeks\r\nand neck, \xe2\x80\x9cif you only knew . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do know, dear . . . everything,\xe2\x80\x9d he said with infinite gentleness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd can you ever forgive?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have naught to forgive, sweetheart; your heroism, your devotion,\r\nwhich I, alas! so little deserved, have more than atoned for that\r\nunfortunate episode at the ball.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you knew? . . .\xe2\x80\x9d she whispered, \xe2\x80\x9call the time . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes!\xe2\x80\x9d he replied tenderly, \xe2\x80\x9cI knew . . . all the time. . . . But,\r\nbegad! had I but known what a noble heart yours was, my Margot, I\r\nshould have trusted you, as you deserved to be trusted, and you would\r\nnot have had to undergo the terrible sufferings of the past few hours,\r\nin order to run after a husband, who has done so much that needs\r\nforgiveness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey were sitting side by side, leaning up against a rock, and he had\r\nrested his aching head on her shoulder. She certainly now deserved the\r\nname of \xe2\x80\x9cthe happiest woman in Europe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a case of the blind leading the lame, sweetheart, is it not?\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid with his good-natured smile of old. \xe2\x80\x9cOdd\xe2\x80\x99s life! but I do not know\r\nwhich are the more sore, my shoulders or your little feet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe bent forward to kiss them, for they peeped out through her torn\r\nstockings, and bore pathetic witness to her endurance and devotion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut Armand . . .\xe2\x80\x9d she said, with sudden terror and remorse, as in the\r\nmidst of her happiness the image of the beloved brother, for whose sake\r\nshe had so deeply sinned, rose now before her mind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! have no fear for Armand, sweetheart,\xe2\x80\x9d he said tenderly, \xe2\x80\x9cdid I not\r\npledge you my word that he should be safe? He with de Tournay and the\r\nothers are even now on board the _Day Dream_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how?\xe2\x80\x9d she gasped, \xe2\x80\x9cI do not understand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYet, \xe2\x80\x99tis simple enough, m\xe2\x80\x99dear,\xe2\x80\x9d he said with that funny, half-shy,\r\nhalf-inane laugh of his, \xe2\x80\x9cyou see! when I found that that brute\r\nChauvelin meant to stick to me like a leech, I thought the best thing I\r\ncould do, as I could not shake him off, was to take him along with me.\r\nI had to get to Armand and the others somehow, and all the roads were\r\npatrolled, and everyone on the look-out for your humble servant. I knew\r\nthat when I slipped through Chauvelin\xe2\x80\x99s fingers at the \xe2\x80\x98Chat Gris,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nthat he would lie in wait for me here, whichever way I took.
